## Introduction

### Core Premise: A Template for Understanding Debt Crises

- **Ray Dalio** writes on the 10th anniversary of the 2008 financial crisis to share his template for understanding how all debt crises work
- Goal: reduce likelihood of future debt crises and improve crisis management
- Perspective differs from economists/policymakers: as an investor betting on market movements, Dalio focuses on **relative values and capital flows** that drive cycles

### Dalio's Historical Learning Method

- After being "repeatedly bit by events I never encountered before," Dalio studied all major economic/market movements in history chronologically
- Method: experience them as **virtual experiences**—day by day, month by month—placing market bets as if knowing only what happened until that moment
- Provides broader perspective than personal experience alone
- Personal crises experienced: Bretton Woods breakdown (1966–1971), 1970s inflation bubble and 1978–82 bursting, Latin American inflationary depression (1980s), Japanese bubble (late 1980s) and 1988–1991 bursting, tech bubble (2000), Great Deleveraging (2008)
- Historical crises studied: Roman Empire collapse (5th century), US debt restructuring (1789), Germany's Weimar Republic (1920s), Great Depression and WWII (1930–45), many others

### The Power of Pattern Recognition

- By examining many cases of each economic phenomenon and averaging them, Dalio visualized cause-effect relationships
- Created **archetypal models** (templates): archetypal business cycle, archetypal big debt cycle, archetypal deflationary deleveraging, archetypal inflationary deleveraging
- Pattern recognition: "Rather than seeing lots of individual things happening, I saw fewer things happening over and over again, like an experienced doctor who sees each case of a certain type of disease unfolding as 'another one of those'"

### Development with Bridgewater Associates

- Template developed with partners at **Bridgewater Associates** to prepare for unprecedented storms
- Built computer decision-making systems detailing reactions to virtually every possible occurrence
- Example: Eight years before 2008, built a **"depression gauge"** programmed for 1929–32 scenarios, allowing Bridgewater to perform well when others failed

### Book Structure

Three perspectives on debt crises:
1. **Part 1**: Template for the "Archetypal Big Debt Cycle" (most synthesized)
2. **Part 2**: Three Iconic Case Studies examined in detail (most granular)—US 2007–2011 (Great Recession), US 1928–1937 (deflationary depression), Germany 1918–1924 (inflationary depression)
3. **Part 3**: Compendium of 48 Case Studies covering last 100 years (chart form)

> "I guarantee that if you take the trouble to understand each of these three perspectives, you will see big debt crises very differently than you did before."

- Comparing all three shows cases transpiring essentially the same way while revealing differences that prompt deeper understanding
- Includes glossary of economic terms and reference to 30-minute video "How the Economic Machine Works" at www.economicprinciples.org

## Part 1: The Archetypal Big Debt Cycle

### How I Think about Credit and Debt

**Fundamental definitions:**
- **Credit**: the giving of buying power, granted in exchange for a promise to pay it back (debt)
- **Debt**: the promise to repay borrowed buying power

> "Clearly, giving the ability to make purchases by providing credit is, in and of itself, a good thing, and not providing the power to buy and do good things can be a bad thing."

- Problem with debt: inability to pay it back
- **Central question**: whether rapid credit/debt growth is good or bad hinges on what credit produces and how debt is serviced

### Personal Perspective on Debt

- Dalio's personal preference: saving over borrowing (influenced by father)
- Learning: **too little credit/debt growth can create equally bad economic problems as having too much**—costs come as foregone opportunities
- For society, policymakers have controls individuals don't

### The Productivity Test

> "Whether or not more credit is desirable depends on whether the borrowed money is used productively enough to generate sufficient income to service the debt."

- If productive use occurs: resources well allocated, both lender and borrower benefit economically
- Must consider secondary/indirect economics alongside primary/direct economics
- Example: Education, infrastructure replacement may be cost-effective despite fiscal conservatism opposing borrowing

### Cost-Benefit Analysis of Bad Debt

**Subway system example:**
- $1 billion project financed with debt
- Revenues only 50% of expected, debt written down 50%
- Question: Is subway worth $500 million more than budgeted? (~2% per year over 25-year lifespan)
- May still be better than not having the subway

**Real-world bad debt scenario:**
- Really bad debt losses: ~40% of loan value can't be paid back
- Bad loans ~20% of all outstanding loans
- Losses = ~8% of total debt
- Total debt ~200% of income (GDP)
- Shortfall ~16% of GDP
- If "socialized" and spread over 15 years: ~1% per year (tolerable)

> "The downside risks of having a significant amount of debt depends a lot on the willingness and the ability of policy makers to spread out the losses arising from bad debts."

**Two critical factors for policy maker success:**
1. Whether debt is **denominated in the currency they control**
2. Whether they have **influence over creditor/debtor behavior**

### Are Debt Crises Inevitable?

- Throughout history, only a few well-disciplined countries have avoided debt crises
- Lending never done perfectly, often done badly due to **cycle effects on psychology** producing bubbles and busts
- Policy makers typically err on side of being too loose with credit because:
  - Near-term rewards (faster growth) seem to justify it
  - Politically easier to allow easy credit (guarantees, easing monetary policies) than tight credit
- This is the main reason for big debt cycles

### Why Do Debt Crises Come in Cycles?

> "I am talking about nothing more than logically-driven series of events that recur in patterns."

- In market-based economies, credit expansions/contractions drive economic cycles for logical reasons
- Patterns similar but sequences neither pre-destined nor identical in timing

**The fundamental cycle mechanism:**
- Borrowing = spending more than you make
- You're borrowing from your **future self**
- Creates future period requiring spending less than you make to pay back
- This pattern (borrow → spend more → spend less) resembles a cycle
- True for individuals and national economies
- Borrowing sets a **mechanical, predictable series of events** into motion

### The Monopoly Analogy

**Standard Monopoly:**
- Early game: lots of cash, few properties → convert cash to property
- As properties accumulate, more cash needed for rents
- Some forced to sell property at discounted prices
- Early: "property is king," later: "cash is king"
- Best players balance property and cash mix

**Monopoly with Banking:**
- Adding loans/deposits: players borrow to buy property
- Deposit idle cash to earn interest (giving bank more to lend)
- Players buy/sell properties on credit
- Debt-financed hotel spending grows to multiples of money in existence
- Eventually debtors short on cash for rents and debt service
- Bank faces trouble as depositors withdraw while debtors fall behind
- Without intervention: banks and debtors go broke, economy contracts
- Over time, repeated expansion/contraction cycles create conditions for **big, long-term debt crisis**

### The Self-Reinforcing Nature of Credit Cycles

**Upward phase:**
- Lending supports spending/investment → supports incomes/asset prices
- Increased incomes/asset prices support further borrowing/spending
- Borrowing lifts spending/incomes **above consistent productivity growth**
- Peak lending based on expectation that above-trend growth continues indefinitely
- Eventually income falls below loan costs

**Characteristics of vulnerable economies:**
- Growth significantly supported by **debt-financed fixed investments, real estate, infrastructure**
- Fast building rates not sustainable
- Example: $10M/year office building → $0/year when finished
- Demand for workers/materials collapses
- Growth/income/debt-servicing ability depends on other demand
- Very typical of **emerging economies** (so much building to do)

### Emerging Market Cyclicality

- Changes in **competitiveness** due to relative income changes amplify cycles
- Typically: cheap labor, bad infrastructure → build infrastructure → export boom → rising incomes
- Growth rate from exports slows as income rises, wage competitiveness declines
- Example: Japan's 70-year experience

### Bubble Dynamics

- **Bubbles**: unrealistic expectations and reckless lending create critical mass of bad loans
- Eventually apparent to bankers and central bankers → bubble deflates
- Classic warning sign: **money borrowed to make debt service payments** (compounds indebtedness)

### The Reversal at the Top

- When money/credit growth curtailed and/or higher lending standards imposed: credit growth and spending slow
- More debt service problems emerge
- **Top of upward phase** at hand
- Central banks tighten monetary policy to contain dangerously fast credit growth
- Often accelerates decline (though would have happened anyway, just later)
- When debt service costs > amount that can be borrowed to finance spending: **upward cycle reverses**
- New lending slows, pressure on debtors to pay increases
- Clearer debtors are struggling → less new lending
- Spending/investment slowdown slows income growth further, asset prices decline

### Lender Crisis and Systemic Risk

- When borrowers can't meet obligations to lending institutions → lending institutions can't meet obligations to their creditors
- Policy makers must handle lending institutions first
- Most extreme pressures on: **most highly leveraged lenders with most concentrated exposures to failed borrowers**
- These pose biggest **knock-on effect risks** for creditworthy buyers and broader economy
- Typically banks, but broader set now includes: insurance companies, non-bank trusts, broker-dealers, special purpose vehicles

### Two Main Long-Term Problems from Debt Cycles

1. **Losses from expected debt service payments not being made**
   - Can lead to smaller periodic payments and/or debt write-downs (accepting less than owed)
   - Annual shortfall if expecting 4% and getting 2% or 0%
   - If debt marked down: much bigger one-time loss (e.g., 50%)

2. **Reduction of lending and spending it was financing going forward**
   - Even after crisis resolved, entities that borrowed too much unlikely to generate same spending levels
   - Has implications that must be considered

### Can Most Debt Crises Be Managed Well?

- Some cycles moderate (bumps), some extreme (crashes)
- This study examines extreme cases: real GDP declines >3%
- Based on examinations, Dalio believes policy makers **can manage them well** in almost every case where debts denominated in country's own currency
- Policy maker flexibility allows spreading harmful consequences so **big debt problems aren't really big problems**
- Most terrible economic problems occurred **before** policy makers took steps to spread them out
- Even biggest debt crises (1930s Great Depression) resolved once right adjustments made

**Biggest risks are:**
- Failure of policy makers to do right things due to lack of knowledge/authority
- Political consequences of adjustments that hurt some to help others

### The Critical Importance of Currency Denomination

> "I want to reiterate that 1) when debts are denominated in foreign currencies rather than one's own currency, it is much harder for a country's policy makers to do the sorts of things that spread out the debt problems, and 2) the fact that debt crises can be well-managed does not mean that they are not extremely costly to some people."

### The Key to Successful Crisis Management

> "The key to handling debt crises well lies in policy makers' knowing how to use their levers well and having the authority that they need to do so, knowing at what rate per year the burdens will have to be spread out, and who will benefit and who will suffer and in what degree, so that the political and other consequences are acceptable."

**Four levers to reduce debt/debt service relative to income/cash flow:**

1. **Austerity** (spending less)
2. **Debt defaults/restructurings**
3. **Central bank "printing money"** and making purchases (or guarantees)
4. **Transfers** of money/credit from those with more to those with less

### The Beautiful Deleveraging

- Each lever has different impacts: some inflationary and stimulate growth (e.g., "printing money"), others deflationary and reduce debt burdens (e.g., austerity, defaults)
- **"Beautiful deleveraging"**: debt-to-income ratios decline with acceptable inflation and growth rates
- In this scenario: debt-to-income ratios decline while economic activity and financial asset prices improve
- Gradually brings **nominal income growth rate back above nominal interest rate**

### Political Difficulties

- Levers shift who benefits and who suffers, over what time period
- Policy makers in politically difficult position making these choices
- Rarely appreciated, even when handling crisis well

<!-- pp. 21-40 -->

## The Template for the Archetypal Long-Term/Big Debt Cycle

### Study Methodology

- Template based on **48 big debt cycles** across large countries where real GDP fell >3% (**depression** definition)
- Two categories: **deflationary** (21 cases) vs. **inflationary** (27 cases) — split based on foreign currency debt
- ~75% correlation between foreign debt levels and inflation levels
- Analysis covers: period leading to depression, depression itself, and deleveraging afterward
- Timeline: 5 years before depression bottom through 7 years after

### Core Debt Crisis Mechanism

- Debt crises occur when **debt and debt service costs rise faster than incomes needed to service them**
- Central banks can alleviate typical debt crises by lowering real/nominal interest rates
- Severe debt crises (depressions) occur when **interest rates hit 0%** — no more room to ease
- Short-term business cycles compound into long-term debt cycles because each cycle ends with higher debt-to-income ratios
- US chart since 1910 shows interest payments stay flat/decline while debt rises — central bank (Fed) lowers rates to sustain debt-financed expansion until **hitting 0% bound**, triggering deleveraging

### Long-Term vs. Short-Term Debt Cycles

- **Short-term cycles**: produce mini-booms/recessions, controlled by central bank rate adjustments
- **Long-term cycles**: produce big booms/busts, more extreme debt burdens, less effective monetary policy
- US experienced two long-term debt crises: 1920s boom → 1930s Great Depression, and early 2000s boom → 2008 financial crisis
- Human nature drives the pattern: people prefer borrowing/spending over repaying debt → debts rise faster than incomes over long periods

### The Self-Reinforcing Upswing

- Rising spending generates rising incomes and net worths → raises borrowing capacity → enables more buying/spending
- **New financial intermediaries and instruments** develop outside regulatory supervision, offering higher returns, more leverage, greater liquidity/credit risk
- Borrowers spend unsustainably, appearing prosperous; lenders become complacent
- Debts cannot rise faster than money/income to service them forever → headed toward debt problem

### The Self-Reinforcing Downturn

- When debt growth limits reached: asset prices fall, debtors struggle, investors pull back → **liquidity problems**
- One person's spending = another's income → incomes decline → creditworthiness falls further
- Stock market crashes, unemployment rises, social tensions increase
- Self-reinforcing contraction not easily corrected — **debt burdens too big, need reduction**
- Unlike recessions: interest rates already at/near zero, liquidity/money cannot be increased by ordinary measures

### Historical Context

- Long-term debt cycles existed since before Roman times
- **Old Testament**: Year of Jubilee — debt wiped out every 50 years
- Pattern recurs throughout history

### Money's Two Purposes and Two Groups

- Money serves as **medium of exchange** and **store of wealth**
- Two groups: **proletariat-workers** (earn by selling time) vs. **capitalist-investors** (earn by lending money)
- Workers seek money for necessities; investors have stored wealth tied to money's value
- Capitalist-investors lend via: (a) debt instruments (promise to repay more), or (b) equity/stocks/real estate ownership

### The Deleveraging Necessity

- One person's financial assets = another's financial liabilities
- When claims on financial assets too high relative to money available → **big deleveraging must occur**
- Free-market credit system ceases working well, works in reverse via deleveraging
- Government must intervene: central bank becomes **lender of last resort**, central government becomes **redistributor of spending and wealth**
- Needs **debt restructuring** — claims on future spending (debt) reduced relative to money
- Imbalance between debt claims and money supply resolved via **four levers** (previously described)
- Process painful for all players, sometimes causing battle between workers and investors
- Can impair or outlaw lending — why usury considered sin in Catholicism and Islam

## Two Types of Depressions

### Deflationary Depressions

- Policy makers lower interest rates initially, but **lever ineffective at ~0%**
- Debt restructuring and austerity dominate without adequate stimulation (money printing, currency depreciation)
- Debt burdens rise as percent of income because incomes fall faster than restructuring/paydowns reduce debt stock
- Borrowers rack up more debts to cover higher interest costs
- Occur in countries where unsustainable debt financed **domestically in local currency**
- Produces forced selling and defaults, but **no currency or balance of payments problem**

### Inflationary Depressions

- Occur in countries reliant on **foreign capital flows** with significant **foreign currency debt** that can't be monetized
- When foreign capital slows → credit creation turns to credit contraction
- **Capital withdrawal drives up lending/liquidity** while **currency decline produces inflation**
- Especially difficult to manage — policy makers' abilities to spread pain more limited

## The Phases of the Classic Deflationary Debt Cycle

### 1) The Early Part of the Cycle (Goldilocks Period)

- Debt not growing faster than incomes, though debt growth is strong
- Borrowed money finances activities producing fast income growth (e.g., expanding productive businesses)
- Debt burdens low, balance sheets healthy — plenty of room to lever up
- Debt growth, economic growth, inflation "neither too hot nor too cold"

### 2) The Bubble

#### Bubble Dynamics

- **Debts rise faster than incomes**, producing accelerating strong asset returns and growth
- Self-reinforcing: rising incomes/net-worths/asset values raise borrowing capacity
- Lenders assess: (1) projected income/cash flows, (2) net worth/collateral, (3) own lending capacity
- Unsustainable because debt growth rates exceed incomes needed to service them
- Borrowers **feel rich, spend more than earned, buy assets at high prices with leverage**
- Example: earning $50K with $50K net worth, borrow $10K/year → spend $60K/year for years despite only earning $50K

#### The Up-Wave Duration

- Promises to deliver money (debt) rise relative to money supply and debtors' money/credit
- Typically lasts **decades**, with variations from central bank tightenings/easings
- Multiple short-term debt cycles compound into one long-term debt cycle

#### Central Bank Role in Sustaining Bubbles

- Central banks **progressively lower interest rates** → raises asset prices and wealth via present value effect
- Lowers monthly payment cost of credit-bought items, keeps debt service burdens from rising
- Cannot continue forever — eventually debt service payments ≥ amount debtors can borrow
- Debt cannot rise any more relative to money/credit coming in → **process works in reverse, deleveraging begins**
- Borrowing = pulling spending forward → person spending $60K/earning $50K must cut spending to $40K for equal years

#### The Start of a Bubble: The Bull Market

- Bubbles start as **over-extrapolations of justified bull markets**
- Lower interest rates make stocks/real estate more attractive → prices rise, conditions improve → economic growth, profits, better balance sheets
- Asset value increases raise net worths and spending/income levels
- Confidence increases → supports leveraging-up process
- New buyers enter market (don't want to miss out) → fuels bubble emergence
- Often **uneconomic lending** due to implicit/explicit government guarantees encouraging reckless lending

#### Shadow Banking and Financial Engineering

- New speculators/lenders enter, credit standards fall
- Banks lever up; **new unregulated lending institutions develop** ("shadow banking system")
- Less under government protection blanket
- New lending vehicles invented, **financial engineering** takes place
- Lenders/speculators make fast, easy money → reinforces bubble by increasing equity/collateral for new loans

#### The Self-Feeding Cycle

- Stock example: rising prices → more spending/investment → raises earnings → raises prices → lowers credit spreads → encourages lending (based on collateral/earnings value) → affects spending/investment
- Assets seem like fabulous treasure to own — anyone not owning them is missing out
- Large **asset-liability mismatches** build up: (a) borrowing short-term to lend long-term, (b) liquid liabilities for illiquid assets, (c) risky debt/assets with borrowed money, (d) currency mismatches
- **Debts rise fast, debt service costs rise even faster**

#### Market Consensus and Extrapolation

- When consensus exists, it gets **priced in**
- Believed to be good picture of future, though history shows otherwise
- Humans move in crowds, weigh recent experience more heavily than appropriate
- Consensus view reflected in price → **extrapolation tends to occur**

#### Bubble Indicators at Peak

- Debt-to-income ratios rise very rapidly
- Typical bubble: leveraging up at **20-25% of GDP over ~3 years**
- At bubble top: total debt-to-income ~**300% of GDP**
- Key conditions during bubble: (1) Debt growing faster than incomes 40% (range 14-79%), (2) Debt growing rapidly 32%, (3) Income growth high but slower than debt 13%, (4) Equity markets extend rally 48% (range 22-68%), (5) Yield curve flattens 1.4% (range 0.9-1.7%)

### The Role of Monetary Policy

- **Monetary policy helps inflate the bubble rather than constrain it**
- Especially when inflation and growth both good, investment returns great
- Interpreted as productivity boom → reinforces investor optimism as they leverage to buy assets
- Central banks focus on inflation/growth, reluctant to adequately tighten money
- Examples: Japan late 1980s, much of world late 1920s and mid-2000s

#### Central Bank Blind Spot

- Central bankers target inflation or inflation+growth, **don't target bubble management**
- Debt growth enabled can finance bubble creation if inflation/real growth don't appear too strong
- Dalio's opinion: **very important for central banks to target debt growth** with eye toward sustainable levels
- Central bankers say too hard to spot bubbles, not their role — but they control money/credit
- When money/credit goes into unpayable debts → huge implications for growth and inflation down the road
- **Greatest depressions occur when bubbles burst** — imprudent for policy makers to ignore them

#### Inadequate Tightening

- Central banks do tighten somewhat when inflation/growth get hot
- Short rates rise on average, but typical policies **not adequate to manage bubbles**
- Bubbles occurring in some parts of economy, not others
- Central banks fall behind curve during such periods
- Borrowers not yet squeezed by higher debt-service costs
- Interest payments increasingly covered by borrowing more rather than income growth — **clear sign trend is unsustainable**

#### The Reversal

- When bubble pops: same linkages that inflated it make downturn self-reinforcing
- Falling asset prices decrease equity/collateral values → lenders pull back → forces speculators to sell → prices fall more
- Lenders/investors "run" (withdraw money) from risky intermediaries/investments → liquidity problems
- Affected markets big enough and leveraged enough that losses on accumulated debt **systemically threatening** (threaten to topple entire economy)

### 3) The Top

#### Triggers for the Top

- When things so good they can't get better — yet everyone believes they will get better — **tops of markets being made**
- Tops triggered by different events, most often when **central bank starts to tighten and interest rates rise**
- Sometimes tightening brought by bubble itself (growth/inflation rising, capacity constraints pinching)
- Sometimes externally driven — for countries reliant on external creditors, pulling back of lending due to exogenous causes leads to liquidity tightening
- Tightening of monetary policy in debt's currency can cause foreign capital pullback
- Unrelated reasons: cyclical conditions in reserve currency country → tightening in that currency, or financial crisis → capital pullback
- Currency rise relative to debt's currency can cause severe squeeze
- Unanticipated shortfalls in cash flows for any number of reasons can trigger debt crises

#### The Wealth Effect and Credit Squeeze

- Debt-service squeeze hurts asset prices (e.g., stock prices) → negative **"wealth effect"** as lenders worry about getting cash back
- Borrowers squeezed as increasing share of new borrowing goes to debt service and/or isn't rolled over
- Spending slows down
- Result of buying investment assets at high prices with leverage, based on overly optimistic assumptions about future cash flow

#### Early Warning Signs

- Credit/debt problems emerge **~6 months ahead of peak** in economy, first in most vulnerable/frothy pockets
- Riskiest debtors miss payments, lenders worry, credit spreads tick up, risky lending slows
- Runs from risky assets to less risky assets pick up → **broadening of contraction**

#### Yield Curve Dynamics

- In early top stages: rise in short rates **narrows or eliminates spread with long rates** (extra interest rate for lending long vs. short)
- Lessens incentive to lend relative to holding cash
- **Yield curve flat or inverted** (long-term rates at lowest relative to short-term rates) → people incentivized to move to cash just before bubble pops
- Slows credit growth, causes previously described dynamic

#### The Sowing of Recession Seeds

- Early on: some parts of credit system suffer, others remain robust → not clear economy is weakening
- While central bank still raising rates and tightening credit, **seeds of recession being sown**
- Fastest rate of tightening typically **~5 months prior to stock market top**
- Economy operating at high rate, demand pressing against capacity to produce
- Unemployment normally at cyclical lows, inflation rates rising
- Short-term interest rate increase makes holding cash more attractive, raises interest rate for

<!-- pp. 41-60 -->

### The Bubble Peak and Post-Bubble Dynamics

- **Short rates peak just before stock market top** — monetary tightening discounts future cash flows, weakens asset prices, slows lending and demand
- **Leverage amplifies vulnerability** — more leverage + higher prices = less tightening needed to burst bubble, bigger bust follows
- **Sector-level analysis critical** — magnitude of downturn depends less on overall tightening than on each sector's sensitivity to it and how losses cascade
- **Wealth effects dominate monetary policy post-bubble** — people underestimate this impact, mistakenly seeing early declines as buying opportunities while failing to account for coming earnings collapse
- **Self-reinforcing cycle**: wealth falls → incomes fall later → creditworthiness declines → lending contracts → spending/investment drop → fundamentals deteriorate → selling accelerates

## 4) The "Depression"

### When Monetary Policy Stops Working

**Normal recessions**: central banks cut rates enough to 1) create positive wealth effect, 2) stimulate activity, 3) ease debt-service burdens

**Depressions**: interest rates can't be cut materially because:
- Already near 0%, or
- Currency outflows/weakness creates floor due to credit/currency risk

> "This is precisely the formula for a depression."

**Historical pattern**: both 1930-32 and 2008-09 depressions followed this dynamic

**2007-08 US response**: Fed lowered rates quickly, then moved to alternative stimulation (learning from 1930s mistakes when Fed was slower to ease and even tightened to defend dollar's gold peg)

### Debt Defaults Hit Like an Avalanche

- **Banks face runs** — justified fears feed on themselves, institutions without cash to meet withdrawals fail unless government-protected
- **Interest rate cuts don't work** — risk-free rates already hit floor, credit spreads rise, making risky debt service harder
- **Liquidity becomes critical concern** — high ratio of financial assets to money means massive conversion demand during panics; central bank must print money or allow defaults

### Solvency vs. Cash-Flow Problems

**Solvency problem**: entity lacks enough equity capital (is "broke"), accounting/regulatory issue
**Cash-flow problem**: entity lacks cash to meet needs (often from "run"), immediate and severe

- Cash-flow problems trigger most debt crises
- Both problem types often exist simultaneously
- Each requires different approach:
  - **Solvency**: provide equity capital OR change accounting/regulatory rules to hide problem
  - **Cash-flow**: provide cash OR guarantees

**1980s vs. 2008 banking crisis**: Less mark-to-market accounting in 1980s (loans not traded daily) meant banks appeared less insolvent. 2008 required capital injections/guarantees due to mark-to-market accounting.

### Systemic Protections Rarely Adequate

**Typical protections** (learned from past): deposit insurance, lender-of-last-resort supports, capital injections, nationalization

**Why they fall short**:
- Lending migrates to unregulated **"shadow banking system"**
- New instruments have unanticipated risks and inadequate regulations
- Exact nature of crisis not well thought through in advance
- Success depends on policymaker capabilities and freedom to act

### Depressions Are Not Primarily Psychological

**Common misconception**: investors scared, moving money from risky to safe assets; restore confidence → restore economy

**Reality**: deleveraging driven by supply/demand of credit, money, goods/services — not mainly psychology

> "If everyone went to sleep and woke up with no memory of what had happened, we would be in the same position, because debtors' obligations to deliver money would be too large relative to the money they are taking in."

**Credit creation/destruction**:
- Credit appears "out of thin air" in good times (store credit card example)
- Credit disappears same way when promises won't be kept
- Much "wealth" was merely promises to give money — deleveraging reveals this

**Panic-induced runs test ability to get paid** — when conversion fails, sell-offs cascade, especially hitting entities relying on short-term funding

### The Self-Reinforcing Deleveraging Spiral

**Dynamic**: Income/borrowing insufficient to meet obligations → sell assets, cut spending → asset values fall, collateral value drops, incomes decline → creditworthiness judged by (assets/debt) and (income/debt-service) ratios → both net worth and income fall faster than debts → lenders more reluctant → self-reinforcing

**Depression phase dominated by**: deflationary forces (defaults, restructurings, austerity) WITHOUT material money printing to offset debt burdens

### Leverage Magnifies Write-Down Impact

**Example**: 30% debt write-down
- Creditor's assets reduced 30%
- But if creditor leveraged 2:1 → **60% decline in net worth** (assets = 2x net worth)
- Banks typically leveraged 12:1 or 15:1 → **devastating** for them and economy

**Debt burdens rise even as debts written down** — spending/incomes fall faster; debt-to-income and debt-to-net-worth ratios rise, making credit contraction self-reinforcing on downside

**Capitalist-investor class devastated**:
- Investment portfolios collapse (~50% equity price declines typical)
- Earned incomes fall
- Higher tax rates
- Response: defensive — move money out of country (currency weakness), dodge taxes, seek liquid non-credit assets (government bonds, gold, cash)

### Real Economy and Social Catastrophe

With monetary policy constrained, uncontrolled credit contraction produces:
- **Workers**: income collapse, severe job losses, loss of meaningful work → destitution or dependence
- **Families**: lose homes (can't pay mortgages), retirement accounts wiped out, college savings lost
- Can persist many years without sufficient monetary stimulation

## Managing Depressions

### Four Broad Policy Categories

1. **Austerity**
2. **Debt defaults/restructurings**
3. **Debt monetization/money printing**
4. **Wealth transfers** (haves to have-nots)

**Key**: get the mix right so deflationary/depressive forces balance with inflationary/stimulative ones

### Why Policy Makers Get the Mix Wrong Initially

**Taxpayer anger**: don't want taxes bailing out debtors and institutions whose excesses caused crisis

**Moral hazard concern**: lenders/borrowers must suffer downsides or excesses will recur

**Result**: policymakers reluctant, slow to provide support → debt contraction and agony increase quickly

**Eventually**: after ~couple years of unnecessary pain, decisive action taken with guarantees, money printing, debt monetization → lifts economy into reflationary deleveraging

**Success examples**:
- Short "depression" following US 2008 crisis (acted quickly)
- **Failure examples**: Great Depression 1930s, Japan's "lost decade" post-late-1980s bubble

> **The two biggest impediments to managing a debt crisis are: a) the failure to know how to handle it well and b) politics or statutory limitations on the powers of policy makers to take the necessary actions.** In other words, *ignorance and a lack of authority are bigger problems than debts themselves.*

**Policy making harder than investing**:
- Investors: understand machine, anticipate next
- Policy makers: must do that PLUS navigate political impediments requiring smarts, willingness to fight, political savvy, heroism
- Even with all that, constraints may prevent success

## Austerity

**Why tried first**: natural desire to make those responsible bear costs

**Problem**: even deep austerity doesn't rebalance debt/income — when spending cut, incomes also cut; takes "awful lot of painful spending cuts" for significant debt/income reduction

**Government revenue falls, demands increase** during contraction → deficits increase despite fiscal responsibility attempts → governments raise taxes

**Both moves are big mistakes.**

## "Printing Money" to Stop Bleeding and Stimulate

**Runs on unprotected institutions** force decisions: which depositors/lenders/institutions to protect from losses, which to let sustain them, which systemically important to save

**Money comes from**: government (budget appropriation) and/or central banks ("printing")

**Governments do both** in varying degrees, providing money to:
- Essential banks
- Essential nonbank entities

### Three Immediate Actions When Systemically Important Institutions at Risk:

1. **Curtail panic and guarantee liabilities** — increase deposit/debt guarantees; inject money into systemically important institutions; occasionally force deposit freezes (undesirable but sometimes necessary)

2. **Provide liquidity** — central bank lends against widening collateral range to widening range of institutions not normally in their lending practices

3. **Support solvency of systemically important institutions**:
   - Incentivize private sector (support mergers of failed/healthy banks, regulatory pushes for more capital)
   - Accounting adjustments to reduce immediate capital need, buying time
   - **Recapitalize/nationalize/cover losses** when above insufficient — must stabilize lenders and maintain credit supply; certain institutions are "plumbing of the system" — lose them even if unprofitable → like losing shipping port in depression

### While Monetary Stimulus Critical, It's Typically Not Sufficient

**Policy makers must act immediately to**:
- Curtail panic, guarantee liabilities
- Provide liquidity (widening collateral/institutions)
- Support solvency of systemically important institutions

**Money printing alone inadequate** — modest initial attempts fail → progressively larger doses applied → early efforts cause temporary relief (bear-market rallies in stocks, increased activity)

**Great Depression example**: six big stock rallies (16%-48%) in 89% total decline bear market, all triggered by government actions to reduce imbalance

**Shift from depression to expansion** via well-managed money printing, asset purchases, guarantees

## Debt Defaults/Restructurings

**Ultimate necessity**: cleansing bad debts critical for future money/credit flow and return to prosperity

**Best-managed cases**:
- Swiftly recognize credit problem magnitude
- Don't save every expendable institution (balance benefits of allowing failures vs. risks of detrimental effects on other creditworthy entities)
- Create/restore robust credit pipes for future creditworthy borrowing
- Ensure acceptable growth/inflation while bad debts worked out

**Longer term key decision**: fix root causes of debt problems OR simply restructure debts to distribute pain over population/time

### Resolution Typically Slow and Inadequate Initially

**Pattern**: policy makers fail to recognize magnitude initially → enact insufficient one-off policies → only after "usually a couple of years and a lot of unnecessary economic pain" do they act decisively

**Speed and aggressiveness** of response = most important factor determining severity/length of depression

**Cost allocation** among government (society), bondholders (various seniorities), equity holders, depositors = important question

### Handling Nonsystemically Important Institutions

**Forced to absorb losses** — allowed to fail/go bankrupt if nonsystemically important

**Resolution forms**:
- **Mergers with healthy institutions** (~80% of studied cases)
- **Asset liquidation or transfer to government AMC** (Asset Management Company) for piecemeal sale

### Handling Systemically Important Institutions

**Banking system level measures**: liquidity/solvency support increasingly common in developed world; sometimes government-financed recapitalization across all banks (not just systemically important)

### Clear Lines for Creditor Protections:

1. **Small depositors**: minimal/no losses in nearly every case via deposit insurance (coverage often expanded during crisis); ~30% of cases saw depositor losses (often foreign-exchange deposits via below-market conversion)

2. **When institutions fail**: equity, subordinated debt, large depositors absorb losses regardless of systemic importance (senior/subordinated debt holder protections, dilutive equity recaps mostly in developed world)

3. **Domestic creditors sometimes prioritized over foreign** (especially private-sector, lower-capital-structure loans) as deposit insurance runs low; BUT governments often prioritize **IMF and BIS loans** to maintain availability of support from these lender-of-last-resort public entities

### Regulatory Reforms Accompany Lender Resolution

**Range**: modest to very large, better to worse
- Bank operation changes (1930s deposit guarantees, 2010 Dodd-Frank/Volcker)
- Credit standards improvements
- Opening banking to competition (including foreign)
- Raising capital requirements, removing lender protections
- Labor market reforms

**Politics determines reforms** — can distort market-based lending incentives (limiting creditworthy credit flow, increasing future risks) OR improve credit flow, protect households, reduce future debt problems

### Managing Bad Assets: Two Main Approaches

**40% of cases**: transfer to separate AMC for restructuring/disposal
**60% of cases**: remain on original lender's balance sheet

**Disposal levers**:
- Restructuring (extended terms)
- Debt-for-equity swaps, asset seizures
- Direct sales to third parties
- Securitizations

### AMC Benefits

**Accelerates debt problem management** — frees existing banks to return to lending, consolidates bad debts to centralized entity for sales/restructurings

**Transfer mechanism for wealth to banks** — pricing above market prices

**Publicly owned**, mandated to sell within targeted timeframe (e.g., 10 years) minimizing taxpayer costs and market disruptions:
- Quickly sell performing assets
- Work over time to manage/sell nonperforming assets
- Some have explicit goals to restructure nonperforming debts to reduce debt burdens

**Financed** by direct/pseudo-government debt; can't work well when legal/political/funding constraints limit bad debt recognition/restructuring ability

**Original lender manages bad debts when**: state-sponsored (closer to public AMC), losses not too large, technical expertise for centralized AMC lacking, effective resolution mechanisms already exist

### Systemically vs. Strategically Important Borrower Distinction

**Systemically/strategically important**: keep businesses intact via debt restructuring (debt-for-equity swaps, debt reduction, lower rates, terming out); occasionally new lending programs for ongoing liquidity

**Nonsystemically important**: restructure with private lenders or allow bankruptcy/liquidation

**Household sector**: governments/AMCs often take steps to reduce debt burdens vs. foreclosing (part of maximizing recovery values)

### Frequency of Policy Levers (48 Historical Cases)

**Liquidity Support**: Emergency Lending 88%, Bank Guarantees 58%, Deposit Freeze 21%
**Insolvent Lenders**: Bank Restructuring/Mergers 81%, Recapitalization 73%, Nationalizations 60%, Losses on Depositors 29%
**Bad Debts**: Asset Purchases/Transfers 44%, Centralized AMCs 38%
**Sovereign Issues**: Default/Restructuring 35%, IMF Program 52%

## Redistributing Wealth

**Wealth gaps increase during bubbles**, particularly galling for less privileged during hard times

**Political/economic conflict risk**: if rich share budget with poor during downturn → populism emerges (left and right)

**US historical parallel**: today's inequality/populism matches 1930s levels (top 0.1% net worth ≈ bottom 90% combined in both eras)

**Tax increases on rich politically attractive** because:
- Rich made money in boom (especially financial sector)
- Perceived to have caused problems via greed
- Central bank asset purchases disproportionately benefit rich (who own more such assets)

**Big leftward political shifts** hasten redistributive efforts, driving rich to move money to protected locations → currency/asset market effects → economic "hollowing out" (big earners = big taxpayers leave → revenue drops, property values/services decline)

**Typical increased taxation**: income, property, consumption taxes (most effective revenue raisers)

**Wealth/inheritance taxes** sometimes increased but raise little (wealth often illiquid, difficult to collect; forcing liquid asset sales undermines capital formation)

**Transfers rarely contribute meaningfully to deleveraging** (unless "revolutions" with massive property nationalization)


<!-- pp. 61-80 -->

## 5) The "Beautiful Deleveraging"

### Definition and Mechanics

A **beautiful deleveraging** occurs when the four policy levers are balanced to reduce shocks, produce positive growth with falling debt burdens, and maintain acceptable inflation. Specifically: enough stimulation (money printing/debt monetization and currency devaluation) offsets deflationary deleveraging forces (austerity/defaults) and brings nominal growth above nominal interest rates—but not so much that inflation accelerates or a new debt bubble forms.

### Central Bank Role in Negating Deflation

Best approach: central bank provides adequate liquidity and credit support; central government provides capital as needed. When spending can't be financed with debt (too much debt relative to money available to service it), increased spending and debt-service relief must come from increased money—the central bank must expand the money supply.

**Central bank mechanisms:**
- Lending against wider range of collateral (lower quality, longer maturity)
- Buying (monetizing) lower-quality and/or longer-term debt
- Right amounts: a) neutralize deflationary credit market collapse, b) get nominal growth marginally above nominal interest rate to spread out deleveraging

### Income Growth vs. Debt Growth

For debt burdens not to increase, **nominal income growth must exceed nominal interest rates**.

**Example:** Country with 100% debt-to-income ratio and 2% interest rate. If no debt repaid, debt becomes 102 after one year. If income grows 1%, it reaches 101, so debt burden increases from 100/100 to 102/101. The higher nominal income growth is above nominal interest rates, the better (provided it doesn't produce unacceptable inflation/currency declines).

### Why Money Printing Won't Necessarily Cause Inflation

> "People ask if printing money will raise inflation. It won't if it offsets falling credit and the deflationary forces are balanced with this reflationary force. That's not a theory—it's been repeatedly proven out in history."

**Key insight:** A dollar of spending paid for with money has the same effect on prices as a dollar paid for with credit. Money printing makes up for credit disappearance. Traditional economists see velocity of money declining, but what's happening is **credit destruction being offset by money creation**. If the balance is right, this isn't inflationary.

### Risk of Abusive Stimulants

Because stimulants work so well relative to alternatives, **real risk of "ugly inflationary deleveraging"** (Weimar hyperinflation 1920s, Argentina and Brazil 1980s). Key: avoid printing too much money. Achieving balance is harder in countries with:
- Large percentage of debt denominated in foreign currency
- Debt owned by foreign investors
- Cannot easily monetize or restructure such debt (as in Weimar Germany and South American countries)

### Historical Pattern: Money Printing Is Inevitable

Printing money/debt monetization and government guarantees are **inevitable in depressions where interest rate cuts won't work**, though these tools have little value in countries:
- Constrained from printing
- Don't have assets to back printing
- Can't easily negotiate redistributions of debt burdens

All past deleveragings (most over past hundred years) eventually led to **big waves of money creation, fiscal deficits, and currency devaluations** (against gold, commodities, and stocks). Policy makers varied which levers they used based on their monetary systems.

**Chart shows archetypal money printing path over 21 cases:**
- Two classic waves:
  1. Central banks provide liquidity to stressed institutions (initial liquidity injection)
  2. Large-scale asset purchases to broadly stimulate economy

### Currency Devaluation Patterns

Governments with **gold-, commodity-, or foreign-currency-pegged money systems** are forced to have tighter monetary policies to protect currency value. But eventually debt contractions become so painful they relent, break the link, and print (abandon systems or change commodity pricing).

**Example:** During Great Depression, suspending promise to convert dollars into gold so currency could be devalued and more money created was key to creating bottoms in stock and commodity markets and economy. 2008 financial crisis easier—didn't require legalized currency regime change.

**Gold price pattern:** In US Great Depression, gold rose overnight when Roosevelt broke gold peg; in recent financial crisis, Fed moves pushed down dollar value versus all currencies, including gold.

> "In the end, policy makers always print. That is because austerity causes more pain than benefit, big restructurings wipe out too much wealth too fast, and transfers of wealth from haves to have-nots don't happen in sufficient size without revolutions."

**Historical comparison:** Those who did it quickly and well (US in 2008-09) derived much better results than those who did it late (US in 1930-33).

### Typical Magnitudes Required

On average to create turn from depression to beautiful deleveraging:
- **Money printing:** ~4% of GDP per year
- **Currency devaluation:** ~50% against gold initially
- **Fiscal deficits:** widen to ~6% of GDP
- **Timing:** This aggressive stimulation comes 2-3 years into depression, after stocks fallen 50%+, economic activity fallen ~10%, unemployment risen to 10-15%

**Note:** When monetary and fiscal policies rolled out faster and smarter, results much better than these averages.

### Key to Beautiful Deleveraging

Balance inflationary forces against deflationary ones. Right amounts:
- a) Neutralize deflationary credit-market collapse
- b) Get nominal growth rate above nominal interest rate enough to relieve debt burdens, but not so much it leads to run on debt assets

### Summary: Well-Managed vs. Poorly-Managed Phases

**BUBBLE - Well Managed:**
- Central banks consider debt growth and asset market effects in policy
- Use macroprudential policies to target restraints where bubbles emerging, allow debt growth where not excessive
- Fiscal policies tightened

**BUBBLE - Poorly Managed:**
- Big bubbles fueled by speculators/lenders over-extrapolating, central banks focusing only on inflation/growth, not debt bubbles in investment assets
- Credit kept cheap too long

**TOP - Well Managed:**
- Central banks constrict bubble with broad monetary policy or macroprudential policies, then ease selectively

**TOP - Poorly Managed:**
- Central banks continue tightening well after bursting bubble

**DEPRESSION - Well Managed:**
- Central banks provide ample liquidity, ease short rates quickly to 0%, then pursue aggressive monetizations and macroprudential policies
- Governments pursue aggressive sustained fiscal stimulus
- Systemically important institutions protected

**DEPRESSION - Poorly Managed:**
- Central banks slower to cut rates, provide limited liquidity, tighten too early, wait too long for aggressive monetization
- Governments pursue austerity without adequate easing
- Systemically important institutions left damaged or failed

**BEAUTIFUL DELEVERAGING - Well Managed:**
- Reflations begin with aggressive monetizations through asset purchases or big currency declines, enough to bring nominal growth above nominal rates
- Stimulative macroprudential policies protect systemically important entities and stimulate high-quality credit growth
- Nonsystemically important institutions allowed to fail orderly
- Policy makers balance depressive forces (defaults/austerity) with reflationary forces (debt monetization, currency declines, fiscal stimulus)

**BEAUTIFUL DELEVERAGING - Poorly Managed:**
- Initial monetizations stutter and start; asset purchases more muted and consist more of cash-like instruments rather than risky assets, so don't produce wealth effect; stimulation undermined by fiscal austerity
- Overindebted entities protected even though not systemically important, leading to zombie banks and malaise
- Ugly inflationary depressions arise when policy makers allow faith in currency to collapse and print too much money

## 6) "Pushing on a String"

### When Monetary Stimulus Loses Effectiveness

Late in long-term debt cycle, central bankers sometimes struggle to convert stimulative policies into increased spending because **effects of lowering interest rates and central banks' purchases of debt assets have diminished**. Economy enters period of low growth and low returns on assets. Central bankers must move to other forms of **monetary stimulation in which money and credit go more directly to support spenders**.

**"Pushing on a string"** (1930s phrase): Biggest risk at this stage is too much printing of money/monetization and too severe currency devaluation relative to deflationary alternatives, producing "ugly inflationary deleveraging."

### Three Types of Monetary Policy

#### Monetary Policy 1 (MP1): Interest-Rate Driven

**Most effective because broadest impact on economy.** When central banks reduce interest rates, they stimulate by:
- a) Producing positive wealth effect (lower rate raises present value of investments)
- b) Making items easier to buy on credit (monthly payments decline), raising demand for interest-rate-sensitive items like durable goods and housing
- c) Reducing debt-service burdens (improves cash flows and spending)

**MP1 typically first approach** to debt crisis, but when short-term interest rates hit around 0%, it no longer works effectively—central banks must move to second type.

#### Monetary Policy 2 (MP2): Quantitative Easing

**"Printing money" and buying financial assets, typically debt assets.** Works by affecting behavior of investors/savers (not borrowers/spenders), because driven by purchases of financial assets.

When central bank buys a bond, it gives savers/investors cash, which they typically use to buy another financial asset they think is more attractive. **What they do with that money and credit makes all the difference.** When they invest in assets that finance spending, that stimulates economy. When they invest in assets that don't (such as financial assets), there must be very large market gains before any money trickles down into spending—and that spending comes more from those who enjoyed market gains than those who haven't.

**Result:** QE benefits investors/savers (those who own financial assets) much more than people who don't, **thus widening the wealth gap**.

**MP2 effectiveness:** Generally less effective than interest-rate changes, but most effective when risk and liquidity premiums are large—causes those premiums to fall. When risk premiums large and money added to system, actual risks reduced at same time there is more money seeking returns, triggering purchases of riskier assets offering higher expected returns, driving prices up and producing positive wealth effect.

**Diminishing returns over time:** Risk premiums pushed down and asset prices pushed up to levels beyond which difficult to push further, and wealth effect diminishes. **At higher prices and lower expected returns, compensation for taking risk becomes too small to get investors to bid prices up, which would drive prospective returns down further.** Reward-to-risk ratio could make cash more appealing. **If QE provided and private credit growth doesn't pick up, policy makers feel like pushing on a string.**

At this stage, policy makers sometimes monetize debt in even larger quantities to compensate for declining effectiveness. **Real risk that prolonged monetization will lead people to question currency's suitability as store hold of value**, leading to moves to alternative currencies like gold.

### The Fundamental Economic Challenge

**The claims on purchasing power are greater than the abilities to meet them.**

There are only goods and services. **Financial assets are claims on them.** Holders of investments/assets (investors/capitalists) believe they can convert holdings into purchasing power. Workers expect to exchange a unit's worth of contribution for buying power. But **since debt/money/currency have no intrinsic value, the claims on them are greater than the value of what they are supposed to be able to buy**—so they have to be devalued or restructured.

When too many debt liabilities/assets, they either have to be reduced via debt restructurings or monetized. Policy makers tend to use monetization at this stage primarily because **stimulative rather than contractionary**. But **monetization simply swaps one IOU (debt) for another (newly printed money)**. Situation analogous to a **Ponzi scheme**. Since there aren't enough goods and services likely to be produced to back up all the IOUs, there's a worry that people may not be willing to work in return for IOUs forever.

**Low interest rates together with low premiums on risky assets pose structural challenge for monetary policy.** With MP1 (interest rates) and MP2 (QE) at their limits, central bank has very little ability to provide stimulus through these two channels—**monetary policy has little "gas in the tank."** This typically happens in later years of long-term debt cycle (e.g., 1937-38 and now in the US), which can lead to "pushing on a string." When this happens, policy makers need to look beyond QE to **Monetary Policy 3**.

#### Monetary Policy 3 (MP3): Direct Money to Spenders

**Puts money more directly into hands of spenders instead of investors/savers and incentivizes them to spend it.** Because wealthy people have fewer incentives to spend incremental money/credit than less wealthy people, when wealth gap is large and economy is weak, **directing spending opportunities at less wealthy people is more productive**.

**Continuum of actions with varying degrees of control:**
- **Coordinated fiscal and monetary actions:** Fiscal policy makers provide stimulus directly through government spending or indirectly by providing incentives for nongovernment entities to spend
- **"Helicopter money":** Central bank sends cash directly to citizens without coordination with fiscal policy makers

Typically coordination of monetary and fiscal policy in way that creates incentives for people to spend on goods and services. Central banks can also exert influence through **macroprudential policies** that help shape things in ways similar to how fiscal policies might.

**Forms of MP3:**

- **Increase in debt-financed fiscal spending** (sometimes paired with QE that buys most of new issuance): Japan 1930s, US during WWII, US and UK in 2000s

- **Increase in debt-financed fiscal spending where Treasury isn't on hook for debt** because:
  - Central bank can print money to cover debt payments (Germany 1930s)
  - Central bank can lend to entities other than government for stimulus projects (lending to development banks in China in 2008)

- **Not bothering to issue debt, instead giving newly printed money directly to government to spend** (printing fiat currency in Imperial China, American Revolution, US Civil War, Germany 1930s, UK during WWI; or debasing hard currency in Ancient Rome, Imperial China, 16th-century England)

- **Printing money and doing direct cash transfers to households ("helicopter money")**: Directing money into hands of spenders (US veterans' bonuses during Great Depression, Imperial China). Money can be directed same amount to everyone or aim to help one or more groups over others (giving to poor rather than rich). Money can be provided as one-off or over time (perhaps as universal basic income). Variants can be paired with incentive to spend it (money disappears if not spent within a year). Money could also be directed to specific investment accounts (retirement, education, accounts earmarked for small-business investments) targeted toward socially desirable spending/investment. Another way: **distribute returns/holdings from QE to households instead of to government**.

- **Big debt write-down accompanied by big money creation** ("year of Jubilee"): Ancient Rome, Great Depression, Iceland

**Most effective approaches involve fiscal/monetary coordination**, because ensures both providing and spending of money will occur. If central banks just give people money (helicopter money), typically less adequate than giving them money with incentives to spend it. However, sometimes difficult for those who set monetary policy to coordinate with those who set fiscal policy—in which case other approaches used.

Also, **sometimes policies don't fall exactly into these categories**, as they have elements of more than one. For example, if government gives tax break, that's probably not helicopter money, but depends on how it's financed. Government can also spend money directly without loan financed by central bank—that is helicopter money through fiscal channels.

### Macroprudential Policies

While central banks influence costs and availabilities of credit for economy as whole, they also have **powers to influence costs and availabilities of credit for targeted parts of financial system through their regulatory authorities**. These **macroprudential policies** especially important when desirable to differentiate entities—e.g., when desirable to restrict credit to overly indebted area while simultaneously stimulating rest of economy, or when desirable to provide credit to some targeted entities but not broadly. Macroprudential policies take numerous forms valuable in different ways in all seven stages of big debt cycle. Because explaining them here would require too much digression, they are explained in some depth in the Appendix.

## 7) Normalization

Eventually the system gets back to normal, though **recovery in economic activity and capital formation tends to be slow, even during beautiful deleveraging**. Typically takes roughly **5 to 10 years** (hence "lost decade") for real economic activity to reach former peak level. Takes longer, around **a decade, for stock prices to reach former highs**, because takes very long time for investors to become comfortable taking risk of holding equities again (equity risk premiums are high).

**Recovery conditions (averages):**
- Length of equity drawdown: 119 months (range 60-249)
- Length of GDP drawdown: 72 months (range 25-106)
- Change in debt-to-GDP post-stimulation: -54% (range -70% to -29%)

**Recommended next steps:** Now that you have this template for deflationary depressions in mind, read the detailed accounts of US 2007-2011 and 1928-1937 big debt cycles shown in Part 2, then look at summary statistics and auto-text of 21 case studies shown in Part 3.

<!-- pp. 81-100 -->

## Which Countries/Currencies Are Most Vulnerable to Severe Inflationary Deleveragings or Hyperinflations?

### Vulnerability Factors
Countries most susceptible to inflationary depressions share key characteristics:
- **Don't have a reserve currency** (no global bias to hold their currency/debt as store of wealth)
- **Have low foreign-exchange reserves** (small cushion against capital outflows)
- **Have large foreign debt** (vulnerable to rising debt costs via interest rates or currency depreciation, or shortage of dollar-denominated credit)
- **Have large and increasing budget and/or current account deficit** (forcing need to borrow or print money)
- **Have negative real interest rates** (interest rates significantly below inflation, inadequately compensating lenders)
- **Have a history of high inflation and negative total returns** (eroding trust in currency/debt)

> "Generally speaking, the greater the degree to which these things exist, the greater the degree of the inflationary depression."

Most iconic case: **German Weimar Republic** (early 1920s), examined in Part 2.

**Reserve-currency countries** can still experience inflationary depressions, though less severe. They emerge more slowly after sustained overuse of stimulus to reverse deflationary deleveraging. Even reserve currencies can lose status if policy makers permit excessive inflation to maintain growth, undermining demand for the currency and turning deleveraging inflationary.

## The Phases of the Classic Inflationary Debt Cycle

Archetype created by averaging **27 inflationary deleveragings** with significant foreign-currency-denominated debt. Five stages mirror deflationary deleveragings but differ in critical ways.

### 1) The Early Part of the Cycle

**Healthy upswing driven by good fundamentals:**
- Country is competitive with potential for productive investment
- Debt levels low, balance sheets healthy
- Capital inflows fund productive investments yielding good returns
- Debt and incomes rise at comparable rates
- Strong debt and equity markets encourage borrowing and investing

**Capital flows are the most volatile and important flows to watch.** Positive self-reinforcing cycle: rising incomes make debt easier to service → early leveraging attracts more capital.

**Currency dynamics:** If currency cheap enough to attract foreign investors, country's balance of payments becomes favorable (demand for currency > supply). This makes central bank's job easier—can achieve more growth per unit of inflation by using inflows to:
- Appreciate the currency
- Lower interest rates
- Increase reserves

**Foreign-exchange reserves:** Central banks sometimes sell own currency for incoming foreign currency to prevent appreciation. Must invest acquired foreign currency in foreign bonds. Reserves are like savings—cushion currency movements and enable strategic asset purchases. Accumulating reserves stimulates economy by lessening upward currency pressure, maintaining export competitiveness, and injecting money into economy (via domestic currency creation to buy foreign currency).

### 2) The Bubble

Emerges during self-reinforcing cycle of strong capital flows, good returns, and strong conditions. Early productive capital investment leads to asset appreciation, attracting more capital. Currency and/or assets get bid up and increasingly debt-financed, making prices too high to produce adequate returns. Borrowing continues as prices rise, debts rise rapidly relative to incomes.

**Key bubble indicators:**
- Foreign capital flows around **10% of GDP**
- Central bank accumulating foreign-exchange reserves
- Real FX overvalued by around **15% on PPP basis**
- Stocks rally over **20% for several years** into peak

**Structural long currency positions build up** without participants fully recognizing it. Foreign businesses set up operations, hold deposits in local currency, don't hedge exposures. Local businesses borrow in weaker foreign currency (attractive to foreign lenders in hot market).

- Influx finances consumption boom
- Imports rise faster than exports, current account worsens

Rising incomes increase creditworthiness and willingness to borrow; lenders more willing to lend. High export prices (commodities) increase income and incentivize investment.

**Fundamental attractiveness fades** as rising currency erodes competitiveness. Growth increasingly financed by debt rather than productivity gains. Country becomes highly reliant on foreign financing.

**Bubble characteristics:**
- Debt to GDP rises at **~10% annual rate over three years**
- Foreign-currency debt rises to around **35% of total debt** and **45% of GDP**
- Economic activity very strong with GDP gap around **+4%**

Emerging countries borrow primarily from abroad in foreign currencies due to: underdeveloped local financial system, less faith in local currency, smaller domestic savings pool.

During bubble, debt as % of GDP rises from ~125% to ~150%, current account deteriorates by ~2% of GDP.

### 3) The Top and Currency Defense

**Balance of payments crisis and inflationary depression** begin when bubble bursts—flows causing bubble become unsustainable. Mirror-opposite spiral: weakening capital inflows and asset prices → deteriorating conditions → further capital/asset weakness.

Even minor event can trigger reversal when optimism is fully priced in. Worsening trade balances play a role (high currency, excessive consumption), but **adverse capital flow shifts usually more important.**

**Triggers for top-reversal:**
1. Income from selling goods/services to foreigners drops (currency made exports expensive; commodity prices fall)
2. Costs of items bought abroad or borrowing rise
3. **Declines in capital flows into country** (foreign investors reduce lending/investment) because:
   - Unsustainable pace naturally slows
   - Greater worries about economic/political conditions
   - **Tightening of monetary policy in local currency and/or currency debts are denominated in** (or tightening abroad creates pressure for capital to exit)
4. Country's own citizens want to get money out

Weakening capital flows directly reduce growth (less financing for investment and consumption), making borrowers less creditworthy and lenders less willing. Self-reinforcing weakness.

**Early indicators:**
- Growth slows relative to potential
- Domestic capital outflows pick up
- Export earnings fall (prices or quantities)

Shift in flows drives asset prices down and interest rates up, slowing growth dependent on inflows. Worsens company fundamentals, drives out more capital. Economy suffers debt bust—asset prices fall, banks fail.

**Policy maker dilemma:** Central bank's job becomes more difficult—gets less growth per unit of inflation as negative flows cause currency depreciation, interest rate increases, and/or reserve declines.

**Currency defense attempts:** Central banks try to defend currencies by:
- Spending down reserves, and/or
- Raising interest rates

**These defenses rarely work** because selling reserves and raising rates creates more opportunity for sellers without moving currencies/rates to sustainable levels.

**Critical relationship:** Interest rate differential and spot/forward currency pricing. Expected currency decline is priced into forward discount. If market expects 5% decline over a year, currency must yield 5% higher interest. **If market expects 5% decline in a month, needs 5% higher monthly rate = ~80% annual rate**—producing severe economic contraction.

Managed currency decline with falling reserves causes market to expect continued depreciation → pushes up domestic interest rates (tightening when economy already weak) → encourages capital withdrawals and devaluation speculation → forces more reserve spending. No sensible policy maker will run out of "savings."

Policy makers defending pegs typically make boldly confident statements vowing to stop weakening—all happening just before letting currency go.

**Forward/spot dynamics:** During defense, forward currency price declines ahead of spot (consequence of interest rate differential relationship). Country tightening to support currency just increases rate differential to artificially hold up spot. Forward continues declining relative to spot—whip-like effect where forward leads spot downward as rate differential increases. Spot eventually catches up after currency released, allowing rate differential to narrow and forward to rally relative to spot.

**Capital controls** are third (often last-ditch) lever. Can seem attractive (directly reduce outflows) but usually fail because:
- Investors find ways around them
- Trying to trap people makes them want to escape
- Analogous to inability to get money out of bank—can trigger run

Temporary fix at best, never sustained.

Currency defense phase typically **brief (around 6 months)**, with reserves drawn down **10-20%** before defense abandoned.

### 4) The Depression (Often When the Currency Is Let Go)

Unlike families, countries can change currency amount/value—**critical lever for managing balance of payments pressures.** Currency devaluation changes price of goods/services for foreigners at different rate than for citizens.

> "If a family's breadwinner lost his/her job and would have to take a 30 percent pay cut to get a new one, that would have a devastating economic effect on the family. But when a country devalues its currency by 30 percent, that paycut becomes a 30 percent pay cut only relative to the rest of the world; the wages in the currency the family cares about stay the same."

Currency declines allow countries to offer price cuts to world without domestic deflation.

After unsustainable defense (expending reserves, tightening, strong assurances, sometimes capital controls), **policy makers typically let currency decline** (though try to smooth fall).

**Typical post-release dynamics:**
- Big initial depreciation—around **30% decline in real terms**
- Not offset by tighter short rates, so currency holding losses significant (**~30% first year**)
- Policy makers try to smooth decline, continuing to spend reserves (another **~10% for year into bust**)

**Devaluation benefits (stimulative):**
- Assets rise in value measured in weakened currency
- Stimulates export sales
- Brings spending back in line with income (helps balance of payments adjustment)
- Lowers imports (more expensive), favoring domestic producers
- Makes assets competitively priced and attractive
- Creates better profit margins for exported goods
- Sets stage for earning more income from abroad through cheaper, competitive exports

Central banks should NOT defend currencies to point of depleting reserves or raising rates too high. **Devaluations are stimulative and helpful during contraction.** Dangers of over-defense exceed dangers of devaluation.

**But currency declines are double-edged swords.** How managed greatly impacts pain during adjustment:

**Risks of gradual/persistent decline:**
- Weakness translates to higher import prices → sharp inflation rise
- Market expects continued depreciation → encourages capital withdrawal and speculation
- Widens balance of payments gap, forces more reserve spending
- Makes inflation persistent, feeding inflation psychology

**Better approach: Large, one-off devaluation** achieving two-way market where no broad expectation of continued weakening. Higher inflation less likely sustained. If surprise (unexpected), policy makers don't have to spend reserves or allow rate rises to defend going into devaluation.

> "This is why policy makers generally say they'll continue to defend the currency right up until the moment they stop doing it."

After initial release, people push to exit currency positions. Many had acquired asset-liability mismatches (profitable at the time). Reversal becomes self-sustaining—when currency


<!-- pp. 101-120 -->

### Capital Flight and Economic Collapse

- When currency weakens, **capital suddenly becomes unavailable** and spending is forced to stop
- Ripple effects: one person's spending is another's income → job losses → less spending → growth halts
- Domestic banks face debt problems; foreigners become unwilling to lend
- **Capital inflows dry up fast** (more than 5% of GDP in under 12 months)
- Capital outflows continue at -3% to -5% of GDP
- Central bank money printing typically **cannot offset the pullback** in capital—printing risks enabling more people to exit the currency, worsening capital flight
- Weaker growth causes investors to pull money; assets shift from "fabulous treasure" to "trash" very quickly
- Nominal short rates rise (typically by ~20 percentage points); yield curve inverts
- Printing limited to 1-2% of GDP on average
- **Equities fall ~50% in local currency** terms; perform even worse in foreign currency as depreciation compounds the sell-off

### Foreign Currency Debt: The Critical Mismatch

- As local currency depreciates, **debtors with foreign-currency debt face rising debt burdens** (in local currency)
- Borrowers typically sell local currency to pay back debts, hedge positions, and move savings into foreign currency
- All these actions contribute to **further downward pressure** on the local currency
- **Debt service rises >5% of GDP** because incomes fall and FX-denominated debt becomes more expensive in local currency
- FX debt burdens rise ~20% on average due to falling incomes and currency depreciation
- Currency declines also **push up inflation** as imports become more expensive
- **Inflation rises typically by 15%**, peaking around 30%
- Inflation stays elevated for ~2 years from the top

### "Hitting Bottom" — The Catharsis Phase

- Pendulum swings from "everything looks great" to "everything looks terrible"
- Different problems reinforce each other: debt, economic, political, currency
- **Hidden problems surface**: fraudulent accounting, corruption typically exposed during this phase
- Bad environment discourages foreign capital inflows and encourages domestic capital flight

> "This is when countries usually 'hit the bottom.' The bottom is the mirror opposite of the bubble stage. While investors during the bubble are aggressively getting in, investors during the catharsis are aggressively getting out."

- Those losing money in asset/currency positions flee in panic; supply/demand imbalance creates shortage of buyers, surplus of sellers → prices plummet
- **Most severe and painful part** of inflationary deleveraging—downward spiral is self-reinforcing and rapid
- "Hitting bottom" is so painful it produces a **radical metamorphosis** in pricing and policies, ultimately creating the changes needed for recovery

> "That is why I use the word 'catharsis' when describing hitting bottom. In theater (or for that matter, in one's own personal life) crisis sows the seeds for change and ultimately renewal."

- Because currency becomes very cheap, **spending on imports is finally cut enough** to restore balance of payments
- Sometimes international aid (IMF, BIS, multinational organizations) creates necessary adjustments
- Often **big political shifts** occur: from fundamentally bad policies to economically sound ones

### Key Economic Developments at Bottom

- Economic activity (GDP gap) falls significantly (on average by ~8%)
- Unemployment rises
- Bottom in activity comes after ~1 year; GDP gap trough typically near -4%

### Phase 5: Normalization

- Reversal to normalcy occurs when **supply/demand balance is restored** for the currency relative to other currencies
- Balance primarily achieved through **capital flows** (not just trade adjustments)
- Normalization comes when central bank succeeds in **making it desirable to hold the currency again**; secondarily when spending/imports have fallen enough

### The Counterintuitive Currency Policy

- Most policy makers think the best thing is to **defend the currency** during the defense phase
- **Actually the opposite is true**: a currency level that is:
  - a) good for trade balance
  - b) produces positive total return
  - c) has appropriate interest rate for domestic conditions
  - **...is a LOW currency level**

> "The best way to bring that about is to let the currency depreciate sharply and quickly. While that will hurt those who are long that currency, it will make it more attractive for investors who will get long after the devaluation."

- At a sharply depreciated level, **total return becomes positive** without requiring an intolerably high interest rate
- This ensures positive expected returns at relatively low real interest rate (what weak domestic conditions need)

### The Cocaine Analogy

> "Devaluing currencies is like using cocaine, in that it provides short-term stimulation but is ruinous when abused."

- Watch what central banks do before deciding whether to take a long position
- If investors are burned with negative returns too long and currency keeps falling, that's frequently the **break-point** determining whether there will be an inflationary spiral
- Central bank objective: allow currency to get cheap enough for needed stimulation and balance of payments adjustment, while running tight enough policy to **make returns on owning the currency attractive**
- Returns to holding currency for foreigners start negative but rally about a year after devaluation

### Managing the Crisis — Even When Not at Debt Limits
