- Even if country hasn't hit debt limits, certain entities within the country may have
- Policy makers must **recapitalize systemically important institutions** and provide targeted liquidity to manage bad debts
- Providing targeted liquidity (typically via printing money) can help avoid debt crisis that could cause additional capital flight
- But inflationary nature of money printing must be balanced carefully

### What Happens at the Bottom

- Collapse in imports improves current account significantly (~8% of GDP)
- Capital inflows stop declining and stabilize
- Capital flight abates
- Countries frequently turn to **IMF or international entities** for support and stable capital source, especially when reserves are limited
- Short rates start declining after ~1 year; long rates stay relatively elevated
- After peaking, short rates fall back to pre-crisis levels in ~2 years—the decline is stimulative
- As interest rates fall, forward currency price rallies relative to spot
- As currency stabilizes, **inflation comes down** (takes nearly 2 years after bottom to reach pre-crisis levels)

### Painful Adjustment Closes the Gap

- Sizable, painful decline in domestic conditions helps close balance of payments gap by reducing spending and imports
- Through crisis, average country's **imports contract by ~10%** as growth collapses and equity markets fall >50%
- Classically, collapse in imports brings current account into **surplus of 2% of GDP**, rising from deficit of -6% of GDP about 18 months into crisis
- Exports play smaller role in earlier stages; they actually tend to **contract during worst of crisis** (other countries seeing slowdowns too)
- Exports rebound in subsequent years

### Well-Managed vs. Poorly Managed Adjustments

**MANAGING THE CURRENCY**
- **Well-managed**: Policy makers bluff that they'll never allow currency to weaken much; when they devalue, it's a surprise; devaluation is large enough that people no longer broadly expect more weakening (creates two-way market)
- **Poorly managed**: Widely expected to allow weakness → more downward pressure and higher interest rates; initial devaluation is small, further devaluations needed → market expects this → higher interest rates and inflation expectations

**CLOSING EXTERNAL IMBALANCES**
- **Well-managed**: Tight monetary policy causes domestic demand to contract in line with income falls; policy makers create incentives for investors to stay in currency (higher interest rates compensating for depreciation risk)
- **Poorly managed**: Policy makers favor domestic conditions; monetary policy too loose → domestic pain delayed, inflation stoked; attempt to stop capital outflow with capital controls or restrictive measures

**SMOOTHING THE DOWNTURN**
- **Well-managed**: Use reserves judiciously to smooth foreign capital withdrawal while working to close imbalances
- **Poorly managed**: Rely on reserve sales to maintain higher spending levels

**MANAGING BAD DEBTS/DEFAULTS**
- **Well-managed**: Work through debts of over-indebted entities, making up gap with credit elsewhere
- **Poorly managed**: Allow disorderly defaults that lead to increased uncertainty and capital flight

### Recovery Timeline

- Takes a few years for country to recover
- Investors burned in last cycle are reluctant to return
- But price of domestic goods and labor fell with currency → country becomes **attractive destination for foreign investment**
- Higher exports and foreign direct investment kickstart growth
- If policy makers protect and recapitalize critical financial institutions, domestic financial pipelines support recovery
- Country returns to early part of cycle: new virtuous cycle where **productive investment opportunities attract capital** → capital drives up growth and asset prices → attracts more capital

**Recovery Metrics:**
- Incomes and spending pick up (usually after 1-2 years)
- Takes several years (usually ~3) from bottom before activity level returns to average
- **Real FX is undervalued** (typically by ~10% on PPP basis) at start of stabilization and stays cheap
- Exports pick up a bit (1-2% of GDP)
- Capital inflows return a few years later (on average 4-5 years)
- Equities take about same time to recover in foreign currency terms

## The Spiral from Inflationary Depression to Hyperinflation

- While many policy makers can engineer recovery with transitory balance of payments crises, **subset of inflationary depressions spiral into hyperinflations**
- **Hyperinflations**: extreme inflation (goods/services prices more than doubling every year or worse) + extreme wealth losses + severe economic hardship
- More common than one might think

### The Critical Characteristic of Hyperinflations

**Policy makers don't close the imbalance between external income, external spending, and debt service, and keep funding external spending over sustained periods by printing lots of money.**

- Sometimes involuntary: **Weimar Germany** had crushing external debt service burden (war reparations) that couldn't be defaulted on
- Amount of capital that needed to flow out was so great that big inflation was nearly destined
- Other cases: policy makers choose to keep printing to cover external spending—aiming to prop up growth rather than bringing spending in line with income
- If done repeatedly over years at large scale, country may face avoidable hyperinflation

### Why It's Hard to Stop Printing

- Contrary to popular belief, **not easy to stop printing during crisis**
- Stopping printing when capital is flowing out can cause extreme liquidity tightness and deep economic contraction
- The longer crisis continues, the harder it becomes to stop printing
- **Weimar Germany example**: literally a shortage of cash because hyperinflation meant existing money stock could buy less and less
  - By late October 1923, Germany's entire 1913 money stock would have bought just "a one-kilo loaf of rye bread"
  - Stopping printing would have meant virtually no cash for commerce (until alternate currency emerged)
- In inflation spiral, printing money can seem like **prudent choice at the time**—but continuing to print feeds the spiral until there's no way out

### How the Spiral Plays Out

- Over time, as currency declines and printing increases, people shift behavior and **inflationary psychology sets in**
- Currency declines inspire additional capital flight → escalating feedback loop of depreciation, inflation, and money printing
- Eventually, linkages that drove growth in earlier rounds decline and **money printing becomes less effective**

**With each round of printing, more printed money is transferred to real or foreign assets instead of being spent on goods and services that fuel economic activity.**

- Investors who shorted cash and bought real/foreign assets repeatedly did better than those who saved/invested domestically
- Domestic currency holders shift from productive assets to **real assets (gold) and foreign currency** to hedge inflation and wealth deterioration
- Foreign investors stay away
- Because economy is weak and investors buying real assets, **stocks suffer** and no longer provide wealth effect that drove earlier spending
- Result: currency devaluation doesn't stimulate growth
- This dynamic is **important to inflationary deleveragings**

### The Wage-Cost Spiral

- When continual currency declines lead to persistent inflation, it becomes self-reinforcing, nurturing inflation psychology and changing behavior
- Key mechanism: inflation spreads to wages → **wage-cost spiral**
- Workers demand higher wages to compensate for reduced purchasing power
- Producers raise prices to compensate
- Sometimes happens mechanically through **wage indexing**—contracts where employers agree to increase wages with inflation
- Vicious cycle established: currency depreciates → internal prices rise → quantity of paper money increases → value of currency falls → prices rise again

### Behavioral Changes

- Savers, burned before, now move quickly to protect purchasing power
- Quicker to short cash and buy foreign and physical assets
- **Bank depositors want access on short notice** → shorten lending to banks
- Deposits move to short-term checking accounts rather than longer-term savings
- Investors shorten duration of lending or stop entirely (worried about default or worthless repayment)
- During inflationary deleveragings, **average debt maturities always fall**
- Also cheap to short cash (higher inflation and money printing lowers real interest rates) → withdrawal of capital and faster borrowing cause **illiquidity in financial system**
- Banks find it practically impossible to meet demand for cash
- No longer able to fulfill contracts due to cash shortages → businesses also suffer
- At this point, central banks face choice: extreme illiquidity vs. printing at accelerating rate
- Path is obvious: **print to provide liquidity**
- Print money to support banks, often lend directly to businesses
- When interest rates insufficient to compensate for future currency declines, this liquidity provision enables investors to continue borrowing and investing abroad and in inflation hedges (real assets, gold) → **further contributes to inflation and depreciation spiral**

### Rising Foreign Currency Debt Burdens

- Much of country's debt is foreign-currency-denominated
- Debt burdens rise when currency falls → requires spending cuts and asset sales
- While initially overcome by falling-currency stimulation, **becomes increasingly devastating** as that effect fades and debt burden grows
- Higher debt burdens mean foreign investors want higher interest rates as compensation for default risk
- Currency declines and inflation often **increase debt service and debt burdens**, making it harder to stimulate through currency

### Tax Evasion and Capital Flight

- Many governments respond to rising debt burdens by **raising taxes on income and wealth**
- With net worths eroding from bad economy and failing investments, wealthy desperately try to preserve rapidly shrinking wealth at all costs
- Leads to **extremely high rates of tax evasion** and increased capital flight abroad
- Typical in deleveragings

### Credit Creation Without Growth

- As growth weakens further, lack of foreign lending shuts down important source of credit creation
- Lots of domestic credit creation and borrowing, but **doesn't result in much growth**—so much spent abroad on foreign assets
- Of local spending, much doesn't contribute to GDP
- Example: investors buy lots of gold, factories, imports (even rocks in Weimar Republic!) as store holds of wealth
- Capital investments like machinery and tools purchased as **stores of value, not because they were needed**

### Complete Loss of Faith in Currency

- Easy to see how these forces create feedback mechanism: inflation and currency declines escalate until **people completely lose faith in currency**
- Money loses role as store of value (people hold at most a few days' reserves)
- Long list of zeros makes it impossible unit of account
- **Money breaks down as medium of exchange**: currency instability makes producers unwilling to sell for domestic currency; producers demand payment in foreign currencies or barter
- Shortage of foreign exchange → illiquidity peaks and demand collapses
- **This form of illiquidity can't be relieved by money printing**
- Stores close, unemployment rises
- As economy enters hyperinflation, **contracts rapidly** because currency declines that were once beneficial now just create chaos

### Hyperinflation's Wealth Destruction

- Economic contraction
- **Extreme wealth redistributions**: lenders see wealth inflated away, as do debtor's liabilities
- Chaos creates political tensions and clashes
- Frequently public servants (police officers) strike because they don't want to work for worthless paper money
- **Disorder, crime, looting, violence** typically reach peak during this phase
- **Weimar Germany**: government issued "state of siege," granting military greater power over domestic policies (arrests, breaking up demonstrations)

### Investment Principles During Hyperinflation

- Get short the currency
- Do whatever you can to get money out of country
- Buy commodities
- Invest in commodity industries (gold, coal, metals)
- **Buying equities is mixed bag**: investing in stock market becomes losing proposition as inflation transitions to hyperinflation
- Instead of high correlation between exchange rate and share prices, there's **increasing divergence**
- During this time **gold becomes preferred asset** to hold
- Shares are disaster even though they rise in local currency
- Bonds are wiped out

### Ending Hyperinflation

- Once inflationary deleveraging spirals into hyperinflation, **currency never recovers** its status as store hold of wealth
- Creating new currency with very hard backing while phasing out old currency is the classic path countries follow to end inflationary deleveragings

---

## War Economies

- War economies are **totally different from regular economies** in production, consumption, and accounting
- Increasing GDP from armaments production that get destroyed in war
- Reduced unemployment from increases in military service
- Shifts in production and profitability from top-down resource allocation
- Nature of borrowing, lending, capital flows differ from peace periods
- Understanding these statistics requires whole different orientation
- Trying to adequately convey how war economies work would take a whole different book

### The Economic/Geopolitical Cycle

- Cycle of economic conflicts leading to military conflicts between emerging/established powerful countries is **obvious to anyone who studies history**
- Well-described by historians (though typically from geopolitical perspective, less from economic/market perspective)
- Well-recognized as classic by historians

**When 1) within countries there are economic conflicts between the rich/capitalist/political right and the poor/proletariat/political left that lead to conflicts that result in populist, autocratic, nationalistic, and militaristic leaders coming to power, while at the same time, 2)**
<!-- pp. 121-140 -->

## War Economies and Economic/Geopolitical Cycles

### Economic Rivalries and International Conflict

- When conflicts arise between comparably strong economic and military powers, economics and politics become especially intertwined—**probabilities of disruptive conflicts (wars) become much higher than normal**
- Economic rivalries within and between countries often lead to fighting to establish which entities are most powerful
- During wars: **war economies** emerge, followed by hangover effects on markets, economies, and geopolitics
- Wars profoundly affect currency values, debt burdens, equity valuations, and the entire **social-political fabric**
- After wars: extended **periods of peace** where dominant powers set rules because no one can challenge them—until the cycle begins again with a rival power emerging

### Economic/Geopolitical Cycle Timeline

- Understanding the big cycle of empire ascendancy/decline requires a **250-year time frame** (Dalio will explore in future report)
- At times of economic rivalry: **emotions run high**, **firebrand populist leaders** preferring antagonistic paths are elected, wars occur

### Two Types of International Relationships

**a. Cooperative-competitive relationships:**
- Parties consider what's important to the other, try to give it in exchange for what they want
- Tough negotiations done with respect and consideration
- Like "two friendly merchants in a bazaar or two friendly teams on the field"

**b. Mutually threatening relationships:**
- Parties think about how to harm the other, exchange painful acts
- Hope to force the other into a position of fear to make them give in
- Interact through "war" rather than "negotiation"

### Path Selection and Power Dynamics

- Either side can force the threatening/war path onto the other, but **both sides must cooperate** to maintain the win-win path
- Relative powers determine outcomes in both cases
- In cooperative relationships: parties should realize what the other could force on them and appreciate quality of exchange
- In threatening relationships: **power defined by relative abilities to endure pain vs. inflict it**
- When power balance is unclear (many untested ways to reward/punish), **cooperative path is safer**
- Threatening path will clarify—**through the hell of war**—which party is dominant and which must be submissive
- After wars: typically extended periods of peace with dominant country setting rules for as long as it takes for the cycle to repeat

### War Economy Priorities

**During wartime:**
- Most important priority: **maintain access to financial and non-financial resources** required to sustain a good war effort
- Countries must either borrow or have large foreign exchange reserves (cannot fund war + tolerable non-war spending from current income alone)
- Access to borrowing depends on: creditworthiness, capital market development, especially **soundness of local currency debt market**
- Maintaining access to critical non-financial resources (for war effort + acceptable domestic conditions) is essential

**Post-war period:**
- Market consequences of **debts and war outcome (won or lost) will be enormous**
- > "The worst thing a country, hence a country's leader, could ever do is get into a lot of debt and lose a war because there is nothing more devastating. ABOVE ALL ELSE, DON'T DO THAT. Look at what it meant for Germany after World War I in the 1920s (which is explained in Part 2) and for Germany and Japan after World War II in the late 1940s and the 1950s."

### Wartime Economic Shifts

**Typical patterns (charts show averages across war cases):**
- Government spending surges relative to private spending—**govt accounts for large share of total spending in wartime**
- Military personnel increases ~5x as % of population
- Military spending increases ~5x as % of GDP
- During WWII: **20% of US workforce shifted to military**

**Post-war adjustment:**
- Both winners and losers saddled with debt and need to transition from war-economy to normal economy
- Big contraction of military spending causes **postwar recession** (factories retooled, people formerly employed in war effort need new jobs)
- Countries enter periods of **deleveraging**, working through big war debts with same dynamics as other depressions/delevagings
- **Losers experience significantly worse economic conditions:**
  - Much deeper depression
  - Resort to more money printing
  - Spend down savings/reserves meaningfully
  - Much higher inflation rates (sometimes **hyperinflation**)

### Post-War Economic Divergence (Winners vs. Losers)

**Charts demonstrate patterns:**
- **Growth vs Potential:** Economy generally runs hot early on, then slows down (losing side suffers deeper recession)
- **Inflation:** Picks up on both sides early on, but diverges materially when losing side resorts to printing
- **Fiscal Balance:** Governments run sizable fiscal deficits to fund wartime spending
- **Money Supply:** Losing side resorts to significant money printing as situation becomes more dire
- **Reserves (USD, Est., Indexed):** Central banks accumulate war chest of reserves; losing side has to draw it down meaningfully

### Case Study References

- Dalio recommends: **Weimar Germany** and **US Great Depression** case studies in Part 2
  - Weimar: good picture of postwar period for a war loser
  - US Great Depression: shows how economic conflicts initiate sequence of events leading to shooting wars
- Also recommends: charts of **US and UK post-WWII** (examples of war winners)
- No charts for Germany, Japan, and other WWII losers post-1945: consequences for their currencies, markets, and economies were so devastating that **statistics were either ridiculously unreliable or unavailable**

## In Summary (Key Principles)

### Managing Debt Crises: Core Insight

> "I want to reiterate my headline: managing debt crises is all about spreading out the pain of the bad debts, and this can almost always be done well if one's debts are in one's own currency. The biggest risks are typically not from the debts themselves, but from the failure of policy makers to do the right things due to a lack of knowledge and/or lack of authority. If a nation's debts are in a foreign currency, much more difficult choices have to be made to handle the situation well—and, in any case, the consequences will be more painful."

### Policy Maker Variation

- **Understandings and authorities** of policy makers varies greatly across countries
- Leads to dramatically different outcomes
- Policy makers tend not to react forcefully enough until crisis is extreme
- Authorities vary based on strength of regulatory and checks-and-balances systems
- **Trade-off:** Strong checks/balances bring benefits but also risk that required policy moves can't get done due to rigid rules and agreements

### The Impossibility of Perfect Rules

- Impossible to write rules well enough to anticipate all possibilities
- Even most knowledgeable and empowered policy maker unlikely to manage crisis perfectly
- Unforeseen circumstances must be responded to **instantly, often in hours**, within a legal/regulatory system lacking crystal-clear rules

### Checks and Balances: Double-Edged Sword

- Normally critical protection from too much concentration of power
- **Can exacerbate crisis** by slowing decision making and allowing those with narrower interests to block necessary policy moves
- Policy makers attempting bold actions typically **criticized from all sides**
- **Politics is horrendous during debt crises**—distortions and outright misinformation are pervasive

### Long-Run Perspective

- While big debt crises can be devastating over short to medium-term (3-10 years), **in the long run they fade in importance relative to productivity** (more forceful though less apparent because less volatile)
- **Political consequences** (e.g., increases in populism) from these crises **can be much more consequential than the debt crises themselves**
- Charts of real GDP per capita help put big debt crises (and "little" ones we call recessions) in perspective
- Contractions of >3% shown in shaded areas
- **Growth rates over time far more important than bumps along the way**
- Biggest bumps came more from **wars** than worst depressions (though wars were often caused by political fallout from depressions)

## PART 2: Detailed Case Studies

**Three case studies presented:**
1. German Debt Crisis and Hyperinflation (1918-1924)
2. US Debt Crisis and Adjustment (1918-1924)
3. US Debt Crisis and Adjustment (2007-2011)

## German Debt Crisis and Hyperinflation (1918-1924)

### Overview

- Most **iconic inflationary depression cycle in history**
- German debt crisis and hyperinflation followed WWI end, carried into mid-1920s
- Set stage for economic and political changes of 1930s
- Similar to 2008 US Financial Crisis and 1930s Great Depression accounts
- Goes through particulars with reference to "Archetypal Inflationary Depression" template
- Though nearly a century ago amid exceptional political circumstances (Germany's WWI defeat + huge reparation burden from Allies), **basic dynamic of debt cycles, economic activity, and markets drove what happened**
- Differences between inflationary vs. deflationary depression cases highlight what makes some inflationary and others deflationary
- Includes newsfeed at end of chapter for vivid sense of real-time events

## July 1914–November 1918: World War I

### War Setup

- WWI (July 1914–November 1918) set stage for this big, dramatic cycle
- During war years Germany:
  - Left the gold standard
  - Accumulated large stock of domestic and foreign debts
  - Began money printing to finance ever-growing fiscal deficits
  - Experienced first bout of currency depreciation and inflation
- Germans expected short war based on Franco-Prussian War of 1870 experience
- Assumed war would ultimately be paid for by large indemnities levied on defeated Allied powers
- Instead: **extremely long and expensive affair** financed primarily through domestic debt
- Germany ended up having to **pay a huge war reparation bill rather than collecting one**

### Classic War Debt Pattern

- Classic case of war debts built up by a country that loses the war (more extreme than most)
- Classic case of country with **large foreign currency denominated debt held by foreign creditors**
- Knowing "Archetypal Inflationary Depression" dynamics from Part 1, you should have good idea how this story will play out

### Background: Gold Standard Era

- Like most countries at the time, Germany had been on **gold standard** at beginning of war
- All paper currency, including government debt, was **convertible to gold at a fixed rate**
- However, by 1914, the central bank didn't have enough gold to back the stock of money in circulation at the fixed price (as one might expect)
- As soon as war broke out: **smart German citizens rushed to exchange paper money for bullion**—caused run on banking system
- Within weeks: central bank (Reichsbank) and Treasury paid out **195 million marks' worth of gold** to public (~10% of total gold reserves)
- To prevent further losses, ensure liquidity in banking system, avoid major money supply contraction: policy makers **suspended conversion of money to gold on July 31, 1914**
- Government also authorized Reichsbank to buy short-term Treasury bills and use them + commercial bills as collateral for money it was printing
- Pace of printing rapid: by end of August, **quantity of Reichsbank notes in circulation increased ~30%**

### Currency as Medium of Exchange and Store of Wealth

- Classic dynamic: **Currency is both medium of exchange and store hold of wealth**
- When investors hold many promises to deliver currency (i.e., lots of debt denominated in a currency) and supply of that currency is tied to something that backs it, the central bank's ability to produce currency is limited
- When investors want to convert bonds to currency/money and spend it: puts central bank in difficult position of choosing between:
  - Lot of debt defaults, OR
  - Floating lot of currency (which can debase its value)

> "So, whenever (a) the amount of money in circulation is much greater than the amount of gold held in reserves to back the money at the designated price of conversion, and (b) investors are rushing to convert money into gold because they are worried about the value of their money, the central bank is in the untenable position of either reducing the supply of money in circulation (i.e., tighten credit) or ending convertibility and printing more money."

- Central banks almost always choose suspending convertibility and printing more money versus allowing credit contraction, **because it's less painful**

### Money Printing and Currency Depreciation Effects

- Printing lots of money and depreciating currency causes just about anything denominated in that depreciated money to go up in price
- People like it when things they own go up in value and they have more money to spend
- Also true in times of war
- Policy makers marshaling economic resources toward war effort print money to give themselves more to spend
- Printing helps prevent:
  - Liquidity crisis in banking system
  - Economic contraction from taking place
- Either would be very disruptive to war effort
- **Most countries fighting in WWI ended up suspending gold standard at one point or another**

### Financing the War

- Fighting war required German government to significantly increase expenditures (**government spending as share of GDP increased 2.5x between 1914 and 1917**)
- Financing this spending meant either:
  - Raising new revenues (taxation), OR
  - Increasing government borrowing

> "As there was huge resistance to increasing taxation at home, and as Germany was mostly locked out of international lending markets, the war had to be financed by issuing domestic debt."

- In 1914: German government debt was insignificant
- By 1918: Germany had amassed total local currency debt stock of **100 billion marks, about 130% of German GDP**

### Debt Denomination: Local vs. Foreign Currency

- Although debt stock was huge, prior to German surrender and imposition of war reparations, **most was denominated in local currency**
- Policy makers recognized this was a good thing
- According to Reichsbank: > "the greatest weakness in the war financing of the enemies is their growing indebtedness abroad [particularly to the US]"—forces them to scramble for dollars when needing to make debt-service payments
- In contrast, most German debt taken on to finance the war (prior to reparations) was **in local currency and financed by Germans**

### Public Willingness to Finance War (1914-1916)

- Until second half of 1916: German public both willing and able to finance entire fiscal deficit by purchasing government debt
- War bond issuances regularly **oversubscribed**
- However, as war dragged on and inflation accelerated: Treasury found public no longer prepared to hold all debt it was issuing
- Partly due to size of deficit (increasing substantially as war progressed)
- Also because **wartime inflation had caused real interest rates to become very negative** (government war bonds paid fixed 5% interest throughout war, whereas inflation had climbed above 30% by early 1915)
- Result: **lenders not adequately compensated** for holding government debt
- Inflation driven by:
  - Wartime disruptions and shortages
  - Capacity constraints in key war industries
  - Currency weakness (mark fell ~25% against dollar by 1916)
- While some naive lenders clung to hope that:
  - Government would return to gold standard at old exchange rate once war over, OR
  - Compensate them for losses due to inflation
- Others feared they would most likely be paid in money that had lost most of its purchasing power, so they **ran out of debt denominated in that currency**

### Debt Monetization Spiral

- Currency remained effective medium of exchange while losing effectiveness as store hold of wealth
- So: government borrowed money to pay for war expenditures, and Reichsbank was forced to **monetize the debt** as investors came up short in supplying the money
- Effect: **increased money supply** by amount equal to fiscal deficit not financed by public

> "As debt monetization is inflationary (there is more money in the economy chasing the same quantity of goods and services), a self-reinforcing spiral ensued—i.e., debt monetization increased inflation, which reduced real interest rates, which discouraged lending to the government, which encouraged additional debt monetization."

- As deficit was huge (averaging ~40% of GDP between 1914-1918): led to **money supply increasing by almost 300% over course of war**

### Money Creation Acceleration (1917-1918)

- Pace of money creation accelerated after 1917 as German citizens became increasingly unwilling to purchase government debt
- Central bank forced to monetize growing share of deficit
- Although number of marks in circulation almost doubled between mid-1917 and mid-1918: **did not cause material decline in currency**
- In fact: **mark rallied** over this period as Russia's withdrawal increased expectations of German victory
- Mark only began falling in second half of 1918 as German defeat began looking increasingly likely

### Foreign Currency Borrowing (Last Two Years)

- In last two years of war: German government began **borrowing in foreign currencies** because lenders unwilling to take promises to pay in marks
- > "When a country has to borrow in a foreign currency, it's a bad sign."
- By 1918: Reichsbank and private firms each owed about **2.5 billion gold marks in FX to external lenders**
- **Gold mark** = artificial unit used to measure value of paper mark to gold
- In 1914: one gold mark equaled one paper mark
- Debt of 5 billion gold marks = debt denominated in gold, with bill equal to amount of gold that could be purchased by 5 billion marks in 1914

### Hard Currency Debt Cannot Be Printed Away

> "Unlike local currency debt, hard currency (foreign currency and gold denominated) debt cannot be printed away."

- Debtors would have to get hands on either gold or foreign exchange to meet these liabilities
- While hard currency debt was <10% of total debt stock, it was still larger than entire public gold reserves of the Reich
- Hope: once Germany won war, mark would appreciate, making debt burdens more manageable
- And: **losing countries would be forced to pay for most of Germany's foreign and domestic debts**

### Policy Maker Warnings About Losing

- Policy makers recognized that if Germany lost war or failed to extract large reparations: **would be extremely difficult to pay back debts with hard money**
- According to **Rudolf Havenstein** (president of Reichsbank): covering those debts > "will be extraordinarily difficult if we do not get a large war indemnity"
- According to German economist **Edgar Jaffé**: unless England paid between 1/3 and 1/2 of Germany's war costs, result would be > "monstrous catastrophe" of "currency collapse" once German citizens learned domestic debts would likely be paid in depreciated money, and government agencies and private firms scrambled to get hands on foreign exchange to pay off external liabilities

### War's End: The Debt Trap

- Breaking peg to gold and monetizing ever-growing fiscal deficit, combined with wartime economic disruptions and shortages: led to declining exchange rate and pickup in inflation
- By beginning of 1918: mark had lost ~25% of value versus dollar and **prices had tripled**
- However, in context of WWI, this was pretty typical—i.e., what most countries did to fund their wars
- German inflation, while high, not significantly higher than other war participants

### Conditions for Inflationary Depression

- WWI (and accompanying monetization of debt) did NOT directly cause Germany's postwar inflationary depression
- As mentioned in archetype template: > "while inflationary depressions are possible in all countries/currencies, they are most common in countries that:"

**Key vulnerability factors:**
- **Don't have a reserve currency:** No global bias to hold their currency/debt as store hold of wealth
- **Have low foreign exchange reserves:** Not much cushion to protect against capital outflows
- **Have a large stock of foreign debt:** Vulnerability to cost of debt rising via increases in either interest rates or value of currency the debtor has to deliver, or shortage of available credit denominated in that currency
- **Have a large and increasing budget and/or current account deficit:** Need to borrow or print money to fund the deficits
- **Have negative real interest rates:** Lenders not adequately compensated for holding the currency/debt
- **Have a history of high inflation and negative total returns in the currency:** Lack of trust in value of currency/debt

### Germany's Post-War Vulnerability

> "By the end of the war, the German economy met all of these conditions."

- Losing war meant mark was not going to be reserve currency of postwar era
- Large stock of external debts acquired
- Very likely Allies would force Germany to pay them additional sum in war reparations
- Foreign exchange reserves not sufficient to meet existing stock of external debts, let alone any additional reparation payments
- Real interest rates very negative, offered little compensation to creditors holding German currency/debt
- Budget and trade balance in very large deficits—Germany would remain dependent on borrowing/monetization to finance expenditures and consumption
- Finally: experience of high inflation, money printing, and negative total returns in holding mark had begun to reduce trust in German currency/debt as store hold of value

<!-- pp. 141-160 -->

## November 1918–March 1920: The Treaty of Versailles and the First Inflation

### Initial Capital Flight and Currency Collapse

- **German surrender (November 1918)** triggered massive capital flight as citizens rushed to convert wealth into foreign currencies and assets
- Mark declined ~30% vs dollar over next few months; stock market lost almost half its real value; government debt rose ~30%
- Money supply grew ~50%; inflation hit 30%
- Capital flight driven by uncertainty about peace terms and Germany's ability to pay massive liabilities with hard money

### Treaty of Versailles Shock

- Germans initially optimistic about lenient terms—hoped reparations would be limited to occupied territories, paid in goods, no annexations without referendum
- **Treaty reality (June 28, 1919)**: Germany lost 12% of territory, 10% of population, 43% of pig-iron capacity, 38% of steel capacity
- Required to compensate Allied citizens for all wealth seized during war, but received no compensation for its own assets confiscated abroad
- Had to honor all prewar debts to Allied creditors (even private citizens' debts)
- **Reparations**: Commission to determine final bill in 1921; interim payment of 20 billion marks in gold, commodities, ships, securities

### Currency Collapse Accelerates (July 1919–January 1920)

- Mark fell 90% vs dollar; inflation surged to 140% by year-end
- **Key mechanism**: Currency weakness driven primarily by capital flight, not money printing
- Citizens feared debt obligations would force extortionately high taxes and wealth confiscation to raise hard money
- As private wealth was at risk, getting out of the currency made rational sense

> "German citizens rushing to get their capital out of the country because they justifiably feared that these promises to deliver currency (i.e., these debt obligations) would make it very difficult, if not impossible, for the German government to meet its liabilities with hard money."

### The Self-Reinforcing Spiral

- **German debtors with external liabilities** saw real expenses soar as mark fell—rushed to pay off foreign debts, flooding FX market
- This further weakened mark, triggering more capital flight
- **Classic dynamic** in countries with large foreign currency debt during balance of payments crises

> Hamburg industrialist: "We are driving ourselves to destruction if everyone now…secretly sells mark notes in order to be able to meet his obligations. If things keep on this way, the mark notes will become unusable."

### Critical Insight: Causality of Currency Weakness and Inflation

**Money printing was not the source of currency weakness—currency weakness was the cause of money printing.**

- Capital flight from currency/country drove currency down → drove higher inflation
- This is **how inflationary depressions happen**
- Central bank faced choice: allow liquidity/debt markets to tighten vs print money to fill void
- Central banks typically print to fill void → currencies decline

### Benefits of Currency Devaluation

**While currency declines hurt importers and those with foreign currency debts, devaluations are stimulative for the economy and asset markets:**

- Boost exports and profit margins (goods cheaper on international markets)
- Make imports more expensive (supports domestic industries)
- Assets rise in local currency value; attract capital from abroad (financial assets become cheaper in global terms)
- From July 1919–March 1920, mark decline and negative real rates boosted German economy, equity and commodity markets

### Economic Recovery Phase

- Export industry thrived; unemployment declined; business profitability improved; real wages remained low
- Hope that encouraging exports/discouraging imports would bring balance of payments into equilibrium

> German official: "I regard our gravely ailing currency as an admirable means of dispelling the hatred felt abroad towards Germany, and of overcoming the reluctance to trade with us by our enemies. The American who no longer gets for his dollar 4.21 marks worth of goods from us, but 6.20 marks worth, will rediscover his fondness for Germany."

### Erzberger Financial Reform (December 1919)

- Policy makers began addressing domestic debt burden and fiscal deficit
- **Finance minister Matthias Erzberger** proposed comprehensive tax-reform package
- Transfer wealth from "haves" to "have-nots" via highly progressive taxes: income (approaching 60%), wealth (65%)
- Increased Reich's income from direct taxes to 75% (1914: ~15%)
- Revenue sufficient to pay all government expenditures except reparations by 1922
- Previously, majority of revenue came from public enterprises (railroads), duties on exports/imports/coal

### Inflation as Alternative to "Confiscatory Taxation"

- Some officials advocated **currency weakness and inflation** as effective alternative to confiscatory taxation
- **Dr. Friedrich Bendixen** argued collecting monstrous sums through taxes would weaken productivity, reduce receipts, drive Reich to collapse
- "Only the transformation of war loans into money can bring salvation"
- Inflation would "cleanse" Germany of local currency war debts, allow new beginning
- Central bank officially rejected this, but recognized things might "develop along these lines anyway"
- By end of 1919, inflation had reduced domestic war-debt burden to ~25% of original 1918 value
- Wealthy scrambled to buy foreign currencies/real assets to prevent wealth being inflated/taken away

### Central Bank's Alternative: Deflationary Recession

**The classic dilemma: help creditors (long currency) vs debtors (short currency).**

- Central bank's alternative to allowing inflation to "naturally" reduce real debt: **tighten monetary policy and engineer deflationary recession**
- Would allow Reich to pay citizens closer to true value of loaned wealth, but crush domestic credit/demand, generate significant unemployment

**In economic crises, wealth redistribution from "haves" to "have-nots" more likely because:**
- Conditions of "have-nots" become intolerable
- There are more "have-nots" than "haves"

### Political and Social Context

- Unemployment still high, food shortages rampant, mass of returning soldiers needed jobs
- Clashes between capitalists and workers (typical in depressions) happening across Europe
- Communist revolution in Russia (1917); Communist ideas spreading
- **John Maynard Keynes**: "The inflation is unjust and deflation is inexpedient. Of the two perhaps deflation is the worse, because it is worse in an impoverished world to provoke unemployment than to disappoint the rentier [i.e., the capitalist lender]."

### Late 1919/Early 1920 Progress

- By late 1919/early 1920, Germany had inflated away most domestic debt, passed comprehensive tax reform, beginning to see pickup in economic activity
- Good news on reparations: Allies invited Germany to submit own proposal; critics like **John Maynard Keynes** finding sympathy abroad
- Exchange rate began to stabilize

### Kapp Putsch (March 1921)

- **Wolfgang Kapp** led right-wing nationalist attempt to overthrow Weimar government and institute autocratic monarchist regime
- Collapsed within days after workers refused to cooperate and declared general strike
- Though complete failure, reminder of fragile political environment
- **Economic pain of deleveragings/depressions gives rise to populist and reactionary leaders on both Left and Right**

> Berlin businessman: "Just at the moment when we begin again to work more than before…when in London the recognition is mounting that through the imposition of the Versailles Treaty one has committed a fearful political stupidity, and that accordingly the exchange rate begins to improve, the military party…under the leadership of a man who is a notorious reactionary, again throws everything overboard and forces our workers into a general strike and demonstrations that are unnecessary because nothing will be achieved that way."

## March 1920 to May 1921: Relative Stabilization

### Stabilization Period Overview

- 14 months of **"relative stabilization"**: mark halted slide, prices remained stable, German economy outperformed developed world
- Germany wasn't collapsing from economic/political chaos as predicted
- Those shorting the mark lost considerable sums (notably **John Maynard Keynes**, who lost £13,000)

### Global Backdrop: Severe Contraction

- US and UK tightened monetary policy severely
- 1920-1921: US industrial production fell 20%, UK fell 18.6%; unemployment climbed to 22% (US) and 11.8% (UK)

### German Monetary Policy: Accommodative

- **Reichsbank kept discount rate at 5% until 1922** (contrast to other central banks)
- Regularly intervened to inject liquidity when credit tightened
- Spring 1921: when business liquidity tightened moderately, Reichsbank accelerated commercial bill purchases (from 3.1% to 9% of outstanding bills)
- Fiscal policy remained accommodative: real expenditures (ex-reparations) rising in 1920-1921
- Budget deficit remained huge (~10% of GDP), continued to be financed by floating debt issuance

### Stimulative Policies Enable Germany to Escape Global Contraction

- 1919-1921: industrial production increased 75%
- However, economic activity remained extremely depressed (industrial production and real GDP still well below 1913 levels)
- Considerable poverty and suffering in German society
- This period should be understood as **growth within a larger period of economic contraction**

### Inflation Dynamics: Domestic vs Global Forces

- Rising economic activity and reflationary policies did not produce much inflation (March 1920–May 1921)
- **Domestic inflationary pressures offset by global deflationary forces**
- Import prices from US/UK fell ~50%
- Rising capital flows into outperforming German economy helped stabilize currency, allowed slower money supply growth
- Mark rallied, inflation declined; by early 1921 prices stopped rising for first time since 1914

### Foreign Capital Inflows: The Speculative Bubble

- Germany became "hot economy" to invest in
- Foreigners willing to pour money in, financing ever-growing trade deficit
- Some commentators called it **"tremendous" speculative bubble**; **Keynes** called it "the greatest ever known"
- **Classic bubble sign: many flooding market were new buyers with no prior experience**

> Keynes: "[From those] in the streets of the capital…[to] barber's assistants in the remotest townships of Spain and South America…the argument has been the same…Germany is a great and strong country; someday she will recover; when that happens the mark will recover also, which will bring a very large profit."

### Scale of Inflows

- By 1921, almost a third of deposits in seven largest German banks were foreign-owned
- **Speculative inflows supported relative stabilization in mark**
- **Made central bank's job easier by reducing trade-offs between growth and inflation**

**Key insight from archetypal template**: When capital flows into a country, it tends to lower inflation rate and stimulate growth (all else equal); when capital leaves, it does the opposite—**making central bank's job much more difficult**

### Germany's Dependence on "Hot Money"

- German economy became increasingly dependent on **"hot money"** (speculative investments that could be pulled out at moment's notice)
- Hot money continued coming in year after year to finance fiscal and external deficits
- Classic bubble phase of balance of payments crisis: **increasing dependence on capital inflows to maintain spending/economic activity made recovery fragile, sensitive to any minor event that could trigger sentiment shift**

### Policy Maker Concerns: Currency Appreciation

- Mark's sharp appreciation in early 1920 was unwelcome for policy makers
- Falling mark considered essential to maintaining German export competitiveness, supporting employment growth, building hard currency earnings pool
- Considered "one good fortune in midst of misfortune" without which Germany would lose possibility of exports
- Initial appreciation hit exports hard; chamber of commerce said industry had practically "ground to a halt"
- Unemployment surged (trade union members unemployed tripling)

### Government Intervention to Depress Mark (March–June 1920)

- Economic ministry intervened to **deliberately depress mark** and stimulate employment
- **It worked**: mark fell, competitiveness returned, unemployment began to decline

### Policy Priority: Unemployment Over Inflation

- During this period, German policy makers more concerned about deflationary forces spreading to Germany than inflation from stimulative policies
- Rising unemployment and potential social unrest considered much more menacing than return of rising prices

> Reconstruction minister to industrialist: "[I] am not afraid of the inflation…if the crisis which has already broken out to its full extent in England were not to come over to us, we should allow the printing press to do a bit more work and begin rebuilding the country. This activity would enable us to build a dam against the crisis."

- Stabilization of mark, inflation, and economic conditions remained contingent on large speculative inflows and stable balance of payments

## May 1921: The London Ultimatum

### Reparations Ultimatum

- Arguments between Germany and Allies came to head with **"The London Ultimatum"** (May 1921)
- Allies threatened to occupy Ruhr Basin within six days if Germany didn't accept new reparation bill
- **Total reparations: 132 billion gold marks** (~330% of German GDP)
- 50 billion in quarterly installments, adding up to ~3 billion gold marks/year
- **Debt-service burden: ~10% of German GDP, 80% of export earnings**
- Remaining 70 billion would begin whenever Allied powers (not Germany) determined economy capable
- Germany faced huge hard currency debt burden + threat of debt service tripling at moment's notice

### Structure Creates Investor Anxiety

- Reparations demanded were enormous, dashed expectations of conciliatory agreement
- Payment structure deeply unnerving to potential investors and German public
- Debt service burdens would likely get bigger if economic conditions improved
- Chart shows size of hard currency debts imposed on Germany relative to other economies before major inflationary depressions
- **Weimar Germany dwarfs every other case**

### Immediate Market Reaction

- As soon as reparation burden announced, mark began selling off
- Declined 75% by end of year; inflation also returned, prices almost doubling over same period

> German participant at Versailles: "The world must be made to understand that it is impossible to burden a country with debts and at the same time to deprive it of the means of paying them…the most complete collapse of the currency…cannot…be avoided if the peace treaty is maintained in its current form."

### Balance of Payments Crisis

**The reparation schedule created a balance of payments crisis analogous to any payment problem faced by individuals/households/corporations.**

**To come up with money, a country must: 1) spend less, 2) earn more, 3) finance payments through borrowing/tapping savings, or 4) default (or convince creditors to give relief).**

- Unlike domestic war loans, Germany could not print away debt burden (debts not denominated in paper marks)
- Policy makers would need to rely on combination of four levers

### Why Cutting Spending Would Be Extremely Painful and Politically Dangerous

- ~50% of German government's total revenues would go to reparations
- Cuts in nonreparation expenses would need to be drastic to make a difference
- Most nonreparation spending went to essential social services: unemployment relief, subsidies for food/housing, funding for leading public employers (railways, shipyards)
- Large spending cuts considered **"politically impossible"**
- With Bolshevik Revolution in Russia, ongoing bloody civil war, growing Communist movement in Germany—policy makers feared potential revolution from left
- Simultaneously, increasingly humiliating Allied demands and economic pain fueling **far-right nationalism**
- Political chaos intensified: strikes, riots, acts of political violence increasingly common
- Summer 1920: state of emergency declared following widespread looting
- March 1921: Communist groups seized shipyards/factories, dispersed only after firefights with police
- October 1921: **finance minister Matthias Erzberger assassinated** by ultranationalists for role in 1918 surrender
- Government refused to cut social spending; Reichsbank refused to stop monetizing deficit

### Why Tax Burdens Were Already Extremely High

- Cutting spending untenable; so was raising income via additional taxes
- **Erzberger reforms of 1919** had already raised tax burdens considerably
- Increasing burden posed same political/social risks as cutting spending
- Additional tax increases would be immensely difficult to pass (Erzberger reforms had been watered down significantly by opposition in Reichstag)
- Would also likely accelerate capital flight

> Keynes on impossibility of meeting reparation burden through taxation: "the whips and scorpions of any government recorded in history [would not have been] potent enough to extract nearly half…[the required] income from a people so situated."

### Why Existing Savings Were Extremely Limited and Securing Lending Was Impossible

- Virtually no savings to draw on to service debts
- Treaty of Versailles had essentially seized/frozen all Germany's prewar foreign holdings and canceled all debts owed to Germans
- Those with foreign currency savings (primarily exporters) incentivized to keep earnings in foreign bank accounts (feared reparations burden would encourage government seizure of wealth)
- Central bank's gold reserves not enough to cover even first interest payment

### Limited International Credit Appetite

- Little appetite internationally to extend Germany credit on scale to spread out reparation burden
- **Two reasons**: 1) Most developed economies burdened by war debts of their own (owed to US), in midst of severe recessions; 2) German government (and most Germans) weren't creditworthy
- Head of Reichsbank approached Bank of England for 500 million gold mark short-term loan for second reparation installment—**"politely refused"**

> British Chancellor of Exchequer: "the difficulty was that there was a vicious circle. Germany said she could not stop the emission of paper money and repay her obligations unless she was able to raise a foreign loan, and she could not raise a foreign loan unless she could pay her obligations."

### Default Impossible

- Defaulting unilaterally impossible—Germany threatened with invasion
- Though leaders furiously and continuously tried to renegotiate payments, bitter feelings from war made victors (especially France) disinclined to make concessions

### The Currency Management Lever

**Unlike a household facing a payment problem, a country can change the amount of existing currency, and by doing so affect its value. This gives it an additional lever to manage a balance of payments crisis.**

- While Reichsbank could try to defend currency by raising rates and tightening credit (would increase returns on mark-denominated assets/deposits, attract more capital
<!-- pp. 161-180 -->

## Germany's Hyperinflation (continued)

### Policy Choices and the Path to Hyperinflation

**German policymakers faced impossible choices** — currency depreciation would crush domestic demand and close trade deficit but require "unimaginably severe contraction" intolerable to impoverished, conflict-ridden society. Only alternative: allow currency depreciation and print money to alleviate liquidity tightening from capital flight.

> **Key characteristic of hyperinflation**: "policy makers don't close the imbalance between income and spending/debt service; instead, they fund and keep funding spending over sustained periods of time by printing lots of money."

**Moderate money printing is helpful in deleveraging** — prevents severe economic contraction. **But excessive relational printing and severe currency devaluation (reflationary) relative to deflationary levers (austerity, debt restructuring/default) causes severe inflationary depressions.**

**Germany's mistake**: Extreme difficulty of using austerity, dissaving, external borrowing, and debt defaults as levers pushed policymakers to rely exclusively on money printing. They wagered inflation was "least terrible" option but failed to balance deflationary and inflationary forces.

### June 1921–December 1921: The Emerging Inflationary Spiral

**Classic inflationary spiral dynamics emerged** in H2 1921:
- **Germany's impossible foreign debt obligations** → currency decline → inflation + liquidity crisis
- **Central bank provided liquidity** by printing money and buying debt rather than allowing commerce to contract
- This triggered **further capital flight, inflation, tightening liquidity, more money printing** — spiral accelerated
- Central bank depleted substantial gold reserves for first reparation payments

**Reparations and capital flight** caused mark to decline 75% over period, inflation approached 100% per annum. Sharpest declines in October 1921 after **League of Nations ceded Upper Silesia** (major coal/industrial region) to Poland despite residents voting to remain in Germany.

**Rising inflation → surge in retail purchases** — not from economic activity but **"flight of income and savings into real goods before inflation could eat away purchasing power."** American Council of Hamburg noted "vast amount of retail buying"; *Hamburgische Correspondent* called it "monstrous lust for goods."

**"General liquidation"** — foreigners buying because mark was cheap, Germans buying to escape inflation → bare shelves. Berlin official shocked at "plundering of retailers by foreigners with highly valued currencies." British observer: shops closed 1-4pm, refused to sell more than one article per customer, "Germans laying in stores for fear of further price rise or total depletion of stocks."

**Massive increase in durable/real-asset purchases**: Auto sales at all-time highs, textile bookings months ahead, cotton firms refused new orders, industries at full capacity with overtime to meet demand. This was **"classic flight into inflation-hedge assets"** — not economic prosperity.

> Bavarian official: "The fall of the mark...has brought a real anxiety among the propertied classes. Everyone seeks to do something with their money. Everything is bought that can be bought, not only for present need, not only for future use, but in order to get rid of the paper and have objects to exchange when the time comes that it is worth absolutely nothing."

**Real interest rates became very unattractive**, real cost of borrowing plummeted → **surge in borrowing, which became extremely attractive.** Real investment reached prewar highs, monthly bankruptcy rates declined 75%. But investment was unproductive — firms pushed borrowed money into capital for "use value" not "intrinsic value." Firms keeping wealth in debt assets (bonds) suffered devastating losses. Called **"flight from the mark to the machine"** — resulted in excessive poorly-performing investments once inflation passed.

**Growing demand for real goods → increasing employment** in industries producing those goods → **unemployment fell, workers' bargaining power increased** → pushed for wage increases and better working hours. Summer 1921 standoffs led to large nominal wage gains, but these didn't keep up with inflation — **real incomes fell ~30%**, worsening tensions between "haves" and "have-nots."

**Only clear beneficiary: export sector.** Foreign sales increased as German goods became cheaper internationally. But pickup less than expected given large currency decline because: (1) considerable hostility to German exports abroad limited gains, (2) labor costs also declining globally from severe recession, limiting competitiveness gains from depreciating mark.

**Stock market speculation — "an orgy"** — stocks nearly tripled in value (inflation-adjusted) over period. August: Berlin exchange so overloaded it shut down 3x/week. By November: operating 1 day/week, banks refused share orders after 10am. Newspaper: "Today there is no one—from lift-boy, typist, and small landlord to the wealthy lady in high society—who does not speculate in industrial securities and study official quotations as most precious letter."

**This bull market was NOT driven by improving fundamentals or optimistic discounting** — it reflected **rush to get out of money or get short money (borrow it) against long "stuff" position.**

> Observer: "Stock market speculation today is the organized flight from the mark...at a time when the return on an investment diminishes in the same ratio as the value of the paper mark and when therefore even the solid capitalist, if he does not want to impoverish himself from day to day, must acquire real values. This alone has led to an extraordinary increase in the stock market business."

By end 1921: deteriorating conditions, loss of faith in mark, rapidly rising prices threatened economic/political collapse. Inflation nearing 100%. **Only thing preventing total collapse: foreigners' willingness to continue buying marks and fund Germany's massive external deficit** (~10% GDP). Despite loss of confidence at home, foreigners kept purchasing German assets at cheap prices.

### January 1922–May 1922: Negotiating a Reparation Moratorium

**Allied powers concluded German economy needed relief from reparation payments** — alarmed by chaos. Reparation debt burden was most crushing and inescapable. Continuing status quo risked total economic collapse → worsen political chaos → make future reparation collection impossible. But considerable disagreement among Allies on extent of relief and what Germany should give in return.

**Classic debtor/creditor standoff during deleveragings**: Debtors (Germans) demanded maximum relief; creditors (Allied powers) tried to extract maximum money without plunging debtor into insolvency. **Game of power brinksmanship played by all.**

> **J.P. Morgan Jr.** reportedly told confidant: "The Allies must make up their minds as to whether they wanted a weak Germany who could not pay, or a strong Germany who could pay. If they wanted a weak Germany they must keep her economically weak; but if they wanted her to be able to pay they must allow Germany to exist in a condition of cheerfulness, which would lead to successful business. This meant, however, that you would get a strong Germany, and a Germany that was strong economically would, in a sense, be strong from a military point of view also."

**Cannes Conference (January 1922)**: Temporary compromise reached. Reparations Commission reduced debt service bill 75% for remainder of year, provided Germany:
- Raised new taxes (including forced loan of 1 billion gold marks on wealthy citizens)
- Reduced spending and money printing
- Granted **Reichsbank formal independence** from government

**Concessions were mostly symbolic.** Taxes too small to meaningfully close budget deficit. **Reichsbank president Rudolf Havenstein** welcomed independence — said it would allow him to print as much money as needed for liquidity without fiscal policy constraints.

**Renewed optimism about reparation relief halted mark's slide.** By end January: mark risen 30% from 1921 lows, inflation (while high at ~140% per annum) stopped accelerating. **Inflationary spiral halted temporarily**, providing relief to German economy.

German policymakers pressed Allies for additional concessions, arguing **balance of payments, not central bank money printing, was ultimately responsible for inflationary crisis.**

> **Foreign Minister Walter Rathenau** (March 29, 1923 speech to Reichstag): "Over and over again we encounter the notion that if the value of our money has been ruined this can only be because we have printed money. The recipe which we are given against this is: stop your printing press, bring your budget in order, and the misfortune is ended. A grave economic error!...[How is it possible] to make continuous gold payments without the help of foreign loans and at the same time keep the exchange rate intact? The attempt has never been made to give such a prescription and it cannot be given. For a country that does not produce gold cannot pay in gold unless it buys this gold with export surpluses [which Germany did not have] or unless it is borrowed [which Germany could not do]."

**Mechanics of economics and markets were simple and basically same then as now.** While central bank could easily extinguish domestic currency denominated debt (via previously described methods), it could not easily extinguish external debts.

**February–May: currency expectations driven primarily by reparation negotiation news.** Good news (comprehensive agreement) → mark rallied, inflation expectations fell. Bad news (agreement less likely) → mark fell, inflation expectations rose. **Mark experienced numerous 10-20% swings** on sentiment changes. By end May: mark down ~40% vs. dollar as reparation agreement prospects deteriorated.

Chart shows **major mark swings from reparation negotiation updates** — markets "chopped up and down in big moves every time there was essentially any update."

### June 1922–December 1922: Hyperinflation Begins

**June 1922: expectations of reparation settlement collapsed** due to three interconnected events:

1. **French declared they would no longer accept reparations commission conclusions** on Germany's capacity to pay. France would make own determinations, seize German assets (particularly **coal mines in Ruhr**) if Germany defaulted. Instead of possible moratorium, Germany faced paying France whatever French demanded or risk sustained occupation of most valuable territory.

2. **International committee headed by JP Morgan Jr.** investigating gold loan to Germany to rebuild economy and ease external debt burden — contingent on reparation moratorium progress. Following French declaration, **loan committee forced to conclude extending credit impossible.**

3. **June 24: Foreign Minister Walter Rathenau assassinated** by right-wing group. Rathenau, despite belligerent speeches, was one of few German politicians trusted by Allied powers and enjoyed significant domestic support. If anyone could mediate settlement with Reparations Commission and get it through Reichstag, it was Rathenau. Assassination illustrated **threat of nationalism and extremist populism** hanging over Germany.

**Foreigners now rushed to pull capital from Germany.** Previously, ~1/3 of all German bank deposits were foreign-owned, foreign speculation had been huge source of support for economy and balance of payments. **Over next few months, ~2/3 of these deposits disappeared**, capital inflows collapsed. Simultaneously, German capital flight accelerated — wealthy citizens rushed to get wealth out before confiscatory January compromise taxes took effect. **Mark collapsed, hyperinflation began.**

**Acute liquidity crisis in German banking system → runs on banks.** Central bank printing no longer fast enough to keep up with mark flight abroad and rising prices. By July: **banks forced onto 3-day work weeks**, had to inform depositors they lacked enough cash to honor deposits or make weekly wage payments for large business clients. Some banks began printing own marks (illegal).

**The liquidity crisis was self-reinforcing. Depositors, seeing banks struggling to honor liabilities, began withdrawing deposits in ever-growing numbers, which only made liquidity crisis worse.**

By August 1922: economy on brink of financial collapse. **Central bank forced to respond by rapidly accelerating pace at which it printed marks and monetized growing share of government debt.**

Charts show:
- Marks in circulation (billions) and % of Treasury Bills held by Reichsbank both skyrocketing in 1922
- Central bank purchases of commercial bills and credit to loan bureaus both surging exponentially

**Central bank began purchasing commercial bills en masse.** As liquidity crisis deepened in fall, additionally accelerated provision of direct credits to banking system. By year end: Reichsbank holding ~1/3 of all commercial bills in circulation, increased credits to banking system by 1,900%. Such interventions **prevented financial system collapse**, led to ten-fold increase in money supply.

**Unlike past bouts** where inflation picked up substantially but never entered hyperinflation, **this round sent inflation skyrocketing.** Partly due to scale of liquidity injection needed to offset foreign capital pullback, partly due to **changing inflationary psychology** — most people had believed inflation semi-managed, now believed it out of control.

Charts show mark exchange rate vs USD and inflation (Y/Y) both going exponential in late 1922.

**In inflationary depressions, classic pattern: with each round of printing, more money leaves currency instead of going into economic activity.** Domestic currency holders see that investors shorting cash (borrowing in weakening currency) and buying real/foreign assets repeatedly do better than those who save and invest at home → increasingly catch on and shift from investing printed money in productive assets to purchasing real assets (gold) and foreign currency. **Foreign investors no longer return because repeatedly burned.**

**By August, with prices rising 50%+ per month and accelerating, policymakers recognized approaching hyperinflationary spiral** but felt no alternative but to continue printing. Why didn't they stop?

**Once inflationary depression reaches hyperinflationary stage, extremely difficult to stop printing. When extreme capital flight and extreme inflation feed off one another, money becomes harder to come by even as it loses worth.**

> **Keynes** visiting Hamburg (summer 1922, early hyperinflation phase): "The prices in the shops change every hour. No one knows what this week's wages will buy at the end of the week. The mark is at the same time valueless and scarce. On the one hand, the shops do not want to receive marks, and some of them are unwilling to sell at any price at all. On the other hand...the banks were so short of ready cash that the Reichsbank advised them to cash no checks for more than 10,000 marks...and some of the biggest institutions were unable to cash their customers' checks for payment of weekly wages."

**To stop printing would result in extreme shortage of cash and bring about total collapse of financial system and all commerce.** Economist at time: "[To stop the printing press] would mean that in a very short time the entire public, and above all the Reich, could no longer pay merchants, employees, or workers. In a few weeks, besides the printing of notes, factories, mines, railways and post office, national and local government, in short, all national and economic life would be stopped."

**People tend to think hyperinflations caused by central banks recklessly printing too much money, and all they need to do to stop it is turn off printing press.** If that easy, hyperinflations would almost never occur! Instead, **inflation spirals push policymakers into circumstances where printing is least bad of several terrible options.**

For Weimar Germany, cost of not printing was not only potential economic collapse but **political fragmentation.** France's repeated threats to occupy German territory if reparations unpaid made halting printing an invitation to foreign invasion. Also lowered hopes for productive reparations negotiations. Prominent industrialist: "The Reichsbank can no more stop inflation than the Burgermeister of Hamburg can tell the patients in the hospital to stop being ill...as long as it is possible for the French to invade Germany, there can be no talk of a stabilization of our currency."

**By September, Germany trapped in classic hyperinflationary spiral. Extreme capital withdrawals and rapidly rising prices forcing central bank to choose between extreme illiquidity and printing money at accelerating rate. Doing former would result in total collapse in business activity, so really no choice. However, as money supply grew, no one wanted to hold it in such depreciating environment. Velocity of money accelerated, triggering even more capital flight, money printing, and inflation, and so on and so forth.**

Chart relationship (shown logarithmically due to exponential growth): **currency weakness was leading inflation, which was leading money supply growth — not the other way around.**

**Reckless money printing was less the cause of hyperinflation than what was required to prevent massive deflationary defaults by banks (and just about everyone else) and deflationary economic collapse.**

### Currency's Dual Functions Break Down

**Money and credit serve two purposes: medium of exchange and store hold of wealth. As spiral accelerated, mark completely lost status as store hold of value.** People rushed to exchange it for any available alternative — real goods, foreign exchange, capital equipment.

**Very soon, exponential inflation rates made it impractical to trade in marks, so currency also began to lose status as means of exchange.** Foreign currencies (especially dollar) and even makeshift currencies became increasingly common in day-to-day transactions and price quotations. For instance, local Reichsbank branches found they didn't have enough actual paper notes for businesses to meet payroll obligations. So **central bank and finance ministry allowed some large depositors to print their own currencies** — called **Notgeld** ("emergency money"). Soon everyone began considering whether mark would go extinct.

> *Frankfurter Zeitung* (October 1922): "German economic life is...dominated by a struggle over the survival of the mark; is it to remain the German currency, or is it doomed to extinction? During the past few months foreign currencies have replaced it as units of account in domestic transactions to a wholly unforeseen extent. The habit of reckoning in dollars, especially, has established itself, not only in firms' internal accounting practice, but above all as the method of price quotation in trade, industry and agriculture."

**October 12, 1922: government attempted to stop ever-growing flight into foreign currency** by putting restrictions on German citizens purchasing foreign FX. **Such capital controls are classic lever to control inflationary depressions; they are rarely successful.** Reasons: (a) capital controls have limited effectiveness at best because usually easy to get around, (b) **trying to trap people typically leads them to want to escape even more.** Not being able to get money out of country triggers psychology analogous to inability to get money out of bank: produces fear that produces run.

**Stock market was one of few remaining domestic escapes from inflation.** After declining 50% (real terms) since June, stocks rallied in H2 October — but like fall 1921, rally had nothing to do with underlying conditions or future economic prospects. In fact, fall 1922 real profit margins collapsing as hyperinflation chaos hit productivity. **Rally was extremely small in context of overall real stock market decline during debt crisis.**

Charts show: real equity prices and food as % household expenditure both rising sharply through 1922.

### January 1923–August 1923: The Occupation of the Ruhr and the Final Days of Inflation

**January 1923**: Economy already in chaos, prompted by Germany missing promised timber delivery as reparation payment, **French-Belgian force invaded Germany and occupied the Ruhr** (Germany's primary industrial region). French hoped this would pressure Germany to pay reparations more cooperatively and allow France to extract payments in coal.

**Germans responded by declaring "passive resistance."** Miners in Ruhr would strike to make occupation as costly as possible for French government. However, **resistance needed to be subsidized by Reich** — both miners and employers had to be paid. Also meant ~half country's coal supply needed to be imported, adding additional strain on balance of payments. Result: government spending increased, balance of payments deteriorated, liquidity shortages pushed Reichsbank to print even more, and **inflation (already at astronomical levels) accelerated even more.**

**France's aggression left opening for Germany in reparation negotiations** — occupation of country approaching economic ruin was widely denounced. To buy time, **Reichsbank began issuing dollar-denominated debt** (at considerable discount to international prices due to credit risk) to buy marks — central bank targeting peg against dollar. Between January-June 1923, Reichsbank
<!-- pp. 181-200 -->

## Late 1923 to 1924: Ending the Hyperinflation

### The Failed FX Intervention (Early 1923)

- Central bank sold 400 million gold marks of borrowed FX and reserves to defend the mark's peg against the dollar
- Raised rates to 18% (symbolic given inflation ran near 10,000%)
- **Reichsbank president**: Intervention aimed not at permanent stabilization (impossible without reparations solution) but to "recover for the German economy...as long as possible...a time of somewhat calm...to free the market from wild and unscrupulous speculation"
- FX intervention briefly halted the slide—mark appreciated 50% for three months, introducing brief deflation that hurt shorts
- **By May, became clear Reichsbank lacked reserves** to pay dollar-denominated principal/interest and maintain the peg
- Fixed exchange rate policy abandoned six months after implementation
- Hyperinflation returned stronger: **36 trillion percent by November 1923**

### Hyperinflation's Devastating Scale (July 1922 - November 1923)

- Mark depreciated **99.99999997%** vs. dollar (cost of dollars increased 1,570 billion percent)
- Prices rose **387 billion percent**
- In 1913, six billion marks circulated in whole German economy; by late October 1923, entire 1913 money stock would buy **one kilo-loaf of rye bread**
- Living through this chaos was "immensely painful and traumatizing" for German citizens
- Experience later validated many Nazi criticisms of the "disastrous" Weimar era

### Crisis Conditions by Late 1923

- Unemployment rising rapidly
- Inflation well above 1,000,000%
- Real tax revenues diminishing at alarming rate
- Food growing scarce
- Transacting with marks nearly impossible—economic machine ground to a halt
- One local mayor: "I have never encountered such hordes of people starving and wandering about"
- **Rudolf Wissell** (future labor minister): "The inflation in which we find ourselves at this time is murdering the Republic. It will be the gravedigger of our Republic"

### Allied Intervention and Reparations Relief

- Allied powers concluded that without substantial reparation relief, German policy makers remained helpless to avert total economic collapse
- **November 1923**: Suspended reparations payments, reopened negotiations on restructuring the debt
- Gave German policy makers necessary breathing room

### Five Crucial Stabilization Steps

German policy makers took five logically sequential steps:

**1. Renegotiating Reparations Debt**
- Eventually reduced debt service burdens to just **1% of GDP**
- Made crippling reparation burden manageable

**2. Introducing New Currency (Rentenmark)**
- Backed by gold-denominated assets and land, pegged to the dollar
- Could fail if investors believed it would be used to monetize debt payments

**3. Strict Limits on Money Printing**
- Capped amount of rentenmarks that could be printed and debt that could be monetized
- Central bank can only credibly avoid monetizing debt if government can pay its bills
