**4. Raising Revenue, Cutting Expenditures**
- Government raised revenues and cut expenditures with deep, extremely painful cuts
- Central bank capped loans to businesses and raised borrowing rates
- Built faith in new currency

**5. Building Foreign Exchange Reserves**
- Borrowing FX from Allies
- Encouraging German citizens who fled currency during hyperinflation to repatriate savings

> Earlier one-off measures (short-lived currency peg, capital controls) hadn't been enough—Germany needed comprehensive and aggressive policy shift that abolished the currency, accepted hard backing, placed extreme limits on monetization, credit creation, and government spending.

### 1) Restructuring the Reparations Debt

- Without reparation relief, structural drivers of inflation would have remained intact—unlikely any new currency could command faith as store of wealth
- **September 1923**: German industrialists in Ruhr began cooperating with Weimar government in negotiations with French
- Industrial magnates had long resisted concessions but recognized need for diplomacy as conditions deteriorated and workers rioted
- Agreed to resume coal transfers
- By mid-October, Weimar government ended financial support of "passive resistance" to Ruhr occupation—eliminating one of largest expenses
- **End of November**: British and French created **Dawes committee** to review and reduce Germany's reparations obligations
- Committee suspended reparations payments until final conclusions—made it easier for Germany to balance budget during stabilization
- **For 10 months**, Germany made no hard currency payments to reparations commission

### The Dawes Plan (August 1924)

- Significantly and permanently eased Germany's reparations burden
- Payments rescheduled, debt service costs reduced
- **Reparations payments = only 1% of German GNP in 1924-1925**—reduction of over 90% vs. 1923
- Germany still owed full 130 billion gold marks, but payments so spread out it was possible to meet them
- **Comparison chart** shows dramatic reduction:
  - Potential Debt Service (1921-1923): ~33% GDP if Allies demanded full payment
  - London Schedule Payments (1921-1923): ~18% GDP (suspended until Germany capable)
  - Typical Inflationary Depression: ~13% GDP
  - **Dawes Plan (1925): ~1% GDP**

### Debt Burden Relief Through Inflation

- With reparations debt service substantially reduced and domestic debt mostly inflated away, **Germany's debt burden largely relieved**
- Charts show dramatic decline in total government debt from peak during hyperinflation (300%+ GDP) to much lower levels after debt service suspended

### 2) Creating a New Currency

**Creating a new currency with very hard backing** is classic path for countries ending inflationary deleveragings.

### Three Stages of Currency Replacement (August 1923 - October 1924)

**Stage 1: Disorganized Emergency Measures (Summer 1923)**
- Transacting in mark became so difficult major institutions turned to alternatives
- Many used **foreign exchange** in place of domestic currency
- From late 1922 onward, major industries set prices in foreign currencies
- By 1923, much wholesale trade conducted directly in dollars, francs, or florins
- Those without FX access turned to **"emergency money"**—issued by local governments, trade associations, or companies, backed by real assets
- Emergency money illegal but easier to use than paper mark
- By fall 1923, **nearly 2,000 types** actively circulating in Germany

**Stage 2: Gold Loan Bills (August 1923)**
- Government issued very small-denomination debt indexed to dollars as temporary currency
- "Gold Loan" bills could be circulated directly or used as backing for other emergency currencies
- Backed only by stamp claiming "wertbestandig" (stable value) and promise government could "raise supplements to the tax on capital" to honor them
- **Public so desperate** for reliable store of wealth that gold loan bills retained value—in fact, tended to be hoarded rather than used, disappeared from circulation shortly after being issued

**Stage 3: The Rentenmark (October 15, 1923)**
- Government announced creation of **Rentenbank** and new stable-value currency, the **rentenmark** (circulation November 15)
- More ambitious scheme, an immediate "miraculous" success
- **Rentenmarks exchangeable** for either fixed quantity of paper marks OR fixed quantity of hard assets (vice versa)
- Hard backing applied not just to newly issued bills but **to all paper marks already in circulation**
- Rentenmark pegged to paper mark at ratio of **1 to 1 trillion**, and to dollar at **4.2 to 1**—symbolically significant exchange rate setting gold value equal to pre-war, peace-time mark
- Both pegged rates held in following months
- By December, both rentenmark and newly-pegged paper mark trading at par in foreign markets
- Inflation fallen to sustainable levels

### 3) Imposing Limits on Money Printing

- Key to new currency's success: Rentenbank issued relatively little and convincingly backed issues with real assets
- No large debts denominated in it—**total credit Rentenbank could extend capped at 2.4 billion marks**
- Rentenmarks directly secured by mortgages on **5% of all German agricultural and industrial property** ("renten" = annuities paid on mortgages)
- Even more important: **implicit security of Reichsbank's gold reserves**
- By 1923, real value of money supply so reduced by flight from paper marks it could be backed entirely by government's reserves
- Reichsbank cracked down on illegal emergency money, withdrew gold loan bills from circulation
- **Chart shows**: Monetary base in dollars had fallen to equal Germany's gold reserves by 1923

**Third Phase: The Reichsmark (October 11, 1924)**
- After year of relative stability, introduced another new hard currency: the **reichsmark**
- Purchased with rentenmarks at 1:1 ratio
- Unlike rentenmark (formally backed by mortgage bonds), reichsmark **exchangeable directly for bullion** at Reichsbank
- Could be converted into **same quantity of gold as pre-war mark**
- All remaining paper marks withdrawn by June 5, 1925
- Old currency (rentenmark) gradually phased out over next decade

> New currency crucial but not sufficient—took much more than rentenmark and reichsmark to create lasting stabilization. **Currency depends on credibility of institutions issuing it.** Not many promises to deliver currency (i.e., not much debt denominated in new currencies) meant central bank not forced to choose between inflationary monetization or deflationary defaults. Limited amount of currency meant it could be kept stable. Rentenbank succeeded because fundamentals were solid.

### 4) Ending Monetization

**In order to build confidence in new currency, countries in inflationary deleveragings need to stop monetizing debt.** As long as government can force central bank to print to cover liabilities, risk that new currency will be debased and supposedly hard backing abandoned. **Important that central banks be independent of political system.**

**Two Major Announcements**:
- **August 18, 1923** (private): Reichsbank informed Weimar government that beginning 1924, **would not discount any additional government debt**—memo quickly circulated among industrial elite, spurred policy makers to reconsider need for fiscal reforms
- **October 15, 1923** (public): Central bank officials stated new Rentenbank would **cap total government credits at 1.2 billion rentenmarks** and forbid Reichsbank from monetizing any government debt after November 15

**Testing Central Bank Independence**:
- Public and government initially doubted central bank would honor promises
- Rentenbank lent government entirety of 1.2 billion allocation almost immediately
- By December 1923, government requested additional 400 million rentenmarks
- **When Rentenbank officials stood firm**, successfully signaled beginning of new era of central bank independence and end of unchecked monetization

### 5) Closing the Deficit

**When central bank stops monetizing debts, government must either find new creditors, close deficits, or take control over central bank and continue monetizing debt. Since finding new creditors usually impossible in inflationary deleveraging, and monetizing debt only postpones the problem, budget ultimately needs to be balanced.**

- By late 1923, Weimar regime concluded it needed to close deficits—no choice, and with debts largely relieved, now possible
- **German finance minister**: "If we do not succeed in cutting loose from the inflationary economy through ruthless choking off of Reich expenditures, then the only prospect we have is general chaos"

**Fiscal Measures (August - October 1923)**:
- Government ran budget deficits since outbreak of war in 1914
- Took steps to address problem: indexed certain taxes to inflation, passed additional emergency taxes
- By October, indexed all taxes to inflation

**Spending Cuts**:
- Took aggressive measures to reduce expenses
- Dismissed **25% of employees**, cut salaries of remainder by **30%**
- Ended expensive subsidies to workers in "passive resistance" in Ruhr
- Such austerity extremely painful—would have been almost impossible year or two before
- By end of 1923, **German public willing to do almost anything** to bring prices back under control

**Virtuous Cycle of Stabilization**:
- More gradual inflation and stable exchange rate increased yield of existing taxes
- Stabilization increased real tax receipts, helping reduce budget strains and increasing public confidence in government's ability to avoid future monetization
- Following introduction of rentenmark in November, **real tax receipts increased rapidly**: from ~15 million gold marks in October 1923 to **over 300 million in December 1923**
- **By January 1924, government running a surplus**

### 6) Tightening Credit

- Officials tightened access to credit so private credit wouldn't add to inflationary pressures

**Two Channels**:
1. **February 1924**: Government "revalued" some privately held debts (required debtors to give creditors more than face value)—included mortgages, bank deposits, industrial debentures whose values fallen to almost nothing during hyperinflation. Implemented to appease angry creditors, also worked as tightening. Just as debt reductions ease credit/weaken currency/increase inflation, **debt revaluations tighten credit, support currencies, lower inflation**

2. **April 7, 1924** (more significant): Reichsbank **capped total credit** it would extend to private sector—wouldn't call back existing debt, but would extend new credit only as prior debts paid off. Strict cap painful for businesses short term, but also meaningfully stabilized German inflation, which **turned slightly negative in May 1924**

### 7) Accumulating Foreign Exchange Reserves

- Though all programs/policies/agreements put German economy on firmer footing, not everyone convinced they would ensure permanent stabilization
- Between November 1923 and October 1924, speculators continually bet against the mark
- As long as Germany lacked meaningful FX reserve, **speculative attacks remained threat** to continued stability

**Two Major Shifts Restored FX Reserves**:

1. **Transfer of privately held foreign currencies to Reichsbank**: As institutions/individuals gained confidence in rentenmark, began converting foreign currency they'd hoarded during hyperinflation into new bills. Between November 1923 and January 1924 alone, FX holdings at Reichsbank grew from ~20 million gold marks to **nearly 300 million**. These flows paused as inflation rose in early 1924 (individuals began accumulating foreign currencies again), but resumed once credit standards tightened and inflation stabilized

2. **Dawes Plan foreign exchange loan**: In addition to reducing reparations, Dawes Committee extended Germany significant FX loan. Issued October 1924, amounted to **800 million gold marks** worth of foreign currency, divided mainly between dollars, pounds, francs. Though not extraordinarily large, **meaningfully improved Reichsbank's credibility** when defending against speculative attacks, sent reassuring signal to foreign investors. In four years following Dawes Plan implementation, American investors poured money into German debt, attracted by relatively high yields

### Conclusion and Aftermath

- By 1924, crisis largely over
- Germany entered brief recovery period before Great Depression hit hard a decade later
- Second crisis economically devastating, fueled rise of right wing and left wing populists, **Hitler's rise to power**, and all that followed

### The News Section (New York Times Excerpts 1914-1919)

**Key headlines showing crisis evolution**:

- July 29, 1914: "Berlin Very Nervous: Big Banks Will Support Stocks—Keeping Gold in Vaults"
- August 3, 1914: "Reichsbank Hoards Gold: Patriotic Appeal in Germany Not to Demand Coin"
- November 23, 1918: "Ebert and Haase Deny Banks Will Be Seized: Uphold the War Loans"
- January 16, 1918: "Berlin Food Scarcer: Population Forced to Keep to the Ration Quantity"
- May 1, 1919: "Germany to Lose 70% of Iron and One-third of Her Coal"
- June 8, 1919: "If Germany Doesn't Sign—Starvation. Allies Are Ready to Enforce a Blockade More Rigorous Than Ever Before"
- August 10, 1919: "Mark Goes Still Lower" (reached lowest point in history in Switzerland, quoted at 35 centimes vs. peace price of 125 centimes)
- November 15, 1919: "Won't Take German Money; Hanover Tradesmen Refuse Cash Bought at Low Exchange Rates"

<!-- pp. 201-220 -->

## The Weimar Hyperinflation Timeline (Late 1919-1924)

### Post-War Economic Conditions (November 1919 - Early 1920)

- **November 29, 1919**: Imperial war loans and city securities became attractive to American speculators despite weak exchange rates
- **December 1, 1919**: Government imposed temporary export controls to stem the "selling out" of Germany
- **December 5, 1919**: **Matthias Erzberger** proposed 60% tax on largest incomes to fund postwar obligations, arguing for "universal solidarity" in sharing war burdens
- **December 17, 1919**: Government bond loan drastically undersubscribed — only 3.8 billion marks raised vs. 5 billion expected
- **January 2, 1920**: Berlin stock market rallied on expectations of Peace Treaty signing and improved export conditions
- **January 23, 1920**: **Erzberger** faced mounting political opposition
- **January 26, 1920**: Germany began evacuating territories lost under Treaty of Versailles (e.g., Danzig becoming free city)
- **January 31, 1920**: Currency collapse drove hoarding behavior — Germans paid 500 paper marks for 20-mark gold pieces as "iron reserve" against future crisis

### Political Instability and Early Inflation (1920-1921)

- **March 14, 1920**: **Kapp Putsch** — counterrevolutionary movement overthrew Ebert Government, set up new administration in Berlin
- **March 28, 1920**: Popular rage over laxity in prosecuting revolutionaries; strikes and threats of Ruhr occupation
- **June 1, 1921**: Germany began grappling with reparations payment obligations after negotiation period ended
- **June 20, 1921**: Germany sought additional foreign credits and sounded bankers on establishing balances secured by Reichsbank silver
- **June 23, 1921**: Industrial recovery underway — factories "working with all their might," positioned to become "commercially superior to all other European countries"
- **June 23, 1921**: Wirth Government planned to tax Germans 20 billion marks more for reparations, showing "intensive thoroughness and deadly earnestness"

### Currency Deterioration and Reparations Conflict (Late 1921-Early 1922)

- **June 25, 1921**: Payment method shifted — 800 million marks convertible to European currencies instead of dollars, reducing dollar depreciation risk
- **June 30, 1921**: Germany made second 44 million gold mark reparations payment without disorganizing foreign exchanges (unlike first payment)
- **July 7, 1921**: **Wirth** announced 80 billion marks/year in taxes needed — faced dilemma between direct taxes (alienating bourgeois) and indirect (alienating proletariat)
- **July 20, 1921**: Mystery over heavy capital withdrawals suspected of going abroad
- **July 25, 1921**: German industries entered boom phase driven by "artificially cheap labor and coal"
- **August 6, 1921**: German debt rose 8.3 billion marks in June alone, totaling 135 billion
- **August 7, 1921**: Tax plan completion depended on Upper Silesia resolution — additional billions needed for reparations and internal budget deficit
- **August 28, 1921**: **Erzberger** assassinated by Nationalist extremists, consolidating radical opposition
- **September 4, 1921**: **John Moody** warned: "Germany's paper prosperity is leading to a crash" — falling mark and rising prices signaled inflationary crisis

### Accelerating Inflation (Late 1921-Mid 1922)

- **September 30, 1921**: Mark exchange collapse affected "every family in country" through rising food prices
- **November 8, 1921**: Mark touched "lowest figure in its history" — Bank statement showed "vast new inflation" driving quotations down
- **January 2, 1920**: Berlin stock market surged on Treaty of Versailles expectations
- **January 7, 1922**: Reparation Commission refused to grant payment delays until Germany specified postponement length, expected sums, and guarantees
- **January 10, 1922**: Cannes conference generated "unbridled optimism" reflected in Bourse and paper mark strength
- **January 29, 1922**: Germany requested moratorium on 1922 cash payments; proposed re-establishing financial stability through heavier tax burden, forced internal loan, and tribute payments in kind
- **February 6, 1922**: Government considered resorting to "direct issue of paper money"
- **February 27, 1922**: Price rises resumed "independently of the mark's movement on exchanges" — activity in production and trade mirrored great 1921 mark collapse
- **February 28, 1922**: Allies decided to exclude reparations from conference discussion; mark fell further

### Political Crisis and Deepening Hyperinflation (March-August 1922)

- **March 2, 1922**: German industrialists warned reparations deal meant "death blow to German exports, economic slavery and ultimate ruin" if material reparations became operative
- **March 10, 1922**: Allied reply "shattered Germany's hopes" of U.S. participation in future conferences
- **March 22, 1922**: Allied Board planned partial moratorium on limiting German paper money
- **March 25, 1922**: Germany's "quietest crisis" — "deadly calm of utter discouragement" with no political emotion, only despair
- **March 27, 1922**: Mark fell to new low after Reparations Commission shock — financial circles deemed stipulations for German home finance "impracticable"
- **March 30, 1922**: French remained "deaf to German pleas" — would not believe Germany unable to pay for restorations
- **May 11, 1922**: Germany's reparation reply offered tax/loan submission plan to cover expenditures and halt inflation
- **May 12, 1922**: French called reply "evasive," claiming Germany played for time
- **May 26, 1922**: Bankers conference opened in favorable conditions despite surface indications; Poincare warned of penalties if Germany failed May 31 engagements
- **June 1, 1922**: Allies approved German answer and granted partial moratorium on year's reparations payments
- **June 25, 1922**: **Walter Rathenau** assassinated — Germans rallied to defend republic against royalist threats
- **July 3, 1922**: Reichsbank officials predicted next two reparations installments would be paid, but commandeering high-currency export bills to buy marks for paper would "inevitably bring further depreciation"
- **July 26, 1922**: Allied Representatives decided Germany must continue paying 2 million marks/month
- **July 31, 1922**: Mark neared panic as crowds stormed stores "in eagerness to buy before prices go higher" — prospects "all favorable to continued and catastrophic decline"

### Currency Collapse Crisis (August-November 1922)

- **August 2, 1922**: "Practically all of Germany's accruing foreign obligations including purchases of food and material are being paid for with paper marks" — further mark decline required more paper to purchase wheat/cotton or meet reparations
- **August 3, 1922**: **Dr. Andreas Hermes** (Finance Minister) declared "doctoring on symptoms is useless and senseless" — only loan and moratorium could balance budget and coordinate currency
- **August 20, 1922**: Circulation rose 6.8 billion in second week of August (14.9 billion since July)
- **August 21, 1922**: "Frightened stampede of the people to put money into foreign currencies"
- **August 21, 1922**: **Poincare** insisted France would not forgive reparations; must find way to make Germany pay
- **August 30, 1922**: Mark note scarcity reached "acute stage" — Reichsbank paid only 40% of demanded amounts in cash
- **September 2, 1922**: Food rioting began near Berlin; one killed, 20 hurt as police fired on mob — "first blood has flowed in high cost of living riots"
- **September 7, 1922**: New currency records — 22,978 million marks issued in closing week of August 22
- **September 11, 1922**: Prices doubled in August month; "increasing use of gold values in transaction of ordinary business"
- **September 13, 1922**: German consumers launched "concerted attack" against dollar basis for domestic prices
- **September 14, 1922**: 14 billion marks added to currency in first week of September — "second largest on record"
- **October 16, 1922**: German business meant to continue pricing in foreign currencies despite government prohibition
- **October 28, 1922**: New currency record — 35,467 million marks issued in week of October 35
- **October 30, 1922**: Federal Reserve Board bulletin attributed accelerated mark fall to: budget deficit, reparations, trade balance deficit, and capital flight
- **November 10, 1922**: Reparation Commission returned to Paris "empty-handed" after brief note from Wirth predicting complete moratorium, international consortium for temporary/final reparation solution, and permanent mark stabilization
- **November 10, 1922**: **Poincare** declared only hope for reparation payments lay in Brussels conference, but if it failed France would "act alone again"
- **December 2, 1922**: **Poincare** pushed for immediate action — "mark stabilization and reparations loan to follow control of German finance"
- **December 4, 1922**: Money crisis on German market — private banks charged 20% through fees and commissions; bank rate rising; currency inflation "now being increased by rediscount of private bankers at Reichsbank"

### Final Collapse Phase (December 1922 - Early 1923)

- **December 11, 1922**: "All records broken in German inflation" — paper currency, loans on Treasury Bills, and commercial discounts all surpassed precedent
- **December 17, 1922**: German debt rose 123 billion marks in first ten days of December
- **December 23, 1922**: Deficit neared one trillion marks — "even ordinary expenditures are more than double the receipts from taxes"
- **December 25, 1922**: Reichsbank discounts expanded 172 billion in week; currency 123 billion
- **December 27, 1922**: France declared Germany in "willful default" on wood deliveries for 1922 (3-to-1 Allied vote)
- **January 9, 1923**: **Cuno Government** proposed "passive resistance" — independent French Ruhr occupation would "tear up the Versailles Treaty" and void all reparations arrangements
- **January 11, 1923**: French entered Essen at 4:45 AM; Germany recalled envoys in Paris and Brussels; troops ordered home — workers declared apathy about French presence, prioritizing bread over capitalist/French conflicts
- **January 19, 1923**: German bank rate rose from 10% to 12% — "now the highest in the world" (was 5% in July)
- **January 21, 1923**: Speculative mark buying may be approaching end — **London County Westminster Parr's Bank** noted German exports exceeded imports since war, so Germany hadn't been paying for needs "in goods, as should be the case in normal times"
- **January 28, 1923**: Fresh mark slump drove exchange to 28,500 marks/dollar

### Hyperinflation Peak and Stabilization Efforts (1923-1924)

- **January 31, 1923**: Paper marks increased 216 billion in week — "all records broken by German inflation in third week of January"
- **February 12, 1923**: Prices in Germany rose 248.5% in January — "all monthly records of increase broken" (7,159 times prewar average)
- **February 15, 1923**: Germany protested Ruhr export barrier; told France she was "reducing the means of paying the other Allies"
- **February 22, 1923**: Germany announced 45.6 billion marks paid to Allies; Berlin gave official compilation of treaty losses totaling 56.5 billion
- **April 9, 1923**: U.S. fixed $1,187,736,867 in war damages claims from Germany (including Lusitania losses)
- **April 16, 1923**: Germany's public deficit — expenditure in fiscal year 6.25 trillion marks above revenue
- **April 30, 1923**: Reichsbank discount rate increased from 12% to 18% — officials hoped this would check credit/currency inflation and require registration of foreign currency holdings
- **May 15, 1923**: Suicides in Germany reached 80,000 yearly (vs. 1,200 before war) — "poverty is declared the chief cause"
- **June 25, 1923**: German prices rushed upward as mark fell 41% in ten days — "advance increasingly rapid last week"
- **June 25, 1923**: Germany's disordered currency obliterated old investments 100%
- **August 1, 1923**: Printers of paper marks walked out — Berliners called it "meanest strike in history"
- **August 2, 1923**: **Cuno Cabinet** proposed unlimited "near gold" loan backed by scrip currency; others predicted failure, asserting "one worthless currency is bad enough, without adding another"
- **August 16, 1923**: New German Chancellor declared "first energies must be wreaked upon domestic politics"
- **August 20, 1923**: German stocks firm since mark recovery
- **August 20, 1923**: **Finance Minister Hilferding** rejected using last German gold for new currency; planned fixed-values basis and raised Berlin street-car fare to 100,000 marks with government aid demands
- **August 29, 1923**: Hunger drove Germans to eating toadstools

### Currency Reform and Stabilization (September 1923 - August 1924)

- **September 17, 1923**: Basis of proposed new German currency — secured in gold, issued by new bank independent of state
- **September 23, 1923**: **Bodenmark** introduced as new currency unit — "containing .358 of a gram of fine gold and equal to 100 'bodenpfennigs'" backed by mortgages on landed property throughout country
- **September 26, 1923**: All German states agreed to abandon passive resistance program but safeguard country unity
- **October 10, 1923**: First Ruhr agreement — two mine groups to resume work with reparations payments in kind
- **October 13, 1923**: London banking houses outlined **Stresemann Ministry** currency plan — agriculture, industry, trade, and banking would create currency bank ("Waehrungsbank") issuing new money as "ground-mark" or "boden-mark"
- **October 15, 1923**: German stocks rose with dollar; impending Ruhr settlement also stimulated market and advancing bonds
- **October 16, 1923**: Cabinet approved bill for "gold annuity bank" — Reichsbank would cease discounting government Treasury bills, enabling "immediate curtailment of inflation"
- **October 29, 1923**: German "Rentenbank" ready for business — "first step in the Government's efforts at currency reform"
- **November 5, 1923**: With new currency issuance, Berlin believed "old paper marks will disappear, new marks replacing them"
- **November 7, 1923**: **President Coolidge** recognized Germany's serious food situation requiring relief "from the outside world before the winter is over"
- **November 14, 1923**: Reparation Board invited Germany to request hearing on payment failure reasons
- **November 19, 1923**: Germany issued 142 million new rentenmarks; with Reichsbank cessation of Treasury bill discounting and paper mark issuance against discounts, "the new German currency experiment has at least been initiated"
- **December 3, 1923**: Germany's "real wages" — workingmen's pay estimated at 44-60.78% of prewar rate in gold
- **December 10, 1923**: Price declines relieved social strain (some goods fell 50%), but "financial experts are pessimistic of failure"
- **December 14, 1923**: **Coolidge** approved private charity for German relief
- **December 14, 1923**: German treasury low — "barely enough money to keep the government going" for ten days despite reduced salaries and dismissals
- **December 17, 1923**: Many German currencies in circulation — "emergency issues" now one-fifth of Reichsbank circulation
- **December 23, 1923**: **Charles Gates Dawes** and **Owen D. Young** selected to aid German financial solution — "two Americans whose business lives exemplify success in its broadest meaning"
- **January 28, 1924**: Berlin hopeful new gold bank would "restore international faith in German finance" — balanced budget assured; hopes for new inflation check plan and foreign capital attraction
- **January 29, 1924**: Germany awaited **Dawes Committee** arrival with prepared books and data
- **February 1, 1924**: Germany eliminated internal debt through drastic steps — bonds/mortgages restored to 10% of original gold value; inflation taxes on state railroad/postal profits; experts urged immediate gold issue institution creation
- **February 2, 1924**: Government bonds plunged on news of debt program — market excited as war loans/issues scrapped, corporate/private bonds marked to 10% of early gold value
- **February 4, 1924**: Mortgage depreciation fixed at 10% valuation — "savings deposits wiped out"
- **February 18, 1924**: Export surplus for Germany in December — measured in gold marks, 1923 imports and exports "practically balance"
- **February 18, 1924**: German mortgages "valorized" at 15% (expected only 10%) — public debt repayments in paper marks stopped
- **February 18, 1924**: Wartime currency "Darlehnskassen" to be abolished beginning of May (founded August 1914 for easy credit)
- **February 20, 1924**: German revenues increased; surplus of millions of gold marks expected "for first time since war"
- **February 25, 1924**: No halt in trade recovery — continued gradual industry improvement with textile trade boom
- **February 26, 1924**: Britain cut levy on German imports — impost dropped from 26% to 5% with Berlin pledging payment
- **March 10, 1924**: German industry revival continued — unemployment in labor "now decreasing rapidly from the recent figures"; steel trade recovering
- **March 17, 1924**: Further trade improvement aided by government establishment of nine-hour working day
- **March 24, 1924**: Continued recovery in German industry — metal prices rising, textile industry still booming
- **April 3, 1924**: German gold bank ready to start business next week in Reichsbank building
- **April 10, 1924**: Germans criticized Dawes terms as "first official view is that Dawes report is inacceptable as it stands"
- **April 10, 1924**: **Dawes Report** called for mortgage on industry to meet payments of 2.5 billion marks annually (subject to increases per German prosperity) — Germany's four-year protestations of inability to pay were "refuted today"
- **April 15, 1924**: Germany to accept Dawes Board plan as basis for negotiations with Reparation Commission
- **April 16, 1924**: France and Britain approved full Dawes Report in principle; Germany's acceptance as discussion basis "is on way to Paris"
- **April 18, 1924**: Reparation Board adopted Dawes Plan; set Berlin to work drafting laws and naming officials for implementation
- **April 21, 1924**: Credit demand in Germany rising — industrial situation improving, loans doubled in a month
- **April 28, 1924**: Germany re-entering foreign steel trade with large orders in Sweden — "said to be underbidding France and Belgium"
- **April 29, 1924**: **Dr. Marx** defended German government's acceptance of Dawes Plan at Centre Party election meeting
- **May 4, 1924**: German voters went to polls — "most important election since the war fails to arouse great interest" (35 million eligible)
- **May 5, 1924**: Bavarian vote divided — **Hitler-Ludendorff Group** failed to win expected victory
- **May 5, 1924**: German coalition for Dawes Plan retained majority despite Communist/Nationalist gains — middle parties retained majority, would form next government "probably in conjunction with the Socialists"
- **May 6, 1924**: German Coalition held 230 seats to opponents' 192; cabinet expected to "retain office and carry out the Dawes plan"
- **June 30, 1924**: Germany accepted military control with month's delay and inquiry limitation
- **July 31, 1924**: French ports reopened to Germans; Berlin announced from September onward ships from Fatherland would be admitted
- **August 17, 1924**: Allies and Germans signed agreement — **French will quit Ruhr within a year**

### Key Historical Figures

- **Matthias Erzberger**: Finance Minister who proposed 60% tax on high incomes; assassinated August 1921
- **Walter Rathenau**: German Foreign Minister assassinated June 1922
- **Raymond Poincare**: French Premier who insisted on reparations enforcement
- **Wilhelm Cuno**: German Chancellor who implemented passive resistance to French Ruhr occupation
- **Gustav Stresemann**: German Chancellor who ended passive resistance, initiated currency reform
- **Rudolf Hilferding**: Finance Minister who rejected using gold reserves for new currency
- **Andreas Hermes**: Finance Minister who declared only loan/moratorium could stabilize currency
- **Charles Gates Dawes** & **Owen D. Young**: American financial experts who designed stabilization plan
- **Dr. Wilhelm Marx**: German Chancellor who defended acceptance of Dawes Plan
- **John Moody**: President of Moody's Investors' Service who warned of crash in September 1921
- **Calvin Coolidge**: U.S. President who recognized German food crisis, approved private charity

## US Debt Crisis and Adjustment (1928–1937)

### Introduction

- **Great Depression** as iconic deflationary deleveraging, comparable to 2008 dynamics
- Study combines archival research with template analysis from "Archetypal Big Debt Cycle"
- Includes real-time "newsfeed" from newspaper headlines and Federal Reserve statements

### 1927–1929: The Bubble

#### Post-WWI Economic Boom

- After 1920-1921 recession, US experienced rapid technology-led growth
- **Electrification** of rural America opened massive markets for new technologies
- Radio ownership exploded: 60,000 sets (1922) → 7.5 million (1928)
- **Automobile industry** boomed: 23 million cars by 1929 (one per five Americans, 3x higher than 1920)
- Factory worker productivity increased 75% from 1922 to 1928
- Technology breakthroughs drove widespread economic optimism

#### The "Fat Years" (1922-1927)

- Period known as "fat years" — capitalists and workers both gained significantly
- Corporate profits hit postwar highs, unemployment dropped to postwar lows, real wages rose 20%+
- **In pre-bubble years 1923-1926, debt growth aligned with income growth** because it financed productive activities
- Stock market soared 150% (1922-1927) with little volatility
- **Radio Corporation of America** and **General Motors** led tech stock gains

#### Bubble Formation

- Bubble rooted in **dizzying productivity gains** and leveraged bets on their continuation
- **"New Era" belief**: permanent prosperity, end of boom-bust cycles, continuously rising stock prices
- US extremely attractive to foreign investment due to **gold standard**
- Gold flowed into US as foreigners bought dollars (to invest), determining events pre-crash

#### Federal Reserve's Policy Mistake

- Foreign nations (France, Germany, UK) worried about gold outflows, asked Fed to lower dollar interest rates
- Spring 1927: Fed cut discount rate from 4% to 3.5% to make dollars less attractive
- **Knock-on effect: encouraged US credit creation** — typical way central banks inadvertently finance bubbles
- Economy accelerated; H2 1928: industrial production +9.9%, auto production hit all-time high
- *Wall Street Journal* (early 1929): "One cannot recall when a new year was ushered in with business conditions sounder than they are today"

#### Classic Bubble Characteristics

1. **Prices high relative to traditional measures**
2. **Prices discounting future rapid appreciation from high levels**
3. **Broad bullish sentiment**
4. **High leverage financing purchases**
5. **Extended forward purchases** (inventory, contracts) to speculate/protect against price gains
6. **New buyers** entering market
7. **Stimulative monetary policy inflates bubble; tight policy pops it**

#### Extreme Valuations and Sentiment

- After prices nearly doubled (1927-1928), stocks sold at **extremely high multiples financed by borrowing (margin)**
- Some stocks valued at 30x earnings
- **Charles Amos Dice** (*New Levels in the Stock Market*, 1929): broader investor base made high valuations permanent
- Dice proclaimed obsolete the truism "what goes up must come down"

#### New Buyers Flood the Market

- **Unsophisticated investors with no prior stock experience** entered — classic bubble sign
- Brokerage branch offices outside Wall Street increased 50%+ (1928-1929)
- Broker quote (1929): "Wherever one went, one met people who told of their stock-market winnings. At dinner tables, at bridge, on golf links, on trolley cars, in country post offices, in barber shops, in factories and shops of all kinds"

#### Leverage Outside Banking System

- **High and rapidly increasing leverage**, especially **outside regulated banking**
- **New/fast-growing lending markets with heavy leverage are symptomatic of bubbles**
- Banks made new assets seem safe via guarantees or packaging — without crisis stress-testing, hard to know true safety
- **"Innovations" typically seed next crisis if unmonitored/unmanaged**
- **Bankers and speculators in symbiotic relationship**: bankers lent at fat spreads, speculators bought stocks on leverage, pushing prices up

#### Call Loans and Shadow Banking

- **Call loans** (relatively new): huge channel for margin debt, daily adjusting terms/rates, callable anytime
- **Created asset/liability mismatches**: short-term debt funding long-term risky assets, with risky borrowers paying higher rates
- **Classic debt crisis ingredient: squeezing lenders/borrowers with debt/liability mismatches from bubble**
- Non-Federal Reserve lenders (non-banks, foreigners) in call loan market: 24% (early 1928) → 58% (October 1929)
- Added risk: Fed couldn't lend to non-banks in a squeeze
- Foreign capital poured in from London and Hong Kong — historian: "A great river of gold began to converge on Wall Street, all of it to help Americans hold common stock on margin"

#### Investment Trusts

- **Investment trusts** saw rapid growth during bubble, drew new speculators
- First popularized in Great Britain; companies issuing shares and investing proceeds in other companies' shares
- **Irving Fisher** praised "wide and well-managed diversification" for capital-limited investors
- By 1929, new trusts launching at nearly 5 per week, capturing one-third of new capital raised
- **Trust promoters** claimed diversification made financial system safer
- **However**: many trusts used leverage to amplify returns, creating investor risk
- Speculators, unaware of security nature and believing past would continue, took margin loans to lever up already-levered trust shares

#### Self-Reinforcing Dynamics

- Rising prices attracted more buyers to lever up and profit
- **The more prices increased, the more aggressively speculators bet they would increase still more**
- **Higher prices encouraged stock supply increases** (more offerings)
- **Bubble phase paradox: as prices rose, credit standards lowered** (opposite of logical), because lending/buying stocks with borrowed money proved very profitable for both lenders and borrowers

#### Shadow Banking vs. Traditional Banks

- Leveraging mostly in "shadow banking"; traditional banks looked healthier than pre-1920-1921 recession
- Banks posting record earnings, higher capital ratios (17.2% vs. 14.9%), stickier liabilities (35.7% time deposits vs. 23.3%)
- Large bank mergers (1929) viewed as further strength
- **Classic pattern: bank earnings/balance sheets look healthy during good times because assets highly valued and deposits backing them are there. Problems emerge during runs when assets fall**

#### Federal Reserve's Dilemma

- Fed governors debated restraining stock speculation lending but hesitant to raise short-term rates
- Economy not overheating, inflation subdued, higher rates would hurt all borrowers (not just speculators)
- **Worst debt bubbles not accompanied by high/rising inflation, but by asset price inflation financed by debt growth**
- **Central banks accommodate debt growth focused on inflation/growth—not on debt growth, asset inflations, or whether debts will produce servicing incomes**

#### Macroprudential Measures

- Rather than raising discount rate, Fed enacted **macroprudential (regulatory) measures** to constrain bank credit supply
- Lowered acceptance rates for loans, increased supervision of credit facilities
- Fed publicly released letter to regional banks deriding "excessive amount of the country's credit absorbed in speculative security loans," threatening to refuse funding such loans
- **But these policies were largely ineffective**

### Late 1929: The Top and the Crash

#### Tightening Pops the Bubble

- 1928: Fed tightened monetary policy, raising rates 1.5% (February-July) to 5%
- Hoped to slow speculative credit without crippling economy
- August 1929: raised rates again to 6%
- **As short-term interest rates rose, yield curve flattened and inverted**
- **Liquidity declined; return on holding cash increased as yields rose**
- **Loans became costly; cash became attractive vs. longer duration/riskier financial assets**
- **Money moved out of financial assets, causing them to fall in value**
- **Declining asset prices created negative wealth effect**, feeding on itself in markets and economy through declining spending/incomes
- **The bubble reversed into a bust**

#### First Signs of Trouble (March 1929)

- News: Federal Reserve Board meeting daily (no released details) sparked Wall Street rumors of clampdown
- After two weeks of modest declines and unusual Saturday meeting report, stock market broke sharply March 25-26
- Dow fell 4%+, call loan rates hit 20% as panic gripped market
- Record trading volumes; wave of margin calls on leveraged investors forced selling, exacerbating decline
- **Charles Mitchell** (National City Bank president, also NY Fed director) announced bank ready to lend $25 million to market
- Calmed market, rates fell, stocks rebounded
- **Foreshadowed stock vulnerability to credit tightening**

#### Economy Remains Strong (Mid-1929)

- Growth moderated somewhat, but economy remained strong through mid-1929
- June *Federal Reserve Bulletin*: industrial production and factory employment at all-time highs through April; construction rebounded sharply after Q1 fall

#### Final Rally (May-August 1929)

- After short May sell-off, rally accelerated, bubble reached blow-off phase
- Stocks rose ~11% (June), 5% (July), 10% (August)
- Rally supported by accelerating leverage: household margin debt rose $1.2 billion over three months

#### Final Tightening

- Money continued tightening. August 8: Federal Reserve Bank of New York raised discount rate to 6%
- Clear that macroprudential measures failed to slow speculative lending
- Concerns about high stock prices/interest rates caused brokers to tighten call loan terms, raise margin requirements
- Margin requirements at most brokers: dropped to 10% previous year, rose to 45-50%

#### Market Peak (September 3, 1929)

- Stock market peaked September 3 when Dow closed at 381 — **wouldn't reach again for 25+ years**

#### No Single Event Caused Burst

- **No specific event/shock caused bubble burst**
- **Classic with bubbles: rising prices required leveraged buying to accelerate at unsustainable rate**
- Both speculators and lenders near/at max positions; tightening changed leveraging economics

#### Early Decline (September-Early October)

- Stocks declined September and early October as bad news eroded confidence
- September 5: **Roger Babson** speech to National Business Conference warned about collapse from "tight money" → 2.6% "Babson break"
- September 20: **Clarence Hatry's London financial empire** collapsed on fraud charges, forcing British investors to sell American holdings
- September 26: **Bank of England** raised discount rate from 5.5% to 8-year high of 6.5%; European nations followed
- By mid-October: 10% sell-off from highs
- Major papers' view: worst over, recent volatility good for market
- October 15: **Irving Fisher** proclaimed "Stocks have reached what looks like a permanently high plateau"

#### The Stock Market Crashes

- Then the bottom fell out — day-by-day account follows via own description and newsfeed

**Saturday, October 19:**
- Stocks fell sharply; second-highest Saturday trading volume ever
- Decline self-reinforcing on downside
- Wave of margin calls after close required leveraged owners to add cash (hard to obtain) or sell stocks
- Sunday's *New York Times* headline: "Stocks driven down as wave of selling engulfs the market"
- Traders widely expected Monday recovery
- **Thomas Lamont** (J.P. Morgan) wrote President **Herbert Hoover**: "future appears brilliant"

**Monday, October 21:**
- Week began with heavier selling
- Analyst described waves of sell orders as "overwhelming and aggressive"
- Trading volume broke records; another wave of margin calls, distressed selling among levered players prevalent
- Markets rallied into Monday's session end, so losses smaller than Saturday

**Tuesday, October 22:**
- Small gains

**Wednesday, October 23:**
- Opened quietly, but hopes worst had passed shattered before close
- Avalanche of sell orders in last trading hour pushed stocks down sharply, triggering fresh margin calls and forced selling
- **Dow suffered then-largest one-day point loss in history**: fell 20.7 points (6.3%) to close at 305.3
- Because sell-off so sharp and late in day, unprecedented margin calls went out that night, requiring significant collateral to avoid Thursday position closures
- Many equity holders would be required to sell


<!-- pp. 241-260 -->

## Black Thursday and Black Monday: The 1929 Crash

### Black Thursday (October 24, 1929)

- Exchange workers prepared for massive margin calls and sell orders; policemen posted throughout financial district
- **William R. Crawford** (NYSE Superintendent) described "electricity in the air so thick you could cut it" before the open
- After quiet opening, avalanche of selling materialized—sell orders poured in nationwide, pushing prices down and generating cascading margin calls
- Pace of selling so frantic that operators struggled to keep up; exchange telephone clerk: "I can't get any information. The whole place is falling apart"
- By noon of **Black Thursday**, major indices down more than 10 percent

### The Bankers' Pool Intervention

- Small group of biggest bankers met at **J.P. Morgan** offices, hatched stabilization plan
- **"The Bankers' Pool"** committed to buy $125 million in shares
- Traders acting for bankers placed large buy orders above recent prices—news spread, other investors bought aggressively
- Dow hit low of 272 (down 33), bounced back to close at 299, down only six points
- This would prove to be first of many failed attempts to bolster the market

### Black Monday (October 28, 1929)

- **President Hoover** and 35 brokers took out full-page ad in *New York Times* Friday, confidently telling public it was time to buy
- Hoover declared: "The fundamental business of the country, that is, production and distribution of commodities, is on a sound and prosperous basis"
- Stocks steady through rest of week; Sunday papers showed optimism about cheapness supporting rebound
- But collapse resumed Monday—flood of sell orders from all investor types
- Significant selling came from brokers whose loans from corporations were suddenly called amid panic
- Trading volume set record: 9 million shares changed hands (3 million in last hour alone)
- Dow finished down 13.5 percent—largest one-day loss in history, became known as **Black Monday**
- Bankers' Pool met again after close, stirred optimism, but announced no additional buying measures

### Black Tuesday (October 29, 1929)

- Another massive wave of margin calls Monday night; $150 million of call loans pulled before Tuesday's open
- **Federal Reserve attempted to counter collapse in credit by providing liquidity**
- **George Harrison** (New York Fed president) announced before open: Fed would inject $100 million in liquidity to ease credit crunch by purchasing government securities
- Harrison made purchases outside regular Open Market Investment Committee account—didn't want to wait for Fed Board approval in Washington
- **Key insight: Classically the checks and balances designed to ensure stability during normal times are poorly suited for crisis scenarios where immediate, aggressive action is required. In the late 1920s, there were few well-established paths for dealing with the debt implosion and its domino effects.**

### Market Continues Falling

- Fed's liquidity eased credit conditions, likely prevented failures, but wasn't enough to stop stock collapse
- Starting at open, large blocks of shares flooded market, pushed prices down
- Rumor that Bankers' Pool shifted to selling fed the panic
- NYSE members met at noon to discuss closing exchange, decided against it
- Investment trusts hit especially hard—leverage that buoyed returns through bubble now worked in reverse
- **Goldman Sachs Trading Corporation** fell 42 percent; **Blue Ridge** down as much as 70 percent before recovering somewhat
- Dow closed down 11.7 percent, second worst one-day loss in history
- Market had fallen 23 percent over two days; problems with leveraged speculators and their lenders already emerging

### Wednesday Rally and Policy Response

- Stocks snapped back Wednesday, rising 12.3 percent in **one of the sharp bear market rallies that classically occur repeatedly during the depression phases of big debt crises**
- NYSE announced trading would begin at noon next day, exchange closed following Friday and Saturday to catch up on paperwork
- Both Fed and **Bank of England** cut rates Thursday
- Fed dropped bank rate from 6 percent to 5 percent; Bank of England decreased discount rate from 6.5 percent to 6 percent
- Traders cheered news that call loans outstanding had fallen by more than $1 billion from prior week
- Believing worst of forced selling had passed, markets rallied again

### Continued Volatility

- Speculators looking to capitalize on prior week's rally raced to sell when market opened Monday—stocks plunged again
- By Wednesday, Dow was down 15 percent on the week; stocks continued to fall following week as well
- **Railroad bonds and high-grade bonds performed well** during crash as investors sought safer investments after pulling back from stocks and call loans
- Yields between high-grade and lower-grade corporate bonds (rated BAA and below) reached widest level in 1929—riskier corporate bonds were flat to down
- Market action typical in this phase: equities and bonds with credit risk falling, Treasury and other low credit risk assets rising

### Economic Impact Begins

- Financial and psychological impacts of stock plunge began hurting economy
- Politicians and business leaders talked up economy's strength, but stats showed weakness
- Industrial production peaked July; freight car loading and steel utilization released week of November 4 showed ongoing declines
- Sharp drops in commodity markets added to worries
- By mid-November, Dow down almost 50 percent from September peak

## Policy Responses to the Crash

### Hoover Administration's Initial Response

- Hoover's early moves broadly praised, helped drive meaningful stock rally
- November 13: **President Hoover** proposed temporary one percent reduction in tax rate for each income bracket and increase to public construction spending of $175 million
- Two days later, invited "small preliminary conference of representatives of industry, agriculture and labor" to develop plan for fighting downturn
- When they convened following week, Hoover solicited pledges:
  - Business leaders: not cut spending on capital investment or wages
  - Labor union leaders: not strike or demand higher wages
- December 5: Hoover convened conference of 400 reputable businessmen, created leadership committee of 72 top business tycoons headed by **Chairman of US Chamber of Commerce**
- Mix of policies successful for a time, as was Hoover's support for Federal Reserve System's efforts to ease credit

### Federal Reserve Easing

- New York Fed aggressively provided liquidity during crash
- Within a month, cut discount rate from 6 percent to 5 percent, then again to 4½ percent
- These policy moves combined with private sector steps to support stock market, most notably **John D. Rockefeller's** bid for one million shares of **Standard Oil Co.** at $50 on November 13 (effectively flooring price at $50)
- On November 13, market bottomed and began 20 percent rally going into December—sense of optimism took hold

## 1930–1932: Depression

### Early 1930: Misplaced Optimism

- By New Year's Day 1930, widely believed stock market's 50 percent correction was over, helped drive strong rebound in first four months
- Stocks seemed cheap because there wasn't much evidence yet that company earnings would fall much
- Investors biased by memories of most recent downturns (1907 and 1920)—in both cases, worst was over after correction of about 50 percent
- Helping fuel optimism, policy makers continued taking steps to stimulate economy
- Fed cut rates to 3.5 percent in March, bringing total rate cuts to 2.5 percent in just five months (sparking debate within Fed over whether too much stimulation risked weakening dollar)
- March 25: Congress passed two appropriation bills for state road building and construction projects, bringing total fiscal stimulus to about 1 percent of GDP

### Consensus View: Recovery Expected

- Consensus among economists at **American Economic Association** and **Federal Reserve**: simulative policy moves would be enough to support economic rebound
- January 1, *New York Times* captured shifted sentiment: "Lack of widespread commercial failures, the absence of serious unemployment, and robust recovery in the stock market have been factors calculated to dispel the gloominess"
- Banks actually expanding investments through 1930; member banks' holdings of foreign, municipal, government, and railroad bonds all rose
- By April 10, Dow rallied back above 290

### Economy Weakens Despite Stimulus

- Despite stimulation and general optimism, economic weakness persisted
- First quarter earnings disappointing; stocks began sliding starting late April
- **Key insight: In the early stages of deleveragings, it's very common for investors and policy makers to underestimate how much the real economy will weaken, leading to small rallies that quickly reverse, and initial policy responses that aren't enough.**
- Over second half of 1930, economy clearly began to weaken
- May through December: department store sales fell 8 percent, industrial production fell 17.6 percent
- Unemployment rose by over 10 percent (to 14 percent); capacity utilization fell by 12 percent (to 67 percent)
- Housing and mortgage debt collapsed
- Decline more akin to shallow recession at that point—consumer spending remained above lows of previous recessions, many industries not yet suffering severe declines

### Credit Spreads Widen

- As economy weakened, sell-off across markets resumed
- By October 1930, stock market fallen below lows reached in November 1929; commodities also fell sharply
- Market analysts and investors realizing hopes for quick recovery would not materialize; Hoover remained optimistic
- **Although Federal Reserve decreased interest rates and Treasury bond market was strong, spreads continued to widen. This increased the interest rates facing most consumers and businesses.**
- Rates rose on long-term mortgage loans; yields on municipal bonds (which performed well following crash) began to rise as credit anxieties developed
- Some industries hit particularly hard by worsening credit conditions
- **Railroads** had large amounts of debts they needed to roll, facing both tighter credit conditions and decreased earnings
- Because railroads considered vital industry, government wanted to support them, likely with bailout
- (Railroad industry's circumstances parallel struggles auto industry faced in 2008 financial crisis)

## Rising Protectionism

### Smoot-Hawley Tariff Act

- **As is common in severe economic downturns, protectionist and anti-immigrant sentiment began to rise**
- Politicians blamed weakness on anti-competitive policies by other countries, posited higher tariffs would help reverse slump in manufacturing and agriculture, while restricting immigration would help economy deal with unemployment
- Protectionist sentiment resulted most notably in passage of **Smoot-Hawley Tariff Act**, which imposed tariffs on nearly 20,000 US imports
- Investors and economists feared proposed 20 percent increase in tariffs would trigger global trade war and cripple already weak global economy
- As act neared passage early May, 1,028 economists issued open letter to Hoover imploring him to veto the bill if it passed in Congress
- Foreign governments expressed opposition and hinted at retaliation
- Tariffs—particularly on agricultural imports—were one of Hoover's campaign promises, so he reluctant to renege even as pushback against Smoot-Hawley tariffs became more intense
- Stocks sold off sharply as it became clear tariff bill would pass
- After falling 5 percent previous week, Dow dropped another 7.9 percent on June 16, day before tariff bill passed
- While tariffs have sometimes increased during periods of economic downturn, Smoot-Hawley pushed tariffs to near-record levels

### Retaliatory Trade War

- Soon US faced wave of retaliatory protectionist policies
- Most impactful initial response came from US's largest trading partner, **Canada**, which at the time took in 20 percent of American exports
- Canadian policy makers increased tariffs on 16 US goods while simultaneously lowering tariffs on imports from British Empire
- As similar policies piled up in years that followed, they accelerated collapse in global trade caused by economic contraction

### Immigration Restrictions

- **Restricting immigration (both legal and illegal), another common protectionist response to economic weakness, was also pursued by the Hoover administration in 1930**
- September 9: Hoover put ban on immigration, allowing travel only for tourists, students, and working professionals
- Described policy as necessary to deal with unemployment
- Later reflected in memoir: "directly or indirectly all immigrants were a public charge at the moment—either they themselves went on relief as soon as they landed, or if they did get jobs, they forced others onto relief"

## Bank Failures Begin

### Banking System Under Stress

- Banks largely held up well following stock market crash, but as those they lent to were hurt by crash and economy weakened, they began to feel it
- In 1930, bank net earnings declined about 40 percent compared to prior year, but they remained on sound footing
- Several of largest banks even increased their dividends; they looked strong at time compared to markets and economy
- Many analysts believed they would be source of support through downturn
- Majority of early failures confined to banks in Midwest and country banks that had a lot of money in real estate loans, exposed to losses from drought
- While failures started small, they spread as credit problems spread

### Bank Runs Begin

- By December 1930, bank failures had become meaningful risk to broader economy
- Worries about banks led to runs on them
- **Runs on non-guaranteed financial institutions are classic in such depressions/deleveragings, and they can lead to their failure in a matter of days**
- 1930: 8,446 more commercial failures than in 1929, bank failures in 1930 than in 1929
- Liabilities of companies in default 1930 exceeded those in preceding year by $185,000,000
- Number of failures was 15 percent higher and indebtedness 38.3 percent greater

## The Gold Standard Constraint

### Gold Standard as Foreign Currency Debt

- Before discussing banking failures, important to discuss gold standard's role in determining how 1930s debt crisis transpired
- When debts denominated in prior sections described, deleveragings can generally be managed well
- **Being on a gold standard is akin to having debts denominated in a foreign currency** because creditors could demand payment in gold (as often written into contracts), and policy makers couldn't freely print money (too much printing would lead people to redeem their money for gold)
- **So policy makers were working with a limited toolkit until they broke the link to gold**

### Bank of the United States Failure

- Most important bank failure of this period: **Bank of the United States**, which had some 400,000 depositors, more than any bank in country at time
- Run on it began December 10 because of false rumor
- Wall Street financiers—including heads of **J.P. Morgan** and **Chase**—met at New York Fed to determine whether they should provide $30 million required to save bank
- Many within group thought bank was insolvent, not simply illiquid, so they should let it fail
- **New York Superintendent of Banks Joseph Broderick** argued its closing "would result in the closing of at least ten other banks in the city and … it might even affect the savings banks" (i.e., it was systemically important)
- Also noted he believed bank to be solvent
- Broderick's colleagues ultimately did not agree with him
- When bank closed its doors next day, it was biggest single bank failure in history
- *New York Times* would later refer to its failure as "The First Domino In the Depression"—certainly a turning point for public confidence in nation's banking system

### Federal Reserve Constraints Under Gold Standard

- Banks structurally vulnerable to runs because of liquidity mismatch between liabilities (short-term deposits) and assets (illiquid loans and securities)
- Even sound bank can fail if it can't sell its assets fast enough to meet liabilities
- **Because of the gold standard, the Federal Reserve was restricted in how much it could print money, limiting how much it could lend to a bank facing liquidity problems (i.e., act as a "lender of last resort"). There were also legal constraints. For instance, the Fed at the time was only allowed to give direct access to its credit to member banks, but only 35 percent of commercial banks were members. So banks would often have to borrow from the private sector and sell their assets in a "fire sale" to avoid failure.**

### Political Shift

- End of 1930 also saw political winds beginning to shift
- With downturn playing prominently in voters' minds, Democrats swept Congress in November mid-term elections
- This foreshadowed FDR's win in presidential election two years later

## First Quarter, 1931: Optimism Gives Way to Gloom as Economy Continues to Deteriorate

### Brief Optimism Early 1931

- At start of 1931, economists, politicians, and other experts in both US and Europe still retained hope there would be imminent return to normalcy
- Problems still seemed manageable; bank failures of previous quarter thought to be inconsequential, not damaging to overall financial system
- By March, all business indexes pointing to rise in employment, wages, and industrial production
- Bank runs led to less than 10 percent drop in deposits
- News reflected growing economic confidence: March 23, *New York Times* declared depression had bottomed, U.S. economy was on its way back up
- New investment trusts being formed to profit from expected "long recovery"

### Stock Market Rally

- Optimism bolstered by recovery of stock market
- Through end of February, Dow rose more than 20 percent off its December lows
- Optimism was also bolstered by recovery of stock market

### Rally Fades

- Rally wasn't sustained
- Growing concerns over Europe and indications of weak first quarter earnings caused stock prices to slip through March
- End quarter at 172.4, down 11.3 percent from their February highs

## The Growing Debate over Economic Policy

### Politics as Risk Factor in Depressions

- **In a depression, the main ways that politics might play a role are by standing in the way of the implementation of sensible economic policies or by leading to extreme policies. These are important risks that can make a depression worse.**

### Fiscal Policy Debate

- After more than a year of economic contraction, political debate over economic policy was intensifying
- By this time, more than six million people were unemployed in United States and there was no agreement among policy makers and business leaders on how to deal with it
- Understanding this debate is key to understanding why policy makers took certain steps that ultimately worsened the crisis
- Also helps illustrate many of the classic mistakes policy makers make when handling big debt crises

#### The Direct Relief vs. Indirect Relief Debate

- Fiscal policy debate centered on whether or not Federal government should significantly ramp up spending to support economy
- Senate Democrats, joined by some Republicans, pushed President to increase "direct relief" for those facing particularly difficult circumstances
- That would of course mean larger deficits and more debt, and it would mean changing rules of game to shift wealth from one set of players to others, rather than letting game play out in way that would provide good lessons to help prevent such problems in future (moral hazard perspective)
- Strong belief that if this money was just given away and not turned into productivity, it would be wasted
- While Hoover administration had supported earlier fiscal stimulus, it opposed significant direct relief from Federal government that would "bring an inevitable train of corruption and waste such as our nation had never witnessed"
- Hoover's administration instead advocated for "indirect relief"—mix of policies that included lobbying private sector to invest and keep employment steady, reliance on aid from state and local governments, immigration restrictions, and macroprudential policies to encourage lending

#### Budget Deficit Concerns

- While concerns over budget deficits limited stimulus spending, by 1931, federal government budget deficit grew to 3 percent of GDP
- Deficit was due to falling tax revenue (collapsed to nearly half of 1929 levels) and increase in social spending of about $1 billion approved previous year
- **Treasury Secretary Mellon** believed balancing budget was necessary first step to restore business confidence
- Hoover agreed for reasons he later summarized in memoir: "National stability required that we balance the budget"

#### The Austerity Trap

- **Worries over the deficit and a push for austerity are classic responses to the depression phase of a big debt crisis. Austerity seems like the obvious response, but the problem is that one person's spending is another person's income, so when spending is cut, incomes are also cut, with the result that it takes an awful lot of painful spending cuts to make significant reductions in debt/income ratios.**

### Hoover's Approach Proves Insufficient

- For all suffering Depression had caused, sense of crisis-driven urgency hadn't yet developed
- Economy was still contracting over first half of 1931, but at slower rate than year before
- Hoover was certain that indirect relief was meeting needs of people and he did not see need to use additional fiscal supports
- As we'll discuss later, this ultimately tipped debate, and **the Hoover administration to make the classic rookie mistake of leaning too heavily on austerity and other deflationary levers** relative to more stimulative policies until pain of doing these things became intolerable

## Second Quarter, 1931: The Global Dollar Shortage Causes a Global Debt Crisis and a Strong Dollar

### Dollar Squeeze Emerges

- Because dollar-denominated credit was collapsing and a lot of dollar-denominated debt that required dollar credit to service it existed around world, global dollar shortage emerged during first half of 1931
- **Classically, there is a squeeze in a reserve currency that is widely lent by foreign financial institutions when there is a collapse of credit creation in that currency**
- Although other currencies faced shortage amid credit crunch, dollar was particularly impacted because of its role as global funding currency
- At same time, falling US imports reduced foreigners' dollar income, intensifying squeeze
- Note that virtually same dollar squeeze dynamics occurred in 2008 crisis for same reasons

### Germany as Epicenter

- As financial markets and many other markets are global, one can't understand all that happened by looking only at US
- What happened in US had big impact on what happened in Germany, which led to big political changes that were felt around world in 1930s and early 1940s
- In 1931 Germany was epicenter of emerging dollar squeeze
- Had previously faced great difficulty paying back reparation debt it owed and had been forced to borrow as result
- Country had become popular destination for "carry trade," in which investors would lend their dollars to Germany to earn higher yield than they would get in dollars and Germans would borrow in dollars to get lower interest rate
- Once again, this type of behavior is classic in "good times," when there is little perceived risk and large cross-country credit creation, and sets up conditions that make "bad times" worse when reversal happens
- At time, Germany was highly dependent on this flow of money that could easily be pulled, and by 1931, American banks and companies held about a billion dollars in short-term German bills (equal to about six percent of German GDP)
- That made both German borrowers and American banks and companies very vulnerable

### Rising Extremism in Germany

- As is typical in such times, economies and wealth disparities fuel rise of populist and extremist leaders globally, with ideological fight between authoritarian left and authoritarian right
- Both German Communist party and **Hitler's Nazi party** made big electoral gains as German economy struggled—with Nazis going from under 3 percent support in 1928 Reichstag elections to over 18 percent in September 1930
- Meanwhile, largest party (center left Social Democratic Party) slipped to less than quarter of Reichstag seats
- Together, far right and far left parties easily had enough parliamentary support to force Germany into unstable multi-party coalition government
- Germany was essentially becoming ungovernable

### Trade War Intensifies Dollar Squeeze

- Global trade war made economic conditions and dollar squeeze worse
- Collapse in global trade depressed foreigners' dollar income, which in turn made it harder for foreigners to service their dollar debts

<!-- pp. 261-280 -->

## The Global Spread of Crisis (1931)

### Trade Collapse and Dollar Shortage

- US imports fell 50% from 1929 to 1931 (both US and UK trade data show steep declines)
- **Chase National Bank** president warned in *Time* (August 1931): companies couldn't obtain enough dollars to cover debts, hurting business
- Dollar shortage made borrowing more expensive, creating **liquidity squeeze** in central Europe

### Germany's Balance of Payments Crisis (May 1931)

- Germany turned to money printing to finance deficits, raising **hyperinflation** fears (echoing early 1920s)
- **Frederic M. Sackett** (US Ambassador) told President Hoover of Germany's weaknesses: capital flight, currency difficulties, unemployment, credit tightening, debt payment pressures, refusals to renew foreign-held German bank accounts
- Germany faced a balance of payments crisis, not just domestic recession

### Austria: Credit Anstalt Failure

- **Credit Anstalt** (Austria's oldest/largest bank) announced $20 million loss on May 8, 1931
- Loss stemmed from 1929 rescue of another failing bank that nearly wiped out its equity
- Bank run ensued, spreading to run on Austrian currency

> "When risks emerge that systemically important institutions will fail, policy makers must take steps to keep these entities running to limit the impact of their failure on other solvent institutions or the economy at large. Keeping these institutions intact is also important for keeping credit pipes in place for lending to creditworthy borrowers, particularly for financial systems with a concentrated set of lenders."

- **Gold standard constraint**: Austria couldn't print money to provide liquidity
- Other frantic stabilization attempts failed

### Geopolitical Weaponization: France vs. Austria/Germany

- France feared Austria-Germany ties strengthening
- French government encouraged **Bank of France** and other French banks to withdraw short-term credit from Austria
- Geopolitical strains made crisis worse

### US Response: Hoover Moratorium Proposal

- Hoover asked **Treasury Secretary Mellon** and **Secretary of State Henry Stimson** (May 11) to study relieving pressure on German economy
- Proposed relaxing Germany's war debt and reparations payments
- Proposal delayed until early next month

### European Bank Runs Spread (May-July 1931)

- Hungary: bank runs starting in May led to **bank holiday**
- Germany nationalized **Dresdner Bank** (nation's second largest) by buying preferred shares
- Major financial institutions failed across Romania, Latvia, Poland

### German Capital Flight and Monetary Tightening

- Germany's gold/foreign exchange reserves fell **one-third in June** (lowest in five years)
- To stem capital outflow: **Reichsbank raised discount rate to 15%** and collateralized loan rate to 20%
- Stock market collapses: German stocks -14.2%, British stocks -9.8%, French stocks -6.9% (May). US Dow -15% (May), -12.3% (April)

> "The world was imploding."

### Hoover Moratorium (June 1931)

- **June 5**: Hoover suggested one-year moratorium on all intergovernmental payments
- **Paul von Hindenburg** (Weimar Republic president) appealed that Germany was in danger of collapse
- **June 20**: Official announcement—US would forgo $245 million in debt service from Britain, France, and European powers
- Allies had to suspend $385 million in German reparations

### "Moratorium Rally"

- Dow rallied **12% in two days** after announcement, ended month 23% above June 2 low
- German stocks rose 25% first day of trading after announcement
- Commodity prices soared in following weeks

### Moratorium Reality Check

- **July 6**: Final agreement concluded with 15 countries
- German reparations suspended in final deal were lower than Hoover's initial proposal
- France refused to participate but agreed to re-lend reparations back to Germany
- **Dow slumped 4.5%** on the day

### Bear Market Rally Pattern in Depressions

> "The chart below puts things in perspective; the arrow under the grey shaded area shows the moratorium rally. Notice how insignificant that 35 percent rally looks within the bigger moves. I can assure you that those sorts of moves don't seem small when you're going through them."

- Throughout Great Depression: big policy announcements repeatedly produced optimism waves and rallies
- Total decline: almost 90%
- Investors repeatedly disappointed when policy moves insufficient and economy continued deteriorating

> "As noted earlier, bear market rallies like this are classic in a depression, since workers, investors, and policy makers have a strong tendency to exaggerate the importance of relatively small things that appear big close-up."

## Third Quarter, 1931: The Debt Moratorium Fails and the Run on Sterling Begins

### Moratorium Proves Insufficient

- Early July rumors: **Danat Bank** (one of Germany's largest) on verge of failure
- **Reichsbank** viewed it as systemically important, wanted to bail it out but lacked foreign reserves
- **July 8**: Reichsbank president **Hans Luther** reached out to Britain for further debt negotiations and new $1 billion loan
- Policy makers from other countries balked—no one wanted to lend more to Germany

### Hoover's "Standstill Agreement" Proposal

- Required all banks holding German/Central European short-term obligations to keep credit extended
- Exposed them to **big liquidity and potential solvency problems** as they needed cash to meet obligations

> "This was a banker-made crisis… the bankers must shoulder the burden of the solution, not our taxpayers."

> **Hoover's instinct to have the banks bear the cost is a classic but misguided policy response to a debt crisis. Punishing the banks in a way that weakens them makes sense for a few moral and economic reasons, as mentioned in the discussion of the archetypal template, and it can be a political necessity as the public hates the bankers at such times—but it can have disastrous consequences for the financial system and markets.**

- Without forceful policy support, collapse continued and Germany's depression worsened, inciting riots
- **Hitler** gearing up to run for Chancellor with populist stance threatening not to repay reparation debts at all
- July 20 London meeting: foreign ministers slowly arranged three-month loan extension plus standstill agreement
- Result: **classic run on the currency** (as described in archetypal template)

### The Run on Sterling

- Germany's problems proved key **contagion** source
- UK banks had lots of loans to Germany—couldn't get money out
- Foreign investors saw UK banks in trouble, began pulling their money
- **July 24**: France began withdrawing gold from England (interpreted as lack of confidence in pound)
- More countries pulled deposits from Britain—**run on sterling began**

### Bank of England's Defense Measures

- Sold reserves (a third in August alone) and raised interest rates (both classic moves)
- Foreigners watched weekly declines in gold reserves—pressure on pound only increased
- Sought loans from abroad to support currency, but loans effectively **funded flight out of sterling**

### Foreign Loan Requests

- **August 1, 1931**: Bank of England requested US government organize $250 million loan from private US banks (Hoover urged immediate action)
- Flight from sterling continued
- **August 28**: Bank received $200 million from American banks, $200 million from French banks

> "Both loans, however, mostly served to create more fear."

### Britain Abandons Gold Standard (September 19, 1931)

- Having exhausted foreign loans with just over £100 million in gold reserves remaining, Bank of England stopped supporting pound and **let it fall sharply**
- Following day: officially **suspended gold payments** (de facto default)
- Initially, public didn't understand implications—newspapers lamented it as end of an era

### Sterling Devaluation Impact

- Sterling fell **30% over next three months**
- First trading day after gold payments halted: sterling dropped to $3.70 (nearly 25% lower than pre-default $4.86)
- British policy makers didn't intervene to slow fall or maintain stability
