- Sterling exchange rates fluctuated greatly before dropping to low of $3.23 (December)
- Over same period: **UK equity market recovered and rose 11%** in local currency terms

### Global Currency Devaluations Follow

- Other countries abandoned gold convertibility to "print money" and devalue currencies
- Most devaluations roughly **30%** (Nordic countries, Portugal, eastern Europe, New Zealand, Australia, India) in line with sterling's devaluation

### Bond Market Impacts

- Investors feared government bonds would be defaulted on with devalued money
- Led to **run out of bonds**, raising interest rates and driving bond prices down
- US: Fed raised interest rates **2%** to attract foreign capital and hold gold peg
- All governments' bonds hit new lows in 1931 except Switzerland and France (declined at least 20% from 1931 highs)
- Global stocks sold off; some markets stopped trading
- **September 21**: only Paris Stock Exchange open in Europe

### UK Recovery After Devaluation

- UK stocks and bonds sold off during currency defense phase and immediately after devaluation, then rebounded
- UK debt denominated in own currency—**no risk government couldn't pay it back**
- UK 5.5% bonds due 1937 dropped to $92 after devaluation from $104 (as interest rates rose 4.7% to 5.7% in December), but moved back to $100 by year end

### Benefits of Devaluation and Money Printing

- Devaluation helped stimulate export sector
- Bank of England eased significantly, cutting rates **one percent by year end**
- Equilibrium reached: by end of October, London banks receiving money again

> **"In other words, the devaluation and money printing kicked off a beautiful deleveraging"** (referenced again when discussing US leaving gold standard)

- Why these moves work explained in archetypal template
- UK stocks and bonds rallied after selling off through currency defense phase and immediately following devaluation
- Commodity weakness offset by currency devaluation; gold prices broke peg

## Fourth Quarter, 1931: The International Crisis Spreads to the US and the Depression Worsens

### Sterling Devaluation Creates US Deflationary Pressure

- As other currencies devalued and dollar rose, created more **deflationary/depressing pressures in US**
- Sterling's September 1931 devaluation stunned global investors, sent shock waves through US markets
- Investors worldwide questioned whether US safe from default or devaluation
- Started selling out of dollar debt positions, raising interest rates and tightening liquidity
- Brought on **most painful period of depression** (lasted until FDR took US off gold standard 18 months later)

### US Market Deterioration

- Stocks sold off during run on sterling
- Dow finished September **down 30.7%** (largest monthly loss since crisis began)
- **October 5**: market fell 10.7% in single day
- NYSE banned short selling (classic attempt to slow sell-off)
- Previously "safe" treasury bonds now selling off with stocks (reflecting US **balance of payments crisis**)
- Yield on long-term US treasuries rose to 4% (nearly 1% above midyear lows)
- Concerns about US Treasury's ability to roll bonds coming due in following two years (due to debt stock and rising debt service)
- Fear of devaluation led to acute runs on US banks
- Banks needed to sell bonds to raise cash, contributing to rising yields

### US Gold Outflows

- Dollar ceased to be safe haven for first time since global debt crisis began
- Gold reserves flowed out following sterling devaluation as central banks (France, Belgium, Switzerland, Netherlands) converted dollars to gold
- US lost about **10% of gold reserves** within three weeks following sterling devaluation

### Federal Reserve Tightening (October 1931)

- **October 9**: New York Federal Reserve Bank raised discount rate **1.5% to 2.5%**

> **"Classically, in a balance of payments crisis, interest rate increases large enough to adequately compensate holders of debt in weak currency for the currency risk are way too large to be tolerated by the domestic economy, so they don't work."**

- Week later: New York Fed raised rate to **3.5%**
- Rumors: **George Harrison** (New York Fed head) asked French not to withdraw more gold from US

### Bank Runs and Credit Contraction (Late 1931)

- Domestic difficulties led investors to hoard gold and cash
- Series of bank runs in late 1931 caused many banks to close
- Big contraction in deposits for remaining open banks
- As deposits fell, banks called in loans to build cash reserves
- Homes and farms forced into foreclosure
- Several companies went bankrupt as investors didn't roll previously extended loans

### Economic Collapse Accelerates

- Second half of 1931: industrial production contracted **14.3%**, department store sales fell **12.9%**
- By end of 1931: unemployment reached nearly **20%**
- Domestic prices falling **10% per year**

### Hoover Administration Response (Late 1931)

- **National Credit Association** created: pool of private money lent against sound collateral to provide liquidity to at-risk banks (private central bank)
- Funds from banks totaled $500 million, with ability to borrow another billion

### Housing Market Support

- Hoover sought solutions for collapsing real estate market
- Stop foreclosures on "homes and farms of responsible people"
- Created system of **Home Loan Discount Banks** (1932)
- Worked with insurance and real estate agencies to suspend foreclosures on farm loans by Federal Land Banks
- Provided institution with $1 billion to expand lending

### Market Rally (October-November 1931)

- Policies well received, inspired confidence
- Stock market rallied 35% from October bottom to November 9
- **10%+ jump** day National Credit Association announced
- Rally made some believe worst was over (as most significant rallies do)
- No significant change in total money and credit available
- Fundamental imbalance between total debt coming due and money available to service it wasn't resolved
- Rally faded as proposals too small to handle problem
- Stocks reached new low near end of December

## First Half of 1932: Growing Government Intervention Unable to Halt Economic Collapse

### Deepening Depression (1932)

- Depression deepened as economy plunged with deflation and credit problems worsening
- Businesses struggling/failing—aggregate: $2.7 billion in losses and record bankruptcies
- Almost **32,000 failures** and **$928 million in liabilities**
- News of bank failures filled newspapers
- Losses rippled through system, imposing losses on lenders, causing other businesses to close

### Classic Debt Crisis Pattern

> **"It is classic in a big debt crisis: Policy makers play around with deflationary levers to bring down debt for a couple of years but eventually wake up to the fact that the depressing effects of debt reduction and austerity are both too painful and inadequate to produce the effects that are needed. So more aggressive policies are undertaken."**

- Hoover administration announced another policy set (first part of 1932) to provide liquidity and get credit going again

### Reconstruction Finance Corporation (RFC)

- **January 23**: Hoover launched **RFC**
- Funded with $500 million capital
- Could borrow from Treasury or private sources up to $3 billion
- Goal: provide liquidity to solvent banks to shore them up against failure

### RFC's Broader Mandate

- More extensive mandate than Federal Reserve
- Could lend against wider range of collateral and to broader range of entities
- Could lend to state-chartered banks, rural banks not in Federal Reserve System, and railroads (important industry like auto in 2008)

> **"Lending against a widening range of collateral and to an increasingly wide range of borrowers is a classic lever that policy makers pull to ensure that sufficient liquidity gets to the financial system, sometimes provided by central banks and sometimes by central governments."**

### RFC Impact

- By end of August 1932: RFC lent $1.3 billion to 5,520 financial institutions
- Helped reduce bank failures
- **But**: RFC only able to lend against "good" collateral—unable to provide sufficient support to institutions that needed it most

### Banking Act of 1932 and Early Quantitative Easing

- Fed started experimenting with money printing
- Going into crisis: Federal Reserve only able to lend against gold or certain commercial paper (both in short supply)
- Trade-off: further tightening vs. undermining dollar's gold peg

> **"The 1932 Banking Act, signed by Hoover on February 27, attempted to alleviate the liquidity squeeze while maintaining the gold standard by increasing the Federal Reserve's ability to print money, but only to buy government bonds (which 75 years later would be called 'quantitative easing')."**

- Move was contentious (clear weakening of gold standard principles)
- Sense of urgency: bill passed without debate
- Hoover framed it: "in a sense a national defense measure"

> **"Later that year, Congress gave the Federal Reserve additional powers to print money and provide liquidity in an emergency. This provision—Section 13(3) of the Federal Reserve Act—would end up being critical to the Fed's response to the 2008 debt crisis."**

### Federal Reserve Bond Purchases (1932)

- Fed bought nearly $50 million government securities each week (April)
- Nearly $100 million each week (May)
- By June: purchased over $1.5 billion in government securities
- Fed purchases and holdings of government debt shown in charts

### Impact on Treasury Yields

- Once Fed began purchases: yields on short-term Treasury securities fell rapidly
- Three-month T-Bill yields falling more than two percent over first half of year
- Fed purchases relieved pressure in market for longer-term treasury bonds
- Supply-demand imbalance for dollars reached breaking point amid large deficits and foreign reluctance to hold US assets
- After rising above 4.3% (January): yields on ten-year treasuries fell below 3.5% over next six months

### Market Rally (Early 1932)

- Moves ignited optimism and another rally
- Dow Jones increased **19.5%**, reaching January's high
- Closed above 80 in February

### Abandonment of Mark-to-Market Accounting

> **"Another classic move was the abandonment of mark-to-market accounting for banks."**

- January: **Comptroller of the Currency** instructed bank examiners to use par value as intrinsic value of bonds held by national banks with BAA rating or better
- Under prior methodology: banks faced major paper losses on bonds or cash losses if sold
- Losses reduced capital, forcing them to raise money or sell assets
- Further constrained liquidity and pushed down asset prices
- Change in accounting rules relieved immediate pressure on banks

### Macroprudential Pressure on Banks

> **"The Hoover administration also tried to get credit going with macroprudential measures"**

- Applied direct pressure on banks to lend
- Hoover and **Treasury Secretary Ogden Mills** blamed banks for inability to stimulate credit
- Accused them of restricting loans and hoarding gold/cash
- Hoover organized committees in twelve Federal Reserve districts to pressure large regional banks into lending
- Effort met with little success

### Continued Gold Outflows

- None of these moves enough to halt economic collapse
- Pressure on US gold reserves continued (foreigners worried with monetary expansion and expanding deficit, US wouldn't sustain dollar's gold conversion at existing rates)
- They rushed to make conversion—gold left country every month March to June
- **June net gold exports**: $206 million (level last experienced following sterling depreciation)
- Produced credit tightening

### Market Decline (March-May 1932)

- March: stocks sold off
- Market decline extended through 11 weeks
- Dow Jones dropped **50%** (88 on March 8 to 44 on May 31)
- Dow closed May on low for month
- Volume declined to about 750,000 shares per day
- Early in crisis: government efforts to increase lending and spending led to sustained rallies
- At this stage: investors disillusioned
- Worried Hoover's programs not making enough difference to make up for vast cost
- Markets continued trending downward

### Rising Social Unrest (1932)

> **"Social unrest and conflict continued to rise globally."**

- Germany: **Hitler won most seats in Reichstag election**
- Japan slipped toward militarism: invading Manchuria (1931) and Shanghai (1932)
- US: strikes and protests increasing
- Unemployment approaching 25%
- Those employed faced wage cuts
- Outside cities: farmers faced ruin as prices fell and drought destroyed crops

### Bonus Army March

- Thousands of veterans and families marched on Washington (June), stayed there
- Attempted to pressure government to immediately pay them veterans' bonuses
- **July 28**: US Army troops led by **General Douglas MacArthur** cleared camp with tanks and tear gas
- Conflicts within/between countries intensified
- Sowed seeds of **populism, authoritarianism, nationalism, and militarism**
- Led to economic warfare then military warfare in Europe (September 1939) and with Japan (December 1941)

## Second Half of 1932: Further Contractions and the Election of FDR

### Summer Rally (1932)

- By summer: big stimulation and relief to banks appeared helping
- Downward spiral began to moderate
- Asset prices stabilized
- Production actually increased in certain areas (like autos)
- May-June: commodities, stocks, bonds all bottomed
- Markets improved during second half of year
- **August-September**: Dow Jones Industrial Average rallied to peak of **80** (almost double July low)

### Time Magazine on Rally (August 8, 1932)

- Rally occurred because: gold outflow finally ceased, rumors of foreign capital coming to country, railroad merger approved

### Policy Pullback

- As optimism about economy and asset markets increased: policy makers began pulling back on earlier stimulative measures
- RFC weakened significantly by scandal when it bailed out **Central Republic Bank and Trust** (headed by previous chair
<!-- pp. 281-300 -->

## 1932: Political Backlash and Policy Paralysis

### The RFC Crisis and Public Outrage

- Congress ordered the **RFC** to publish names of all institutions receiving loans, effectively requiring borrowers to advertise they were in trouble
- This worsened depositor withdrawal pressure; RFC borrowing slowed and withdrawals accelerated
- **Main Street vs. Wall Street conflict**: Populist calls to "punish the bankers" made it incredibly difficult for policy makers to save the financial system and economy
- Political pressure ended Fed's government bond purchases despite their effectiveness

### Hoover's Fatal Budget Austerity

- Revenue Act of 1932 raised income taxes, corporation taxes, and excise taxes
- **Despite tax increases, budget deficit grew relative to GDP because austerity was contractionary** — economy shrank faster than deficit declined
- **Classic rookie move in depressions**: Hoover's attempt to balance the budget through austerity

### Roosevelt's Populist Campaign (1932)

- Campaign struck anti-speculator tone, calling for federal control of exchanges
- Indicated support for dollar devaluation, increasing currency pressure
- Promised to maintain gold standard publicly, but **politicians' disingenuous promises should never be believed** — they're expedient and inconsistent with economic fundamentals
- **Roosevelt won 22.8 million votes to Hoover's 15.8 million** (most popular vote total to that time)

### Economic and Political Context

- Consumer spending and production fell >20%, unemployment rose >16%
- Severe deflation: prices falling ~1% per month
- **Policy makers' reliance on deflationary debt reduction levers pushed US into severe depression/"ugly deleveraging"** — nominal rates well above nominal growth, so debt grew faster than income
- Debate became antagonistically political with strong populist overtones

### Global Populism Wave

- Weak economy, uneven recovery (elite prospering while common man struggled), and ineffectual policy makers fueled global populism (1920s-1930s)
- **US inequality peaked early 1930s**: top 10% earned 45% of income, owned 85% of wealth; unemployment >20%
- FDR's "New Deal" promised big changes for workers, debtors, unemployed
- **Germany**: hyperinflation + Depression, top 10% earned ~40% income, unemployment >25% — set stage for Nazi party's ascent

### November 1932: Crisis Deepens

- **Nevada declared first statewide bank holiday** (November) — sparked national panic
- Bank failures accelerating, open market operations ended, RFC neutered, government spending cut, devaluation threat looming
- Gold outflows resumed, economy's downward trajectory steepened
- 1932 economic collapse was breathtaking

## 1933: Preinauguration

### Roosevelt's Refusal to Reaffirm Gold Standard

- Gold flowed out in anticipation of reflationary policies
- Roosevelt refused to reaffirm gold commitment despite pleas from advisors
- **Senator Carter Glass** refused Treasury Secretary nomination unless Roosevelt guaranteed gold standard
- **Hoover** wrote personal letter requesting Roosevelt clarify his policies
- European investors worried; *New York Times* reported confusion about dollar's future — "Declaration by Mr. Roosevelt declaring firm resolution to maintain a sound currency would have an extremely reassuring effect"
- Roosevelt stayed silent

### Guardian Detroit Crisis (February 1933)

- **Guardian Detroit Union Group** (largest Michigan financial institution) sought RFC loan but had little collateral
- Main shareholder: **Henry Ford**
- RFC suggested Ford provide support; **Ford refused**, recognizing Guardian Trust was systemically important as Central Republic
- Union Guardian Trust and Guardian National Bank allowed to fail; **Michigan declared statewide bank holiday**

### Systemic Contagion

- **When policy makers fail to rescue systemically important institutions, ripple effects spread quickly**
- Michigan was industrial heartland — impact on other states especially large
- Households and companies rushed to withdraw savings nationwide
- Ohio, Arkansas, Indiana suffered runs; Maryland bank holiday Feb 25
- By March 4: withdrawal restrictions in over 30 states

### Gold Reserve Hemorrhage

- Last two weeks of February: New York Fed lost **$250 million** (nearly 25% of gold reserves)
- Hoover attempted War Powers Act and capital controls but Democrats blocked
- March 1933: GNP hit lowest point of entire depression — **$55.6 billion, 31.5% below 1929 level**

## 1933-1937: The Beautiful Deleveraging

### March 5, 1933: Roosevelt's Decisive Action

- **Four-day national bank holiday**, suspended gold exports (delinking from gold), assembled rescue team
- **Emergency Banking Act of 1933**: extended holiday, gave Fed and Treasury unprecedented powers to provide liquidity and capital
- **Critical innovation**: Fed could issue dollars backed by bank assets instead of gold — **broke link between dollar and gold, allowed Fed to print money and provide liquidity banks desperately needed**
- Roosevelt banned gold exports under 1917 Trading with the Enemy Act

### Bank Audit and Triage

- Auditors worked through every US bank's books, starting with largest and safest
- **Systemically important banks were always supported; smaller banks often allowed to fail**
- Undercapitalized banks: (a) RFC issued preferred shares, (b) merged with healthier bank, or (c) closed
- Sound banks could reopen and borrow from Fed using any assets as collateral
- Treasury agreed to cover Fed losses — **guaranteeing liabilities of every bank they chose to keep open**

### Roosevelt's Radio Address (March 12)

> The new law allows the twelve Federal Reserve Banks to issue additional currency on good assets and thus the banks which reopen will be able to meet every legitimate call...It is sound currency because it is backed by actual, good assets...I can assure you that it is safer to keep your money in a reopened bank than under the mattress.

### Public Confidence Restored

- Instead of runs, public deposited **>$1 billion** into banks when they reopened
- Classic example: **debt and liquidity problems prompted by runs can be rectified by providing liquidity rather than holding it back**
- Within one month: member banks representing 90% of deposits had reopened
- When markets opened Wednesday: **Dow rose 15.3%**, commodities soared

### Dalio's Personal Reflection

- Breaking gold link in 1933 identical to August 1971 (when Dalio was clerking at NYSE)
- In 1971, thought crisis would send stocks/economy down — **was painfully wrong**
- Same dynamics as 1933, same reasons
- **First time surprised by events that hadn't happened in his lifetime but had happened many times in history**
- Drove him to study all big market/economic movements across time frames and economies
- Developed **timeless and universal principles** for dealing with crises
- These principles saved him multiple times (e.g., 2008)
- **Events in 1930s have happened many times before for exact same reasons**

### The Beautiful Deleveraging Mechanism

- Dollar plunged vs. currencies and gold after printing money
- Within two weeks of leaving gold peg: Fed decreased liquidity injections
- Short-term rates fell 1% to 2%, bankers' acceptance rates dropped to 2%, call loan rates decreased to 3%
- Money supply increased 1.5% over three months
- **Dow up almost 100% over next four months**
- Depression ended on a dime (though GDP didn't match 1929 peak until 1936)
- **Leaving gold peg was the turning point** — all markets and statistics bottomed exactly then
- Recovery benefited rich more than poor; post-1933 remained difficult for many (why people think depression lasted through 1930s)

### Roosevelt's Policy Avalanche (First Six Months)

**Regulatory Changes** (typical at end of big debt crises):
- **April 5 and 18**: Outlawed public ownership of monetary gold; outlawed private gold exports (devaluing and printing money)
- **May 27**: Securities Act of 1933 regulated securities sale (increased regulation)
- **June 5**: Banned "Gold Clauses" in contracts (big debt restructuring — gold had increased in value after dollar delinked)
- **June 13**: Home Owner's Loan Corporation (HOLC) refinanced residential mortgages — 1 million people received long-term loans 1933-1935 (restructuring debts)
- **June 16**: Banking Act of 1933 (Glass-Steagall II) — FDIC deposit insurance up to $2,500; Fed regulation of interest rates (Regulation Q); stringent bank regulations; separation of investment and commercial banking (establish deposit insurance, increased regulation)

**Fiscal Stimulus**:
- Federal spending had fallen >$1 billion in 1932 as Hoover tightened
- Though FDR initially campaigned to balance budget, policies increased annual spending **$2.7 billion (5% of GDP) by 1934**
- **April 5**: Civilian Conservation Corps (CCC) — employed 2.5 million in public works over nine years
- **May 12**: Federal Emergency Relief Act — $500 million for household financial support
- **May 18**: Tennessee Valley Authority (TVA) — massive infrastructure investment (power, flood control, irrigation)
- **June 16**: National Industrial Recovery Act (NIRA) created Public Works Administration (PWA) — $3.3 billion for large-scale public works

### Beautiful Deleveraging Formula

- **Balance is key**: Deleveragings become beautiful when there's enough stimulation to offset deflationary forces and bring nominal growth rate above nominal interest rate
- Deflation turned into acceptable inflation
- Heavy machinery orders climbed 100%, industrial production increased ~50%
- March-July: nondurable manufacturing up 35%, durable manufacturing up 83%
- Unemployment fell, wholesale prices jumped 45%
- **Rebounding from very depressed levels fed on themselves to make beautiful deleveraging**

### GDP Growth Above Interest Rates

- Charts show nominal growth rose above nominal interest rates after FDR left gold standard
- Total debt level (%GDP) began declining
- "Beautiful Deleveraging" period clearly marked on charts

## 1935: The Goldilocks Period

### Economic Recovery and Fed Concerns

- Economy and markets recovered through 1934 into 1935
- By 1935: economy recovered, deflation disappeared, stocks soared
- Home prices rising >10% per year
- Equity price recovery even faster
- Wealth and output remained below pre-depression bubble levels

### Fed Debates Excess Reserves

- Spring 1935: Fed increasingly concerned about rising excess reserves
- Feared excess reserves could create credit expansion and future inflation
- March background memo recommended no action for time being
- Explored whether excess reserves would push down government securities yields and encourage private lending (no evidence yet)
- Second issue: how to sell accumulated debt (reverse QE)
- Paper rejected action, saying it would prematurely emphasize inflation concerns before signs materialized

### Stock Market Bubble Fears

- October memo expressed heightened concerns about excess reserves
- Pondered appropriate time to reduce them: (1) asset sales or (2) increasing reserve requirements
- Argument for reducing: get ahead of future inflation
- Argument against: no evidence yet for restraint

### November 1935 Press Release

- Fed discussed stock market boom and inflation concerns
- **Fears of fueling bubble were rampant** — policy makers remembered late 1920s bubble caused stock market bust and contributed to depression
- Very worried steep 1935 stock rise (nearly quadrupling) could fuel recurrence
- Treasury disagreed, noting inflation still far off

### Speculative Credit Monitoring

- Fed paid attention to how stock purchases were financed
- Heightened concerns about "speculative credit" after excess margin-borrowing in late 1920s
- November memo noted purchases financed by money, not credit, so no action taken
- Still, stock advance considered emerging bubble; fears about too-easy monetary policy remained
- **George Harrison** (New York Fed) suggested raising reserve requirements to curtail stock price rise
- **Treasury Secretary Henry Morgenthau** rejected this notion but recognized inflation concern
- **Emanuel Goldenweiser** (Fed head of research) warned of negative psychological reaction to raising requirements
- Recommended press release saying reserve action would be "precautionary" and "there is no need to worry about inflation at this time" given unused plant capacity and unemployment
- End of 1935: Fed press release stated reserves and gold inflows "continues to be excessive" and warned "appropriate action may be taken as soon as it appears in the public interest"

## 1936-1938: The Tightening Causes Recession

### Debate Over Reserve Requirements (1936)

- FDR wanted to signal inflation concern ahead of election — urged reserve requirements be tightened spring 1936
- **Fed Chairman Eccles** worried banks would accumulate bonds/loans at low rates and get burned by inflation
- May 1936: Fed did not move (Banking Act of 1935 meant Morgenthau had to resign from board but still had influence)

### July 1936: The Tightening Begins

- **Eccles met alone with FDR**, explaining intention to raise reserves and assuring president he wouldn't act if interest rates rose and Fed would buy bonds if they sold off
- Fed tightened reserves later that month
- Eccles and Fed moved without informing Morgenthau (who was furious)
- After tiny bond sell-off, Morgenthau ordered Harrison (New York Fed) to purchase bonds using Treasury's accounts
- Fed Board in Washington joined in, buying bonds and selling bills as Eccles had promised
- **August 1936-May 1937**: Fed doubled reserve requirements from ~8% to 16%
- First tightening (August 1936) did not hurt stock prices or economy

### **Classic Pattern: First Tightening Does Not Hurt Stocks and Economy**

<!-- pp. 301-320 -->

### Policy Tightening in 1936-1937

#### Reserve Requirement Increases

- Reserve tightening occurred in three phases: August 1936, March 1937, and May 1937
- March 1937 was the largest increase (about half the total)
- Deposit reserve requirements increased dramatically:
  - **Central Reserve City**: 13.0% → 26.0%
  - **Reserve City**: 10% → 20%
  - **Country**: 7.0% → 14.0%
  - **Time Deposits (All Member Banks)**: 3.0% → 6.0%
- Excess reserves fell from $3 billion to less than $1 billion

#### Currency Devaluation Wars

- Monetary policy tightening intensified by currency devaluations from **France** and **Switzerland**
- **Tripartite Agreement** (September 1936): US, Britain, and France agreed to refrain from competitive exchange devaluations
- By then, all countries could easily devalue in response to others, creating economic turbulence but leaving relative positions unchanged
- All currencies devalued against gold, but not much against each other

### Gold Inflows and Policy Concerns (1936)

#### Three Concerns About Gold Inflows

1. **Rapid rise in the stock market**: Stocks up 4x from 1933 bottom; rose ~40% in 1935 and 25% in 1936. Policymakers worried foreign capital was inflating US stock prices
2. **Inflationary impact**: Inflation rose from ~0% to ~2% by October 1936
3. **Vulnerability to capital outflow**: Fear that European nations would finance war by selling US assets and withdrawing gold, while preventing US holders from repatriating capital

#### Gold Sterilization Policy

- **FDR ordered "sterilization"** beginning December 1936
- Normal gold purchases printed money; sterilization instead drew down Treasury's cash account at the Fed (no new money printed)
- December 23, 1936 to July 1937: Treasury sterilized ~$1.3 billion in gold inflows (~1.5% of GDP)
- Fed also tightened reserve requirements to take money out of circulation

### The 1937 Downturn

#### Early 1937 Conditions

- Economy remained strong: stock market rising, industrial production healthy, inflation ~5%
- Second and third reserve tightenings in March and May 1937
- Neither Fed nor Treasury anticipated that tightening + sterilization would push rates higher, but reduced liquidity caused bond sell-off and short rate spike

#### Policy Clash: Morgenthau vs. Eccles

- **Treasury Secretary Morgenthau** was furious, argued Fed should offset the "panic" through open market bond purchases
- He ordered Treasury into the market to purchase bonds itself
- **Fed Chairman Eccles** pushed back, urged Morgenthau to balance the budget and raise tax rates to retire debt

#### Fiscal Tightening

- Federal government outlays fell 10% in 1937 and another 10% in 1938
- **Revenue Act of 1937**: closed loopholes in Revenue Act of 1935 (the "wealth tax"), which had raised top income tax to 75%
- Federal budget deficit went from ~-4% of GDP to neutral
- Driven largely by Social Security tax increase, with smaller spending cuts

#### Inequality and Labor Unrest

- Recovery perceived as uneven (benefiting elites over common workers)
- Workers saw corporate profit gains but no wage increases
- Strike activity surged: 118% increase in number of strikes, 136% increase in workers involved (1936 to 1937)

#### Economic Collapse

- **Monetary and fiscal tightening** created significant sell-off in risky assets
- Stocks fell most; home prices stopped gaining and dipped negative
- Credit growth slowed across all sectors:
  - Nonfinancial business credit: -2%
  - Household credit: -1%
- Unemployment rose to 15% (though brief compared to early 1930s)
- **Stocks bottomed April 1938, declining nearly 60% total**

### Late 1937-1938: Policy Reversal

#### Monetary Easing

- Fed accelerated twist into longer-dated assets, began small net asset purchases by year-end
- Treasury reversed sterilization program in partnership with Fed by end of 1937
- Money growth picked up in 1938 with reverse sterilization and renewed money printing
- Gold inflows slowed, economy and asset prices deteriorated
- Money growth eventually outpaced gold reserve growth

#### Fed's "Twist" Operation

- Fed bought long-term bonds while selling bills and notes (started in 1936)
- Increased net assets by small amount (~3% by 1938)

#### Spring 1938: Further Stimulus

- Fed lowered reserve requirements back to 1936 levels, releasing ~$750 million
- Federal government increased deficit spending in 1938 and 1939 heading into war
- Deficit rose from nearly balanced at start of 1938 to almost 3% of GDP by start of 1939
- Deficit spending above 2% of GDP continued throughout the year

#### Recovery

- Stock market began recovering in 1938 (didn't fully regain 1937 highs until end of war nearly a decade later)
- Credit flows and economy recovered in 1939 following stimulus and war entry

### The Path to War

#### Economic-Geopolitical Linkages

- Economics and geopolitics highly intertwined in the 1930s
- **Germany** and **Japan**: internal conflicts between haves (Right) and have-nots (Left) led to populist, autocratic, nationalistic, militaristic leaders
- These leaders given autocratic powers to bring order to badly-managed economies
- External conflicts arose as these countries became rival economic and military powers to existing world powers

#### Thucydides's Trap

> "Rivalries between countries lead to wars in order to establish which country is more powerful, which are then followed by periods of peace in which the dominant power/powers get to set the rules because no country can fight them until a rival power emerges, at which time they do it all over again."

- Wars really started ~10 years before official 1939/1941 dates, as economic conflicts progressively grew into WWII
- Germany and Japan competed with UK, US, and France for resources and territorial influence
- War culminated in establishing US as dominant power, dictating new world order
- This peace continues until the same process happens again

### Timeline of Key Events (1930-1941)

#### 1930
- **Smoot-Hawley Tariff** began trade war

#### 1931
- **Japan invaded Manchuria, China** for natural resources due to inadequate resources and severe rural poverty
- US wanted to keep China free from Japanese control; competed for oil, rubber, and tin from Southeast Asia
- Japan's severe depression drove it off gold standard → yen depreciation, big fiscal/monetary expansions
- Japan first country to experience recovery and strong growth (lasted until 1937)

#### 1932
- Internal conflict in Japan led to failed coup, massive upsurge in right-wing nationalism and militarism
- 1931-1937: military took control of government and increased top-down command of economy

#### 1933
- **Hitler came to power** in Germany as populist promising to control bad economy, bring order to political chaos, fight communists
- Within two months: took total authoritarian control via **Enabling Act** (using national security excuse)
- Refused reparations payments, stepped out of League of Nations, took control of media
- Created top-down command economy: involved in Volkswagen setup, German Autobahn construction
- Believed Germany limited by geographic boundaries, lacked raw materials for industrial military complex, German people should be ethnically united

#### 1934
- Severe famine in parts of Japan caused political turbulence, reinforced right-wing militaristic nationalism
- Free market failure strengthened command economy

#### 1936
- Germany took back Rhineland militarily; in 1938, annexed Austria
- Japan signed pact with Germany
- Fed tightening caused fragile economy to weaken; other major economies weakened with it

#### 1937
- Japan's occupation of China spread; **Second Sino-Japanese War** began
- Japanese took Shanghai and Nanking, killing estimated 200,000 Chinese civilians, disarmed combatants in capture of Nanking alone
- US provided China's **Chiang Kai-shek** government with fighter planes and pilots

#### 1939
- Germany invaded Poland; **World War II officially began** in Europe

#### 1940
- Germany captured Denmark, Norway, Netherlands, Belgium, Luxembourg, and France
- Most German and Japanese companies remained publicly owned but production controlled by governments for war effort
- **Henry Stimson** became US Secretary of War; used aggressive economic sanctions against Japan
- **Export Control Act** (July 2, 1940); October: ramped up embargo restricting "all iron and steel to destinations other than Britain and nations of the Western Hemisphere"

#### September 1940-1941
- Japan invaded several Southeast Asian colonies (starting with French Indochina)
- 1941: Japan seized oil reserves in Dutch East Indies to create **"Southern Resource Zone"** within **"Greater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere"**
- Southern Resource Zone: European colonies in Southeast Asia providing oil, rubber, rice
- Greater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere: Asian countries controlled by Japan, not Western powers
- Japan occupied naval base near Manila, threatening attack on Philippines (US protectorate)

#### 1941
- **Lend-Lease policy**: US sent oil, food, weaponry to Allied Nations for free (~$650 billion in today's dollars); ended US neutrality
- Summer 1941: **Roosevelt froze all Japanese assets** in US and **embargoed all oil and gas exports** to Japan
- Japan calculated it would be out of oil in two years

#### December 1941: Pearl Harbor
- Japan attacked Pearl Harbor and British/Dutch colonies in Asia
- Goal: destroy Pacific Fleet threatening Japan, not necessarily win the war
- Japan believed US weakened by fighting on two fronts (Europe and Pacific) and by political system
- Japan thought totalitarianism and command military industrial complex superior to US individualistic/capitalist approach

### News & Federal Reserve Bulletin

*[Pages 315-320 contain historical newspaper headlines and Federal Reserve Bulletin excerpts from 1925-1929, documenting the progression from prosperity through the stock market boom to the crash. Key themes include: record corporate earnings, rising stock prices, Federal Reserve warnings about speculation, increasing broker loans, debate over rate policy, and ultimately the October 1929 crash.]*

<!-- pp. 321-340 -->

### Post-Crash Market Dynamics (Late October 1929)

- **October 30, 1929**: Stock recovery begins as panicky liquidation checked; bona fide buyers replaced hysterical sellers
- **October 31, 1929**: Bank stocks surge 5-500 points with heavy trading; **National City Bank** leads gains, closing up 85 points; investment trusts move irregularly

### Economic Activity Holding Up (November 1929)

- **Federal Reserve Bulletin** reports industrial activity running 10% above 1928 levels for Q3 1929
- Building contracts awarded declined further; bank loans increased mid-September to mid-October, reflecting growth in loans on securities
- **November 1929**: Discount rate cuts begin—**Federal Reserve Bank of New York** reduces rate from 6% to 5% effective November 1
- Buying rates on bills under 90 days reduced from 5 1/8 to 5%, then to 4 3/4%; 7-month maturity reduced from 5 1/8 to 4 3/4%

### International Monetary Response

- **Bank of England** cuts rate to 6% (November 1, 1929)—"bold and unexpected step" after standing at 6 1/2% through five difficult weeks
- **November 5, 1929**: Stocks decline 2-17 points despite orderly trading; sessions cut to 3 hours as selling pressure persists

### Hoover Administration Response (Late 1929)

- **November 22, 1929**: Listed bonds gain in broader trading; US government bonds reach new high records at 5%
- **November 29**: Europe skeptical that **Hoover's program** will allay trade reaction
- **December 1, 1929**: Hoover stabilization plans praised for "prompt and resolute" response but require "careful execution" and "discrimination" in new construction/business expansion
- **Wesley C. Mitchell** characterizes Hoover's approach as "significant experiment toward industrial equilibrium"
- **Fox Theatres, Inc.** to spend $15M on construction work to aid Hoover program

### Market Reactions to Hoover Initiatives

- **December 4, 1929**: Wall Street "well pleased" with Hoover's message to Congress on banking law revision, railroad supervision, and public utilities
- **December 5, 1929**: **Dr. Wesley C. Mitchell** (Taylor Society) says Hoover averted wage cuts and blocked 1921-style reduction
- **December 10, 1929**: **Standard Oil** aided 129 employees buying company stock during slump

### Continued Monetary Easing (December 1929-Early 1930)

- **December 13, 1929**: **Bank of England** cuts rate to 5%—third drop in eleven weeks, astonishing London's financial district
- **December 31, 1929**: Bond prices irregular on Stock Exchange; domestic issues easier on average, foreign loans pointed upward; Liberty bonds and treasury issues slightly lower

### Brief Market Recovery (January 1930)

- **January 1, 1930**: "Burst of holiday enthusiasm" drives speculative year-end rally; market closes with generally higher prices throughout
- Best principal stocks advance 12+ points establishing "greatest appreciation"
- **January 19, 1930**: Building permits continue decline but survey indicates cheap money will aid early recovery

### Further Rate Cuts (Early 1930)

- **February 5, 1930**: Record trading volume—4,362,420 shares, highest since post-autumn break
- **March 14, 1930**: Rediscount rate reduced to 3 1/2%—**fourth cut** by Federal Reserve Bank since stock market slump
- **March 17, 1930**: Lower money at Berlin—day loans down to 3 1/2 and 5%, discounts cheapest since 1927

### Economic Deterioration Begins (April 1930)

- **April 1930**: Industrial production increased in February while factory employment remained flat
- Wholesale commodity prices continued decline; credit extended to member banks further reduced in February but increased in first two weeks of March
- Money rates continued to decline
- **April 2, 1930**: Stocks show greatest appreciation since market decline last fall
- **April 11, 1930**: Farm wages at lowest level since 1923—reflects "big supply of labor due to depression in industrial employment"
- **April 15, 1930**: **Detroit Club Women** resolve to dispel depression fear and bring out hoarded cash through spending pledge
- **April 26, 1930**: Elections after Depression become top political topic—trade reaction and agricultural price collapse will dominate Congressional campaign

### Credit Markets Ease Further (May 1930)

- **May 1930** (**Federal Reserve Bulletin**): Credit situation "relatively easy" in recent weeks
- Demand for commercial credit declined further; demand from securities markets increased
- Rising stock prices accompanied by $785M+ increase in brokers' loans at New York City
- **May 1, 1930**: **Studebaker** cuts dividend rate to $4, citing reduced earnings from decreased auto demand
- **May 5, 1930**: **1,028 economists** urge **Hoover** to veto pending **Hawley-Smoot tariff bill**
  - Led by **Dr. Claire Wilcox** (Swarthmore College), **American Economic Association** members warn measure will harm economy if passed

### Protectionism Escalates (June 1930)

- **June 1930** (**Federal Reserve Bulletin**): Industrial production declined in May by roughly same amount as April increase
- Factory employment decreased more than usual seasonally; prices continued downward; money rates eased to lowest level in 5+ years
- **June 10, 1930**: Calls for tariff cuts to aid world amity
- **June 14, 1930**: Senate passes tariff bill 44-42; Europe takes first move in reprisal
- **June 15, 1930**: Stock prices sag on passage of tariff
- **June 16, 1930**: Hoover announces he will sign tariff bill

### Labor Relations (July 1930)

- **July 29, 1930**: Building trade unions tentatively approve plan to avoid strikes; court to arbitrate disputes
- "Definite agreement for arbitration for all jurisdictional disputes" reached at Strand Hotel conference between national representatives of employers and unions

### Immigration Restriction (September 1930)

- **September 10, 1930**: Labor immigration halted temporarily at Hoover's order
- State Department restricts visa applications to withhold visas from immigrants who may become "public charges"

### Monetary Conditions (October 1930)

- **October 1930** (**Federal Reserve Bulletin**): Money market remained easy through September
- Little change in demand for reserve-bank credit; increase in acceptances holdings by reserve banks reflected further decline of discounts for member banks

### Bank Failures Begin (December 1930)

- **December 11, 1930**: False rumor leads to trouble at **Bank of United States** branch in Bronx
- Small merchant told stock was "good investment," then advised against sale; departed and spread false rumor that bank refused to sell his stock
- **December 11, 1930**: Stocks decline with largest trading in 4 weeks; corn and cotton prices fall
- **December 12, 1930**: **Bank of U.S. Closes Doors**
  - Institution issues statement hoping for early reopening
  - Leading banks provide temporary relief by offering to loan depositors 50% of deposit amounts
  - Despite name, had no connection with federal government
  - Deposits at closing approximately **$160,000,000**

### Severe Market Decline (December 1930)

- **December 17, 1930**: Stock market sees largest trading since early November crash
- Declines of 4-6 points were numerous, with losses in few stocks running even larger
- High-grade stocks showed special weakness; some irregularly distributed recovery before closing
- **December 23, 1930**: **Bankers Trust of Philadelphia** shuts down
  - Directors voluntarily turned over bank's affairs to State Department of Banking after all-night conference
  - Neither main office nor any of nineteen branches opened for business

### Financial Commentary (December 1930)

- **December 24, 1930**: Wall Street views **Chelsea Bank and Trust Company** closing philosophically
- **New York Times**: "Event, while obviously unfortunate for customers, had little significance for the financial world"
- Chelsea Bank operated entirely outside financial district; closing has no bearing on position of other banking institutions
- Not a member of Clearing House or Federal Reserve system

### Economic Summary (December 1930-January 1931)

- **December 1930** (**Federal Reserve Bulletin**): Money rates at opening of December "as low as at any time since records became available"
- Ease in money market accompanied further decrease in demand for credit from security market (rapid decline in brokers' loans to lowest level in 5 years)
- **January 1931**: Year of declining business activity
  - Further decline in output and employment in most manufacturing industries
  - Wholesale prices for many commodities continued declining during last two months
  - Business activity began receding midsummer 1929 after rapid expansion; continued declining rapidly through last half of 1930 following brief spring recovery
  - Almost all branches of industry shared decline; employment declined; total income of wage earners and farmers decreased
  - Wholesale prices throughout world declined considerably; retail prices reflected decline in smaller degree

### Optimism Emerges Briefly (January 1931)

- **January 25, 1931**: Wall Street flooded with optimistic prophecies; **Federal Reserve** bankers' policies compared favorably
- **George F. Baker** (First National Bank chairman) breaks rule of silence to tell country he detected signs of "improvement in business conditions along sound lines"
- **February 11, 1931**: Stocks go higher with public buying again
- Runaway market lifts main body of stocks 2-6 points; sensitive specialties rise 7-14 points
- **February 25, 1931**: Stocks rise briskly; 330 at new highs

### Policy Responses (March 1931)

- **March 1931** (**Federal Reserve Bulletin**): Industrial activity increased in January by slightly less than usual seasonal amount; factory employment and pay rolls declined
- Money rates in open market declined further from mid-January to mid-February
- **March 1, 1931**: **Bacon-Davis maximum wage bill** passes House
- Provides highest wage scale prevalent in community where public works undertaken under federal contract be paid to all mechanics
- **March 2, 1931**: **Muller** optimistic on business in 1931; as **Curb Exchange Head**, finds hope in theory of cyclical changes

### Relief Efforts and Budget Challenges (March 1931)

- **March 13, 1931**: Companies working to provide direct relief for unemployed workers and devise schemes for regularizing unemployment
- **March 19, 1931**: **$700M deficit in budget** feared; experts admit indications point to unexpected cut in income taxes
- **March 23, 1931**: Germany's budget deficit including deficits carried over totals **251,000,000 Marks**
- **March 23, 1931**: France faces 2 billion franc deficit; February public revenue down 104M francs; budget of "supplementary expenditure" up
- **March 25, 1931**: Relief for jobless sets city record—**$3,175,000** spent in February by eleven agencies alone, aiding 30,000 families

### Political Debate on Federal Taxes (March 1931)

- **March 27, 1931**: **Senators Borah and Norris** urge rise in federal taxes; deficit makes increase necessary and advocates "ability-to-pay" basis
- Breach in Republican ranks develops as party leaders incline to delay consideration until after next election
- **March 29, 1931**: Treasury faces large deficit—revenues declined and expenditures increased due to depression
- Federal Government closing accounts June 30 will write down debit side deficit of **$700M or more** if latest unofficial estimates prove correct

### Hoover Relief Initiatives (March 1931)

- **March 29, 1931**: Red Cross to hear Hoover on relief at annual meeting
- President will address organization on drought-relief situation; plan to help large numbers in stricken areas still in want at two-day convention beginning Washington April 13

### Market Instability Continues (March-April 1931)

- **March 31, 1931**: Further irregular decline in stocks with larger trading; grain markets move uncertainly
- Stock market course "plainly enough directed by professional pressure, applied on theory that company dividend reductions would have shaken financial confidence and induced actual selling"
- **April 12, 1931**: Expansion of dollar acceptances described as "without parallel in financial history"

### Monetary Developments (May 1931)

- **May 1931** (**Federal Reserve Bulletin**): Notwithstanding low and declining money rates, continued large inflow of gold from abroad
- Gold imports during first three months totaled **$100M**; proceeding at even more rapid rate after beginning of April
- Particularly noteworthy: receipt of **$19M in gold from France** in course of one week

<!-- pp. 341-360 -->

## US Depression 1930s Case Study: Timeline (August 1932 - October 1938)

### August 1932: Emergency Relief Bill
- **Emergency Relief and Construction Act** signed July 21, 1932
- Authorized **Reconstruction Finance Corporation (RFC)** to provide up to $300M to states and territories for distress relief
- Corporation to bear interest at max **3% per cent**
- Authorized loans for **self-liquidating projects**: bridges, tunnels, docks, housing in slum areas

### September-November 1932: Political Transition
- Stock gains recorded: 240 issues on exchange rise **$4,041,656,665** — sharpest advance in three years (Sep 2)
- **Bank clearings up 3.6%** in a month due to 8.4% increase in exchanges (Sep 10)
- **Governor Ritchie** (Maryland) criticized Hoover's "four major failures": farm relief, tariff relief, prohibition, balanced budget unsolved (Oct 10)
- **Senator Royal S. Copeland** (NY) attacked banking "oligarchy," claiming big banking interests were "85% liquid" while boasting cruelty (Oct 26)
- **Nevada declared 12-day bank holiday** due to low livestock prices creating state crisis (Nov 2)
- **Record voter turnout**: 46,965,230 voters registered, figure 10,166,561 above 1928 record poll (Nov 2)
- **Roosevelt wins presidency** with "bedrock margin" of 281 electoral votes vs Hoover's 15,574,474 (Nov 6)
- Roosevelt pledged effort to restore prosperity in formal statement (Nov 9)
- **Benjamin M. Anderson Jr.** (Chase National Bank economist) stated no justification for doubting US ability to maintain **gold standard** in past 36 years (Nov 11)

### November 1932: International Issues
- **Geneva world economic conference** debated Manchuria, disarmament, depression — Norman H. Davis represented Washington State Dept (Nov 23)

### December 1932: Political Tensions
- Three records set in Nov 8 elections: highest poll for nation, most votes for winner (Roosevelt: 22,314,023), Hoover got most for a loser (Dec 1)
- **Professor Felix Bernstein** (Goettingen University, German govt adviser): "world will never get out of depression unless US lowers tariff barriers" (Dec 12)
- Nevada ends bank holiday (Dec 15)

### January 1933: Currency Tensions
- **Currency demand decreased**: holiday trade ~$120M compared to $255-275M in prior years
- Decreased demand reflected **lower prices** and **continued currency hoarding**
- **$150M of gold** added to monetary stock, more than sufficient to provide funds for currency withdrawals

### February 1933: Retail Failures and Banking Crisis
- Retail failures increased from 404 to 417 (week ending Jan 26), total failures 605 vs 618 prior week (Feb 1)
- **Britain bought back $13,588,900 gold** from Federal Reserve, constituting remaining portion of $95,550,000 bullion earmarked for war-debt installment (Feb 1)
- **Gold supply declined $872,600** from Holland, offset by $3,670,000 in earmarkings (Feb 4)
- Gold supply lower by **$1,601,500 in day** (Feb 8)
- Retail failures up despite declines in other classifications: 376 store defaults vs 353 prior week, total failures 509 vs 567 (Feb 15)
- **Michigan Governor William A. Comstock** declared **8-day moratorium** for state's 550 financial institutions (Feb 15)
- **Michigan banking situation** showed improvement, but Wall Street reflected somber mood with sharp downward revision in bonds (Feb 24)
- Currency talk and news of incoming executive increased unsettlement in stock/bond markets, partly from "mischievous talk of experimenting with currency" (Feb 27)

### March 1933: State Bank Holidays and National Bank Holiday
- Banking difficulties in different states caused governors/legislatures to **close banks** or impose restrictions (Feb-early March)
- **Michigan declared bank holiday Feb 14** for public peace, health, safety, equal safeguarding of depositor rights
- Michigan holiday extended Feb 21, Maryland holiday declared within days, followed by many other states (Feb 25)
- **Congress authorized Comptroller of Currency** to exercise powers over national banks like state officials have over state banks (Feb 25)
- **President Roosevelt asked for bank legislation** in emergency message (Mar 3)
- **Nation-wide bank holiday** for banking facilities possibly for good part of week; manufacturers/wholesalers agreed to continue "business as usual," checking credits by prior customer performance (Mar 5)
- **All trading suspended** for third time in Stock Exchange history on Mar 5 due to bank holiday
- City scrip ready to replace currency (Mar 6)

### March 1933: Roosevelt's Emergency Actions
- **Roosevelt met governors** to discuss relief measures for banking (Mar 7)
- **Bank Bill enacted in record time of 71-2 hours** (Mar 10) — progress partly checked by government's administrative inability to keep pace
- **Stock Exchange planned reopening**; brokers expected brisk demand for stock (Mar 12)
- Industry ready for revival of production when banks reopen (Mar 12)
- **Business reopened as usual** on Stock Exchange on Mar 15; banks continued to resume; security/commodity markets started operating except Chicago Board of Trade and NY Cotton Exchange
- **798 banks in state reopened in full**; end of national holiday finds 80% licensed, most others merely delayed; all savings banks open; checking privileges on nation-wide basis (Mar 16)

### March-April 1933: Business Resumption
- Banks over nation approach normal; reopenings continue in all states as authorities speed restoration (Mar 16)
- **Volume of industrial production increased in January** by less than usual seasonal amount; factory employment and pay rolls continued to decline; commodity prices showed relatively little change in first three weeks of February (Mar 1933)
- Business in latter part of Feb and first half of March largely influenced by **banking crisis**, culminating in **proclamation of national banking holiday by President on Mar 6**
- Production and distribution of commodities declined substantially during this period, but showed some increase after banking operations resumed mid-March (Apr 1933)

### April-May 1933: Roosevelt's Program
- Democrats in House expect to catch up with **Roosevelt's program** before end of next week (Apr 22)
- **Roosevelt ended stalemate over bank bill**; asked **$10,000 limit on deposit guarantee** (Apr 25)
- Roosevelt to seek power to cut debts; will ask Congress for authority to deal individually with debtor nations to reduce war debts (Apr 30)
- **Colossal program of public works** — White House conference drafted bill totaling **$2B/year** (Apr 30)
- **$1.25B sought for construction** financed by RFC advances, urged upon Roosevelt by American Construction Council (Roosevelt was president 1922-1929) (May 1)
- **2-month record set by Roosevelt**: third month begins with major problems solved, others yielding; 14,000 banks reopened (May 5)
- **Recovery measure before Roosevelt** in night council; will study proposal including re-employment tax (May 14)
- **Roosevelt signs Securities Bill**; president hails new law as step to "old-fashioned standards" (May 28)

### June-July 1933: Recovery Developments
- **Industrial activity increased considerably** during April and first 3 weeks of May; wholesale prices advanced, particularly in latter part of April and early May
- Following imposition of **embargo on gold Apr 20**, exchange value of dollar declined; on May 20 was **87% of gold parity** (Jun 1933)
- Cities urged to push public works plans (Jun 5)
- **$3M construction fund** discussed by Cabinet board headed by Secretary Ickes (Jun 23)
- **Recovery program rounds into shape**: swift accord on first code reached in spirit; rate in trade annals — two policies emerge: **40-hour week, $12 pay not model**, and mass-hiring of men will not be forced (Jul 3)
- **Lake states see signs of revival**; Wisconsin finds marked decline in unemployment dependency (Jul 9)

### 1934: Steel Production Recovery
- **Steel production rose** from year-end dip: **34.2% of capacity** reported by American Iron and Steel Institute (Jan 21, 1934) — rise of 3.5 points (11%) over prior week; equaled rate for week ended Dec 23, highest since late Oct
- **Steel production forecast 47.4% capacity** for last week — estimates indicated production close to 50%; highest since series began last Oct, except week ended Mar 10 at 47.7% (Apr 16, 1934)

### November 1934 - January 1935: Financial Recovery
- **Financial markets reach new high levels** for present recovery; stocks continued to reflect returning confidence; domestic bonds also showed improvement (Nov 27, 1934)
- **Secretary Roper** cited spurt in business lines in annual report as Commerce head; past fiscal year saw definite improvement (Dec 24, 1934)
- **Banks' funds rise to new high level**: seasonal currency return from circulation, coupled with Treasury fund disbursement and continued rise in monetary gold stocks, pushed excess reserves of Federal Reserve member banks to **~$1,990M** on Wednesday (Jan 11, 1935)

### April-October 1935: Deposit Growth
- **Deposits in banks now $50B**: Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation reported deposits at end of Dec estimated at close to $50B — gain of over $3B in six months (Apr 7, 1935)
- **Cash circulation $135M higher**: retail trade and payrolls expanded more than seasonally; currency in circulation Jan 23 to Apr 24 showed net increase of $110M (May 23, 1935)
- **Gold stocks in US expand $2B**: gold stock increased more than $2B (~30%) since revaluation at end of Jan 1934 (Jul 2, 1935)
- **Roosevelt signs new banking law** — Omnibus Banking Bill became law Aug 24, marking new program of credit control by government and revision by Federal Reserve Board (Aug 24, 1935)
- **Increased deposits reported by banks** at end of September showed gains in deposits/resources to highest marks in institutional history (Oct 2, 1935)
- **Assets of trusts increase sharply**: nine months ended Sep 30 showed sharp increase in net assets from rise in market value of portfolios (Oct 2, 1935)

### November-December 1935: Excess Reserves
- **Excess funds set record for banks**: excess reserves of Federal Reserve member banks surpassed **$3B** for first time; weekly report showed member-bank reserve balances at **$5,653M**, up $78M last week; **$3,010M was in excess** of legal requirements vs $2,930M prior week (Nov 1, 1935)
- **Federal Reserve Advisory Council** urged Reserve Banks allow some short-term govt securities to run off at maturity, reducing excess bank reserves and threat of credit inflation (Nov 30, 1935)
- **Volume of industrial production and factory employment** increased in Oct, reflecting chiefly resumption of activity at textile mills; wholesale commodity prices advanced in first half of Nov after declining Sep/Oct (Dec 1935)
- **Board of Governors** and Federal Open Market Committee opposed immediate action to reduce excess reserve but stated situation would be watched carefully; appropriate action taken if credit expansion threatened public interest (Dec 19, 1935)
- **Joint statement** indicating no immediate action to reduce excess bank reserves was regarded in Wall Street as most important pronouncement of Federal Reserve policy in years (Dec 22, 1935)

### January-February 1936: Margin Requirements and Gold Flow
- **Raising of margins viewed as gesture**: Board of Governors action on Friday raising **margin requirements effective Feb 1** designed primarily to check spread of inflationary psychology (Jan 26, 1936)
- **New Reserve Body takes reins**: newly organized Federal Reserve Board will assume office officially tomorrow; Banking Act of 1935 provided it take office Feb 1 (Feb 1, 1936)
- President Roosevelt noted **reversal of gold flow** to US at press conference; declined extended comment but remarked export shipments were doing country no harm (Feb 5, 1936)
- **Dollar declines on inflation move**: quotations on foreign exchange market experienced abrupt reversal as fresh wave of inflation talk cropped out in Washington and Wall Street; Representative Patman (Texas) filed petition to pay veterans' bonus in currency (Feb 15, 1936)
- **Federal Reserve Board emphasized margins as brake** on speculative stock demands: reserve bank body intimated no need to fear runaway speculative market so long as margin instrument available (Feb 22, 1936)

### May-September 1936: Capital Flight
- **Flight of capital discerned in Paris**: money market exists for day-to-day loans at 4%; longer-term loans given only by Bank of France; bank's return for Apr 24 showed new increase of almost **400M francs** in bills discounted, while total loans on securities and govt bonds declined only 100M francs (May 4, 1936)
- **Flight of capital depresses franc**: under pressure of continued capital flight, franc and other gold-bloc currencies fell further; French currency declined to **6.58 7-16 cents** (1-16 point of effective gold-shipping price), closed at 6.58 1/2 cents, off 11-16 point; Guilders dropped 2 points to 67.59 cents; Swiss francs off 2 points to 32.35 cents (May 19, 1936)

### August 1936 - February 1937: Market Strength and Reserve Changes
- **Financial markets active and strong**; stock market extended strong advance of Friday and closed at best levels of week (Aug 9, 1936)
- **Franc cut to match dollar and sterling**: French yield at last to economic and budgetary pressure; other nations expected to follow Washington and London to aid (Sep 27, 1936)
- **Swiss to devalue about 30% today**: Swiss Government plans to ask Parliament to make devaluation of Swiss franc about 30% (Sep 28, 1936)
- **"Hot money" magnitude revealed**: US Treasury made public record of flow of foreign money into country since beginning of 1935 (Nov 29, 1936)
- **US Treasury's sterilizing action** considered a sound policy: decision to sterilize gold imports caused no surprise in financial circles; regarded as step toward giving active expression to official determination to check unwanted credit inflation or unrestrained stock market boom (Dec 26, 1936)
- **Gains in industry reach new peaks** for recovery period; figures for Dec highest (Jan 29, 1937)
- **Board announced increase in reserve requirements** of member banks on Jan 30: reserve requirements for member banks increased by **33 1/2%** as follows: reserve cities from 19 1/2 to 26%; banks in reserve cities from 15 to 20%; "country" banks from 10 1/2 to 14%; time deposits at all banks from 4 1/2 to 6% (Feb 1937)

### April-December 1937: Economic Downturn
- **Volume of production, employment, trade increased** more than seasonally in Feb; wholesale prices of industrial commodities continued to advance (Apr 1937)
- **Secretary Morgenthau** sought "orderly" market: Federal Reserve Board and US Treasury have ample funds to keep bond market orderly; money flowed in/out of Treasury all the time and sufficient for that purpose (Apr 2, 1937)
- Roosevelt hopes to get **$100M from tax evaders** (May 30, 1937)
- Roosevelt asks Congress to curb big tax evaders; cited **eight tricks** used by very rich individuals to save millions in public revenues and meet challenge to "decency of American morals" (Jun 2, 1937)
- **Leading stocks down 1 to 7 points**; treasury bonds strong — dollar easier — wheat declines (Nov 20, 1937)
- **Federal deficit reduced**; now below **$695,245,000** estimate of President on Oct 19 (Dec 17, 1937)
- **Total deposits at weekly reporting member banks** continued to decrease in Apr/May, reflecting declines in bankers' balances and US Govt deposits; other deposits increased slightly in following weeks; sales of securities by banks most important factor accounting for decrease in deposits in recent months; member bank holdings of US Govt obligations continued to decline at NYC banks during Apr/May, but decline was less rapid than earlier months; holdings of other reporting banks showed little change; commercial loans by banks increased further, though after first week of Apr rapid growth of prior weeks slackened (Jun 1937)

### October 1937 - April 1938: Federal Reserve Actions
- **Federal Open Market Committee** adopted program of supplying member banks with additional reserve funds to meet seasonal currency/credit demands on Sep 11-12; Committee authorized Executive Committee to purchase in open market from time to time sufficient amounts of short-term US Govt obligations to provide funds to meet seasonal withdrawals of currency from banks and other seasonal requirements; reduction of additional holdings contemplated when seasonal influences reversed or other circumstances made retention unnecessary (Oct 1937)
- **Billion deficit for 1938 forecast**: on eve of sending annual budget to Congress, well-informed officials predicted it would indicate **$1B deficit**; latest official estimate of prospective deficit for current year was $895,245,000; officials indicated message tomorrow would revise figure upward (Jan 5, 1938)
- **Federal Reserve Board** reduced reserve requirements on all classes of deposits of all member banks as part of govt's program for encouragement of business recovery, effective at opening of business tomorrow (Apr 16, 1938) — virtually same schedule as before May 1, 1937
- **Stocks up irregularly** in increased trading; bonds firm — dollar higher — wheat, cotton steady (Oct 16, 1938) — demand for low-priced stocks, especially public utility issues, continued to feature stock market; market as a whole closed irregularly higher; day's business on Stock Exchange reached **1,995,000 shares**, heaviest volume since Oct 19

<!-- pp. 361-380 -->

## US Debt Crisis and Adjustment (2007-2011)

### Introduction: Framework and Approach

This section provides a detailed account of the **2007-2011 US debt crisis**, written with reference to the **Archetypal Big Debt Cycle template**. Key connections to watch:

- **Pooling and securitization of mortgages** = new ways of providing leverage outside regulatory protection
- **Levering up of investment banks** = unregulated leverage expansion
- **Rapid growth of derivatives** traded off regulated exchanges = shadow banking system

> "Please note how well the particulars of this case fit with the generalizations described in the template. For example, when you read about the pooling and securitization of mortgages, the levering up of investment banks, and the rapid growth of derivatives that were traded off of regulated exchanges, see these as new ways of providing leverage outside the protection and regulation of authorities."

**Author's pedagogical goal**: convey the day-by-day experience to help readers think through decisions as both investors and policymakers. The narrative follows a week-by-week (sometimes day-by-day) timeline with contemporary news articles and Bridgewater Daily Observations excerpts.

**Key principle**: Investment positions are proprietary and not described, but the thinking process is fully shared.

---

## The Emerging Bubble: 2004-2006

### The Goldilocks Economy

**Economic context (2004-2006)**:
- Growth: steady 3-4%
- Unemployment: below long-term average at 4-5%
- Inflation: mostly 2-3.5% (higher than ideal but not worrisome)
- **Debt/GDP growth**: 12.6% annually
- Fed raised rates gradually from 1% (2004) to just over 5% (2006)

> "The economy appeared to most people as though it was in a 'Goldilocks' period—not too hot and not too cold."

**Classic bubble dynamics**: Central banks focus on inflation and growth (not a problem) while ignoring debt-financed asset purchases.

**Why bubbles emerge undetected**:
- Often hidden beneath aggregate averages
- Concentrated in specific markets (housing in this case)
- Require **pro forma financial stress tests** to identify vulnerabilities and knock-on effects

---

### The Housing Market Debt Bubble

**Price acceleration**:
- 2004-2006: home prices increased ~30%
- Since 2000: >80% total increase
- **Fastest pace in a century** (except immediate post-WWII period)

**Leverage magnification effect**: Home price gains have **magnified impacts on equity values**
- Example: $50,000 down payment on $250,000 house → house rises to $350,000 → **investment tripled**
- Enabled more borrowing, attracted new buyers and lenders (highly profitable)

**Debt explosion**:
- Household debt rose from 85% of disposable income (2000) to ~120% (2006)
- **Bottom quintile of income earners** showed biggest percentage increase in debt (2001-2007)
- Home equity loans and cash-out refinancing: **$500 billion in 2005** (5x the 1998 level)
- Total US debt exceeded **300% of GDP**

**Self-reinforcing dynamics**: Rising prices → lowered credit standards → more lending/buying → even higher prices

> "The more prices went up, the more credit standards were lowered (even though it would have been logical for the opposite to happen), but both lenders and borrowers found lending and buying houses on borrowed money to be very profitable."

---

### Classic Bubble Characteristics

**Seven defining features** (all present in US housing 2004-2006):

1. **Prices high relative to traditional measures**
2. **Prices discounting future rapid appreciation** from already-high levels
3. **Broad bullish sentiment**
4. **Purchases financed by high leverage**
5. **Extended forward purchases** (built inventory, contracted purchases) to speculate or protect against future gains
6. **New buyers** (previously not in market) have entered
7. **Stimulative monetary policy** helps inflate bubble; **tight policy** contributes to popping

**Speculative behavior**:
- "Home flippers" buying for short-term profit
- New single-family home construction **doubled between 1995 and 2005**
- Peak: just shy of **8% of households buying a home each year** (50% more than today)
- TIME magazine (summer 2005): "Will Your House Make You Rich?"

**Supply-demand irrationality**: Higher prices encouraged production AND leveraged buying—precisely the opposite of what logic would dictate.

> "Logic should dictate precisely the opposite behavior: those betting on price changes ought to be more inclined to deleverage or sell, and those who lend to them should be more cautious when these things are happening. However, this sort of nonsensical thinking is typical in bubbles."

---

### Mortgage Market Froth

**Interest rates at historic lows**:
- Fell to lows in 2003 not seen since the 1950s
- Stayed near those lows well into the housing bubble
- **Leveraging accelerated 2003-2007** even as rates rose ~1.5% (2005-2007)

**Loan-to-value ratio on new housing loans**: fast increase to **80%**
- Higher numbers = smaller down-payments, larger loans
- Banks **more eager to loan, willing to make riskier bets**

**Signs of lending froth**:
- Banks often didn't require **proof of income**
- **Adjustable rate mortgages** with low "teaser" rates now, higher rates later
- **"Subprime" mortgages** (riskier loans) became 20% of market
- **Securitization** packaged debt in ways that obscured underlying risks
- Low interest rates fueled easy credit availability

---

### Fed Policy Mistake: Focus on CPI, Not Debt

Despite massive debt build-up and frenzied housing activity, **economy didn't overheat and inflation remained moderate** → Fed remained unconcerned.

**Critical insight**: The worst debt bubbles (US 1929, Japan 1989) are **not accompanied by high/rising goods and services inflation**, but by **asset price inflation financed by debt growth**.

> "It is typically the case that the worst debt bubbles (e.g., the US in 1929, Japan in 1989) are not accompanied by high and rising goods and services inflation, but by asset price inflation financed by debt growth. Typically, central banks make the mistake of accommodating the debt growth because they are focused on goods and services inflation (as measured by the CPI) and/or growth. They are not focused on debt growth, which is what they are creating, and on whether the debts will produce the incomes to service them."

**Real interest rates**:
- US short-term rates **below inflation** (negative real borrowing costs) from late 2001 to early 2006
- Even when Fed raised short-term rates (mid-2004), **long-term nominal rates remained flat**, real long-term rates **declined**
- Same pattern broadly true across developed world

---

### Paulson's Concerns and the Working Group

**Mid-2006**: **Hank Paulson** confirmed as Treasury Secretary (from Goldman Sachs CEO)
- Market exposure made him concerned about financial excesses
- Convened regular meetings of **President's Working Group on Financial Markets**
- Members: top Bush economic team and key regulators
- **Primary benefit**: built close working relationships among Paulson, **Ben Bernanke** (Fed Chairman), **Tim Geithner** (NY Fed President)

**Key personalities**:
- **Paulson**: extroverted former CEO, bold decision-maker
- **Bernanke**: introverted economist, well-schooled in Great Depression
- **Geithner**: practical operator experienced in government policy
- **Complementary qualities** + hourly coordination + willingness to be bold and evolve quickly = critical to navigating crisis

**What they saw (2006)**:
- Concerned about "dry tinder and gathering storm"
- Tried to lean against perceived excesses
- Noted excesses in **subprime market**
- **Did NOT see** subprime spilling over to overall housing market (which hadn't seen nationwide decline since WWII)
- **Paulson very concerned** about **Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac** (GSEs) risks—also highlighted by **Larry Summers**

---

### Summary: The Bubble Inflates

**Economic surface**: appears healthy (Goldilocks)
**Underneath**: classic debt bubble dynamics
- **Home prices** rising 30% (2004-2006), 80%+ since 2000
- **Household debt** from 85% to 120% of disposable income
- **Total debt** >300% of GDP
- **Credit standards** deteriorating as prices rise
- **Speculation** rampant (flippers, new construction doubling)
- **Fed accommodation** via low rates despite debt explosion
- **Securitization** obscuring risk

**Policy blind spot**: Fed watching CPI and growth, not debt growth or asset price inflation—**classic central bank error** that allows worst bubbles to form.

<!-- pp. 381-400 -->

## The Emerging Broader-Based Bubble

### Economic Imbalances Beyond Housing

- Savings rates declined from low to lower; US aggressively imported capital from abroad
- Manufacturing employment fell sharply as US lost global export market share to emerging economies (especially **China**)
- Housing activity camouflaged structural weakness: construction employment rose ~50% vs. 1995, driven by debt-financed building

### Debt Growth as Red Flag

> **High debt growth to fund consumption rather than investment is a red flag, since consumption doesn't produce an income, while investment might.**

- Typical bubble pattern: foreign capital poured in (current account deficit swelled to 6% of GDP)
- Money came from emerging economies like China with huge surpluses, choosing to save/invest in US assets
- Strong capital inflows enabled US citizens to borrow and consume beyond their earnings
- Foreign demand for US assets kept long-term borrowing costs low even as Fed raised short-term rates (late 2004)

### The Shadow Banking System

**Shadow banking** = growth of lending outside the regulated banking system, a common feature of bubble periods

Five key components fueled leveraging outside traditional banking:

1. **Repo agreements and commercial paper** - huge channels for short-term borrowing by banks and corporations. **Ben Bernanke** notes repo liabilities of US broker dealers increased 2.5x in four years before crisis
2. **Large institutional depositors outside protected banking system** - demand for Treasury securities (especially from foreign investors) outstripped supply, creating shortage of safe assets. Led to demand for substitutes like asset-backed commercial paper and repo
3. **Money market funds** - short-term savings vehicle promising higher returns than bank accounts without much additional risk
4. **Globalization of dollar lending** - explosion of dollar borrowing and lending outside US banks
5. **Securitization of lending** - banks originate loans (auto, home, etc.) and sell them to investors. Creates **moral hazard**: banks incentivized to make risky loans since they can sell them and not bear consequences (as long as investors remain willing to buy)

### Regulatory Failure

- US regulatory system didn't keep pace with shadow banking developments
- No adequate visibility into shadow banks and markets
- Authorities lacked powers to curb excesses (though this wasn't apparent initially)
- Banks and shadow banks inadequately capitalized and over-leveraged—little cushion, exposed to solvency problems in downturn
- 1990s-2000s era: regulators concerned about US financial industry staying competitive with London, discouraging tighter reins

**Critical point**: If debt boom had been financed by traditional banking system, crisis would have been easier to manage and contain. FDIC's systemic risk exemption powers + deposit insurance + Fed's discount window would have had more power and reach. Less forced selling, less dangerous margin spiral.

### The Bubble's Drivers

Not just low interest rates—combination of:
- Easy money
- Lax regulation
- Risky financial innovations
