Fed focused on inflation (not debt growth) when setting rates. Policy makers allowed lax regulation of shadow lending to continue.

### Asset/Liability Mismatches

**Classic ingredient of severe debt crisis**: borrowers/lenders had severe asset/liability mismatches, leaving them vulnerable in downturn

Three common forms:

1. **Borrowing short-term and lending long-term** - squeezed when short-term lenders refuse to roll over or demand much higher rates than earnings on long-term loans already made
2. **Lending to risky borrowers at higher rates to collect credit spread**—until default rates exceed the spread
3. **Borrowing in one currency, lending/investing in another** - when borrowed currency rises, forces repayment at higher exchange rate or interest rate than manageable

All three occurred during this bubble, making financial intermediaries and their investors highly vulnerable to runs and credit problems.

**Example**: European banks actively borrowed dollars short-term, then lent globally. When dollar credit tightened (summer 2007), these banks lost US money market access and became contagion transmitters worldwide.

### The Top: 2007

**First Half of 2007**

- Markets and economy doing great: stocks hitting new highs, strong job market, strong retail sales, strong consumer sentiment
- Housing market and aggressive financers showing cracks
- **SEC** memo (January 4): "business model of many smaller subprime originators is no longer viable" after refinancing and real estate booms ended
- Markets tranquil Feb-March; credit spreads relatively low vs. historical norms

**Subprime Problems Grow**

- Subprime lenders facing considerable losses but not yet affecting broader economy/markets
- Bigger banks reporting rising bad mortgage debts

**(BDO) March 13: Subprime Mortgage Fallout**

> **Over the last few years, investment banks have been hard at work creating fancy new products where they can package up a bunch of assets and sell the package for more than the sum of the parts** (CDOs, CMOs, synthetic CDOs, etc.)

- Tranching creates AAA-rated bonds from lower-quality assets
- **This innovation opened credit to risky borrowers (not just households) who previously had trouble accessing credit markets**
- Explosion in subprime mortgages closely related to this new credit source
- Originators paid less attention to underwriting—just handing off to investment banks for packaging and resale
- Lending standards slipped to absurdly low levels
- Small uptick in delinquencies → banks refused to buy loans → originators stuck with losses

**Early payment defaults** hammered subprime lenders. Agreements required lenders to buy back loans if borrowers missed first few payments. **New Century** (second biggest subprime lender, 2006): 2.5% of loans had first payment defaults. Couldn't come up with cash for buybacks. Dozens of smaller lenders went bust; New Century was the biggest.

**Bernanke** (March 28 Congressional testimony): "impact on broader economy and financial markets of problems in subprime market seems likely to be contained." Dalio had similar assessment initially, though more concerned about leverage and tightening.

**Stock Rally Continues**

- US stocks rallied through April-May, hitting new records
- Mid-June: 10-year Treasury yields hit 5.3% (highest since 2002)
- Mid-July: 90-day T-bill rate hit 5%, very flat yield curve—**cyclical peak**

### Interest Rate Mechanics

**Rising rates increase debt service on both new and existing variable-rate debt**:
- Discourages additional borrowing (credit becomes expensive)
- Reduces disposable income (more spent on debt service)
- Spending slows
- One person's spending = another's income
- Incomes drop
- **When people spend less, prices go down, and economic activity decreases**

**Simultaneously**: rising short-term rates + flattening/inverting yield curve → liquidity declines. Short duration assets (cash) become relatively more attractive vs. longer duration assets (bonds, equities, real estate) and lower-grade credit. **Money moves out of financial assets, causing them to fall in value. Declining asset prices create negative wealth effects, feeding back into economy through declining spending and incomes.**

### The Tightening Pops the Bubble

Rising rates → home prices began declining (higher debt service on new homes, rising payments on existing ARMs for subprime borrowers)

June: tightening pressures flowed through to first broad financial distress sign—rising foreclosures and delinquencies translating to meaningful losses for bigger banks

**Bear Stearns Hedge Funds Crisis** (mid-June):
- Two hedge funds holding subprime mortgage-backed securities (MBS), one leveraged ~20:1, faced growing losses and investor redemption wave
- Required fire-sale of $3.6 billion in securities (large amount for the market)
- Leveraged financial buying shifted to deleveraging selling
- As securities prices fell, hedge funds faced huge losses and forced liquidations
- Bear Stearns eventually promised $3.2 billion loan (later reduced to $1.6 billion) to bail out one fund
- Other banks that seized collateral cooperated to keep market stable (not selling more subprime MBS)
- Funds eventually wiped out
- Small funds, limited initial reverberations

### Summer 2007

Economic growth remained healthy; equity markets hit new highs mid-July. Key question: would Fed tighten (inflation concerns) or ease (housing concerns)?

Stress in housing market gradually building:
- Subprime MBS indices (**ABX**) saw big price declines (even AAA bonds fell ~5%)
- Mortgage lenders reporting increasing missed payments
- One large lender nearing bankruptcy
- Small German bank exposed to mortgage loans faced big losses, acquired by Germany's state bank
- New home sales falling very quickly

Markets sold off (July down 6% from peak)

### Self-Reinforcing Debt Crisis Dynamics

**Mark-to-market accounting + high leverage** made crisis self-reinforcing:

> **Debt crises and downturns are self-reinforcing behaviors because as losses occur, both lenders and borrowers are less able to lend and borrow, which worsens conditions. For example, when losses occur, one's capital declines, and because there are limits to how much one can hold in assets relative to one's capital, that means assets have to be sold, or the buying of assets has to be curtailed. That in turn makes asset prices and lending weaker, producing more losses and reinforcing the cycle further.**

Dalio's team ran detailed mark-to-market stress tests showing financial sector and dependents were incurring losses before reporting them. Pro forma financial projections showed many companies facing debt squeezes.

### Dalio's Warning (July 26, 2007)

**(BDO) July 26: Is This the Big One?**

- Crazy lending and leveraging creating pervasive fragility
- Interest rates will rise until financial system cracks
- Everything will reverse: move from greed to fear, volatilities increase, carry and credit spreads blow out
- No idea exactly when this will occur or if current situation is "the big one"
- Want to avoid/fade this lunacy
- **No one knows how financial market contagion will play out**

**Study of market player positions** (especially via derivatives markets):
- Read government/intermediary studies, gathered all available data, examined 10-K reports
- **Conclusion: no one has a clue—can only vaguely examine exposures one level deep**
- Easy to see some parties' exposures (regulated intermediaries), impossible to see who's carrying positions to ascertain net positions
- Dealers at epicenter know their counterparties but not counterparties' total positions
- **These exposures grown rapidly (~4x larger than five years ago), huge (~$400 trillion)**

### Fed's Complacency

Growth still looked good; debt and tightening hadn't yet passed through to economy (July 31). Fed extremely concerning in its sanguinity.

Fed statement (August 7): "Financial markets have been volatile in recent weeks, credit conditions have become tighter for some households and businesses, and the housing correction is ongoing. Nevertheless, the economy seems likely to continue to expand at a moderate pace over coming quarters, supported by solid growth in employment and incomes and a robust global economy."

### August 2007: Serious Unraveling

- August 9: **BNP Paribas** (France's largest bank, one of world's largest) froze $2.2 billion in three funds due to US subprime exposure
- European banks nervous about lending to each other
- **European Central Bank (ECB)** injected 95 billion euros to get rates back to target, another 61 billion next day
- US saw squeeze in safe Treasury bills, higher yields on riskier commercial paper and interbank lending
- **Money market funds** (main holders of asset-backed commercial paper) saw asset value hits, required assistance from sponsors/banks/fund families to avoid "breaking the buck"

**TED spread** (interbank stress measure) spiked—clear signal the top of debt cycle was being made.

**(BDO) August 10: This Is the Big One**

- Financial market unraveling we've been expecting
- Unwinding of widely held, irresponsibly created positions from financial middlemen investing immense liquidity flooding system
- Like 1998 or 1994, just bigger
- Less certain about specifics than what we don't know—how cards will fall depends on who's holding what and how they knock on with each other
- Despite extensive work mapping this over two years, couldn't map to worthwhile extent—knowledge of positions too imprecise, array of permutations too wide
- **Forecasting weeks ahead like predicting hurricane's course two weeks ahead**
- Confident regulators (with best windows) also can't forecast reliably, reacting to events
- Seen this self-reinforcing dynamic before (1998 most recent)
- Will run through system with hurricane speed (next 4-6 months), leaving weaker credits dead/damaged, stronger ones in catbird seat

**"Depression gauge"**: simple algorithm based on proximity of interest rates to 0%, debt vulnerability measures, beginning of debt deleveraging. Used to trigger portfolio/risk control changes including counterparty risks.

### Countrywide Crisis

Week later: **Countrywide** (US's largest mortgage issuer) exhausted credit line, at risk of bankruptcy. Notable canary in coal mine but not systemically critical.

Central banks responded with liquidity. Fed surprisingly cut rates 0.5% between regular meetings (unusual move). **Bernanke** said he'd do more if needed. **Bank of America** invested $2 billion in Countrywide for large stake. Moves alleviated funding strains; equities recovered. Stock market still near highs.

### Policy Makers' View

Most thought risky mortgage market problems would be contained—flow-through to real economy wouldn't be substantial. Dalio saw differently: "day of reckoning will be pushed forward, probably to when there is a big tightening by Fed or big turndown in economy."

### Why Investors Were Misled: VAR Problem

**Value at Risk (VAR)** = measure of recent volatility in markets/portfolios, commonly used to determine likely magnitude and occurrence of losses

- Uses recent volatility as main input for position sizing
- Simplified example: investor never wants to lose more than 20%. If subprime mortgage's worst monthly loss ever was 5%, models might say "safe" to borrow until they own 3x leveraged subprime

> **Investors looked at recent volatility in their VAR calculations, and by and large expected it to continue moving forward. This is human nature and it was dumb because past volatility and past correlations aren't reliable forecasts of future risks.**

Very profitable approach—clients urged Dalio to increase positions because VAR was low. Dalio refused.

**Extrapolating current conditions forward = bad approach vs. considering true range of possibilities**. Should bet on opposite of what happened lately.


<!-- pp. 401-420 -->

### VAR and Leverage Dynamics

- **Low volatility and benign VAR estimates encourage increased leverage** — at the time, some leverage ratios neared 100:1
- Leverage is a better indicator of future volatility than VAR itself
- In 2007, banks and investors heavily exposed to subprime mortgages — instruments had not experienced a loss cycle or much volatility
- **VAR was self-reinforcing on the downside**: increased market volatility at 2008 crisis peak made statistical riskiness look even higher, triggering more selling

## The Fall of 2007

### Policy Responses to Mortgage Crisis

- Fall 2006: **Paulson** and Treasury began working with **Barney Frank** and House Financial Services Committee to reform **Fannie and Freddie**
  - Focus: curbing excesses, increasing regulator authority
  - Bill passed House spring 2007, stalled in Senate
- No federal funding available to modify mortgages for struggling homeowners due to political opposition
- Bush Treasury worked with lenders, mortgage servicers, counselors to motivate private modifications with "modest but meaningful success"
- Treasury and Fed jointly developed "break the glass" option — authority to purchase illiquid mortgage securities if politically feasible
  - This became forerunner of **TARP (Troubled Asset Relief Program)**

### Fed's Monetary Policy Considerations

- Fed signaled willingness to ease monetary policy to mitigate spillover effects from mortgage stress
- Most market participants believed policy makers could manage the crisis smoothly despite deteriorating fundamentals
- **Bernanke** pushed "blue-sky thinking" (unrestricted brainstorming) with **Hank Paulson** and Treasury staff
  - **As financial contagion spreads beyond banking sector, increasing numbers in real economy lose credit access through usual channels**
  - **Bernanke considered Fed lending directly to broader range of counterparties beyond depository institutions**
  - Found authority in **Section 13(3) of Federal Reserve Act** — unused since Great Depression but still valid

> "Knowing which needed actions in a crisis are permitted (or not permitted) by law and how to have them approved is a classic challenge in democracies with rigid regulations and robust checks and balances systems."

### Rate Cut Debate: Two Tensions

1. **Inflation concerns**: Dollar weakening, oil prices rising — easing would contribute to dollar weakness, higher oil prices, higher inflation
2. **Moral hazard**: Problems stemmed from speculation — ameliorating speculators' problems would encourage excessive future risk-taking

### The Moral Hazard Question

> "How the 'moral hazard' question is dealt with during big debt crises is one of the biggest determinants of how these crises turn out."

- Natural to want those responsible to experience consequences and impose discipline
- **But analogy: putting someone on diet and treadmill after heart attack**
- **Above all else, most important thing is to provide life-blood (stimulants) to keep systemically important parts alive**
- Dangerous to be overly precise in balancing: (a) letting borrowers/lenders experience consequences vs. (b) providing judicious liquidity to rectify severity of contraction
- **Better to err on providing too much than too little**
- Unlike Great Depression (when Fed let banks fail en masse), Fed's priority was saving the economy while minimizing moral hazard when possible

**Tim Geithner** (NY Fed president) shared this view:
- Moral hazard framework = wrong way to think about crisis policy
- Policy must be aggressive in removing catastrophic risk; can't move slowly or precisely
- Providing liquidity during liquidity crisis leaves government open to less risk and leaves system healthier
- Paradox: if you let things burn, government ends up taking on all risk through costly nationalization

**Geithner's outcome data**: Instead of losing 5-10% of GDP on financial rescue, US earned ~2% of GDP — "dramatic outlier in history of financial crises" due to Fed and Treasury's aggressive response and willingness to set moral hazard concerns aside

### September 18, 2007 Rate Cut

- Fed cut rates 0.5% (vs. 0.25% market expectation)
- Bernanke: "the hawks and doves flocked together"
- Sparked "ecstatic" stock rally (*New York Times*) — S&P 500 returned to within 2% of all-time high
- **Message**: Fed willing to take decisive action to contain August turmoil
- **Reality**: **easing wouldn't solve fundamental problems — financial intermediaries, debtors, creditors holding more debt assets and liabilities than could be serviced**

### Bank Balance Sheet Problems

**Asset side**: banks owned subprime mortgages through securitizations

**Liability side**: banks dependent on risky short-term funding sources
- Historically relied on deposits (controllable with guarantees)
- **FDIC** (founded 1933) insured deposits, eliminating depositor flight incentives
- **Modern "short-term wholesale funding"** = uninsured deposits — depositors had big incentive to pull at first sign of trouble

### The Securitization Machine

> "At its heart, the securitization machine started with the issuance of risky mortgages and ended with the sale of very safe bonds to institutional investors."

- Mortgage lender makes loans → sells to bank → bank packages into bundles of 1,000 loans
- Diversification theory: combined cash flows safer than individual loans (one default wouldn't affect other 999 borrowers)
- Bank slices cash flows into tranches:
  - 70-80%: super-safe AAA-rated bonds
  - 10-15%: slightly riskier AA-rated bonds
  - 5-10%: BBB-rated bonds
  - Small unrated residual ("first-loss piece") absorbs first defaults

> "This was a classic case of data-mining history rather than using sound logic to assess risk. People were leveraging themselves up by betting that they were safe, because the thing they were betting against had never happened before."

- When bet went wrong, self-reinforcing upside dynamic shifted to self-reinforcing downside dynamic
- Banks retained first-loss piece, carried bonds as inventory
- Worked until it didn't — encouraged more of the same until demand dried up Q3 2007

### Fall 2007 Write-Downs

**Bear Stearns**: 3Q07 earnings dropped 61% (hedge fund losses, subprime exposure)
**Morgan Stanley & Lehman Brothers**: 7% and 3% earnings hits respectively
**Citigroup, UBS, Merrill Lynch**: meaningful but manageable losses
- **Citigroup's initial write-down**: $5.9 billion (note for comparison to later figures)

### Bridgewater's Loss Estimates (Fall 2007)

- Ran "stress tests" — gathered bank balance sheet data, applied liquid market prices to illiquid holdings
- Anticipated unreported losses before they materialized
- **October 9, 2007**: S&P 500 closed at all-time high — wouldn't be reached again until 2013

### Banks Propose Joint Fund (Abandoned)

- Major banks proposed $75-100 billion fund to buy distressed subprime securities
- Viewed as natural response to credit crunch, would help alleviate contagion risk
- By year end: effort abandoned — banks decided "not needed at this time"

### Crisis Spreads to Europe

**Two mechanisms**:

1. **Short-term wholesale funding reliance**: **Northern Rock** (British bank) relied on money markets → funding dried up summer 2007 → classic "run" (depositors lining up three straight days mid-September to withdraw)
   - UK deposit insurance cap: £35,000 (lower than US)
   - British government guaranteed all Northern Rock deposits

2. **Subprime securitization investments**: Large European banks (**UBS, Deutsche Bank**) owned stakes as corollary to producing securitizations; smaller banks wanted exposure to AAA-rated securities
   - **AAA subprime securities had 0% default rate** historically (S&L crisis 1980s, dot-com bubble early 2000s per **Standard & Poor's**)
   - Offered premium vs. corporate bonds of same rating (hindsight: premium far too small for risk)

- Bridgewater shifted exposures from riskier counterparties to safer ones, moved into safer assets

### Late October 2007: Sentiment Turns

- Predictions of overall subprime losses increasing
- **October 19**: US stocks fell 2.6% after **JPMorgan** $2 billion write-down and **Bank of America** weak earnings
- Losses becoming larger than expected for banks, but not yet clear how severely housing stress would hit US households (consumption ~70% of GDP)

#### (BDO) October 30: Falling Home Prices and Wealth

- Weakening housing affects economy multiple ways: falling construction, falling housing-related spending, less cash from mortgage borrowing for non-housing consumption, falling wealth
- Financing alone (money borrowed against houses) = over 3% at peak, will likely be negative soon
- Construction declining 20% annual pace → ~1% drag on real growth
- **Real estate assets peaked at 167% of GDP → drop in wealth will equal ~50% of GDP**

- Rising mortgage delinquencies, slowing home purchases, slowing retail sales
- ~2 million borrowers with adjustable-rate mortgages scheduled for teaser rate expiration in 2008 → interest cost jumps
- **Paulson** announced mortgage modification measures but stopped short of taxpayer funding

### Bridgewater Special Report (November 21, 2007)

**"What We Think Will Be Contained & What We Think Won't Be Contained"**

**Will be contained**:
- Sub-prime/SIV problems will spread (contagion) but remain manageable, won't sink economy (though will weaken it)
- Size manageable, ownership dispersed, large demand from distressed security buyers given plentiful global liquidity
- Requires wise coordination of central banks, finance ministries, legislators, financial institutions

**Won't be contained**:
- **Larger, more threatening latent credit problems beneath surface** from: (a) enormous liquidity seeking investment, (b) investors reaching for higher returns via structured, levered, illiquid, risky investments
- Exposures grown exponentially, very large, based on imprudent strategies
- **Mark-to-market losses as of today: ~$420 billion globally, ~1% of global GDP**
- Expect much larger unrealized losses and write-downs to come

### Derivatives: The Unknown Risk

- Biggest unknown: how losses might ripple through derivative markets
- **Derivatives**: financial contracts whose value determined by underlying asset, rate, index, or event
- Not used for raising money — primarily for hedging risks and speculating on price changes
- Made through private contracts, unregulated, enormous, opaque

**Over-the-counter (OTC) derivatives**:
- December 2000: Congress clarified OTC contracts between "sophisticated parties" exempt from futures/securities regulation
- Over next seven years, OTC market grew rapidly
- **June 2008**: notional value = **$672.6 trillion**

**Credit Default Swaps (CDS)**:
- Insurance-like: CDS issuer insures buyer against defaults from particular exposure (e.g., mortgage-backed securities)
- Buyers transfer default risk to CDS sellers
- **AIG** sold massive CDS "insurance" but kept very small reserves — didn't have capacity to pay out for large losses

### Policy Maker Reactivity vs. Proactivity

- Author shared concerns with Treasury and White House — thought picture implausible because unprecedented
- Policy makers more reactive than proactive (understandable — not in business of betting against consensus)
- Operate within political systems requiring broad consensus before action
- **Generally don't act decisively until crisis is on top of them**

### Year-End 2007 Market Snapshot

- S&P 500: down 6% from October peak, positive territory for year overall
- December's biggest sell-off: day Fed lowered rates 0.25% (less than 0.5% market expectation)
- Bond yields: declined sharply from ~5% (June pre-credit crunch) to ~4% (year end)
- Dollar index: down 8.6% for year
- Oil: up 55% to $96 (near all-time high)

## Depression: 2008

### January-February 2008

- Economic cracks appearing: poor manufacturing, retail sales, employment reports
- **Major write-downs**: **Citigroup** $22.2 billion, **Merrill Lynch** $14.1 billion
- **Ambac and MBIA** downgraded (bond insurers with ~$1 trillion guaranteed debt, big subprime exposure)
- Losses from market declines + mark-to-market accounting requirements
- January 20: S&P 500 down ~10%; global equity markets fell even more

**Bernanke to Federal Open Market Committee**:
> "We are facing, potentially, a broad crisis. We can no longer temporize. We have to address this...we have to try to get it under control. If we can't do that, then we are just going to lose control of the whole situation."

### Fed Emergency Action (January 22)

- Emergency meeting → cut rates **75 basis points (0.75%)** to 3.5%
  - Cited "weakening economic outlook and increasing downside risks to growth"
- Week later: cut another **50 basis points**
  - Cited "considerable stress" in financial sector, "deepening contraction," tight credit for "businesses and households"
- **Largest calendar month decline in short rates since 1987**
- Senate passed **$160 billion stimulus package** (tax rebates for low/middle income households)

- Stocks bounced but failed to recoup losses by end of February
- Massive write-downs continued: **AIG** $11 billion, **UBS** $14 billion, **Credit Suisse** $2.8 billion
- Service sector growth and consumer confidence: 7- and 16-year lows
- UBS report: losses from mortgage-backed securities could total **$600 billion** in US financial system

### Bridgewater: Deleveraging vs. Recession (January 31)

**(BDO) January 31: The Really Big Picture; Not Just a Normal Recession**

- "R" (recession) misleading — connotes typical contraction like previous US recessions
- Different from Japan 1990s and US 1930s — better characterized as "D" (deleveraging)
- **"R"**: contraction in real GDP from tight central bank policy (fighting inflation), ends when central bank eases, managed via interest rate changes
- **"D"**: economic contraction from financial deleveraging → assets sold → asset prices decline → equity levels decline → more forced selling → credit contraction → economic contraction → worse cash flows → more asset sales in self-reinforcing cycle
- **Financial deleveraging causes financial crisis that causes economic crisis**

## March 2008—Rescuing Bear Stearns

### Early March Stress

- First 10 days: equities down ~4.5% (larger losses for financials)
- High-profile defaults: **Carlyle Capital** ($22 billion AUM), **Peloton Partners** (two London-based funds, $3 billion AUM), **Thornburg Mortgage** ($36 billion AUM) missing margin calls
- All heavily exposed to mortgage-backed securities, lenders increasingly hesitant

- Concerns spread to major brokerages with significant MBS exposure: **Bear Stearns, Lehman Brothers, Merrill Lynch**
- Borrowing costs spiked

> "The problems were passing to systemically important financial institutions, threatening the entire system. Even so, the danger was not widely appreciated."

- Bridgewater Daily Observations (March 10): conditions "slipping away," "Broker/dealers in our experience cannot survive with financing costs close to Bear's current levels"

### Bear Stearns Crisis

- Most stressed major investment bank
- Held $400 billion securities that would be dumped if it failed
- Nearly 400 subsidiaries touching almost every other major financial firm
- 5,000 trading counterparties, 750,000 open derivatives contracts
- **Bernanke**: "size alone wasn't the problem. Bear was big, but not that big compared to largest commercial banks." Not "too big to fail" but **"too interconnected to fail"**
- **Greatest fear**: Bear bankruptcy could trigger collapse in **$2.8 trillion tri-party repo market** (significant credit pipe for financial institutions) → "disastrous consequences for financial markets and, as credit froze and asset prices plunged, the entire economy"

> "Classically, when a financial institution starts to show early signs of stress, it can experience 'runs' that can accelerate into a failure in a matter of days, because runs can lead to losing liquidity."

- Institutions rely on short-term (often overnight) borrowing to hold longer-term illiquid assets
- At first sign of trouble, logical for short-term credit providers to stop lending to avoid losses
- As more participants change behavior this way → liquidity crisis → failure
- This was happening to institutions shown in chart (conveyed by spreads)
- Treasury and Fed had days to figure out responses

### Policy Maker Capabilities for Managing Debt Crises

> "If the debt is in one's own currency, and if policy makers have both the knowledge of what it takes to manage it and the authority to do so, then they are capable of handling these situations in a way that minimizes spill-over effects and limits economic pain (though some pain is inevitable)."

- Theme returns repeatedly throughout the book

### The 2008 US Economic Leadership Team

- US had team that understood debt crisis management as well as could be expected (once-in-a-lifetime magnitude)
- **Treasury Secretary Hank Paulson**: 30+ years financial market experience at Goldman Sachs (8 years as CEO) → understanding of how financial institutions/markets worked, forceful leadership style, experience making tough decisions under pressure
- **Chairman Bernanke**: one of most prominent economists, world's foremost Great Depression expert → critical perspective
- **Tim Geithner** (Federal Reserve Bank of New York president): leading role in overseeing biggest banks and implementing monetary policy; ~20 years economic policy experience including prominent roles at Treasury
<!-- pp. 421-440 -->

### Leadership Team During Crisis

- **Geithner, Paulson, and Bernanke** formed highly effective crisis team
- Trusted each other, had complementary skills, agreed on preventing systemically important failures
- Cooperation and cleverness prevented what could have been decades-long disaster

### Legal Authority Problem

**Critical constraint: lacked legal authority for necessary interventions**
- Treasury could only use funds designated by Congress
- Clear playbook existed for traditional banks (FDIC-administered), but no authority to provide capital to failing investment banks
- Required willing private sector buyer to save investment banks
- **Flexible authority is classic crisis challenge** — systems designed for stability in normal times poorly suited to crisis scenarios requiring immediate, aggressive action

### Bear Stearns Rescue (March 2008)

**Fed's intervention using section 13(3) powers** (unused since Great Depression):
- Announced **$200 billion Term Securities Lending Facility (TSLF)** allowing financial institutions to borrow cash/treasuries using risky assets (including nongovernment MBS) as collateral
- Markets applauded with ~4% stock gains (largest daily gain in 5+ years)

**Bear's liquidity crisis accelerated despite TSLF:**
- March 10-14: **$18 billion cash buffer disappeared** in 4 days as customers withdrew funds
- Paulson feared Bear could collapse within 24 hours
- Bear made loans up to 60 days while relying on overnight funding — extreme maturity mismatch
- Thursday, March 14: Repo market lenders refused even Treasury securities as collateral for Bear overnight loans

**Private sector solution was best option:**
- Another Fed loan wouldn't help — Bear needed equity to fill loss hole, not more debt
- Treasury lacked authority to be that investor
- **JPMorgan** was natural buyer: Bear's clearing bank, intermediary to repo lenders, most familiar with holdings
- Fed provided JPM **$30 billion non-recourse loan** to buy Bear at $2/share (peak was $173), secured by Bear's mortgage pool — future losses borne by Fed/taxpayer
- Created new lending facility where 20 investment banks/brokerages could borrow unlimited sums using MBS as collateral
- Fed cut rates 75 basis points to 2.25%

**Impact:**
- Stocks rallied, month ended flat
- Controversial to use taxpayer money, but alternative was "financial system passing point of no return"
- Markets rebounded but **Paulson, Bernanke, Geithner immediately worried about Lehman**

### Political Response to Bear Rescue

- Paulson and Bernanke met **Barney Frank** (House Financial Services Committee chairman) seeking emergency authority to wind down failing investment banks in panic
- Frank: impossible to get from Congress without compelling public case
- Paulson maintained frequent (unsuccessful) communication with Lehman CEO to convince him to sell or raise equity
- April meeting with Senators **Chris Dodd, Richard Shelby** and GSE CEOs **Daniel Mudd, Richard Syron** led to Senate taking up GSE reform legislation (stalled since May 2007)

## The Post-Rescue Rally: April–May 2008

**Market optimism through April-May:**
- Stocks rallied, bond yields rose
- Growing confidence Fed would do "whatever was necessary"
- Paulson noted economy rebounding, expected faster growth by year-end
- Charts showed temporary rally in stocks, declining Treasury yields, narrowing bank CDS spreads, but widening corporate spreads

### Underlying Deterioration Continued

**Balance sheet expansions slowing:**
- Unemployment climbing, consumer confidence/borrowing falling
- Housing delinquencies and foreclosures rising
- Manufacturing and services activity contracting
- Fresh write-downs: UBS ($19B), Deutsche Bank ($4B), MBIA ($2.4B), AIG ($7.8B)

> "We likened it to a 'currency intervention that temporarily reverses the markets but doesn't change the underlying conditions that necessitated the action.'" — Dalio's Daily Observations

**Simultaneous pressures:**
- Oil hit $130 in late May
- Dollar continued falling
- Fed faced dilemma balancing accommodative policy (economic contraction) vs. price stability concerns
- April Fed minutes: "difficulty gauging appropriate stance of policy in current circumstances"
- Two members expressed "substantial concerns about prospects for inflation"

**Inefficiency of broad monetary policy for sectoral debt problems:**
- Using interest rate/liquidity policies affecting whole economy to address specific sector debt problems "very inefficient at best"
- Macroprudential policies more appropriate (should've been used in 2007 to control emerging bubbles)
- Only deployed much later when circumstances forced their use
- When Fed cut rates late April, markets priced it as end of easing cycle

## Summer of 2008: Stagflation

**June: simultaneous shocks**
- S&P fell 9% due to surging oil prices spiking inflation
- Renewed credit problems in financial sector, poor economic stats

**Credit problems intensified:**
- Downgrades of **Lehman Brothers, Merrill Lynch, Morgan Stanley** by S&P (lost confidence in ability to meet obligations)
- Rumors Lehman approached Fed for emergency funding
- Moody's: **MBIA and Ambac** (two largest bond insurers) likely to lose AAA ratings, severely impairing ability to write new insurance
- Moody's cut insurers' ratings, placed Lehman on credit review
- Home foreclosures and mortgage delinquency rates (underlying drivers) continued accelerating

**Balance sheet analysis revealed systemic danger:**
- Estimated losses, reduced capital from those losses, impact on lending/asset sales showed institutions "headed for serious trouble with serious knock-on effects"
- Getting margin calls meant raising capital or selling assets and contracting lending — bad for markets and economy

**Economic deterioration:**
- Unemployment surged to 5.6% (largest monthly increase in two decades)
- Manufacturing activity declined fourth month in row
- Consumer confidence hit 16-year low
- Headline inflation rose to 4.4% in May (sharpest 6-month increase)
- **Stagflation fears** amidst poor growth and rising inflation expectations

### Policy Dilemma: Ease or Not Ease?

**Cross-currents made answer unclear:**
- Policy makers concerned about both economic growth and price stability
- Bernanke called rising oil prices unwelcome; Paulson emphasized "real headwind" for economy
- Bernanke: Fed would "carefully monitor" exchange rate implications for inflation/expectations
- Paulson suggested he "would never take intervention off the table"

**Fed priority shift from preventing debt/economic risks toward assuring price stability:**
- June 4: Bernanke noted further rate cuts unlikely due to inflation concerns, current policy rate sufficient for moderate growth
- June 25: Fed left rates unchanged noting "although downside risks to growth remain, they appear to have diminished somewhat, and upside risks to inflation and inflation expectations have increased"

**Markets continued declining through July:**
- Oil prices rose, ratings downgrades, write-downs, poor housing stats
- Financial stocks in free fall as became clear Fed behind developments, credit problems wouldn't be fixed by easy policy
- Mortgage crisis and who it would affect next became clearer

### Fannie and Freddie Crisis Emerges (July 2008)

**Lehman report (July 7) triggered investor stampede:**
- Stated **Freddie Mac and Fannie Mae** would need **$75 billion capital infusion** to remain solvent
- Shares declined ~45% each in week following report

**Mid-July interventions:**
- Oil prices declined sharply (giving Fed room to ease)
- SEC placed restrictions on shorting 19 financial stocks (including Fannie/Freddie)
- Fed extended emergency lending for investment banks/brokerages
- **Treasury and Fed announced Fannie/Freddie could tap public funding (bailout) if on verge of collapse**

## Taking Control of Fannie and Freddie

### GSE Background and Structure

**Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac** were government-sponsored enterprises (GSEs):
- Created by Congress in 1938 and 1970 respectively
- Fannie part of Roosevelt's New Deal post-Great Depression
- Mission: stabilize US mortgage market, promote affordable housing
- Mechanism: buy mortgages from approved private lenders, package together, guarantee timely payment, sell back to investors

**Everyone appeared to benefit:**
- Private lenders: ready buyer for mortgages
- Fannie/Freddie: profited from buying risky mortgages and turning them safe (buy cheap, sell for more)
- Banks/investors: greater supply of safe assets earning slightly more than treasury bonds
- Households: cheaper borrowing rates

**Based on implicit government guarantee:**
- Not officially guaranteed by law; government officials denied guarantee for years
- Private market believed government would never let GSEs fail (would hurt too many including homeowners)
- Couldn't be 100% sure because Treasury wouldn't make assurance
- Implicit guarantee allowed securities issued by GSEs to be seen as safe as treasuries
- Spread on their debt to treasuries sometimes hit 0%

### Growth and Risk Accumulation

**Perverse incentives from implicit guarantee:**
- Larger GSEs grew, more "systemically important" they became
- Being systemically important virtually guaranteed government rescue, making them safer, fueling more growth
- **Government guarantees (implicit or explicit) make risky assets appear safer, encouraging investors to lever up, feeding bad debt growth**

**By 2007:**
- Twenty times larger than Bear Stearns
- Owned or guaranteed **$5 trillion** in residential mortgages and MBS — about half of US total
- $1.7 trillion debt outstanding, ~20% held by international investors
- Huge players in short-term lending market, frequently borrowing up to $20 billion weekly
- "Disaster waiting to happen" — only question was what government would do

### Political Power and Regulatory Capture

**Larry Summers** described 1990s challenges dealing with Fannie/Freddie:

> "Fannie and Freddie had vast political power. When we said anything raising concern about them, they had arranged for the Treasury to receive 40,000 pieces of mail saying it is important that Fannie and Freddie be fully enabled to do their vital work. When we testified on Fannie and Freddie, a congressman would pull out an envelope from Fannie with their prepared statements and what their questions were going to be. They would have a set of mayors call if you tried to mess with them. The most disillusioning experience I had with respect to the financial community was at the quarterly dinner for the Treasury Advisory Borrowing Committee. I asked them: 'What do you guys think about the GSEs?' They said that the GSEs were like a massively over-leveraged hedge fund—dangerous. They were pretty emphatic. I said, 'Would you put that in your report?' and they said they would. The report came back basically saying that Fannie and Freddie are vital contributors to our financial systems. I asked what happened. They said that they checked with their bosses and their bosses said we couldn't say that because we all had such important client relationships."

**Paulson on systemic risk:**

> "We had seen what happened in March when Bear Stearns's counterparties...abruptly turned away. We had survived that, but the collapse of Fannie and Freddie would be catastrophic. Seemingly everyone in the world—little banks, big banks, foreign central banks, money market funds—[either] owned their paper or were [their] counterparty. Investors would lose tens of billions; foreigners would lose confidence in the US. It might cause a run on the dollar."

### Treasury Takeover (July-September 2008)

**Equity collapse by July 15:**
- Freddie/Fannie equity prices declined almost 75% in less than a year

**July 23: Congress granted unprecedented authority:**
- Treasury could use "unspecified" (virtually unlimited) amount to provide funds to GSEs (limited only by federal debt ceiling)
- Expanded regulatory oversight
- Treasury acquired "blank check, backstopped by taxpayer, to do whatever it took to keep these institutions solvent"

**Nationalizing too-big-to-fail financial institutions on brink of failure is classic deleveraging move:**
- Usually well-received
- Signifies government willing to provide blanket of safety over system
- When debts denominated in country's own currency, government has power to eliminate default risks

**Trade-off of checks and balances:**
- Undeniable advantages to political environment with checks and balances and laws
- **During crisis, risk that necessary actions might not be done swiftly enough**
- Laws never written perfectly to anticipate/specify how to handle every possible circumstance
- 2008 crisis had numerous close calls requiring getting around rules to do what needed to be done

### Assessment and Conservatorship (July 30-September 7)

**July 30-August assessment:**
- Treasury regulators (with Fed and outside accounting specialists) pored over GSE books
- Discovered both companies papering over massive capital losses
- After properly accounting for questionable intangible assets and improperly valued mortgage guarantees, both companies **tens of billions underwater**
- Paulson: "We'd been prepared for bad news, but the extent of the problems was startling"

**Plan development (mid-August to September 7):**
- Treasury built plan serving economic goals without bumping against legal constraints
- Decided to put GSEs into **conservatorship** while injecting capital through guaranteed preferred stock purchases
- Conservatorship: both companies could keep running relatively normally post-takeover
- Guaranteed stock purchases: Treasury could effectively backstop their debt even after 18-month authority limit expired
- Paulson wouldn't have to give Fannie/Freddie any heads up — only needed go-ahead from direct regulator, **Federal Housing Finance Agency (FHFA)**

**Political vs. economic challenge:**
- More political than economic challenge
- GSE executives still believed they were on sound footing
- FHFA had sent GSEs draft reports concluding sufficient capitalization just weeks before
- If takeover news leaked, executives could mobilize lobbyists/congressional allies to fight
- No guarantee Treasury would win — GSEs "famously the toughest streetfighters in town"

**Coordination to convince FHFA (September 4-5):**
- Required combined influence of Treasury, Fed, OCC, FDIC
- FHFA (which repeatedly blessed Fannie/Freddie books on loose statutory accounting rules) embarrassed at thought of reversing suddenly
- After weeks of pressure from Treasury/allies, FHFA examiners gave in September 4
- September 5: news given to boards of two companies
- Fearing friction/delay might send markets plunging, Paulson set out to "ambush Freddie and Fannie" with no advance warning

### Paulson's Explanation of Strategy

**July 2008 situation:**
- Fannie/Freddie beginning to fail
- Treasury asked for very expansive emergency powers
- Combined, nine times larger than Lehman Brothers, dominant sources of mortgage financing during crisis
- Could not be allowed to fail

**Political constraint:**
- Political advisors told Paulson if they put big dollar number in front of Congress for approval, Congress would likely get spooked
- Couldn't ask for unlimited authority to inject capital
- Decided to ask for "unspecified" authority

**Temporal challenge:**
- Authority temporary (expired October 2009)
- Fannie/Freddie had long-term debt and insured long-term mortgages
- Required creative financial engineering to turn temporary authority into de facto long-term guarantee
- Used ability to immediately issue long-term preferred stock, then used preferred shares to backstop Fannie/Freddie and absorb potential losses

**Unprecedented political maneuvering:**
- Legal finagling required convincing lawmakers to set aside ideological opposition to bailouts while getting Congress to raise debt ceiling for potentially meaningful capital injection
- Paulson: "if Congress failed to come through, markets would implode. The stakes were enormous"

**August relief period:**
- Falling oil prices and Treasury's unprecedented intervention helped usher relief interval
- Equities rallying modestly through August (~2%), financials down only 1%, free fall of Freddie/Fannie stock halted
- Despite growing perception financial markets stabilizing, underlying drivers of credit problems and feedback into real economy unchanged

**Dalio's warning (August 18):**

> **(BDO) August 18: Entering the Second Stage of the Deleveraging**
>
> It seems to me that we have been through much of the first stage and are now entering the second stage (i.e., the avalanche stage) of the deleveraging. While the Fed did a great job of providing liquidity where it reasonably could, the accounting adjustments (e.g., allowing losses to be written down over several years) weren't made, so **we are approaching a solvency crisis that we think is about to result in an avalanche of asset sales.** So now the question is whether they will create a safety net in time to catch these assets so that they don't crash and bring down the financial system and the economy with it. Frankly, we think that this will be a race to the wire.

## The Crash: September 2008

In September the crisis entered new stage with genuine risk world economy would plunge into depression. Transition into nearly day-by-day account of events.

**First week of September: mixed signals**
- Oil prices falling precipitously (reduced inflation concern, provided tailwind to US consumer spending)
- Airlines and retailers hopeful of pickup in consumer spending were particular beneficiaries
- Falling oil prices also reflected weakening global growth

**Financial players struggling but solutions appearing in works:**
- Lehman's stock rose on news of progress negotiating sale to Korea Development Bank
- Freddie successful $4 billion debt sale
- Ambac announced launch of new insurance subsidiary
- Partially softened investor concerns

<!-- pp. 441-460 -->

## Lehman Goes Bankrupt: September 8-15

### Early Week: Fannie/Freddie Nationalization

- Week began with positive developments: **Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac** nationalized over weekend
- Markets initially rose ~2% Monday, Sept 8 — optimism that nationalization "marked a possible turning point"
- **New York Times** suggested government action had "averted a calamity"
- But negative economic data continued: jobless claims spiked, unemployment rose 5.7% to 6.1%, stocks fell 2.5%
- Global slowdown evident from Canada to Australia

### The Lehman Dilemma

- **Lehman Brothers** trading down 80% but seemed potentially salvageable with its 160-year history
- **Hindsight vs. real-time perception**: In moment, rallies suggested "bargain hunters" saw bottom forming
- **NYT** wrote repeatedly (Sept 3, 5, 10) about potential buyers, market down ~20% from peak
- "In investing, it's at least as important to know when not to be confident and when not to make a bet as it is to have an opinion and make one"

### Tuesday Collapse

- No strategic investor materialized — Lehman stock fell almost 50% Tuesday
- Major banks (**Citigroup, Morgan Stanley, Merrill**) sold off 5-10%, overall market down 3%
- **Credit spreads** widened substantially
- Both investors and regulators questioned whether Lehman could survive the weekend

### Legal Constraints on Saving Lehman

- **No clear legal path** to save failing investment banks, yet they were "systemically important"
- Fed could lend to Lehman for **liquidity**, but Lehman faced **solvency problem**
- **Key principle**: "A solvency problem can only be dealt with by providing more equity capital (or changing the accounting/regulatory rules)"
- Neither Fed nor Treasury had authority to provide equity
- Needed private investor/buyer like **Bear Stearns had with JPMorgan**
- Finding buyer harder: Lehman bigger, more complicated, murkier losing positions

### Failed Rescue Attempts

- **Merrill Lynch** also in dire straits — without investor "no more than a week away from bankruptcy"
- Thursday: Lehman shares dropped 42% on rumors **Barclays** and **Bank of America** wouldn't buy without government help
- Lehman rolling **$200 billion in overnight loans** just to operate — huge pullback risk
- Friday: Shares fell 17% on news Fed/Treasury wouldn't backstop any deal
- Lehman's failure would create domino effect hitting **AIG** (stock fell 31%)
- Overall market closed Friday up 0.4% (aided by falling oil) — most still believed problems would be contained

### Weekend Crisis Meetings

- Friday evening: Fed gathered heads of major Wall Street banks (**Goldman Sachs to Bank of New York Mellon**) to urge Lehman bailout
- **Bank of America, Barclays, HSBC** expressed interest but none wanted to proceed without government support
- Treasury publicly insisted no support would come
- **Paulson** hoped consortium of banks could take Lehman's bad loans to facilitate acquisition
- Some consortium progress made, but no buyer emerged
- Without buyer, Fed lacked authorities "effective in preventing the failure of a nonbank in the midst of a panic-driven run"

### Bernanke and Geithner's Constraints

- **Bernanke and Geithner** had many conversations with Paulson about preventing Lehman failure
- Didn't believe Fed loan would be effective (as with Bear Stearns)
- Legal requirement that loan be "secured to their satisfaction" limited lending amount
- Couldn't lend Lehman enough to save it or guarantee its trading book
- Weeks consumed trying to prevent failure within legal bounds
- Willing to be "very creative" and "take a lot of risk" but only within what law allowed
- **Section 13(3)** of Federal Reserve Act allowed emergency lending to wider borrowers but "did not make them alchemists"
- "Loans were not equity, and they had to be guided by what would work in practice"

### Political and Legal Constraints

- Most agree it would have been better if policymakers had authority to liquidate Lehman orderly
- "Classic example of how political constraints together with imperfectly thought-out legal constraints can get in the way of actions that are widely agreed to be beneficial"

### Sunday: Bankruptcy Announcement

- Sunday afternoon: News broke Lehman headed for bankruptcy — "all hell broke loose"
- Shock unprecedented due to **Lehman's size and interconnectedness**
- Government's failure to save it raised doubts about ability to save the system
- **"Lehman's failure was particularly scary because of its large and poorly understood interconnectedness with the rest of the financial system"**

### Lehman's Derivatives Exposure

- Major channel of contagion: **Lehman's substantial presence in derivatives markets**
- Party to **$4-6 trillion in CDS exposure** (~8% of total market)
- Many exposures offsetting — net exposure much smaller — but failure sent clients scrambling for new counterparties
- No one knew Lehman's net exposure size or who was on other side
- "We were crossing the line into a big, disastrous unknown"

> "The uncertainty of this situation is tremendous. What happens when you go to settle a currency forward transaction with a counterparty that suddenly doesn't exist? Maybe everything goes fine, but maybe some unexpected condition bites you in the ass. What if you haven't been collecting mark-to-market gains from one of your dealers (we collect constantly from everyone), they go down, and now you are a general creditor? Who do you transfer the risk to? Maybe Merrill is right behind Lehman. What do you about that? And who might be behind it? If everyone is asking these questions the natural path is to cut back on trading and concentrate positions with a few firms. But these few firms have the incentive to ration their capacity to the highest quality financial institutions and managers. The inevitable result is substantially lower liquidity, higher transactions cost, and higher volatility. Higher volatility then feeds back into the real economy because people and businesses transact at these prices. And capital constraints in the financial sector mean that credit growth remains low, which undermines economic growth. We are getting very close to crossing this line." — **Daily Observations**, Sept 11

### The "Avalanche" Begins

- Lehman bankruptcy largest in US history (still is) — **$600 billion in assets**
- Only 2/3 size of **Goldman Sachs**, 1/4 as large as **JPMorgan**
- All connected, losses and liquidity problems spreading fast
- **"We called this stage of the crisis the 'avalanche'—the point at which a smaller problem in one corner of the financial system (subprime mortgages) was building in self-reinforcing ways into much bigger problems, and fast"**

## Aftermath of the Lehman Collapse: September 15-18

### Monday September 15: Markets Crater

- Lehman filed for bankruptcy Monday morning
- Stock market fell nearly 5%, no industry spared
- Financial sector took "brunt of the pain" — banks and insurers falling ~10%
- **Credit spreads blew out**, credit flow ground to halt
- Week spent figuring out Lehman ripple effects — interrelationships and exposures too complex and opaque
- **"It was clear to us that blanket protections would have to be put into place, because the consequences of the uncertainties themselves would be devastating as everyone ran from any entity that could go under. But if policy makers couldn't or wouldn't save Lehman Brothers, how could they save the system?"**

### Fed's Emergency Response: Primary Dealer Credit Facility Expansion

- Fed announced night before: unprecedented expansion of **"Primary Dealer Credit Facility"**
- Willing to lend to investment banks against almost any collateral: equities, subprime mortgages, junk bonds
- "Should have been seen as an enormous step for a central bank to take, and in a more normal environment, it would have been"
- Lehman collapse overshadowed it

### Communication Challenge

- **Paulson** later wrote he felt constrained from even explaining why Lehman failed without creating more problems
- Common issue policymakers face during crisis communication
- Per Paulson: "I was in a painful bind that I all too frequently found myself in as a public official. Although it's my nature to be forthright, it was important to convey a sense of resolution and confidence to calm the markets and to help Americans make sense of things... I did not want to suggest that we were powerless. I could not say, for example, that we did not have the statutory authority to save Lehman—even though it was true. Say that and it would be the end of Morgan Stanley, which was in far superior financial shape to Lehman but was already under an assault that would dramatically intensify in the coming days. Lose Morgan Stanley, and Goldman Sachs would be next in line—if they fell, the financial system might vaporize and with it, the economy"

### Bridgewater's Client Note (Sept 15)

**"(BDO) September 15: Where We Are Now"**

- Financial institution losses known for some time, discussed repeatedly over past year
- Described as "known and manageable" via sensible government policies
- Liquidity (Fed), accounting rule changes, safety net (Treasury + Congress), clear policy articulation needed
- Recent Treasury/Fed moves made some things clear through action
- "Willing to take the chance of diving into the depths of the scary unknown without a clear safety net in place"
- **"So, now we sit and wait to see if they have some hidden trick up their sleeves or if they really are as reckless as they seem"**
- Interest rates heading toward 0%, financial intermediaries broken, deleveraging underway
- **"It appears that we are headed into a new domain in which the classic monetary tools won't work and the Japan in the 1990s and US in the 1930's dynamic will drive things"**

### Real Economy Impact

- Fed report: industrial output down sharply in August
- **AIG** credit ratings downgraded, triggering additional collateral requirements
- **Hewlett Packard** announced cutting 25,000 jobs
- Financial crisis quickly spread globally — European and Asian central banks announced new liquidity measures

### Credit Markets Seize Up

- Financial players sorting through **counterparty risks** from Lehman's failure
- **Interbank lending seized up**, **Libor** settled at almost twice prior week's levels
- Contagion spreading to strongest institutions
- Blue-chip firms like **GE** admitted trouble borrowing in commercial paper market
- **Prime money market funds** started showing stress, high redemptions, losses
- By day's end, credit spreads on **Morgan Stanley** wider than Lehman's on Friday

### AIG Crisis Escalates

- **AIG** one of largest insurers, ~$1 trillion in assets at peak
- Had issued hundreds of billions in insurance contracts on bonds (**CDS and CDOs**)
- Required to pay out if bond faced losses
- Many insured bonds were repackaged subprime mortgages — **AIG exposed to staggering losses**
- Other institutions counting on these insurance contracts — **AIG systemically important**
- Heading toward failure fast — Sunday said needed **$40 billion**, Monday suggested **$85 billion**

### Tuesday September 16: Two Bold Fed Moves

**1. Kept Interest Rates Unchanged (Disappointing Markets)**

- Regularly scheduled meeting — Fed decided *not* to change rates when market expected cut
- Significant disappointment, hurt markets
- Even as market on verge of depression, Fed remained concerned about inflation
- Statement: "The downside risks to growth and the upside risks to inflation are both of significant concern"
- **Bernanke** later wrote "in retrospect, that decision was certainly a mistake"
- Caused partly by "substantial sentiment at the meeting in favor of holding our fire until we had a better sense of how the Lehman situation would play out"

**2. Provided $85 Billion Emergency Loan to AIG (Redefining Central Banking)**

- Deal drafted in rush Tuesday afternoon, Sept 16
- Tough terms: **11.5% floating interest rate**, **80% government ownership stake**
- AIG pledged nearly everything as collateral: insurance subsidiaries, financial services companies, real estate holdings (including ski resort)
- Loan worked because market believed AIG solvent (due to insurance subsidiaries' investment-grade ratings)
- Insurance subsidiaries as collateral critical to Fed's decision
- Unusually risky loan for Fed — collateral not as easy to value/sell as normal AAA securities
- Risk AIG could still fail despite Fed help
- **Geithner**: "Deciding to support AIG was one of the most difficult choices I have ever been involved in in over 20 years of public service"

### Wednesday September 17: Markets Reject AIG Bailout

- AIG bailout didn't lift markets Wednesday
- Stocks slid 4.7%, major financial institutions down double digits
- Commercial paper rates continued rising
- Three-month treasury bills fell to just above 0% (from ~1.6% week before) as investors fled to safety
- Regulators announced stabilizing measures:
  - **SEC** tightened controls over short sellers
  - Bank regulators proposed accounting rule revisions to help bank balance sheets

### Mark-to-Market Accounting Explained

- Some bank assets **"marked-to-market"** — valued daily at potential sale prices
- Other assets valued differently, often by in-house methodology
- When required mark-to-market assets sell at fire-sale prices, banks appear to take significant losses
- Reduces capital, requires raising money or selling assets
- Further strains liquidity, puts downward pressure on assets, scares counterparties
- Accounting changes allowing banks to realize losses over longer time period (not marking to market) prevents some problems
- But changing accounting rules to hide losses during crisis doesn't engender confidence either
- Regulators must be careful

### Recapitalization Needs

- Accounting changes wouldn't solve fundamental issue: **overindebted US households and financial institutions defaulting because overleveraged**
- Financial institutions needed recapitalization (equity investment)
- Needed to find buyers for troubled assets
- **Paulson** turned to Congress for funding and authorization for Treasury to play that role

## The Government Comes Up with a Bailout Fund: September 18-31

### The TARP Proposal

- **Paulson, Bernanke**, and congressional leaders (especially **Barney Frank**) thought best way to restore confidence: buy troubled assets through **Troubled Asset Relief Program**
- Could have nationalized banks, but no US precedent
- When banks nationalized in other countries, penalized with harsh terms
- Banks reluctant to accept capital/nationalization until just before or after failure
- Paulson didn't believe this was the way to go — would be more damaging than helpful to reviving capital flows

### Why Buy Assets Instead of Nationalizing

- Big source of banks' problems: large amounts of complex, highly illiquid mortgage securities on balance sheets
- Theory: if government provided market for them, prices would rise, capital freed up, confidence restored
- Banking system could begin to recapitalize

### Thursday-Friday Rally

- When Wall Street learned about possible TARP plan Thursday, market rose
- Rally continued Friday — stocks up 4% as more details emerged
- **President Bush** and **Secretary Paulson** announced federal government prepared to spend **$500 billion** to buy troubled mortgages
- Congressional leaders promised quick action
- Fed unveiled **$180 billion in new swap lines for global central banks** — easing dollar liquidity crunch fears
- **SEC** instituted ban on short-selling nearly 800 financial stocks
- **Goldman Sachs** and **Morgan Stanley** came under government legal authority to provide blanket protection by voluntarily becoming bank holding companies

### Money Market Fund Guarantee

- **Treasury unveiled creative new move to shore up troubled money market funds holding $3.5 trillion**
- Money market funds very popular alternative to bank deposits for retail and institutional investors
- Attracted by high interest rates, undeterred by lack of FDIC protection
- Didn't appreciate delivering higher rates by investing in higher-yielding, higher-risk loans
- Believed wouldn't lose money as principal protected

### Money Market Funds as Liquidity Source

- **Prime money-market funds** crucial liquidity source for businesses
- Buy **commercial paper** — short-term debt businesses use to fund operations
- Commercial paper generally diversified and highly rated — funds usually considered almost riskless (like CDs or bank deposits)
- Few prime funds took losses when Lehman failed, specifically **Reserve Primary Fund** "broke the buck" Sept 16
- Fears others might take losses caused many investors to pull money
- As dollars flowed out, funds had to liquidate commercial paper holdings
- Result: **hundreds of billions of dollars funding day-to-day business operations dried up in days**

### Morning Crisis Calls (Sept 19)

- After Reserve Fund broke buck, **Ken Wilson** (Treasury) got 7am call from **Northern Trust**
- Followed by calls from **Black Rock, State Street, Bank of New York Mellon**
- All reported runs on money-market funds
- **GE** in news explaining couldn't sell paper
- **Coca-Cola CFO Muhtar Kent** called — unable to make $800 million quarterly dividend payment because couldn't roll paper
- AAA-rated industrial and consumer-products companies couldn't roll paper
- "Situation was very quickly metastasizing from Wall Street to Main Street"

### Treasury's Money Market Guarantee Solution

- To stop run, Paulson decided to guarantee money market funds outright
- Problem: funds would need substantial backstop, Treasury couldn't immediately find cash
- Creative solution: tapping **$50 billion Exchange Stabilization Fund (ESF)** to back guarantee
- Plan announced Friday, Sept 19 — four days after Lehman collapse
- Treasury Secretaries can get in big trouble spending money that hasn't been appropriated
- Paulson got general counsel opinion he could use $45 billion "since if the whole economy went down it wouldn't be good for the dollar"
- Some colleagues questioned if $45 billion enough given $3.5 trillion money-market funds
- Paulson didn't know, but didn't have better idea

### FDIC Head's Complaint and Solution

- Treasury team moving so fast **Sheila Bair** (FDIC head) called and complained
- Not only wasn't she consulted, but guarantee would shift money from bank deposits to Money-Market Funds
- Good point — Treasury clarified guarantee only applicable to money-market funds in trouble as of Sept 19
- Guarantee worked incredibly well, markets immediately turned
- Per Paulson: when you say something is a guarantee (not just backstop), "much more reassuring to investors"

### ESF Creativity

- ESF meant to defend dollar against runs, but mandate flexible enough to divert to more pressing uses
- Could be done quickly with only presidential approval
- **"This was exactly the kind of quick thinking and creativity that was required to navigate the regulatory and political minefield and get what was needed done"**

### Optimistic Daily Observations (Sept 18)

**"(BDO) September 18: Great Moves!"**

- "The Treasury, Fed, and Congress finally agreed to agree to build the safety net!!!!"
- "Overnight central banks added $180 billion in liquidity!"
- "Regulators moved against short sellers"
- "Morgan Stanley was frozen and is about to be dealt with, and Goldman isn't far behind, but moves are in the works to deal with them"

### Weekend Optimism Fades

- Week's optimism faded as TARP details failed to emerge over weekend
- Formal proposal Saturday, Sept 20: three pages long
- Intended as outline rather than fait accompli to Congress
- Proposal called **TARP (Troubled Asset Relief Program)** — **$700 billion** in mortgage-related asset purchases
- Offered few details on administration or other actions
- Amount "a pittance in comparison to the need"
- Per client explanation when bill unveiled: troubled asset purchases couldn't have much impact on their own

### Bridgewater's Skeptical Assessment (Sept 25)

**"(BDO) September 25: The Proposed Plan Disappoints:"**

- Buying $700 billion in mortgages (plus some other assets) "will hardly help us at all"
- If bought at market prices: won't change financial conditions materially, mortgages small percentage of amount needed, won't deal with most existing problems
- If bought at premium: "unethical direct subsidy that is on the wrong side of the line," amount spent will buy less and still won't contain problems

### Congressional Resistance

- Legislators put off by unchecked authority bill would give Treasury
- Getting through Congress wasn't assured
- Monday markets opened: stocks sold off, closing down 3.8%
- Dollar fell against most major currencies

### Week of September 22: Political Struggle

- TARP bill's difficult journey through Washington DC set against backdrop of poor economic releases and icy credit markets
- Political struggles between those wanting to provide support vs. those who didn't drove markets Monday-Wednesday
- **Bernanke** and **Paulson** urged immediate action in Congressional testimony Tuesday
- **President Bush** addressed nation supporting TARP Wednesday
- Little apparent progress from Congress
- Legislative momentum interrupted by debates over:
  - More comprehensive bill with more homeowner aid
  - Better-defined limits on Treasury authority
  - Executive compensation at banks became hot-button issue
  - Many other issues causing arguing, little progress

### Populist Overtones

- **"As is classic in deleveraging scenarios, this political debate took on populist overtones"**
- Congressional leaders mostly supported, rank-and-file argued like handout to banks causing trouble
- Arguing over who bears costs typical during deleveragings, highly counterproductive
- "Can be like doctors in the emergency ward arguing over who will pay the bill"
- "All attention needs to be directed to saving the patient—how the costs should be handled can be decided later"

### Risks of Proposing TARP

- Even proposing TARP risky — if didn't pass, likely extremely negative market reaction
- Needed it passed in very difficult circumstances:
  - As soon as possible
  - Weeks before presidential election
  - Amid populist uproar from left and right over unprecedented size and scope
- Bill would need bipartisan passage (by this point extremely rare for any new important law, even rarer since)
- If either presidential candidate opposed, nearly impossible to pass
- **McCain** and **Palin** initially anti-bailout position put bill at risk (eventually supported)
- Paulson on phone almost daily with both candidates
- Only factor working in bill's favor: usually takes crisis to get Congress to act
- Financial crisis in most acute stage
- "Difficulties in getting TARP passed are a good illustration of why regulators need broad emergency authorities versus needing to rely on Congress to act"

### Market Turmoil Continues

- Financial markets pulsated between "they will" and "they won't" do what necessary in time
- **CDS spreads** on **Goldman Sachs** and **Morgan Stanley** (which narrowed after transforming to bank holding companies over weekend) widened through week
- Huge outflows from prime money-market funds into government funds continued pressuring commercial paper
- Thursday night: **FDIC** seized **Washington Mutual** — largest bank failure in American history
- Shifted assets to **JPMorgan** in $1.9 billion deal (would seize **Wachovia** days later)
- Friday Sept 26: "There is so much jam packed into each day that it is hard to pick what to comment on. The big picture through which we see all the daily news is that we are in the avalanche phase of the deleveraging"

### Sunday Breakthrough

- Political stalemate seemed to break early Sunday morning
- **Secretary Paulson**, flanked by **House Speaker Nancy Pelosi** and **Senate Majority Leader Harry Reid**, announced agreement reached on $700 billion bailout bill

### Monday Vote Fails

- When bill came up for vote Monday afternoon, it failed
- Stocks fell 8.8% — **largest single-day drop since 1987**
- Around world, reverberations sent markets spiraling downward
- Oil prices fell $10 because in depression demand would be much less
- Central banks scrambled to offer emergency loans to shell-shocked institutions
- **Interbank lending markets froze**, rates on short-term treasuries fell to just above zero

### Importance of Authority and Speed

- **"Again, among the most important aspects of successfully managing a crisis is having wise and knowledgeable decision-makers who have the authority to do whatever it takes"**
- Congressional vote was a


<!-- pp. 461-480 -->

### TARP Passage Politics and Implementation

#### The Credibility Crisis (Sept 29)

- **BDO September 29 note**: Treasury's struggle for authority signaled credibility test
- Global financiers (China, OPEC) would ultimately decide if US passed the test
- House failure to pass bailout bill shined "global spotlight on US decision makers"
- Even if passed, maintaining global faith would be difficult due to "chaotic process and lack of leadership"
- **Key warning**: "There is still a lot to lose"

#### Political Obstacles to TARP

- TARP considered just one month before "hotly contested presidential election"
- **Republicans**: Hated looking like they abandoned free-market principles and fiscal responsibility for bank bailout
- **Democrats**: Worried about giving legislative win to outgoing Republican administration before election
- **Obama and McCain**: Both worried opponent would gain populist credentials by opposing "Wall Street bailout"
- **Paulson's concern**: If either candidate took populist stand, bill would have "little chance of passing"

#### Bank Failures and European Contagion

- While Treasury worked with Congress, **WaMu and Wachovia** failed—two of biggest US bank failures in history
- Several European countries had to bail out their own banks
- Treasury officials recognized $700B in toxic asset purchases wouldn't be enough
- **But**: If money put directly into banks as capital, they could leverage and "buy many times the $700 billion"

#### Capital Injection Strategy

- Treasury pushed for authority to inject capital despite saying publicly they wouldn't
- **Key insight**: Rather than distinguish healthy from unhealthy banks (analytical nightmare, time-consuming, stigmatizing), Treasury offered to buy **preferred stocks on very attractive terms**
- This allowed capital injection into 700 banks very quickly
- **Dalio's defense**: Move was "enormously unpopular" but "necessary and appropriate"
- Prevented catastrophic collapse worse than Great Depression
- Taxpayers ultimately profited ~$50 billion from TARP capital programs
- **Dalio on leadership**: "This willingness to do the unpopular but right things to benefit people is both heroic and underappreciated"

> "Too many people who have never actually been on the field throw beer cans from the stands. This can lead to disastrous results, unless the players are smart enough and courageous enough to do what is right despite the unpopularity of doing it." — Ray Dalio

#### TARP Passage (Oct 3)

- TARP eventually passed—"extraordinary accomplishment" as rare, consequential bipartisan action
- **Pattern**: "Political systems typically make the right decisions only after heated fighting and literally just hours before disaster is about to strike"
- By time bill passed Oct 3, broad agreement among investors that bill "wouldn't be enough"
- Stocks sold off 1.4%

## October 2008

### Early October: Maximum Scramble

- First days of October = **"a scramble to get as much done in as short a time as possible"**
- Economy deteriorating daily, nearly every regulatory department had major policy change in works

#### FDIC Deposit Coverage Increase

- FDIC worked on raising deposit insurance ceiling
- Needed more coverage (Wachovia bank runs made clear) but worried raising limit too high would:
  - Draw depositors from foreign banks with lower limits
  - Choke off liquidity in Europe and Asia
- **Compromise**: Raised limit from $100K to $250K (Oct 3)
- Later added **Transaction Account Guarantee Program**—fully guaranteed non-interest bearing transaction accounts at participating banks

#### Economic Deterioration Data

- **First week of October alone**:
  - PMI (purchasing managers survey) well below expectations
  - Factory orders down 4% in August
  - Payroll report: lost 159,000 jobs in September—worst month in five years
- Following week: retail sales down 7.7% year-over-year

#### Stock Market Collapse Without Single Winning Day

> "During this period of constant bad news, stocks sold off literally every day."

- **Oct 1–Oct 10**: S&P 500 lost 22% total, without single day of gains
- Crude oil fell rapidly, ending first half of month at $75/barrel
- **Oct 9**: Stocks sold off 7.6% on record volume "with virtually nothing important enough to warrant it"

#### Credit Crisis Beyond Finance

- Major nonfinancial corporations relying on credit lines as corporate paper markets shut out
- Companies slashing dividends to preserve cash
- Money-market fund outflows continued
- Consumer credit fell in August—first time since 1998
- Similar stories globally as liquidity dried up everywhere

### Policy Response: Commercial Paper Funding Facility (Oct 7)

- Fed announced plan to purchase unsecured commercial paper
- Created **Commercial Paper Funding Facility (CPFF)**: technically independent entity buying commercial paper using Fed Section 13(3) loans
- In practice: Fed financing commercial paper purchases directly, no Treasury backstop
- **Extraordinary authority**: Took Fed to edge or beyond statutory authority—central banks generally not permitted such risky credit exposure
- Fed "bravely did what it needed to do" and hoped CPFF fees could cover losses

### Shift to Bank Capitalization

- Days after TARP passage, **Paulson began hinting** funds might capitalize banks instead of just purchasing troubled assets
- **Oct 9**: White House officials signaled TARP money might go to capital injections

### Columbus Day Weekend: G7 Coordination (Oct 11–13)

- **"Single largest push on the part of policy makers"**
- **Saturday, Oct 11**: President Bush met G7 members in Washington for coordinated international effort
- Agreed G7 members would:
  - Inject capital into banking institutions
  - Increase deposit insurance guarantees
- Next two days: Treasury finalized America's commitment
- **Two bold centerpieces**:
  1. Huge expansion of FDIC insurance coverage
  2. Massive injection of capital into banking system

#### Temporary Liquidity Guarantee Program

- **FDIC authority stretched** under new Temporary Liquidity Guarantee Program to:
  - Guarantee debt of any single systemically important bank
  - Backstop losses on all newly issued unsecured debt by banks and bank-holding companies
  - Guarantee all noninterest-bearing transaction accounts
- Amounted to guarantee of nearly all bank debt—extraordinary measure with feared unintended consequences

> "To be frank, I hated these options, but I didn't want to preside over a meltdown." — Hank Paulson

#### Capital Purchase Program

- Treasury planned to take equity stakes in as many banking institutions as possible
- Limit: 3% of risk-weighted assets or $250B
- Investments as preferred stock with 5% dividend
- **Key**: Paulson needed even healthiest banks to participate—otherwise participation would stigmatize and encourage runs
- **Monday, Oct 13**: Paulson invited nine major bank CEOs to private conference room
- Explained he expected everyone to participate, prescribed amount of capital each should take
- None left without taking government money
- By meeting end: Paulson pledged $125B of $700B Congress gave him

### Market Deterioration Continues

- Even with cash injection, markets in US, Europe, Japan continued to worsen
- Became clear to **Paulson, Bernanke, Geithner**: had to act with even greater force
- Very consequential meetings with G7 central bankers and finance ministers

#### Warning About Speed

- Several people warned Paulson teams "perhaps moving too fast" and doing so could be dangerous
