- **Paulson's belief**: If policy makers didn't move quickly, would have nothing that works when markets opened Tuesday after Columbus Day weekend

### Temporary Liquidity Guarantee Program—"Most Powerful Step"

- **Paulson**: Most powerful step was Temporary Liquidity Guarantee Program
- FDIC used funds (established to protect savers) to **guarantee liabilities of financial institutions**, including unsecured liabilities of bank holding companies
- FDIC general counsel initially said this was illegal
- Paulson and others spent time convincing head **Sheila Bair** this was right thing to do
- She "ultimately made the very courageous decision to back it—which made a huge difference"

### Market Reaction (Oct 13)

- As news leaked Monday Oct 13 (and policy makers worldwide announced similar projects):
- **Stocks rallied 11.6%—largest single-day increase in S&P 500 since 1939**

### Significance of October Announcements

- **Turning point**: Up to this point, most government responses were ad hoc reactions to individual disasters
- Fed had borne disproportionately large burden, unclear if other agencies would adequately support
- **Now**: Increasingly clear US and global policy makers committed to "taking extraordinary, coordinated action"
- Still huge uncertainties ahead, underlying economy continued deteriorating

#### BDO Oct 13 Assessment

> "(BDO) October 13: The Governments Are Doing Everything Possible; Now We Will Have to See If It's In Time"

- These are great moves, doing everything hoped for
- **But**: Would have worked in stage one (e.g., instead of Lehman bankruptcy)—crisis now spread to stage 3
- "We just have to wait and see"
- Big question: Will massive liquidity injections and bank recapitalizations "get to those who are at the periphery of the system"?
- Many dominoes falling beyond government reach, hard to know effects
- **First time confident**: "Major developed countries' governments are doing all in their power to deal with this crisis"

### Continued Volatility

- Fears and uncertainties kept volatility extremely high
- **Wednesday**: Stocks fell 9% on grim retail sales and Bernanke warning recovery would be slow
- **Thursday**: Market rebounded 4.3%

#### Credit Conditions Begin Thawing (Oct 20)

- **By Monday, Oct 20**: Markets registered meaningful easing in interbank lending and commercial paper
- Commercial paper rates touched four-week lows, short-term treasury yields crept up
- Thawing of credit conditions helped lift stocks, represented significant easing of pressure on banks

### Global Dollar Squeeze

- **Financial mismatches** still squeezing institutions (borrowing short/lending long, borrowing one currency/lending another)
- **Dollar squeeze**: Foreign financial institutions borrowed dollars and lent them out, now had to pay back and/or deal with debtors who had to pay back in dollars when dollars, money, credit hard to come by
- Fed continually expanded dollar swap lines throughout October with developed-world central banks
- **Not able** to provide enough dollar liquidity to alleviate global dollar squeeze
- Part of problem: Central banks reluctant to lend Fed-provided dollars against locally denominated collateral (fears of default, logistical issues)
- **Largest squeeze**: Emerging markets, where dollar debts built up and debtors scrambling for dollars
- **Dollar rallied 8% in October**

#### BDO Oct 22: The Dollar Squeeze Explanation

> "A debt is a short cash position—i.e., a commitment to deliver cash that one doesn't have. Because the dollar is the world's reserve currency, and because of the dollar surplus recycling that has taken place over the past few years…lots of dollar denominated debt has been built up around the world. So, as dollar liquidity has become tight, there has been a dollar squeeze. This squeeze…is hitting dollar-indebted emerging markets (particularly those of commodity exporters) and is supporting the dollar. When this short squeeze ends, which will happen when either the debtors default or get the liquidity to prevent their default, the US dollar will decline."

### Price Volatility Driven by Positioning

- Actual price = spending on item / quantity of item sold (P = $/Q)
- **Key insights**:
  - a) Knowing who is spending and who is selling (and why) ideal way to understand prices
  - b) Prices don't always react to fundamentals as characterized by those seeking to explain price movements in connection with news
- **During this period**: Volatility extremely high for reasons having nothing to do with fundamentals, everything to do with who getting in/out of positions for various reasons
  - Being squeezed, no longer squeezed, rebalancing portfolios, etc.
- **Example**: Tuesday Oct 28, S&P gained 10%+; next day fell 1.1% when Fed cut rates another 50 basis points
- **Month end**: S&P down 17%—largest single-month drop since October 1987

## November–December 2008

### Obama Election (Nov 4)

- Barack Obama elected President amid record turnout
- Would enter office with big majorities in both houses
- Had promised billions in government spending on infrastructure, unemployment insurance, Medicaid
- Supportive of TARP, control of Congress would allow quick action

### Economic Freefall Accelerates

> "While the financial contagion may have been slowed by the Treasury and the Fed's actions so far, it became clearer in the last couple months of 2008 that the economy was falling at a far faster pace than even the most pessimistic observers feared, and that we were heading for the worst downturn since the Great Depression and into the great unknown."

- Economy now in **classic early deleveraging/depression** phase
- Monetary policy could not work normally, interest rates could no longer be lowered, innumerable credit avenues dried up

#### November Economic Data

- Most important stats worse than already very poor expectations
- Consumer spending fell at extraordinary rate
- Retail sales fell 8%+, auto sales down 30% year-over-year
- Businesses reacted with historic layoffs
- Unemployment rate moved past 6.8% (highest since 1994), projections for layoffs/unemployment increased dramatically
- December: worst manufacturing reading since 1982
- **"The economy was imploding"**

### Auto Industry Crisis

- Auto industry in particular remained in dire straits, actively sought federal backstops
- Treasury reluctant to broaden $700B TARP to include industrial companies
- Unwilling to assist major automakers
- **Early November**: Treasury turned down General Motors request for $10B to help finance possible Chrysler merger
- Without federal funding and nonfunctional credit markets, automakers turned to selling assets
- Ford and GM sold stakes in other automakers during month

#### Major Financial Institution Losses

- **Nov 10**: AIG reported $25B quarterly loss (secured additional $150B from government to curtail contagion)
- **Fannie Mae**: Posted $29B loss, said might need more than $100B Treasury already pledged

### Paulson Shifts TARP Strategy (Mid-November)

- Paulson hadn't said anything publicly about changing thinking (didn't want to influence election)
- Market expecting significant asset repurchase program
- **Mid-November postelection speech**: Announced plans for modifying Treasury's TARP use:
  - No longer planned to buy illiquid assets (market frozen)
  - Funds channeled to banks and nonbank financial companies (not auto) as equity-like capital
  - New lending program targeted at consumer lending markets (auto loans, credit cards, student loans)
- **Market reaction**: Negative to adjustment

> "As I feared, the markets focused on the fact that there wouldn't be a program to purchase mortgage-related assets." — Hank Paulson

- S&P dropped 5.2%

#### Stock Market New Low (Nov 20)

- Stocks reached new low Nov 20, down 20%+ for month (52% from highs)
- Oil collapsed (now below $50/barrel)
- Home prices continued falling
- **But**: New low met with relatively quick reversal on news Obama would nominate:
  - **Timothy Geithner**: Treasury secretary
  - **Larry Summers**: Director of National Economic Council
  - Both justifiably considered highly capable

#### Larry Summers Background

- Came into job concerned about possibility of major debt crisis for a while
- In speech early March before Bear's collapse:

> "I believe that we are facing the most serious combination of macroeconomic and financial stresses that the United States has faced in a generation—and possibly much longer than that"

- Was advocate of big policy moves in response to crisis
- Would be key decision-maker in administration's policy toward auto companies
- **Bernanke** stayed at Fed—good continuity among economic team leaders
- Moves helped assure policy continuity between administrations

### First Quantitative Easing (Nov 25)

- **Federal Reserve and Treasury announced $800B** in lending and asset purchases aimed at pushing down mortgage rates (help housing market)
- Central bank committed to purchases of $600B in debt tied to home loans
- **First Quantitative Easing (QE) program**—classic and critical step in managing deleveraging
- Central bankers in crises forced to choose between:
  1. "Printing" more money (beyond what's needed for bank liquidity) to replace decline in private credit
  2. Allowing big tightening as credit collapses

> "They inevitably choose to print, as they did in this case, which is when things changed dramatically."

#### Comparison to 1930s Depression

> "I hope you will read the prior section, about the US debt crisis in the 1928–38 period (as well as look at the other cases) to see how true this is. What was different in 2008 was the speed with which the policy makers made this crucial step. The 1930–33 depression went on so long because policy makers were so slow to react—not because their problems were worse, because they weren't. Still, the 2008 crisis would have been a lot less painful if the policy makers had acted even earlier."

- 30-year fixed mortgage rates fell nearly 1% on news
- 10-year Treasury yields declined 22 basis points
- Stock market ended month down 7.5%—"wasn't clear if these moves were too little too late"

### Auto Industry Bailout Debate

#### TARP and Auto Industry

- TARP enacted to deal with financial institutions
- **Paulson** repeatedly said didn't intend to use TARP funds for autos
- **Bush Administration**: Didn't want auto bankruptcy, worked diligently with Congress to prevent one
- Tried to get legislative authority to use portion of $25B Congress already appropriated (for autos to meet fuel efficiency standards) for emergency loans for restructuring
- Real progress in House, but legislation stalled in Senate mid-December

#### Bush Extends Emergency Loans (Dec 19)

- **Dec 19**: Just before leaving office, President Bush officially announced plans to extend $13.4B in emergency loans to Chrysler and GM
- By month end, government expanded bailout package unexpectedly, delivering additional support to auto industry (buoyed stocks)
- Because TARP money could only go to financial institutions, funds directed through auto companies' financing arm
- **GMAC** (General Motors financing affiliate) approved to reorganize as bank (receive federal aid)
- GMAC started making new loans to less credit-worthy borrowers
- Then helped auto companies by recapitalizing and rescuing their finance companies just before leaving office
- **Market optimism**: Fiscal stimulus being pledged by president-elect Obama
- Transition exemplified "very best of political behavior" on part of both transitioning presidents
- Continuity of Bernanke and Geithner on economic leadership team helped

### Uncertainty About Recovery

- While all moves were big, good reason to question whether damage already done too great for full recovery
- Stocks rallied on hope of stimulus and progress with automakers
- December ended with volatility (uncertainty) priced to remain high

#### Bridgewater's 2008 Performance

- Bridgewater closed out 2008 with significant gains for investors when most had significant losses
- "What a year! What a relief!"

<!-- pp. 481-500 -->

## Transition from an "Ugly" to a "Beautiful" Deleveraging: 2009

### Historical Parallel: 2008 Crisis and the Great Depression

- Understanding the **Great Depression template** and prior deleveragings proved critical to navigating 2008-2009
- Chart comparing 1925-1940s to 2008-2009 shows parallel patterns: interest rates hit virtually 0% in both cases, followed by "money printing"
- **Only two times since 1900** that rates reached 0% and QE followed—in both cases, markets and economy bottomed immediately after

> "Quantitative easing is like a giant shot of adrenalin to save a patient that is having a massive heart attack. The only question I had in late 2008 was if this overdue and great move would work or if it had come too late."

- Money supply (M0) spiked dramatically in both the 1930s and 2008, coinciding with zero interest rates

### Economic Conditions: Early 2009

**January 2009 assessment (BDO note):**
- Economy in **freefall**—credit contraction hitting employment hardest
- Cascade: financial sector → demand → employment
- January saw 62,000 job cuts reported in a single day
- Five major institutions at risk of failure: **Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac, AIG, Citigroup, Bank of America** (each larger than Lehman)
- Untested Obama administration inheriting the crisis

**Credit contraction dynamics:**
- Businesses must generate cash flow internally—cannot rely on loans during credit freeze
- This **magnifies pressure to lay off workers**
- Payroll reductions exceeded 500,000/month in November-December as businesses tried to sustain operating earnings

### Obama Administration's Early Moves

**February 10: Geithner's Financial Stability Plan**
- Announced plan to "clean up and strengthen the nation's banks"
- Would **stress test** major banks to determine capital needs
- Capital would be shored up via combination of public and private funds
- Market reaction: **S&P fell 3% during speech, ended day down 4.9%**
- Investors uncertain: Would there be nationalizations? Who bears losses—shareholders or taxpayers?
- Only broad strokes provided, leaving investors expecting the worst

**Joint statement to counter nationalization rumors:**
- Treasury, FDIC, OCC, OTS, and Federal Reserve issued statement
- **"Strong presumption"** banks should remain in private hands under the Capital Assistance Program
- Market still unconvinced—**S&P fell 3.5% on the day**

### The Stress Test

**Design:**
- Fed would assess how banks would withstand major contraction: **3.3% GDP contraction, 8.9% unemployment, 22% fall in housing prices**
- Banks lacking capital would turn to private markets first, then public funds
- Dalio's team had run similar estimates for 18 months—marked holdings to market, ran scenario analysis
- "Pretty confident that our estimates were good"—eager to see if Fed's numbers would align

### Fiscal Stimulus Measures

**American Recovery and Reinvestment Act (February 17):**
- **$787 billion total**: $288B tax reductions, $144B state/local governments, $105B infrastructure, rest federal spending
- Tax reduction funneled to taxpayers **within days**—virtually instantaneous stimulus
- Infrastructure spending would take years to ramp up—mattered less in short term

**Homeowner Affordability and Stability Plan (February 18):**
- **Up to $275 billion** to address housing crisis
- Goal: help "as many as nine million American homeowners refinance their mortgages or avert foreclosure"
- $75B direct spending to keep at-risk homeowners in homes
- Incentives for lenders to alter loan terms for troubled borrowers
- Additional **$200B to Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac**

**Term Asset-Backed Securities Loan Facility (TALF):**
- Fed policy lending up to **$1 trillion on non-recourse basis** to holders of AAA asset-backed securities
- Helped stimulate consumer loans (auto, credit card, student)
- Extension of liquidity programs set to expire end of April
- Despite all this stimulus, **markets continued to fall**

### March Crisis Deepens

**Sunday, March 1: AIG news**
- AIG to report **$62 billion 4th quarter loss** (largest quarterly loss in US corporate history)
- Treasury and Fed agreed to provide additional **$30 billion in capital** and loosen earlier loan terms
- Markets plunged Monday on **knock-on effects fears**
- February economic data: economy shed 651,000 jobs, auto sales fell 5.8% to weakest level since early 1980s

**March 9: The Bottom**
- World Bank issued pessimistic report
- **Warren Buffett** said economy had "fallen off a cliff"
- Stock market fell 1%, investor sentiment extremely bearish, selling exhausted
- **Day the bottom in US stock market and top in dollar were made** (unknowable at the time)

**March 10: Rally begins**
- Stocks surged **6.4%**, led by 38% jump in Citigroup shares
- Citigroup CEO memo: bank "once again profitable"
- **Chairman Bernanke** speech on financial regulation reforms well-received
- Reports lawmakers close to re-instituting uptick rule to slow short-selling

## Policy Makers Launch Coordinated Counterattack: March-April 2009

### The "Shock and Awe" Campaign

**Policy makers at the Fed and Treasury planned coordinated set of policies designed to shore up the financial system and provide money needed to make up for contracting credit**

- Much more aggressive than earlier easings
- Released in sequence of mega-announcements
- **How they were announced magnified the impact on markets**

**March 18: Expanded QE**
- Fed announced expanding QE purchases: **$750B Agency MBS, $100B agency debt**
- Would expand to **US government bonds—up to $300B over next six months**
- Expanded TALF collateral eligible to wider set of financial assets
- Stated expectations of **"keeping rates exceptionally low for an extended period"**

**Market reaction to $1 trillion+ announcement was huge:**
- Enormous Treasury rally—**48 basis point fall in yields** (biggest change in couple decades)
- Stocks rallied, dollar sold off, gold rallied
- Intraday charts show magnitude of moves following announcement

**March 23: Public-Private Investment Partnership (PPIP)**
- **Geithner** announced plan to buy **$500B to $1 trillion** worth of troubled assets from banks
- Three-part PPIP incentivized private investment firms to buy banks' bad assets using their own capital
- Allowed firms to **leverage investments in troubled assets** using Fed-borrowed money
- Guarantee: would not lose more than initial investment if assets fell below initial value
- Also announced possible TALF expansion to finance residential/commercial MBS and legacy securities eligibility

**Market reaction:**
- S&P rose **7.1%**, led by 18% rally in financial shares

**March 24: Financial Regulation Overhaul**
- Fed and Treasury announced plans to overhaul financial regulations
- Expand government power to seize **"too big to fail" banks**, insurers, investment banks, investment funds
- Two days later, Geithner outlined wider overhaul of financial regulations
- Greatly increased federal regulatory oversight of insurance companies, hedge funds, private equity funds
- Expanded regulatory powers over any company deemed "too big to fail"
- Well-received by markets though not key part of stimulative counterattack

### GM and Chrysler "Cushioned Bankruptcy"

**End of March:**
- **Summers** and **Geithner** oversaw team led by **Steven Rattner** to create plan for GM and Chrysler
- **Larry Summers** described it as **"cushioned bankruptcy"**
- Bankruptcy would force trade unions and creditors to negotiate debt reductions
- Ample US government support (including large guarantee of GM's car warrantees) would ensure GM could remain functioning during restructuring
- Auto companies felt they couldn't function in bankruptcy
- Summers thought with sufficient support, bankrupt auto company could function—**no reason debt needed to be paid in full**

### April 2: G20 and FASB Announcements

**First announcement: G20 IMF funding expansion**
- G20 agreed to **greater than expected increase to IMF funding**
- Immediately provide **$250 billion** in additional IMF financing
- Aim to eventually add **up to $500 billion** in new lending capacity
- Would combine with IMF's existing ~$250B liquid resources
- Combination of dramatically expanded IMF lending capacity and more flexible terms expected to **dramatically reduce immediate liquidity needs** of emerging-market countries
- **Emerging currencies soared** following announcement

**Second announcement: FASB eases mark-to-market rules**
- **Financial Accounting Standards Board** passed two proposals to ease mark-to-market accounting
- Changes gave banks **more discretion in reporting value of mortgage securities**
- Markets embraced the move
- Dalio's view at the time: **relatively little impact** on banks' abilities to write off losses over time, while relieving some (but not all) accounting pressures on insurance companies

### Scale of Government Response

**Unprecedented coordination:**
- Dalio characterized moves as **"an enormous wave"**
- Table shared with clients (April 2009): added up all US government purchases and guarantees announced by then
- **Remarkably, US government was backstopping two-thirds of all debt, about $29 trillion dollars**

### Obama's Hands-On Leadership Style

**Contrast with Bush:**
- **Bush** took hands-off approach, believing team knew best
- **Obama** took hands-on approach—digging into facts, numbers, actively engaged in discussions
- Instituted **presidential daily economic briefing** (analogous to daily national security briefing)
- Every morning: president met with economic team, every meeting about ongoing crisis for first months
- According to **Larry Summers**: president read every word they sent him, very much into understanding approach, why recommended, alternatives being turned down
- **"It was a time when market and economic developments were more important than anything else"**

### Investor Behavior in the Bear Market

**Three broad categories:**
1. **Clobbered investors who reduced risk as fear grew**—sold "risky" assets the more they got clobbered
2. **Clobbered investors with blind faith**—held on or bought more risky assets
3. **Those with good understanding** of what was happening who sold high and bought low—**very few in this group**

**Dalio's position:**
- Had done good job up until that point
- **Didn't want to take on hardly any bets at this stage**
- In 2007 bubble: gap between market pricing discounts and likely outcomes appeared very large
- Now (early 2009): market pricing discounting terrible conditions, range of potential outcomes enormous
- While policy makers making right moves, **whether they would work and what else lay beneath surface in exposures remained unknown**

### The Unprecedented Nature of the Response

> "Like pandemics, D-processes come along very infrequently, so we don't have many to look back on and, in those that we have, this antidote was never administered in this dosage."

**No such thing as getting everything exactly right, especially in eyes of everyone:**
- **Public uproar** over Treasury's actions
- Especially about how "generous" deal was for recapitalized banks
- Criticism that bankers weren't being punished
- Reports that **AIG paid large, previously-committed bonuses** after receiving bailout focused on **Geithner's** knowledge and whether he allowed them
- Infuriated public already upset with bailouts, put Treasury's plans for further action at risk

### The Classic Dilemma

> "As economic pain increases, populist calls to 'punish the bankers that caused this mess' are the norm and they make it difficult for policy makers to take the actions that are necessary to save the financial system and the economy."

- At such times, bankers can want to stop "being bankers" by stopping investing or lending
- Their doing so in midst of crisis **would make the crisis much worse**

**Crisis handling vs. populism:**
- While financial crisis and handling contributed somewhat to rise of populism in subsequent years
- **In the end, saving the system is much more important than striving for precision**
- **Larry Summers** comparison to **battlefield medicine**—it's never perfect, you're going to realize you made mistakes, you're going to look bad even if you do the best possible job
- Dalio: "I can't say this enough: in my opinion, judging the policy makers in this way is unfair. The fact that they do their job anyway, and that they help as many people as they do, is what makes them heroes in my eyes."

**The AIG bonus controversy:**
- Mid-March, peak of controversy: Congress and media publicly calling for **Secretary Geithner's resignation**
- Though he had executed job with great skill, wisdom, and care
- Had they succeeded in forcing resignation or derailed Treasury's bold plans to recapitalize banking system, **bad economic consequences would have been large**

**Geithner on public outrage:**
> "The public outrage was appropriate, and I understood why the President wanted to embrace it, but I didn't see how we could ever satisfy it. We had no legal authority to confiscate the bonuses that had been paid during the boom. We had no power to set compensation for most private firms. We had more authority over firms receiving TARP funds, but we couldn't reduce bonuses to levels that the public might find acceptable without unleashing an exodus of talent from those banks, reducing their prospects of navigating their way to safety. In any case, I thought the public's rage on these issues was insatiable. I feared the tougher we talked about the bonuses, the more we would own them, fueling unrealistic expectations about our ability to eradicate extravagance in the financial industry."

**House response:**
- After what was seen as Geithner's lack of action, House passed bill March 19 putting **90% tax on bonuses** paid by companies receiving government bailouts worth $5B or more
- Scope limited mostly to AIG bonuses
- Sense of distrust for government support among many executives in financial sector (who saw tax as government changing rules after the fact) would be continuing source of tension

### Market Dynamics: Tops and Bottoms

**Fortunately, bottoms in markets and economy were being made:**
- Had things gotten worse or gone on longer, capitalist and democratic system would've been at risk of breaking
- All else being equal: **prices for goods, services, and investment assets go down when rate of buying lessens and go up when rate of selling lessens**

**Key insight:**
> "Tops are typically made when the rate of buying is unsustainable (which is also when people think prices will rise) and bottoms are made when the rate of selling is at a pace that's unsustainable (typically when most people are bearish)."

- In weeks before and after big announcements: pressures eased, signs of economic rebound emerged, markets rallied
- Economic releases first week of April: while economy continued to contract during March, **pace of contraction was slower than expected**
- Charts show major economic stats contracting through March at fastest pace in decades, but **contractions looked to be leveling off and maybe reversing**

**Mid-April rally:**
- Stock and commodity markets around world had rebounded sharply from March lows
- **S&P up 25%**, oil up over 20%, bank CDS spreads fell almost 30%
- But in level terms remained near extremes—appeared more due to **slower rate of selling than pickup in buying**

### Question: Bottom or Bear Market Rally?

**Historical context:**
- There had been classic bear market rallies along the way
- S&P staged **19% rally** over a week at end of October
- **24% rally** over last six weeks of 2008 before giving up gains and hitting new lows

**Uncertainty remained about whether this was the true bottom or another false rally**

<!-- pp. 501-520 -->

## The Beginning of the Beautiful Deleveraging: June–December 2009

### Recovery Phase Begins

- Second half 2009: policies (QE, fiscal stimulus, macro-prudential measures) reduced risk, increased buying of risky assets, economy began recovering
- Shift analogous to previous **"beautiful deleveragings"**
- S&P 500 chart shows recovery from March 2009 lows:
  - $600bn in asset purchases announced
  - Further $1.1 trillion announced and started
  - Market climbed from ~650 to ~1,100 by December

### Inflation Fears Prove Unfounded

- Frequent concerns about inflation from central bank "printing money" didn't materialize
- Fed's printing wouldn't cause inflation acceleration if it was **replacing contracting credit**

> "Reflations don't necessarily cause inflation because they can simply negate deflations, depending on how far they are taken and what the money goes to."

- Inflation is oversimplistic—it's an average of many different things:
  - During reflations/depressions: little/no inflation in labor costs and production assets (real estate, equipment)
  - Inflation in assets benefiting from currency devaluation (commodities, gold, internationally traded goods)

### Regulatory Reform Timeline

Congress and **Obama administration** shifted to significantly increasing financial regulation:

- **June 17**: Obama speech proposing comprehensive financial reform (eventually **Dodd-Frank**)
  - Heightened regulation, consolidation of regulatory bodies
  - Greater authority to Fed, consumer protections
  - More regulation of credit rating agencies
  - Updated bank winding-down rules
  - Bill wouldn't pass until 2010

- **June 24**: SEC proposed money market fund regulations
  - Required highly liquid investments
  - Restricted holdings to high-quality securities

- **June 30**: Treasury proposed **Consumer Financial Protection Agency**
  - Would control consumer protections from Fed, Comptroller, Office of Thrift Supervision, FDIC, FTC, National Credit Union Administration

- **July 23**: Fed proposed changes to **Regulation Z (Truth in Lending)**
  - Improve consumer disclosure for closed-end mortgages and home-equity credit
  - Required APR and monthly payments communicated to buyer on adjustable-rate loans

- **October 22**: Fed proposed review of 28 banking organizations' incentive compensation policies
  - TARP Special Master released executive compensation determinations for top 25 highest paid at seven firms receiving assistance

- **December 11**: House passed Financial Stability Council and Consumer Financial Protection Agency

### Pattern: Post-Crisis Regulatory Tightening

**Changing laws to prevent the crisis is typical at the end of big debt crises.** Then over long time frames (~25 years):
- Hangover wears off, new euphoria sets in
- Laws increasingly flouted
- New leverage forms produced by new entities
- Leads to new debt crisis evolving similarly

## 2010 through Mid-2011

### Economic Disconnect

- Early 2010: financial markets strong (up 65% from March 2009 lows)
  - Flush with liquidity from Fed's QE
  - Safer due to fiscal/regulatory changes
- But economy labored: borrowers weaker/cautious, lending standards tightened

### Markets Price In Premature Normalization

- Credit markets priced in Fed would tighten 2-3 times within the year
- Odd given conditions: unemployment still near postwar highs, wage growth stuck, home prices flat and well-below peak
- Many middle-class mortgage borrowers remained underwater
- Credit standards tightened
- Financially okay borrowers disinclined to lever up; those inclined were financially dead
- Hard to imagine normal pickup

### Global Stimulus Slowdown

- Most central banks/governments slowing aggressive stimulus
- Fed ended first QE round March 2010 after purchasing $1.25 trillion in mortgage-backed securities
- Fiscal stimulus (America Recovery and Reinvestment Act) set to peak later in year
- Abroad: pockets of tightening (China increased interest rates)

### The QE Debate: Flow vs. Amount

**Critically misunderstood**: merely slowing/ending QE was equivalent to tightening—**not different from raising interest rates**

- Some thought pumping money into economy enough to stimulate (Fed had printed over $2 trillion)
- But **flow of money more important than amount**—the flow of asset purchases sustained value increases and lending growth
- Credit growth remained slow
- Fed's stimulus then believed too much and irresponsible
- **Dalio's view**: doubted developed economies would tighten as fast as priced in

### BDO February 17: The Coming Tightening

> "(BDO) February 17: The Coming Tightening: It is now the established view among the electorate, central bankers, and elected officials that central banks printing and buying of financial assets and central governments budget deficits must be reined in because these actions are financially irresponsible. We think that this universally accepted view is at best premature and at worst dangerous….When we take a sharp pencil to these plans and their implications, we conclude that it is too much restraint—unless there is either major pickup in private debt growth or a major realignment of developed and emerging country currencies, both of which appear unlikely to happen in the amounts required."

### European Debt Crisis Emerges

- Pro forma financial projections showed debt crisis brewing in Europe
- Mismatch between: a) borrowing debtors needed to rollover maturing debt, and b) lending banks could provide with already-stretched balance sheets
- **February**: Portugal, Ireland, Italy, Spain, especially **Greece** struggled with debt obligations and deteriorating conditions
- Day-to-day volatility in global markets
- Most assessed issue contained to Greece (potentially Portugal), wouldn't pose larger problems for European monetary system or global economy
- Early February calculation: **problem would be much worse**

### BDO February 4: Tightening + Over-indebtedness = High Risk

> "We judge the over-indebtedness problems of the European debtor countries (PIGS) to be comparable in magnitude to some of the worst emerging-market debt problems of the past."

- 5Yr CDS spreads chart shows spreads widen early 2010 (mainly Greece and Portugal)

### European Policy Mistakes

European debt crisis a different story, but **same sequence of events**:
- Policy makers didn't believe they'd face debt crisis until they had it
- Made same rookie mistakes:
  - Leaning too heavily on deflationary levers like austerity
  - Not printing money
  - Not providing default protections for systemically important entities
  - Until pain became intolerable

### Market Correction: May–July 2010

- US equity market rallied nearly 10% through late April
- Fell over 15% May–July on:
  - Contagion worries about Europe
  - Softness in US economic numbers
- Weakness led to realization Fed likely to maintain 0% interest rate policy
- US bond yields fell over 100 basis points over next four months
- Economic improvement slowed summer 2010
- Labor market showed modest improvement (unemployment claims), but unemployment rate still near highs
- Still lot of slack in economy—weakness at this level would have been terrible

### Bernanke's Jackson Hole Speech (August 2010)

**Bernanke** addressed further QE at Jackson Hole, making clear it was key policy option:

> "a first option for providing additional monetary accommodation, if necessary, is to expand the Federal Reserve's holdings of longer-term securities."

- Emphasized belief QE had been effective and "made an important contribution to the economic stabilization and recovery"
- 10-year break-even inflation rate fallen 50 basis points in months leading to speech
- Reflected concerns of sustained very low inflation or deflation
- After signaling further QE was strong possibility, markets rebounded strongly
- Real economy response naturally lagged but picked up soon

### Fed Officials Signal More Action Needed

**October 2010**: New York Fed President **Bill Dudley** described conditions as "wholly unsatisfactory," argued "further action is likely to be warranted"

- Assessment of US growth drivers broadly similar to Dalio's (from October 1 BDO):
  - Consumers facing slow income growth, lower asset prices, much lower ability to borrow
  - Result of lower wealth, higher debt levels, lower incomes
  - Households hadn't responded to lower rates by saving less or borrowing

### BDO October 6: The Next Shoe to Drop: More QE and Devaluations

> "(BDO) October 6: The Next Shoe to Drop: More QE and Devaluations: What is happening is all very classic. Though they're all different, in most ways deleveragings are basically the same and transpire via a similar sequence of events."

- References to *Daily Observations* and "Template for Understanding What's Going On"
- Won't dwell on full sequence, but key reminders especially relevant:

**All deleveragings** require:
- Declines in private sector credit growth
- Increases in central bank money creation AND central government deficits to offset
- Though many financially conservative and feel printing money to bail out debtors/creditors is unethical:
  - **Austerity to deal with debt-deleveraging has never worked when problems were big**
  - When tried persistently, eventually abandoned by all governments (didn't work, too painful)
  - Decreased borrowing/spending (and employment consequences) make deleveraging self-reinforcing on downside
  - Increased debts/spending that cause bubbles self-reinforcing on upside
- Result: all studied deleveragings (most over last couple hundred years) eventually led to:
  - Big waves of money creation
  - Fiscal deficits
  - Currency devaluations (against gold, commodities, stocks)

### QE2 Broadly Worked

Despite continued Europe debt problems, US economy/markets finished 2010 on high note:
- Growth picked up after brief lull between QE1 and QE2
- S&P 500 had 13% total returns for 2010
- Inflation expectations re-anchored by Fed's proven determination to continue stimulating as long as necessary

### BDO March 15: Transitioning Beyond the "Sweet Spot"

US economy going through post-contraction growth spurt supported by monetary policy, fiscal policy, credit growth improvement. With:
- Considerable slack domestically (and Europe/Japan)
- Overheating demand in emerging countries
- Limited inflation pressures (largely from items demanded by emerging countries)

> "Said differently, 2010/2011 in a cyclical context appears quite like the 'sweet spot' part of the cycle that typically occurs during the first two years of a recovery, when there remains adequate slack and low inflation pressures."

- However, recovery from contraction during deleveraging more dependent on Fed's money printing and central government's fiscal stimulus

### US Financial System Success Metrics

By this point, government programs to support financial system broadly worked. Compared to other countries, US experienced:
- Relatively fast speed at which financial system recapitalized (and TARP capital repaid)
- Relatively fast speed at which they unwound emergency credit programs
- Good overall financial returns on rescue across various programs

### Case Study Endpoint: Q2 2011

End case study here because **Q2 2011**: real GDP returned to pre-crisis levels

- Wasn't end of recovery by any means
- Still plenty of slack and self-reinforcing upward cycle
- Charts show through 2018 (tenth anniversary of 2008 Lehman crisis):
  - Unemployment rate
  - S&P 500
  - RGDP Growth (Y/Y)
  - GDP Gap (showing slack in economy's capacity to produce)

### Debt-to-GDP Projections

- Second set of charts: existing and projected debt-to-GDP ratios 1920 until 10 years from now
- Charts for four sectors:
  - Government (rising to ~140% projected)
  - Non-Financial Business (rising to ~100% projected)
  - Households (declining from peak ~100% to ~80% projected)
  - Financials (declining from peak ~130% to ~80% projected)
- **Note**: numbers don't include non-debt obligations (pensions, health care) which are considerably larger—"another issue to be explained at another time"

### News & Bridgewater Daily Observations (BDO) Timeline

**Historical progression of bubble recognition:**

- **January 22, 2004**: *Home Building Keeps Driving the Economy* (Commerce Dept data, best year for housing in quarter century)
- **April 22, 2004**: *Greenspan Calms Investors On Growth* (Greenspan: deflation threat disappeared, productivity/low inflation allow rock-bottom rates longer)
- **September 26, 2004**: *Next Up on Reality TV: Flipping Real Estate, for Fun and Profit*
  - Quote: "But in markets where prices rise every month, flipping looks like an easy way to get rich."
- **October 20, 2004**: *Mortgage Debt Not Big Burden, Greenspan Says* (Greenspan disputed analysts worried about speculative housing bubble)
- **November 6, 2004**: *When Good Debt Turns Bad* (accumulated debt could become unsustainable, policy makers should admit possibility of debilitating crunch)
- **April 28, 2005**: *Mortgage Applications Up* (people took advantage of borrowing cost decline)
- **May 10, 2005** (BDO): *The U.S. Housing Bubble*
  - Full-blown bubble, major source of US economic strength
  - Popping would have dire consequences beyond equity market fall
  - Selling house at loss traumatic—largest leveraged asset
  - Equity loss can devastate household net worth, paralyze economy
- **May 25, 2005**: *Steep Rise in Prices for Homes Adds to Worry About a Bubble* (Home prices rose more quickly in last year than any point since 1980, Greenspan said national drop unlikely)
- **May 31, 2005** (BDO): *Fed Debates Pricking the U.S. Housing 'Bubble'*

<!-- pp. 521-540 -->

## US Housing Bubble (2007-2008): Market Timeline and Policy Response

### May-June 2007: Early Warning Signs
- **May 25**: Home sales surge dampens rate cut hopes — largest 14-year jump signals continued market stress
- **June 2**: Strong economic data (jobs, manufacturing, inflation) boosts market confidence
- **June 8**: **Treasury yields climb above 5%** for first time since summer 2006 — inflation fears driven by strong global economy override growth concerns; stocks/bonds see steepest three-day decline since February

### Mid-June to July 2007: Subprime Contagion Begins
- **June 15**: Subprime mortgage delinquencies/foreclosures rise sharply in California, Florida, other hot markets
- **June 21-23**: **Bear Stearns hedge fund crisis** — two funds collapse, requiring bailout; early signal of broader subprime exposure
- **July 3**: Implied volatility reaches 10-year lows across currency, bond, commodity markets — "as low as they have been in more than 10 years" (BDO)
- **July 14**: Dow/S&P 500 set record highs despite subprime worries
- **July 16**: **ABX crash** — subprime mortgage debt market deteriorates significantly; triple-A tranches of 2007 ABX mortgages spread from 9bps to 440bps

> "The market for subprime mortgage debt got significantly worse Monday...The Bear Stearns collapse in June hit the low rated tranches; last Friday and Monday the rout began in the higher rated tranches." (BDO, July 16)

### August 2007: Credit Crisis Intensifies
- **Aug 1**: Consumer confidence rises despite mild inflation; personal spending at nine-month low
- **Aug 3**: Stocks fall sharply on credit fears
- **Aug 7**: Fed holds rates at 5.25% despite growing credit anxiety
- **Aug 9**: **BNP Paribas freezes €1.6B in funds** due to subprime exposure — "complete evaporation of liquidity" makes asset valuation impossible; spreads 3+ percentage points
- **Aug 10**: **Central banks inject massive liquidity** — Fed adds $19B against mortgage-backed securities + $19B in reverse repurchase; Bank of Japan adds liquidity first time since crisis began; ECB injects €61B (second day, after €95B prior)
- **Aug 16**: **Countrywide Financial** (largest US mortgage lender) faces bankruptcy risk; taps entire $11.5B credit line
- **Aug 18**: **Fed cuts discount rate 0.5%** — "tighter credit and increased uncertainty have the potential to restrain economic growth"
- **Aug 22**: Major banks (**Citigroup, JPMorgan Chase, Bank of America, Wachovia**) each borrow $500M through Fed discount window

### September 2007: Policy Responses and Market Volatility
- **Sept 1**: Fed Chairman **Ben Bernanke** reassures markets Wall Street won't be left alone with mortgage/credit problems
- **Sept 6-7**: Credit crisis contained (briefly); foreclosure rate hits record
- **Sept 7**: **BDO's "The Bigger Problem"** commentary:

> "What is happening here is bigger than what the world now commonly refers to as the 'credit crisis.' Normally, credit problems occur when borrowers get into a lot of debt/debt flows suffer...But imagine a dynamic in which the credit keeps flowing and debts keep increasing. That is the dynamic that's happening. It is an extension of having too much credit/liquidity, not too little...The American household sector as a whole is now in pretty bad shape (i.e., has a bad balance sheet and poor cash flow outlook), so that pushing more money into its hands will lead to worse not better financial problems pretty quickly." (BDO)

- **Sept 8**: Labor report shows abrupt employment drop; 10-year Treasury yield falls to 4.37% (lowest in >1.5 years)
- **Sept 14**: **Northern Rock** (UK mortgage lender) receives emergency Bank of England financing — represents crisis broadening beyond US subprime to European banks
- **Sept 19-20**: Fed cuts rates; Bernanke calls for tougher mortgage restrictions
- **Sept 21**: **Earnings reports reveal unequal pain** — Goldman Sachs posts 79% profit increase (third-best quarter ever); Bear Stearns earnings fall 61% on hedge fund/subprime losses
- **Sept 24**: **"Moral hazard"** debate intensifies (Financial Times):

> "The term 'moral hazard' originally comes from the area of insurance. It refers to the prospect that insurance will distort behaviour...In the financial arena the spectre of moral hazard is invoked to oppose policies that reduce the losses of financial institutions that have made bad decisions. In particular, it is used to caution against creating an expectation that there will be future 'bail-outs.'"

- **Sept 27**: SEC investigates credit-rating agencies for conflicts in rating mortgage-backed securities

### October 2007: Financial Sector Stress Mounts
- **Oct 2**: Markets rally on hopes credit crisis is over — Citigroup/UBS predict earnings declines
- **Oct 10**: Democrats pass bill requiring **Fannie Mae/Freddie Mac** to channel up to $900M/year into affordable rental housing fund
- **Oct 15**: **Citigroup, Bank of America, JPMorgan Chase create $75-100B "conduit" fund** to buy highly rated bonds/debt from struggling SIVs (structured investment vehicles) — credit markets frozen since early August
- **Oct 17**: **Hank Paulson**: "Housing woes to worsen"; foreigners shedding US securities
- **Oct 22**: China Bank buys $1B stake in Bear Stearns
- **Oct 24**: **Merrill Lynch** reports first quarterly loss in six years after $2.9B write-down increase (total $7.9B) — "Much of the loss and write-down was tied to problems in the subprime mortgage market and writing down the value of collateralized debt obligations"
- **Oct 25**: European capitals report reduced growth forecast (Germany); Bank of England warns markets vulnerable to US mortgage crisis shocks

### November 2007: Recession Warnings and Leadership Changes
- **Nov 2**: NY State Appraiser inflated home values
- **Nov 3**: **Citigroup CEO exits** amid losses; auto sector role dwindles, spending suffers
- **Nov 3**: Merrill drops sharply on hints of additional high-risk credit exposure write-downs
- **Nov 6**: **Bond buyers losing confidence** — "accelerating pace of write-downs and credit downgrades in the subprime mortgage area, but they are also starting to question the value of bonds in related areas like commercial mortgages and consumer debt"
- **Nov 7-8**: GM posts biggest quarterly loss; Morgan Stanley takes mortgage hit
- **Nov 10**: Major banks (Wachovia, Bank of America, JPMorgan) warn of continuing credit losses; Barclays denies massive write-down speculation
- **Nov 20**: Credit worries drive down stock markets
- **Nov 24**: **Federal Reserve forecast**: no recession coming despite severe housing slowdown — "If the Fed is right, and the economy does stay out of recession, with the unemployment rate barely rising at all, then it will be the first time ever that a housing slowdown this severe has not coincided with a recession"

### December 2007: Coordinated Central Bank Action
- **Dec 3**: Bush mortgage relief effort expected to help only small share of at-risk borrowers
- **Dec 11**: Bank of America closes multibillion-dollar high-yield fund after investor withdrawals
- **Dec 11-12**: **Fed cuts rate 0.25%**; announces **"most aggressive infusion of capital into the banking system since the terrorist attacks of September 2001"** (coordinated with North American/European central banks)
- **Dec 13**: Three big banks (Citigroup, Merrill Lynch, Bear Stearns) see no relief as write-offs mount
- **Dec 19**: **ECB makes $500B infusion**
- **Dec 19**: Fed **approves plan to curb risky lending** — broad restrictions on exotic mortgages/high-cost loans for weak-credit borrowers
- **Dec 21**: **MBIA** (biggest bond insurer) shares fall 26% after disclosing billions in complex debt guarantees; Standard & Poor's downgrades outlook to negative for four companies including MBIA
- **Dec 22**: Major fund to prop up securities scrapped
- **Dec 24**: Merrill gets $6.2B cash injection
- **Dec 31**: **Markets end 2007 lower** — first time since 2002 bear market, Treasuries outperform S&P 500; including dividends/interest, S&P returned 5.5% while Merrill Lynch government-backed debt index returned 8.5%

### January-March 2008: Recession Fears and Emergency Interventions
- **Jan 2**: Manufacturing unexpectedly shrinks (December) — revives recession fears
- **Jan 4**: Unemployment surges to 5% (December) — smallest monthly increase (18,000 jobs) in four years
- **Jan 15**: Citigroup announces $9.8B quarterly loss; retail sales fall
- **Jan 17**: MBIA/Ambac downgrade rumors trigger 300+ point Dow plunge
- **Jan 18**: **Bush proposes $145B economic aid package** to keep economy "fundamentally strong"
- **Jan 21**: Stocks plunge worldwide on US recession fears
- **Jan 22**: **Fed cuts 0.75%** — biggest short-term cut since October 1984
- **Jan 23**: Fed emergency action stems global market sell-off
- **Jan 31**: Fed cuts again as $161B stimulus plan advances
- **Feb 5**: Dow drops 370 points on weak business survey — ISM non-manufacturing sector contracted (January) for first time since March 2003
- **Feb 11**: White House predicts economy escapes recession; unemployment remains low at 4.9% (more optimistic than Wall Street analysts)
- **Feb 16**: Consumer sentiment falls to 16-year low (Reuters/University of Michigan index) — associated with past recessions
- **Feb 28**: AIG write-down sends to $5B loss
- **Feb 29**: **Ben Bernanke warns** large banks would probably recover but other banks at risk; three small banks failed since summer
- **Mar 6**: Home foreclosures reach all-time high (7.9%) in 2007; mortgage defaults jump to 7.9% from 7.3% (Sept) and 6.1% (Dec 2006) — never risen past 7% since 1979 survey began
- **Mar 7**: Economy loses 63,000 jobs (February) — unexpected, ominous decline
- **Mar 10**: **BDO's "Slipping Away"** warning:

> "As you know we have a vague fear that the degree of levered counterparty positions that have built up over the years creates a kind of house of financial cards. With financial markets making new lows, new problems are popping up. More and more entities are failing on margin calls, and this is flowing through to the dealers who have the exposures when entities fail on margin. Financials were crushed Monday as rumors of liquidity trouble at Bear Stearns flew...Bear Stearns has entered a non-equilibrium situation, as its business, in all likelihood, cannot be sustained at current market prices. Either things are going to get a lot better, or a lot worse for Bear."

- **Mar 11**: **Fed announces $200B Term Auction Facility** — allows financial institutions to borrow ultra-safe Treasury money using riskiest investments as collateral
- **Mar 11**: Dow climbs 416.66 points (biggest gain in >5 years) after Fed liquidity injection
- **Mar 11**: **BDO on required interventions**: "1) providing liquidity to financially strained financial intermediaries, 2) changing accounting rules so that the losses can be written off over adequate time to prevent their ruin and/or material contractions in their balance sheets, and c) improving accounting by making it clear that these actions will keep the financial system operating effectively. The Fed is doing its part. The Treasury, Congress and accounting regulators aren't doing their parts yet."
- **Mar 14**: **JPMorgan/Fed move to bail out Bear Stearns** — Fed provides secured funding for up to 28 days
- **Mar 14**: Stocks tumble on Bear Stearns troubles — "rocked Wall Street's confidence in the fragile credit market"
- **Mar 17**: **Fed approves $30B credit line** to facilitate Bear Stearns takeover; announces **open-ended lending program** for biggest investment firms
- **Mar 17**: **BDO commentary on Fed action**:

> "We think the Fed is doing a great job. We wouldn't do anything different because doing anything different would produce intolerable results. Of course we have moral hazard concerns; we believe the Fed does too. But the line has to be drawn somewhere and we think that it is at that point that the equity of the financial intermediary is essentially gone and/or at the point that the credit problems pass to others -- and that is taking it right to the edge (perhaps a bit too far). While we believe the Fed is acting appropriately, that does not mean that we are confident that things will be all right. That's because the Fed can't do it alone (i.e., as you know, we think we need the accounting changes and they are for others to provide). Also, what probably will be required of the Fed boggles the mind. The good thing is that they now realize how serious the problems are. The question is whether they can move fast enough. As mentioned, an avalanche can be prevented, but it can't be reversed."

- **Mar 18**: Dow surges 420 points on Fed rate cut and earnings
- **Mar 23**: "With the Fed to the Rescue, Stocks Surge" — extraordinary Federal Reserve intervention and wild price swings
- **Mar 27**: Durable goods orders drop sharply (February) — Dow falls 110 points; disappointing to see 1.7% dip after strong durable goods demand (costly items like refrigerators, cars, computers); followed 5.3% January decline

### April-June 2008: Mixed Signals and Ongoing Deterioration
- **Apr 1**: Despite discouraging write-downs ($19B UBS, $4B Deutsche), investors hopeful "the bad news could signal the last of Wall Street's subprime woes"
- **Apr 2**: **BDO warns** "the loan losses are still to come" — losses from old bad loans just beginning
- **Apr 3**: **BDO**: "The real economy is still weak (close to zero growth) and still gaining momentum on the downside. Employment, production, demand, and investment are all weak and weakening."
- **Apr 4**: Unemployment rises after 80,000 jobs cut (manufacturing/construction lead decline — biggest in five years)
- **Apr 15**: Producer prices jump 1.1% (March) — Labor Department reports sharp acceleration from 0.3% February increase
- **Apr 17**: **BDO**: "The Fed's efforts to stimulate the economy...have so far prevented a total collapse of the financial system but have not improved conditions in the real economy"
- **Apr 30**: **Fed cuts 0.25%** for seventh time in seven months; signals pause from additional cuts
- **Apr 30**: **BDO**: "On Wednesday the Fed eased for what is priced in to be the last time in this easing cycle"
- **May 1**: **BDO**: "The Bear rescue was the equivalent of a currency intervention that temporarily reverses the markets but doesn't change the underlying conditions that necessitated the action"
- **May 2**: Fed increases Term Auction Facility to $75B (from $50B)
- **May 9**: AIG CEO **Martin J. Sullivan** concedes company "badly underestimated the extent of the problems"
- **May 21**: **BDO on inflation vs. growth dilemma**: "The headaches for central bankers, and most investors for that matter, come when the growth and inflation aspects of the central banks' mandates give diverging signals...The recent market action and Fed rhetoric suggests there is growing concern about the divergence of growth and inflation"
- **June 2**: **BDO**: "Real events are starting to feed back onto financial events" — S&P cuts long-term debt ratings of Morgan Stanley, Merrill Lynch, Lehman
- **June 3**: Lehman/Merrill/Morgan Stanley downgraded; shares sink on lost confidence
- **June 4**: Lehman Brothers rumors about $4B capital raise/approaching Fed for money cause 100-point Dow plunge
- **June 3**: US manufacturing declines (May) for fourth consecutive month; inflation surges to four-year high
- **June 4**: **Bernanke signals end to rate cuts** due to inflation concerns
- **June 5**: Moody's may downgrade MBIA/Ambac bond insurance arms — could cripple ability to write new insurance
- **June 7**: Oil/joblessness punish shares — worst losses in >2 months; crude spikes >$138, unemployment rises
- **June 9**: **BDO**: "Global shift in inflation expectations" — short rates hammered as central bankers emphasize inflation over growth
- **June 11**: Fed shifts focus to fighting inflation, leaving growth concerns behind
- **June 14**: Moody's reviews Lehman for possible downgrade (citing president/CFO demotion)
- **June 15**: Consumer Price Index rises at 4.2% annual rate (fastest in six months)
- **June 20**: Moody's strips Ambac/MBIA of AAA ratings (impaired capital-raising ability)
- **June 25**: **Consumer confidence drops to 16-year low**; home values fall in 20 metro areas
- **June 26**: Markets advance modestly as Fed leaves rates unchanged
- **June 28**: Oil hits new high as Dow flirts with bear territory — 145-point Friday slide; Dow down 20% from Oct 9, 2007 high
- **June 30**: **BDO's major warning**:

> "Markets are tightening while the economy is deteriorating and financials are in free-fall...the tax refunds and a few other unsustainable sources of money have stabilized spending and the economy, market prices that directly impact the economy have responded in a way that is uniformly restrictive...the bounce in financial stocks that originally conveyed a more positive tone to the financial landscape has largely disappeared. Instead, deteriorating credit conditions in the real economy are feeding back onto the financial system, leaving behind a very big pile of financial institutions whose stock prices are in free-fall and whose market value of leverage is exploding. This high market value of leverage (assets divided by market cap) implies that on the margin, ever smaller declines in the value of their assets will wipe out ever larger chunks of equity value, the classic over-leveraged death spiral. Bigger losses lie ahead and the banking industry does not have enough healthy entities to absorb the dying ones. And, the sovereign wealth funds have lost their appetite for large doses of bank equity. The inadequacy of bank capital combined with the coming need to liquidate ever-larger portfolios of bank assets will further constrain credit growth in the economy."

<!-- pp. 541-560 -->

## US Financial Crisis 2008: July-March 2009 Timeline

### July 2008: Mounting Pressure on GSEs and Markets

**July 20, 2008 - Treasury Secretary Paulson proposes GSE rescue**
- Broad rescue plan for **Fannie Mae** and **Freddie Mac**
- Federal Reserve grants both GSEs access to cheap loans from Fed's discount window
- GSEs critical to global faith in US markets due to perceived government backing

**July 22, 2008 - Markets trading as one**
- Turning point: July 15 marked by $17 drop in oil prices, limits on shorting financials, positive bank earnings
- "All markets are trading as one" - extreme sentiment correlation common when emotions peak
- No reversal in underlying economic conditions evident in July data
- **Henry M. Paulson Jr.** urges patience: housing market must stabilize first before financial stress eases

**July 25, 2008 - Bank failure expectations rising**
- Fed liquidity provision helped avoid disorderly collapse (except **IndyMac**)
- Each near-failure bailout/buyout maintains system liquidation capability
- Market pricing ~4% of financial institutions going bankrupt in next 6 months, implying $600 billion asset liquidation
- Banking sector has only half that amount in free equity capital available
- Limited capacity to absorb credit losses on old loans

**July 29, 2008 - Bank stocks retreat after Paulson announcement**
- Four major banks planning new bond type to aid mortgage market
- Sell-off intensified across all three major indexes after announcement

### August-September 2008: Accelerating Crisis

**August 4, 2008 - BDO: Developed world entering recession**
- Growth rates collapsing at accelerating rates across developed world
- Central banks expected to ease while markets still pricing in tightening

**August 7, 2008 - AIG posts $5 billion loss** as housing troubles persist

**August 12, 2008 - UBS: $43 billion in write-downs**, plans to split main businesses

**September 2, 2008 - Oil plunges to five-month low**
- Commodities sector dragged down, initially lifting stock markets
- Hope that cheaper energy could boost consumer spending

**September 3, 2008 - Mixed markets amid financial sector jitters**
- Bright spots: **Ambac Financial Group**, **Freddie Mac**, **Lehman Brothers Holdings** drew bargain hunters

**September 4, 2008 - Dow Jones plummets 344.65 points**
- Poor economic news confluence, though no single overriding reason identifiable

**September 5, 2008 - Government preparing to place Fannie and Freddie under federal control**
- Senior officials from Bush administration and Federal Reserve brief executives
- Jobless rate rises past 6%, highest since 2003

**September 6, 2008 - Freddie's accounting overstated capital cushion**
- Treasury Department concluded **Freddie Mac** accounting methods overstated capital base
- Government takeover announced hurriedly after advisers reviewed books

### September 7-15, 2008: The Breaking Point

**September 7, 2008 - Relief tempered by deeper economic concerns**
- Fannie/Freddie takeover assured continued credit flow through wounded mortgage system
- Reinforced concerns about US economy troubles
- Highlighted significant reliance on foreign investors, particularly Asia

**September 8, 2008 - BDO: GSE inevitability and Treasury delay**
> "At a big picture level, this is playing out in all the obvious ways... It was long ago inevitable that these GSEs were going to fail... And, it was inevitable that, when faced with this choice, the US government would stand behind its implied guarantee and defacto nationalize the GSE.... Given that the big picture was so obvious, what isn't obvious to us is why the Treasury waited so long before acting."

**September 9, 2008 - Lehman concerns trigger fresh jitters**

**September 10, 2008 - After sell-off, bargain hunters step in**
- Markets ended moderately higher Wednesday
- Investors bought energy, materials, consumer-staple companies but remained cautious about financials
- **Washington Mutual** stock falls on investor fears - nation's largest savings and loan

**September 11, 2008 - Market climbs after bleak beginning**
- Oil price drop helped placate fears about biggest banks' problems

**September 12, 2008 - Federal Reserve summons Wall Street leaders**
- Friday evening: Fed officials召集 major Wall Street firm heads to Lower Manhattan meeting
- Insisted they rescue **Lehman Brothers** and develop plans to stabilize financial markets

**September 13, 2008 - Lehman bankruptcy looms**
- Unable to find savior, investment bank **Lehman Brothers** headed toward bankruptcy Sunday
- Would become one of biggest failures in Wall Street history

**September 14, 2008 - Merrill Lynch agrees to sell to Bank of America**
- $50.3 billion in stock
- Lost more than $45 billion on mortgage investments

**September 15, 2008 - Wall Street turmoil sends stocks reeling**
- Lehman filing for bankruptcy, **AIG** in distress
- Stocks fell almost 5% Monday
- Investors worried consumers and companies would face difficulty getting loans

### September 16-18, 2008: Emergency Interventions

**September 16, 2008 - Fed's $85 billion bailout of AIG**
- Federal Reserve reversed course Tuesday: $84 billion bailout giving government control of **American International Group**
- Decision came only two weeks after Treasury took over Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac
- **Most radical intervention in private business in central bank's history**

**September 16, 2008 - BDO: Fed's balance sheet as new safety net**
> "After allowing the system to go over the edge and seeing the avalanche begin, the safety net is now being quickly patched together via the Fed having to use its balance sheet because there is nothing else in its place. While this is exactly what we would have done if you put us in their position today, it's tragic that this is the position they are in both because the Fed should not be in this position and because it is not clear that taking these actions now will save the day at this late stage."

**September 17, 2008 - Financial crisis enters new phase**
- Many credit markets stopped working normally Wednesday
- Investors frantically moved money into safest investments like Treasury bills

**September 18, 2008 - Vast bailout proposed to stem financial crisis**
- Treasury head and Federal Reserve began discussions with Congressional leaders Thursday
- Plan to become biggest bailout in United States history
- Details to be worked out: likely authorize government to buy distressed mortgages at deep discounts from banks and institutions

**September 18, 2008 - BDO: Great Moves!**
> "The Treasury, Fed, and Congress finally agreed to agree to build the safety net!!!! Overnight central banks added $180 billion in liquidity! Regulators moved against short sellers. Morgan Stanley was frozen and is about to be dealt with, and Goldman isn't far behind, but moves are in the works to deal with them."

### September 21-30, 2008: TARP Development and Market Turmoil

**September 21, 2008 - BDO: Treasury's disappointing move**
> "The newest move to contain the credit crisis (The 'Temporary Asset Relief Plan') is extraordinary in: 1) The breadth of the authority it gives the Treasury, and 2) The lack of specifics it provides. So, it doesn't engender confidence. In fact, on the heels of moves that both happened and didn't happen, it undermines our confidence."

**September 22, 2008 - Markets tumble amid bailout uncertainty**
- Fresh concerns about biggest government bailout in history
- Dollar weakening sparked frantic rush into commodities

**September 22, 2008 - BDO: What markets are saying**
> "The market action Monday for the most part was a disaster for US policy makers. US assets were abandoned, as stocks and treasury bonds declined while the dollar collapsed and commodities surged. This action is consistent with a loss of faith in the US as a reserve currency, and, if it continues, puts the US economy and financial system even more at risk."

**September 24, 2008 - Fed Chairman Bernanke warns**
- **Ben S. Bernanke** described nation's economy as barely limping along Wednesday
- Could buckle if financial institutions don't get $700 billion crutch from government

**September 26, 2008 - BDO: Fed continues liquidity fight, but not enough**
> "The big picture through which we see all the daily news is that we are in the avalanche phase of the deleveraging, and we suspect the steps being considered in Washington now are not nearly adequate to reverse the situation."

**September 28, 2008 - BDO: Plan pretty good, but timing question**
> "The plan allows the government all that we had hoped for in order to restore liquidity, solvency, and confidence, but it is not as forceful or as timely as we had hoped."

**September 29, 2008 - House rejects $700 billion bailout**
- Defying President Bush and leaders of both parties
- Rank-and-file lawmakers rejected plan in revolt that rocked Capitol
- Markets plunging, top lawmakers groping for resolution

**September 29, 2008 - BDO: A credibility test**
> "Today's failure in the House of the bill, whose passage was assured by those supposedly in control, has shined the global spotlight on US decision makers. The question of whether the US can do what needs to get done has been further complicated. In the end, the world's financiers (China, OPEC) will decide whether US policy makers have passed the test."

**September 30, 2008 - Share recovery on bailout hopes**

### October 2008: TARP Passage and Continuing Deterioration

**October 1, 2008 - Credit tensions mount as Senate prepares vote**
- Money markets under tension Wednesday
- Many companies and banks had trouble borrowing money
- Where credit available, typically only on overnight basis rather than weeks/months

**October 2, 2008 - Persistent anxiety over tight credit sends stocks plunging**
- Economy's worsening health and continued credit choking
- Unnerved investors ahead of crucial Washington vote on financial rescue plan

**October 3, 2008 - Bailout bill fails to reassure investors**

**October 3, 2008 - BDO: Horrible market action**
> "Price action around the TARP has been very bad, consistent with our view that the TARP won't be a game changer. Friday's price action in stocks held to the classic buy-the-rumor, sell-the-fact pattern. Except that normally you get a big rally into good news and a selloff after the news is fact, culminating in a net gain...this time...the stock market traded to new lows after the vote became fact."

**October 3, 2008 - 159,000 jobs lost in September**, worst month in five years

**October 6, 2008 - Fed considers plan to buy companies' unsecured debt**
- Program would buy unsecured short-term debt companies rely on for day-to-day activities
- "This facility should encourage investors to once again engage in term lending in the commercial paper market"

**October 7, 2008 - Markets plunge despite hint of rate cut**

**October 8, 2008 - BDO: Supply and illiquidity**
> "You've got increasing government supply with tight liquidity; dealers can't finance inventory, hedge funds can't borrow, foreigners are losing confidence in the dollar as a reserve currency, and the desire for cash is trumping all forms of risk, even the yield curve risk of a treasury bond."

**October 8, 2008 - AIG to get additional $37.8 billion**

**October 9, 2008 - Treasury considering cash injections into banks**
- Having tried without success to unlock frozen credit markets
- Considering taking ownership stakes in many US banks to restore confidence in financial system

**October 10, 2008 - Whiplash ends a roller coaster week**
- Stock market collapsed in last hour of trading Friday
- One of wildest moves in stock market history
- Perhaps fitting conclusion to worst week in at least 75 years
- Dow and broader **S&P 500** both closed down 18% for the week

**October 10, 2008 - Battered money funds find relief**
- Investor confidence in money funds shaken September 16 when losses at multibillion-dollar money fund triggered weeks of withdrawals
- Hardest hit: **prime money funds** (buy commercial paper and short-term assets financing business operations)
- Thursday: both institutional and retail prime funds collected fresh assets

**October 11, 2008 - White House overhauling rescue plan**
- International leaders gathered Saturday to grapple with global financial crisis
- Bush administration embarked on overhaul of own strategy for rescuing foundering financial system
- Two weeks after persuading Congress to spend $700 billion buying distressed mortgage securities
- **New approach: government inject capital directly into nation's banks** - in effect, partially nationalizing the industry

**October 11, 2008 - Bush vows to resolve crisis**
- President sought to present united global front in responding to financial crisis Saturday
- World's leading industrialized countries agreed on common steps to stabilize markets and shore up banking system
- Principles: prevent collapse of important financial institutions, protect depositor savings

**October 13, 2008 - Stocks soar 11% on aid to banks**
- Monday: Dow Jones industrial average ended day higher than it began
- 936 points higher (biggest single-day percentage gain in 75 years)
- Governments and central banks worldwide mounted aggressive, coordinated campaign to unlock global credit flow
- Effort investors said they had been waiting for

**October 20, 2008 - Signs of easing credit and stimulus talk lift Wall Street**
- Tentative re-emergence of trust among lenders (rare commodity of late)
- Raised hopes immediate financial pressures on banks, businesses, municipalities could ease somewhat
- Cushioning blow of likely recession
- Dow Jones industrial average rose 413 points (4.7%)

**October 20, 2008 - Fed Chairman Bernanke endorses new round of stimulus**
- **Ben S. Bernanke** supported second round of additional spending measures Monday to help stimulate economy

**October 20, 2008 - Treasury urging bank mergers**
- Treasury Department hopes to spur new round of mergers by steering some of $250 billion rescue package to banks willing to buy weaker rivals

**October 21, 2008 - Fed adds to efforts to aid credit markets**
- Another bold gambit to restore confidence in financial system
- Fed announced it would provide backstop for short-term debt many money-market funds hold
- Central bank will buy certificates of deposit and certain types of commercial paper funds hold
- Hopes of restoring free flow of credit and easing worries about investments
- **Third program of its kind Fed announced this month**

**October 23, 2008 - Alan Greenspan concedes error on regulation**
- Congressional testimony: **Alan Greenspan** was marquee event for years
- Lawmakers doted on him as economic sage, markets jumped on his words
- Politicians wanted maestro on their side
- Thursday, almost four years after stepping down as Fed chairman: humbled Greenspan admitted he put too much faith in self-correcting power of free markets
- Failed to anticipate self-destructive power of wanton mortgage lending

**October 27, 2008 - White House explores aid for auto deal**
- Bush administration examining range of options for emergency financial help
- Spur merger between **General Motors** and **Chrysler**
- Wanted to provide financial assistance to deeply troubled Big Three Detroit automakers
- Possibly using Treasury Department's wide-ranging authority under $700 billion bailout program

**October 27, 2008 - BDO: Fed continues pushing unprecedented liquidity**
> "The Fed continues to push unprecedented liquidity into the system across a variety of channels. The unprecedented push of liquidity has more than doubled the Fed's balance sheet by increasing their assets and liabilities by nearly a trillion dollars, but it has still not offset the private sector need. The world has accumulated so many dollar debts, and the ability to roll and grow these debts was so ingrained in the financial system's architecture, that the breakdown requires the unprecedented push from the Fed. Nonetheless, the risks the Fed faces in embarking on this course are numerous, and so much of the global financial system lays out of the Fed's reach."

**October 29, 2008 - Concerned Fed trims key rate by half point**

**October 29, 2008 - Zero percent interest rates possible**, some analysts say

**October 30, 2008 - Fed adds $21 billion to loans for AIG**

### November 2008: Obama Election and Continued Deterioration

**November 1, 2008 - BDO: Template for understanding what's going on**
> "We believe that the world economy is going through a deleveraging/depression process that will be quite painful for many people.... Contrary to popular thinking, a deleveraging/depression is not simply a severe version of a recession – it is an entirely different process."

**November 4, 2008 - Obama sweeps to historic victory**
- According to early exit poll data: 62% of voters said economy was top concern
- All other issues (terrorism, Iraq war) far behind
- With strong majorities in Congress, **President-elect Obama** likely to start fast with large economic-stimulus package

**November 7, 2008 - Jobless rate at 14-year high** after October losses

**November 7, 2008 - BDO: Creating liquidity but failing to create credit**
> "Essentially, through some asset purchases and a series of swaps, the Fed has exchanged T-bills for other more illiquid, lower grade and longer duration credits, and through the process has provided many key entities with short term liquidity. But it hasn't been able to get a privately-funded credit expansion going because it is uneconomic for creditors to lend, especially when they are squeezed. Without a credit expansion, the deleveraging/depression will continue until ultimately there will be a global debt restructuring (i.e., diminishing the size of creditors' claims on debtors)."

**November 10, 2008 - AIG secures $150 billion assistance package**

**November 10, 2008 - Fannie Mae loses $29 billion on write-downs**

**November 11, 2008 - Oil prices drop to 20-month low**

**November 11, 2008 - BDO: Thrilled with Fed's management**
> "While the Fed sowed the seeds of this crisis by allowing credit growth to be fast enough to cause rapid deteriorations in Americans' balance sheets (at first under Greenspan and then under Bernanke), and the Treasury allowed the deleveraging crisis to move beyond that which was manageable, the Fed behaved superbly once the deleveraging crisis became apparent to it, thereby mitigating the implosion in credit. It has quietly, imaginatively and aggressively redefined the optimal way that central banks should behave in depressions by essentially replacing, rather than relying on, impaired financial institutions to provide credit to key entities."

**November 12, 2008 - Major indexes fall sharply**
- Financial markets had traded all morning but began sharp slide just before **Treasury Secretary Henry M. Paulson Jr.** appeared at news conference
- Discussed $700 billion financial bailout package
- Government assets would NOT go to buying troubled securities as originally planned
- Instead: buying stock in banks and infusing money into other financial institutions

**November 13, 2008 - BDO: Understanding the changing plans for TARP**
> "Paulson's statements Wednesday and Thursday show another shift in the plans for the use of the TARP. The shift away from directly buying mortgage assets (unlevered) to injecting more capital into banks and now working on other mechanisms that further lever the funds outside the banking system via potentially guaranteeing new securitization vehicles make sense to us."

**November 17, 2008 - Need for bankruptcies and risks of preventing them**
- BDO analysis: Economy's most basic problem is that many individuals' and companies' debt service payments are too large relative to cash flows they produce
- As a result, must go through debt restructurings writing down debts to levels reducing required debt service payments to levels consistent with debtors' abilities to pay
- **Bankruptcy is the most common way of bringing about these restructurings**
- The more bankruptcies we have, and sooner we have them, the quicker we can get this economic crisis behind us

**November 19, 2008 - Stocks drop sharply and credit markets seize up**
