- **S&P 500** fell 6.7%, leaving benchmark down about 52% from October 2007 peak

**November 20, 2008 - New jobless claims reach 16-year high**

**November 20, 2008 - Oil closes below $50**, lowest price since May 2005

**November 21, 2008 - BDO: The balance sheet problem**
> "The thaw in credit markets since the Fed decided to inject equity into banks has been insufficient in preventing the avalanche of credit market selling. The freeze that continues in credit markets is evidenced by the breakdown of basic financing relationships in markets that once taken for granted as arbitrages when financing was available. The economy will be in free fall until new credit is available at rates that make sense given economic conditions, and new credit is unlikely to be made available at such rates while there are so many dislocations to take advantage of first. Today, there is just no willingness to use up balance sheet (create credit) for even arbitrages, much less the financing of new economic activity."

**November 25, 2008 - Fed details $800 billion loan plans**
- Mortgage markets electrified by Fed announcement it would swoop in and buy up to $600 billion in debt tied to mortgages guaranteed by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac
- Interest rates on 30-year fixed-rate mortgages fell almost a full percentage point, to 5.5% from 6.3%

**November 28, 2008 - Wall Street caps 5-session rally**
- Biggest five-day rally in more than 75 years
- Even as investors digested signs of bleak holiday season for retailers
- Stock market closed three hours early after Thanksgiving
- Locked in gains of 16.9% for Dow since rally began November 21
- **S&P 500** up 19.1%

### December 2008-February 2009: Deepening Recession

**December 17, 2008 - OPEC agrees to another cut in production**

**December 17, 2008 - Fed flattens rates, dollar bruised**

**December 18, 2008 - BDO: How investors lost money in 2008 & lessons**
From BDO perspective, most investors lost money because:
1. Much more exposure to beta than alpha
2. Beta exposure much more heavily in assets that do badly during economic bad times (stocks, real estate, bonds with credit risk) than assets that do well in bad times (Treasury bonds)
3. Risk and liquidity premiums rose a lot (happens in bad times)
4. Alphas typically had systematic biases to do well in good times and badly in bad times
- Average "hedge" fund been about 70% correlated with stocks
- Not surprising hedge funds down a lot when stocks down a lot

**December 30, 2008 - GMAC makes it easier to get a car loan**
- Would begin making loans immediately to borrowers with credit scores of 621 or higher (significant easing from 700 minimum score required two months ago)
- Would offer new round of low-rate financing, including zero percent interest on some models

### January-March 2009: Obama Administration Takes Office

**January 2, 2009 - Manufacturing reports show depth of global downturn**
- US manufacturing activity fell to lowest level in 28 years in December
- **Institute for Supply Management** manufacturing index: 32.4 in December, down from 36.2 in November

**January 4, 2009 - Auto industry damage from 2008**
- Each of six largest automakers expected to say US sales fell at least 30% in December

**January 5, 2009 - BDO: Putting the stimulus plan in perspective**
> "Our estimates suggest the lack of both supply and demand for credit will create a hole in the economy that is around $1.2 trillion, and that at least based on what we know now, the government stimulus in 2009 will offset only about 1/3 of that."

**January 9, 2009 - Jobless report sends shares tumbling**
- Stocks slid on news unemployment rates hit highest levels in 16 years as economy slipped further into recession

**January 12, 2009 - Bush agrees to Obama bailout request**

**January 16, 2009 - Wall Street ends higher after new bank bailout**
- Some investors cheered news Friday: federal government agreed to inject additional $20 billion into **Bank of America**
- Absorb as much as $98.2 billion in losses
- More financial bailouts and huge losses at Bank of America and Citigroup were dark omens for direction of financial markets and broader economy

**January 20, 2009 - Obama sworn in as 44th President**

**January 26, 2009 - Senate confirms Geithner for Treasury**

**January 28, 2009 - Bank stocks lead Wall Street rally**
- Reports government considering deal to set up "bad bank" to absorb toxic assets ignited broad rally Wednesday
- Financial companies leading the way

**February 6, 2009 - Markets rise despite report**
- "Not even the loss of 598,000 jobs could dampen Wall Street's soaring mood."

**February 10, 2009 - Secretary Geithner introduces Financial Stability Plan**

**February 10, 2009 - Stocks slide as new bailout disappoints**

**February 10, 2009 - BDO: There wasn't much of a surprise**
> "The key takeaway for the members and staff present at the briefing seems to have been that the Treasury's plan was at its infancy and far from where members of Congress expected it to be."

**February 13, 2009 - Stimulus plan approved by Congress**

**February 18, 2009 - $275 billion plan seeks to address housing crisis**
- President Obama announced plan Wednesday to help as many as nine million American homeowners refinance mortgages or avert foreclosure

**February 20, 2009 - BDO: The eve of nationalization?**
> "While nationalization seems to us the best option it is still extremely dangerous. The goal of nationalization is to re-capitalize these institutions in an acceptable way, while sustaining the basic underlying infrastructure of the financial system."

**February 23, 2009 - 3rd rescue would give US 40% of Citigroup**

**February 25, 2009 - Markets lose gains after bank test details disclosed**
- Reflection of market's recent volatility: stocks fell in early trading Wednesday
- Giving back most gains from 236-point rally in Dow Tuesday
- Rebounded in afternoon as federal regulators announced details on stress tests for banks worth more than $100 billion
- In last minutes of trading: major indexes dipped back into red

**February 27, 2009 - GDP revision suggests long, steep downfall**
- Fourth quarter: gross domestic product fell at annualized rate of 6.2%
- Steepest decline since 1982 recession
- Sharper than 3.8% reported earlier

**March 1, 2009 - US said to offer another $30 billion in funds to AIG**

**March 1, 2009 - Warren Buffett concedes a tough year**

**March 2, 2009 - US Treasury and Federal Reserve Board announce participation in AIG restructuring plan**
- Restructuring of government's assistance to **AIG** to stabilize systemically important company in manner that best protects US taxpayer
- Government's restructuring designed to enhance company's capital and liquidity
- Facilitate orderly completion of company's global divestiture program

**March 18, 2009 - Fed plans to inject another $1 trillion to aid the economy**
- Federal Reserve sharply stepped up efforts to bolster economy Wednesday
- Announcing it would pump extra $1 trillion into financial system by purchasing Treasury bonds and mortgage securities
- Having already reduced key interest rate nearly to zero
- Central bank increasingly turned to alternatives like buying securities as way of getting more dollars into economy
- **Tactic amounts to creating vast new sums of money out of thin air**

**March 18, 2009 - BDO: The inevitable and classic central bank purchases**
> "Rather than being a surprise, today's Fed moves were an inevitable, necessary and very classic step in the D-process. In fact, the way we run our calculations, the Fed's purchases of Treasury securities will end up being in the vicinity of $1.5-$2.0 trillion.
>
> At the big picture level, events are transpiring in the very classic way that happens in depressions and that is outlined in our 'Template for Understanding What's Going On,' except the Fed is undertaking these things earlier in the process than is typical. Those who run the Fed are clearly trying to prevent the debt restructuring phase and go directly to the credit creation phase by doing all the classic debt relief things now. So are the folks in the administration. In addition to printing money, these will include initiatives to encourage credit creation (e.g. TALF, PPIF, etc.), and accounting and regulatory forbearance."

**March 20, 2009 - Financial shares lead the market down**
- Stocks dropped Friday as investors worried about consequences of efforts on Capitol Hill to claw back bonuses from companies that received government bailouts
- Thursday: House of Representatives responded to growing furor over bonuses at **American International Group**
- Passed bill imposing 90% tax on bonuses awarded this year by companies that received $5 billion or more in bailout money
- Senate expected to take up its version next week

**March 22, 2009 - US rounding up investors to buy bad assets**
- Obama administration officials worked Sunday to persuade reluctant private investors to buy as much as $1 trillion in troubled mortgages and related assets from
<!-- pp. 561-580 -->

## US 2008 Crisis Timeline: Recovery Phase (March 2009 - March 2011)

### March-April 2009: Banking System Stabilization

**March 23, 2009** - *Banking Plan Propels Wall St. to Best Day in Months*
- Markets rallied on **Treasury Secretary Timothy F. Geithner's** announcement of expanded troubled asset purchases
- Three programs unveiled could buy up to **$2 trillion in real estate assets and securities**
- Plan more generous to private investors than expected, but put taxpayers at greater risk
> "The three programs...could buy up to $2 trillion in real estate assets that have been weighing down banks, paralyzing credit markets and delaying the economic recovery." –*New York Times*

**March 26, 2009** - Major financial regulation overhaul announced
- Obama administration planned wide-ranging reforms for **hedge funds, traders of exotic financial instruments**
- Potentially strict new government supervision for largest, most freewheeling Wall Street players

**March 27, 2009** - **Bankers Pledge Cooperation With Obama**
- 13 chief executives met with administration for 90 minutes
- Pledged to cooperate on efforts to shore up banking industry and broader economy
- United message: "We're all in this together"

**April 2, 2009** - Accounting rule changes lift markets
- **Financial Accounting Standards Board (FASB)** voted to ease **mark-to-market standards**
- Companies gained more leeway in valuing mortgage-backed securities
- Market prices of securities (particularly subprime mortgages) had plunged to fractions of original prices
- Forced banks to report hundreds of billions in losses
- **G20 Agreement on IMF Funding**: US expected to commit ~$100bn, bringing total IMF resources to $350bn

### April-May 2009: Bank Stress Tests and Capital Raising

**April 21-24, 2009** - Markets rally on **Geithner's reassurances on banks**
- Stock markets closed solidly higher after biggest losses since early March
- **Geithner testimony**: "vast majority" of banks had more capital than needed
- Written testimony stated most banks well capitalized
- **Stress test results** scheduled for release (preliminary on April 22, details May 4)

**May 6-7, 2009** - **Bank Stress Test Results Split Financial Landscape**
- Released by Obama administration Thursday, May 7
- **First stark dividing line** through landscape of American finance
- Of nation's **19 largest banks** (holding 2/3 of all deposits):
  - **Nine received clean bill of health**
  - Banking industry generally in better shape than feared
  - Some banks needed billions in additional capital to withstand deeper economic declines

**May 8, 2009** - **2 Banks Cited in Stress Tests Find Ready Investors**
- Day after stress tests released: **Wells Fargo and Morgan Stanley** raised billions in capital markets
- **Bank of America** laid out plans to sell billions in new stock
- Market validation that banks could raise private capital

**May 18, 2009** - **Geithner Says He Favors New Policies, Not Pay Caps**
- Treasury Secretary opposed pay caps on institutions receiving federal bailouts
- Should instead set **policies discouraging excessive risk-taking**
- Rejected hard caps in favor of structural incentives

**May 20, 2009** - **Fed Considered Increasing Its Purchase of Debt**
- Federal Reserve debated expanding program to buy **mortgage and Treasury securities**
- Goal: keep interest rates in check and heal credit markets

**May 20, 2009** - **Bank Raised Billions, Geithner Says**
- Country's biggest banks made moves to bolster balance sheets by **~$56 billion**
- Since government disclosed stress test results two weeks prior

### June 2009: Exit from Crisis Programs and GM Bankruptcy

**June 1, 2009** - **Obama Is Upbeat for G.M.'s Future**
- President Obama marked **lowest point in General Motors' 100-year history** (bankruptcy filing Monday)
- Barely mentioned bankruptcy, instead focused on second chance GM would have with more government aid
- Optimistic framing of historic corporate failure

**June 4, 2009** - Mixed employment signals
- **Jobless Claims Decline Slightly, the First Time in 20 Weeks**
- Small decline in unemployment insurance rolls
- Still significantly above levels associated with healthy economy

**June 5, 2009** - **Hints of Hope Even as Jobless Rate Jumps to 9.4%**
- American economy shed **345,000 jobs in May**
- Unemployment rate spiked to **9.4%**
- Losses far smaller than anticipated
- Labor Department described as "unambiguous sign of improvement, yet also clear evidence of broadening national distress"

**June 9, 2009** - **10 Large Banks Allowed to Exit U.S. Aid Program**
- Major milestone in bank rescue effort
- 10 big banks allowed to **repay federal aid** sustained through worst of crisis
- Bank holding companies including **American Express, Goldman Sachs, JPMorgan Chase, Morgan Stanley**
- Plan to return combined **$68.3 billion**

### June-July 2009: Regulatory Reform and Continued Weakness

**June 15, 2009** - **Shares in Retreat on Fear of Slow, Late Recovery**
- Wall Street took cover in broad sell-off
- Investors faced prospect recovery could be slow and long way off
- Two new reports underscored difficult times ahead

**June 17, 2009** - **Financial Regulatory Reform** announcement
> "While this crisis had many causes, it is clear now that the government could have done more to prevent many of these problems from growing out of control and threatening the stability of our financial system...We must act now to restore confidence in the integrity of our financial system...put our economy on track to a sustainable recovery." –*US Treasury Press Release*

**July 2, 2009** - **Joblessness Hits 9.5%, Deflating Recovery Hopes**
- American economy lost **467,000 more jobs in June**
- Unemployment rate edged to **9.5%** (sobering indication)
- **Longest recession since the 1930s** had yet to release its hold

### August-October 2010: Debates Over Continued Stimulus

**July 14, 2010** - **The Template and the Slowdown**
- **BDO observation**: Long-term debt cycles transpire slowly over a lifetime
- Most people haven't experienced many, unlike business cycles
> "We suspect that the most important difference between our views and others concerns the long-term debt cycle. As long-term debt cycles transpire slowly—essentially over a lifetime—most people haven't experienced many of them, unlike the business cycle which most of us have seen many of. So, while recessions are well understood, deleveragings are not well understood." –*BDO*

**July 29, 2010** - **More Will Likely Be Necessary From the Fed**
- **BDO**: Monetization should not be viewed as light switch
- Should be viewed like spigot controlling flow in degrees
- Works similar to interest rate cuts or putting foot on car accelerator
- Judge right amount by watching reactions
> "When doing either, you judge the right amount primarily by watching the reactions. When things start to pick up, you start to let off. When things start to slow down, you start to press the pedal harder." –*BDO*

**August 10, 2010** - **Fed Move on Debt Signals Concern About Economy**
- Federal Reserve officials acknowledged confidence in recovery had dimmed
- Moved to keep interest rates low and encourage economic growth
- Signaled more aggressive measures could follow if job market and indicators continued to weaken

**October 1, 2010** - **In Comments, Fed Officials Signal New Economic Push**
- Federal Reserve officials signaled preparedness for new economic stimulus

**October 14, 2010** - **ECB Policy** warning
- **BDO**: Overall growth rates decelerating
- Debt problems on periphery remain intense weight on growth
- Fiscal policy of austerity clearly a drag
- ECB "normalizing" its balance sheet (letting longer-term lending facilities roll off, decreasing asset purchases)
- **Pushing up short-term interest rates and the euro**
> "This de facto tightening of monetary policy seems like a mistake to us, and we suspect will cause conditions to deteriorate (likely further pressuring peripheral credits) and eventually cause the ECB to reverse course (pushing down rates and probably the euro)." –*BDO*

### November 2010 - February 2011: QE2 and European Concerns

**November 3, 2010** - **Fed to Spend $600 Billion to Speed Up Recovery**
- Federal Reserve moved Wednesday to jolt economy into recovery
- **Bold but risky plan to pump $600 billion into banking system**
- Getting ahead of political battles over next two years

**November 12, 2010** - **Shares and Commodities Fall on Currency Concerns**
- Stocks fell Friday, commodity prices declined
- Reflecting concerns about global issues and possibility of slower economy in China
- Investors reacting to signs of financial pressures in Europe
- China's higher-than-forecast inflation rate (4.4% in October) could lead to economy-slowing measures

**November 18, 2010** - **Stocks Surge Worldwide on the Prospect of a Rescue for Ireland**
- Stocks in US rose Thursday after Ireland indicated seeking billions in aid from international lenders
- Eased concerns about health of Europe's financial system
- Helped buoy investor sentiment globally

**January 3, 2011** - **Wall Street Starts Year With a Surge**
- First day of trading of year: broader market reached highest level since 2008
- Led by gain of more than 2% in financial shares
- **Bank of America** up more than 6% after announcing **$1.34 billion net cash payment to Fannie and Freddie Mac** ($1.28 billion to buy back troubled mortgages on December 31)
- Tackled issue overshadowing the markets

**February 8, 2011** - **Fed Casts A Wide Net In Defining Systemic Risk**
- Federal Reserve took comprehensive view of types of companies deemed essential to financial system
- Should be subjected to greater oversight
- Dodd-Frank legislation required proposal outlining initial criteria for identifying **"systemically important financial institutions"**
- Collapse would pose serious threat to economy

**March 10, 2011** - **Increasing Global Divergences** (BDO analysis)
- **Division of world**: debtors vs. creditors; each subdivided into independent vs. linked monetary policies
- **Debtors with linked monetary policies** (can't print money): experience prolonged years of hardship and economic weakness
- **Creditors who can't stop printing money** (have linked exchange rates): go through extended period of overheating
- Pressures will intensify over next 18 months
- Leading to **cracks and seismic shifts in linkages**
> "As you know, we divide the world into debtors and creditors, and we divide each of these groups into those with independent monetary policies and those with linked monetary policies. We believe that debtors with linked monetary policies (i.e., who can't print money) will experience many years of hardship and economic weakness, and that creditors who can't stop printing money because they have linked exchange rates will go through an extended period of overheating." –*BDO*

- Influences market positioning in **credit spreads, yield curves, currencies, commodities, equities**

<!-- pp. 581-600 -->

## Appendix: Macroprudential Policies

- **Note on data limitations**: Blank charts indicate unavailable data; post-WWII Germany, Italy, and Japan excluded due to data inadequacies

## Glossary of Key Economic Terms

### Balance of Payments and Currency Terms

- **Balance of payments**: Net transactions (goods, services, financial assets, payments) between a country/currency and rest of world
  - Worsening balance of payments = inflows decline relative to expenditures (like family financial condition deteriorating)
  - Improving balance of payments = reverse dynamic

- **Balance-of-payments crisis**: Country entities lack adequate buying power in world markets to meet needs; essentially run out of cash and credit

- **Capital inflows/outflows**: Cross-border money/credit movement for capital/investment assets (bonds, currency, equities, factories)
  - Foreigners buying/selling country's assets = 'inflows'
  - Domestic players buying/selling foreign assets = 'outflows'

- **Currency peg**: Exchange rate policy fixing currency value to another currency, currency mix, or asset (e.g., gold)

- **Current account balance**: Exports minus imports plus net income receipts
  - Essentially net income (income minus expenses)
  - Deficit means expenses exceed income, requiring capital transactions (borrowing/selling equity) to make up difference

- **FX (Foreign exchange)**: Exchange rate between currencies

- **FX debt**: Debt denominated in currency other than investor's home country

- **Real FX**: Imprecise measure of currency cheapness/expensiveness based on relative currency and price levels versus past
  - Positive real FX = more expensive currency
  - Negative = cheaper currency
  - Usually measured versus trade partners (trade-weighted index/TWI)

- **Reserves**: Country's holding of foreign currency/gold savings used to make purchases and affect supply, demand, price of own currency

### Debt and Crisis Terms

- **Bubble**: Self-reinforcing, unsustainable rises in debt, asset prices, growth
  - Key word: "unsustainable" — temporary boom conditions followed by bust
  - Like borrowing heavily for expensive lifestyle; continues near-term but unsustainable, results in bad times when adjustment happens

- **Depression**: Severe economic downturn with self-reinforcing declines in asset prices and growth
  - Most classically occurs when central banks limited in ability to ease monetary policy

- **Debt service**: Cost of maintaining debts over given period (interest + principal payments)

- **Deleveraging**: Process of reducing debt burdens

- **Deleveraging attribution**: **Bridgewater analysis** decomposing debt burden changes
  - Black dot = annualized change in debt as % of GDP over period (positive = increased debt, negative = decreased)
  - Shows factors that increased debt burdens (above 0) and decreased burdens (below 0)
  - Displays GDP factors (inflation, real growth) and debt factors (borrowing to cover interest/new borrowing)
  - **Note**: Attribution methodology differs between countries based on data availability

### Growth and Economic Measurement

- **GDP (Gross domestic product)**: Total value (price × quantity) of all final goods/services produced in country
  - Most common means of representing economy size
  - Other economic concepts often expressed as % of GDP for context

- **GDP gap**: Imprecise measure of whether economy operates at high/low capacity
  - Based on difference between current production vs. sustainable level over longer period (economy's "potential")
  - Negative GDP gap = slack (e.g., factories not at full capacity)
  - Positive GDP gap = very little slack
  - Often called "output gap" or "slack"

- **Nominal growth**: Change in value (price × quantity) of what country produces (GDP)
  - "Nominal" = includes inflation cases, as opposed to real growth

- **Real growth**: Imprecise measure of change in quantity of goods/services produced (inflation-adjusted, not nominal)

- **Real GDP**: Imprecise measure of quantity of goods/services produced (vs. total value influenced by inflation)

- **Potential**: Imprecise measure of production level economy can sustain when operating near capacity
  - GDP gap represents whether current production higher/lower than potential

### Inflation and Interest Rates

- **Core inflation**: Inflation excluding especially volatile goods (e.g., commodities)

- **Real interest rates**: Interest rates adjusted to remove inflation effects
  - Negative real interest rate = inflation exceeds interest earned, meaning lenders lose buying power over time

- **Liquidity**: Measure of money/credit scarcity vs. ready availability
  - Low liquidity = money/credit scarce, creditworthy borrowers pay higher rates
  - High liquidity = creditworthy borrowers borrow easily at lower rates

- **Long rate**: Interest rates on longer-term debt (typically 10-year government bond yield shown)

- **Short rate**: Interest rates on lending for very short periods (usually 3 months or less)

### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

- **Easing/Stimulation**: Central bank monetary policy or government fiscal policy making money/credit more available
  - Methods: lowering interest rates, printing money, changing regulations, changing spending/taxation/regulations

- **Tightening**: Policy reducing money/credit availability, slowing economic growth
  - Methods: increasing interest rates, allowing money supplies to shrink, cutting government spending, changing rules to restrict bank lending

- **Fiscal balance**: Whether government spending exceeds tax revenue
  - Deficit = spending exceeds earnings (must borrow or spend down savings)
  - Surplus = earning more than spending

- **Money 0 (M0)**: Total amount of printed money in currency
  - Based on physical currency in circulation plus reserves held at central bank
  - Also referred to as M0

- **Reflation**: Instances when monetary policy easy/stimulative and helps produce economic recovery

### Yield Curve

- **Yield curve**: Difference between shorter-term and longer-term interest rates
  - Inverted yield curve = short rates above longer-term rates (short-term rates priced to fall)
  - Normal = short rates below longer-term rates (short-term rates priced to rise)

## 48 Debt Crises

### Methodology and Purpose

- Section examines 48 debt crises systematically screened across major countries over last century
- Criteria: Real GDP decline >3% + triangulation against IMF work and prominent academics
- **Not comprehensive** of all debt crises, but good sample highlighting key similarities (from Part 1) and differences

### Case Analysis Format

- Each case includes computer-generated text analysis + charts showing basic stats
- "Auto-text" comments = simplified version of algorithmic analysis
- Provided to show how viewing through simplified lens (based on Part 1 template) reveals important patterns
- Contrasts with more complete details in Part 2
- Helps see principle-level commonalities and differences from "Archetypal Big Debt Crisis" template

## United States 1926-1936 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase (1926-1929)

- **Classic deflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1926-1936

- Self-reinforcing cycle: rising debt + strong equity returns + strong growth
- **Debt peaked at 125% of GDP** (pre-crisis)
  - Debt in domestic currency, majority domestically owned
- Growth strong at 3%; economic activity high (GDP gap peaked at 13%)
- **Equities averaged 31% annualized returns** over bubble period
- Policy makers initiated moderate tightening (short rates rising ~250 bps)
- Bubble pressures + tightening money/credit = unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (1929-1933)

- Dynamic turned → self-reinforcing bust and "ugly deleveraging" (1929-1933)

- High debt levels left US vulnerable to shock: **1929 stock market crash**

- **Self-reinforcing declines**:
  - GDP fell 26%
  - Stock prices fell 84%
  - Home prices fell 24%
  - Unemployment increased 23%

- Financial institutions under considerable pressure

- **Paradox**: Even though US needed deleveraging, debt as % GDP rose 98% (26% annualized)
  - Driven by falling real incomes, deflation, interest payments financed with new debt

- **Deleveraging attribution chart** composition:
  - Mix of falling real incomes, deflation, interest payments financed with new debt drove debt/GDP increase

### The Reflation Phase (1933-1936)

- After longer-than-average bust, policy makers provided enough stimulation for "beautiful" deleveraging/reflation (began 1933)

- **Monetary policy**:
  - Government broke peg to gold
  - M0 increased 6% of GDP
  - Interest rates pushed down to 0%
  - Real FX averaged -5% during stimulative phase

- **Aggressive financial institution management**: Pulled 8 out of 9 classic policy levers
  - Provided liquidity
  - Directly purchased troubled assets

- Stimulation brought nominal growth well above nominal interest rates
  - Growth averaged 8% during period
  - Sovereign long rates fell to 3%

- **Unemployment** declined 14%

- **Debt as % GDP fell 70%** (21% annualized) per attribution chart

- **"Beautiful" period mechanics**: Debt-to-income ratio reduction driven primarily by rising real incomes, secondarily by inflation

- **Recovery timeline**:
  - 7 years before real GDP reached prior peak
  - Equity prices in USD terms recovered within 25 years

### Political Impact

- Crisis had notable impact on US politics
- Set stage for **FDR** (considered populist leader) to take power

### Chart Deck Components

**Gauges** (rough measures of bubble/depression conditions and monetary tightness/easiness):
- Difference from zero = extent of bubble
- Crossing above/below zero = shifting into/out of bubble

**Bubble & Depression Gauge**: Shows bubble peak 1929, depression trough ~1934, reflation recovery through 1939

**Tightening & Easing Gauge**: Peak tightening 1929, peak easing ~1934-1935

**Core Inflation (Y/Y)**: Fluctuated 0-4% pre-crash, plunged to -10% by 1933, recovered to 4% by late 1930s

**Indebtedness Charts**:
- Total Debt peaked ~125% GDP (1933), Debt Service peaked ~225% GDP
- FX Debt: No data available
- Change in Debt-GDP Ratio: Spiked to +30% annualized ~1933, fell to -20% during reflation

**Monetary and Fiscal Policy**:
- Nominal Short Rate: Rose to ~5.5% by 1929, crashed to near 0% by 1934
- Money0 Level: Rose from ~7% GDP (1929) to ~13% GDP (1939)
- Fiscal Balance: Went from +1% GDP surplus to -5% GDP deficit during depression

**Economic Conditions**:
- Real GDP (Indexed): Rose to ~100 (1929), fell to ~75 (1933), recovered to ~110 (1939)
- Real Growth (Y/Y): Swung from +8% to -12%
- GDP Gap: Peaked ~+15% (1929), fell to -20% (1933)
- Core Inflation: Fell from ~+4% to -10%
- Nominal Long Rate vs. Nominal Growth: Long rate stable ~4%, nominal growth swung wildly -30% to +20%

**Real Short Rate**: Rose to ~13% during deflationary crash, fell to -3% during reflation

**Markets**:
- Equity Price USD (Indexed): Rose to ~120 (1929), crashed to ~20 (1933), partially recovered to ~60 by 1939
- Nominal Long Rate: Declined from ~4.5% to ~2.5%
- Nominal Short Rate: Declined from ~5.5% to near 0%

<!-- pp. 601-620 -->

## United Kingdom 1927–1936: Deflationary Deleveraging Case Study

### Overview: Classic Deflationary Cycle

- UK experienced **classic deflationary deleveraging** 1927-1936
- Unlike other cases, UK lacked broad-based domestic bubble but was **tied to other economies experiencing bubble conditions**
- Built substantial debt stock: **210% of GDP** before crisis
- Debt denominated in **domestic currency**, majority owned domestically
- Shock came externally: ripples from **US stock crash** and early Great Depression

### The Bubble Phase (Pre-1929)

#### Limited Domestic Bubble, High Debt Vulnerability

- No broad-based bubble in UK itself
- Connected to other markets with bubble-like conditions
- Debt reached 210% of GDP—a critical vulnerability threshold
- Debt composition: domestic currency, domestically owned

### The Depression Phase (1929-1931)

#### "Ugly Deleveraging" Dynamics

- Depression ran 1929-1931: **self-reinforcing bust**
- External shock: US stock crash + early Great Depression
- **GDP fell 10%**
- **Stock prices fell 61%**
- **Unemployment rose 7%**

#### Debt-to-GDP Paradox During Depression

- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt/GDP rose 13%** (6% annualized)
- Primary drivers:
  - **Interest payments** financed with new debt (main driver)
  - **Falling real incomes** (secondary factor)
- Partially offset by paying down existing debt
- Depression attribution shows interest + monetization dominated the debt increase

### The Reflation Phase (1931-1936)

#### Policy Response: Breaking the Gold Peg

- Policymakers achieved **"beautiful deleveraging"** after shorter-than-average bust
- Key monetary policy: **broke peg to gold**
- **M0 increased 2% of GDP**
- **Interest rates pushed to 0%**
- **Real FX depreciated -8%** during stimulative phase

#### Policy Effectiveness: 1 Out of 9 Levers

- UK **not aggressive** in managing financial institutions and bad debts
- Used only **1 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- Other stimulative measures helped bring nominal growth above nominal rates

#### Recovery Outcomes

- **Nominal growth averaged 4%** during reflation period
- **Sovereign long rates fell to 3%**
- **Unemployment declined 8%**
- **Debt/GDP fell 29%** (5% annualized)

#### Deleveraging Attribution: Reflation Period

- Primary driver: **paying down existing debt**
- Secondary: **rising real incomes**
- Partially offset by: **interest payments financed with new debt**
- Took **5 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **Equity prices in USD** recovered within **8 years**

### Gauges and Metrics

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge

- Composite measure of bubble/depression conditions + money/credit tightness
- Pre-crisis: moderate bubble reading (~0.5)
- 1929-1931: sharp drop to peak depression (~-3.0)
- Post-1931: gradual reflation back toward zero

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge

- Pre-crisis: peak tightening conditions (~1.0+)
- Crisis period: sharp easing
- 1934: reached **peak easing** (~-1.0)
- Then gradual return toward neutral

### Chart Deck Data Summary

#### Indebtedness

- Total debt peaked ~210% GDP (1929)
- Debt service peaked ~25% GDP (1929)
- Both metrics declined steadily through reflation phase
- No FX debt data available for this period

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

- **Nominal short rate**: fell from 7% peak to <1% by mid-1930s
- **Money (M0) level**: increased from ~12% GDP to ~14% GDP
- **Fiscal balance**: positive surplus throughout (~1-2% GDP), gradually declining

#### Economic Conditions

- **Real GDP**: V-shaped recovery, surpassed pre-crisis level by 1934
- **GDP gap**: -10% trough in 1932, recovered to near-zero by 1936
- **Core inflation**: highly volatile, -4% to +5% range, turned positive mid-1930s
- **Nominal growth vs. long rate**: growth initially collapsed below rates, then recovered above by mid-1930s

#### Markets

- **Equity prices (USD)**: 61% decline trough-to-peak, recovered to ~70 by 1939 (from ~110 peak)
- **Yield curve (SR-LR)**: inverted (+2%) at peak, normalized to -3% during reflation
- **Real FX TWI**: volatile, -25% trough during crisis, recovered to near-zero
- **FX return for foreign investors**: sharp drop during crisis (to ~75), recovered to ~110+

#### External Position

- **Gold reserves**: fell from ~35 troy oz to ~20 during crisis, then surged to ~50+ by 1939
- **Current account**: positive throughout, ~1% GDP average
- **Imports/Exports**: both declined as % GDP during depression, partial recovery during reflation
- Capital flow data: not available


<!-- pp. 621-640 -->

## Japan 1925–1936 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase

- **No broad-based domestic bubble**: Unlike many other cases, Japan didn't experience a broad-based bubble domestically, but was tied to other countries experiencing bubble-like conditions
- **Substantial debt buildup**: Debts reached **65% of GDP** prior to crisis
- **Domestic currency debt**: Debt was in Japan's domestic currency, and majority owned domestically
- Connected to global bubble dynamics despite lack of internal speculation

### The Depression Phase

#### The Trigger and Initial Decline (1927-1931)

- **"Ugly deleveraging" period**: Ran from 1927 to 1931
- **1929 global stock crash as shock**: High debt levels left Japan vulnerable when the global market crashed
- **GDP declined 4%**, stock prices fell **47%**
- **Financial institutions under pressure**: Banking system came under considerable stress
- **Debt-to-GDP paradox**: Despite needing deleveraging, debt as % GDP rose **36% (8% annualized)** during depression
  - Debt service burdens increased even as real economy contracted
  - Denominator effect: GDP falling faster than debt reduction

#### Policy Response Metrics

- Japan pulled **3 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- Gauges measured bubble/depression conditions and tightness/easiness of money and credit
- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: Shows shift from modest bubble phase through depression into reflation
- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: Shows peak tightening at crisis onset, then shift to easing during reflation

### The Reflation Phase

#### Policy Intervention (1931 onward)

- **Longer than average bust phase** before successful policy response
- **"Beautiful deleveraging"**: Policymakers provided enough stimulus to turn depression into reflation starting 1931
- **Gold standard abandoned**: Government broke peg to gold
- **Interest rates cut to 2%**
- **Real FX averaged -26%** during stimulative phase (currency depreciation)
- **Somewhat aggressive** in managing financial institutions and bad debts

#### Economic Recovery

- **Nominal growth exceeded nominal interest rates**: Growth averaged **4.7%**, sovereign long rates fell to **3.9%**
- **Debt-to-GDP fell 18% (4% annualized)** during reflation period
- **Deleveraging mechanism**: Reduction in debt-to-income ratios came mostly from **income rising** (higher real growth), not from nominal debt reduction
- Reflation created positive growth dynamics that allowed debt burdens to fall naturally

#### Political Consequences

- Crisis helped set stage for **Hideki Tojo** to take power
- Tojo considered a **populist leader** by many
- Economic crisis influenced Japan's political trajectory toward militarism

### Deleveraging Attribution Charts

**Depression Period (1927-1931)**:
- Real growth: negative (contractionary)
- Inflation: positive but insufficient
- Change in domestic debt: large positive contributor
- Net result: Debt %GDP rose **8% annualized**

**Reflation Period (1931-1936)**:
- Real growth: large positive contributor (blue)
- Inflation: large positive contributor (red/pink)
- Change in domestic debt: positive contributor (green)
- Net result: Debt %GDP fell **-4% annualized**

### Chart Deck Data Highlights

#### Indebtedness
- Total debt climbed from ~35% GDP (1922) to peak ~75% GDP (1932)
- Debt service as % GDP data available but limited
- Debt growth steady at ~4-6% annually pre-crisis, spiked to 10%+ during crisis, then normalized

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- **Nominal short rate**: Declined from 8-10% (1922) to ~2-3% (1932-1937)
- Money supply data: Not available
- Fiscal balance data: Not available

#### Economic Conditions
- **Real GDP indexed**: Shows steady growth through 1920s, dip 1927-1931, strong recovery 1932-1937
- **Real growth Y/Y**: Volatile, ranging from +8% peaks to -6% trough
- **Core inflation Y/Y**: Volatile between -15% and +8%, with severe deflation at crisis trough, then reflation to +5-8%
- **Nominal growth vs nominal long rate**: Crossed in 1931 when growth exceeded rates, enabling deleveraging

#### Markets
- **Equity prices (USD, indexed)**: Climbed to ~100 (1929), crashed to ~40 (1932), recovered to ~75 (1937)
- **Yield curve (SR-LR)**: Flattened from +4% spread to near-flat/-2% by reflation phase
- **Real FX vs TWI**: Depreciated ~20-30% during crisis, recovered somewhat during reflation
- **FX return for foreign investors**: Collapsed ~40% during crisis, stabilized during recovery
- **Gold price (local FX)**: Flat pre-1931, then tripled from index 100 to 300 after breaking gold peg

#### External Position
- **Gold reserves**: Declined steadily from 0.030 troy oz billion (1922) to 0.010 (1932), slight recovery after
- **Imports %GDP**: Fell from ~25% to ~15% during depression
- **Exports %GDP**: Fell from ~20% to ~10% during depression
- **Current account %GDP**: Persistent deficits of -5% to -7% through most of period, improved during reflation
- Capital flow data: Not available

### Key Insights

- Classic example of how **breaking a currency peg** (gold standard) can enable reflation
- Demonstrates that **deleveraging works best through growth**, not austerity
- Shows danger of **debt denominator effect**: debt ratios worsen during contraction even if absolute debt stable
- Political instability often follows severe economic crises
- Currency depreciation was critical tool for restoring competitiveness and growth

<!-- pp. 641-660 -->

## France 1926–1938 (continued)

### Gauges and Measurements

- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: Composite statistical measure tracking bubble/depression conditions
  - Distance from zero indicates extent of bubble
  - Crossing above/below zero shows transition into/out of bubble phase
  - France showed bubble peak in 1929, followed by sustained depression through mid-1930s

- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: Tracks monetary/credit conditions
  - Peak tightening occurred at 1929 crisis onset
  - "Peak Easing" reached ~1937, indicating most accommodative point

### Deleveraging Attribution: Depression Period (1929-1936)

- **Real growth contribution**: Modest positive (blue bar)
- **Inflation contribution**: Major negative drag (pink bar) — deflation hurt deleveraging
- **Change in domestic debt**: Largest positive contributor (green bar) — debt reduction of ~6-8% of GDP
- **Net debt %GDP change**: +1% despite deleveraging needs
- Key insight: Deflation prevented effective deleveraging despite debt reduction efforts

### The Reflation Phase (1936 onwards)

**Monetary Policy Shifts**:
- Government **broke the peg to gold** (devaluation)
- Interest rates pushed down to **2%**
- Real FX averaged **-4%** during stimulative phase (currency depreciation)

**Inflation as Deleveraging Tool**:
- Policymakers **allowed inflation to run high** — averaged **10%** during this period
- High inflation boosted nominal growth and reduced domestic debt burden
- France pulled **3 out of 9 classic policy levers** for financial institution/bad debt management

**Nominal Growth Above Interest Rates**:
- Growth averaged **15%** during reflation period
- Sovereign long rates fell to **4%**
- Positive carry (growth > rates) enabled debt reduction

**Deleveraging Results (Reflation Period 1936-1938)**:
- Debt/GDP fell **37%** (**15% annualized**)
- Attribution chart shows:
  - **Inflation** (pink): Massive negative contribution (-35% to -40%) — inflating away debt
  - **Real growth** (blue): Small positive (~-5% to 0%)
  - **Change in domestic debt** (green): Positive contribution (~20%)
  - Net **-15%** debt/GDP ratio improvement

> "Throughout this 'beautiful' period, the reduction in debt-to-income ratios came mostly from income rising, driven primarily by higher inflation."

- Recovery took **21 years** before real GDP reached prior peak

### Political Impact

- Crisis helped **Leon Blum** (described as populist leader) take power in 1936
- Economic distress drove political transformation

## France 1926–1938 Chart Deck Appendix

### Indebtedness

**Total Debt (%GDP)**:
- Peaked ~70-75% in 1924-1926
- Fluctuated 50-70% range through 1929-1938
- Ended period ~40-50%

**Debt Service (%GDP)**:
- Data shown but scale indicates moderate levels throughout

**FX Debt (%GDP)**:
- No data available

**Debt Growth vs Debt-GDP Ratio**:
- Debt growth (red line): Started ~20%, fell sharply to negative ~-30% by mid-1930s, recovered to ~10-15% by 1939
- Change in debt-GDP ratio (blue): Volatile, ranging from -40% to +30% annualized

### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

**Nominal Short Rate**:
- Peaked ~8% in late 1920s
- Gradually declined through 1930s
- Reached ~2-3% by 1939

**Money Supply (M0) Level (%GDP)**:
- No data available

**Fiscal Balance (%GDP)**:
- Surplus of ~+1% in late 1920s
- Deteriorated steadily through 1930s
- Reached deficit of ~-12% by 1939

### Economic Conditions

**Real GDP (Indexed)**:
- Climbed to peak ~105 in 1930
- Fell to ~85 during depression (1934-1936)
- Recovered to ~90 by 1939
- Never fully regained 1930 peak by 1939

**Real Growth (Y/Y)**:
- Highly volatile: +15% to -10%
- Depression period showed negative growth
- Modest recovery in late 1930s

**GDP Gap**:
- Negative gap (below potential) of -5% in late 1920s
- Improved briefly to ~+10% in 1930
- Turned sharply negative (reaching -5%) mid-1930s
- Exploded to +20% gap by 1939 (unclear if this is rearmament effect)

**Core Inflation (Y/Y)**:
- Extreme volatility in 1920s: -20% to +40%
- Late 1920s spike to ~20%
- Deflation through early-mid 1930s
- Sharp reflation to ~30% in late 1930s
- Moderated to ~10% by 1939

**Nominal Long Rate vs Nominal Growth**:
- Long rate (blue): Relatively stable 5-10% range
- Nominal growth (red): Wildly volatile -10% to +30%
- 1920s: Growth peaks ~30%, rates ~10%
- Mid-1930s: Both compressed to 0-5% range
- Late 1930s: Growth spiked to ~25%, rates remained ~5%

**Real Short Rate**:
- Ranged from -30% to +25%
- Mid-1920s: Deeply negative (inflation > rates)
- Late 1920s/early 1930s: Positive ~10-20% (deflation period)
- Mid-1930s: Dropped to -20% (reflation working)
- Late 1930s: Returned toward -5%

### Markets

**Equity Prices USD (Indexed)**:
- Started ~50 in 1924
- Rose to ~110 peak in 1929
- Crashed to ~30 by 1939
- Sustained bear market throughout 1930s

**Interest Rates**:
- Long rates (blue): 4-5% range, relatively stable
- Short rates (red): More volatile, 1-8% range
- Convergence in mid-1930s around 3-4%

**Yield Curve (Short Rate - Long Rate)**:
- Mostly negative to flat (-4% to +2%)
- Indicates inverted/flat curve during much of period
- Brief steepening episodes

**Real FX vs Trade Weighted Index**:
- Real FX (blue): Declined from -20% to +35% range
- Shows significant volatility and eventual depreciation pressure

### External Position

**Gold Reserves (Troy Oz Bln)**:
- Built from ~0.05 billion in 1924
- Peaked ~0.18 billion in 1932-1933
- Declined to ~0.08 billion by 1939
- Classic gold standard flow dynamics

**Capital Inflows/Outflows (%GDP)**:
- No data available

**Imports (%GDP)**:
- Started ~18-20% in 1924
- Declined steadily throughout period
- Reached ~10% by 1939
- Reflects economic contraction and trade collapse

**Current Account (%GDP)**:
- Surplus of ~+3-4% in mid-1920s
- Deteriorated through depression
- Deficit of ~-3 to -4% by late 1930s

**Exports (%GDP)**:
- Started ~23% in 1924
- Steady decline throughout period
- Reached ~8% by 1939
- Even steeper decline than imports

## United Kingdom 1941–1967 Case Auto-Summary

### Overview

**Classic wartime deflationary deleveraging** between 1941-1967:
- War winners experience brief postwar recession as economy transitions from war production
- Followed by more orderly deleveraging process

### The War Phase (1941-1943)

**Unusual Characteristics**:
- Debt crisis rooted in **WW2 financing**, not bubble dynamics
- UK borrowed heavily to finance large fiscal deficit
- Economy shifted to war production
- Workforce shifted to armed services and war production
- "Economic stats are not reflective of typical economic linkages"

**Debt Structure**:
- Debt denominated in **UK's domestic currency** (pound sterling)
- Majority **owned domestically**
- Provides more policy flexibility

**Economic Performance**:
- **Real growth**: Strong at **6%** (aided by wartime spending)
- **GDP gap**: Peaked at **10%** above potential
- **Equity returns**: Averaged **16% annualized** during war period
- Strong asset returns stimulated growth despite war conditions

### The Postwar Phase (1943-1947)

**Recession Dynamics**:
- Postwar recession ran **1943 to 1947**
- Less severe than for war losers
- Economy transitioned away from war production

**Self-Reinforcing Decline**:
- GDP fell **15%** during postwar adjustment
- Attribution chart shows even though UK needed deleveraging:
  - **Debt/GDP went UP 102%** (**25% annualized**)
  - Incomes declined
  - Government shouldered war-related costs
  - Peak fiscal deficit: **31% of GDP** during "ugly period"

**Gauges**:
- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: War boom peak followed by postwar retooling trough, then reflation
- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: Peak tightening during war, peak easing ~1948
- Composite measures track credit/monetary conditions

### The Reflation Phase (1947 onwards)

**Monetary Policy**:
- Currency devalued versus gold by **30%**
- **M0 actually decreased 8%** of GDP (unusual for reflation)
- Interest rates pushed down to **1%**
- Real FX averaged **-10%** during stimulative phase

**Inflation Strategy**:
- Policymakers **allowed inflation to run high** — averaged **4%**
- Boosted nominal growth
- Reduced domestic debt burden

**Stimulative Measures**:
- Brought **nominal growth well above nominal interest rates**
- Growth averaged **7%** during period
- Sovereign long rates fell to **2%**
- Unemployment rates remained flat

**Deleveraging Results**:
- Debt/GDP fell **139%** (**7% annualized**)
- Attribution chart shows:
  - Reduction came **mostly from income rising**
  - Driven **primarily by inflation**

<!-- pp. 661-680 -->

## United Kingdom 1941-1967 Case Study

### Deleveraging Attribution: Reflation Period (1947-1967)

- **Debt reduction mechanics**: UK debt/GDP fell -7% over 1947-1967 period
- Debt reduction came from three components:
  - Real growth contribution (blue): positive contributor to deleveraging
  - Inflation contribution (red/pink): significant negative real rates eroded debt burden
  - Change in domestic debt (green): actual reduction in nominal debt levels
- **Recovery timeline**: took 10 years before real GDP reached prior peak
- Context: Post-WWII deleveraging through combination of growth and inflation

### UK Chart Deck Appendix - Data Overview

**Indebtedness metrics (1938-1957)**:
- Total debt peaked ~260% GDP around 1948, declined to ~200% by 1953
- Debt service burden peaked ~25% GDP in 1948, fell to ~20% by 1953
- Both total debt and debt service tracked similar downward trajectories post-war
- FX debt data: not available for this period

**Debt dynamics**:
- Debt growth peaked ~30% annual in mid-1940s war period
- Change in debt-GDP ratio peaked ~30% annual around 1948
- Both metrics declined sharply through early 1950s as deleveraging progressed

**Monetary and fiscal policy**:
- Nominal short rates: spiked to 3.5% in early 1940s, held low ~0.5-1% through late 1940s, rose to 2.5% by mid-1950s
- Money supply (M0): rose from ~12% GDP in 1938 to peak ~17% GDP around 1948, then declined gradually to ~12% by 1957
- Fiscal balance: massive war deficits of -30% GDP during 1943-1945, swung dramatically to surpluses of +7% GDP by 1948-1953

**Economic conditions**:
- Real GDP: sharp war boom to 1943, dip in immediate postwar 1945-1947, then strong recovery through 1950s
- Real growth: volatile in 1940s (ranging -5% to +15% annual), stabilized to +3-5% range in 1950s
- GDP gap: peaked at +10% in mid-1940s (wartime boom), fell to -2% trough in 1948, closed by early 1950s
- Core inflation: highly volatile - peaked ~17% in early 1940s, fell during war, spiked to ~14% in 1947-1951 period, then moderated to ~4-6% through mid-1950s
- Nominal growth: reached +24% peak in early 1940s, remained elevated +10-12% range in late 1940s/early 1950s
- Nominal long rates: relatively stable 4-6% range throughout period
- Real short rates: deeply negative through 1940s and early 1950s (reaching -16% in 1947), only turning positive in mid-1950s

**Markets**:
- Equity prices: war trough around 60 (indexed), strong rally to peak ~145 by 1946-1947, corrected to ~80 by 1949, gradual recovery to ~80-90 range through 1950s
- Yield curve (short-long spread): inverted -3% in early 1940s, gradually steepened to -1% to -1.5% range through 1950s
- Real FX trade-weighted index: declined from ~18% in early 1940s to ~10-15% range in mid-1940s, brief spike to +8% in late 1940s, then fell sharply to -20% by mid-1950s
- FX returns for foreign investors: declined from indexed 130 to ~70 over 1938-1957 period - poor returns for foreign holders

**External position**:
- Gold reserves: peaked ~70 troy oz million around 1946-1947, volatile decline to ~35 million trough in early 1950s, recovered to ~50-60 million by mid-1950s
- Capital inflows/outflows: no data available
- Imports: rose from ~14% GDP in 1938 to ~20% peak in 1943, dip to ~13% in 1946, then sharp rise to ~22% peak in early 1950s
- Current account: deteriorated from -5% GDP in 1938 to -17% trough in 1943 (wartime imports), sharp improvement to near 0% by 1948, then -2% to -4% deficit through 1950s
- Exports: collapsed from ~9% GDP in 1938 to ~3% trough in 1943, strong recovery to ~18% peak by early 1950s

**Government and military**:
- Military personnel: ramped from ~1% population in 1938 to ~11% peak in 1945-1946, rapid demobilization to ~2% by 1953
- Military spending: surged from ~7% GDP in 1938 to ~44% peak in 1944-1945, fell sharply to ~8-12% range in early 1950s
- Government debt: peaked ~235% GDP around 1948, steady decline to ~165% by mid-1950s

---

## United States 1943-1951 Case Study

### War Phase (1941-1945)

- **Atypical debt crisis**: unlike typical bubble-driven crises, rooted in WWII financing needs
- **Debt buildup**: government borrowed heavily to finance large fiscal deficits
- Economic structure shifted massively toward war production and armed services deployment
- **Debt levels**: rose to 150% GDP during war
- **Currency denomination**: debt in US domestic currency, majority owned domestically
- **Growth environment**: strong growth at 13% annual aided by wartime spending
- **Economic activity**: GDP gap peaked at 19% (overheated wartime economy)
- **Asset returns**: equities averaged 15% annualized returns during war period, helping stimulate growth

> "For these reasons, the economic stats are not reflective of typical economic linkages."

### Postwar Phase (1945-1950)

- **Recession timeline**: US entered postwar recession 1945-1950 after fighting ended
- **Winner advantage**: postwar slump less severe than for war losers
- **GDP contraction**: self-reinforcing declines, GDP fell 13%
- **Unemployment**: rose 5 percentage points
- **Debt dynamics**: debt as % GDP roughly flat through postwar period despite need for deleveraging
- Attribution chart shows inflation and real growth roughly offset, leaving debt/GDP stable

**Deleveraging Attribution: Depression Period (1945-1950)**:
- Real growth (blue): negative contribution as economy contracted
- Inflation (red): positive but moderate
- Change in domestic debt (green): largest component - actual debt increase of ~10% GDP
- Net result: debt/GDP rose ~2% over period

### Gauges and Indicators

**Bubble & Depression Gauge**:
- War boom peaked at +1.0 in 1944
- Sharp decline through postwar period to trough ~0.0 in 1949
- Labeled "Post-War ReTooling" for 1946-1949 adjustment period
- Modest recovery to ~0.5-0.8 range by 1954

**Tightening & Easing Gauge**:
- Negative/neutral range -0.5 to -1.0 during 1939-1945 war period
- "Peak Tightening" reached +2.3 around 1949-1950
- "Peak Easing" reached approximately -0.5 by 1954
- Shows dramatic policy tightening during immediate postwar years

**Core inflation**:
- First wartime spike ~11% in early 1940s
- Dip to ~3% in 1944
- Second spike to ~14% peak in 1947-1951 period
- Gradual decline to ~1% by 1954

### Reflation Phase (1950-1951)

- **Policy success**: "beautiful" deleveraging achieved through sufficient stimulation
- **Timeline**: reflation began 1950 after relatively long bust phase
- **Monetary policy**:
  - M0 actually decreased 0.9% of GDP during stimulative phase
  - Interest rates pushed down to 1% during stimulative phase
- **Growth outcomes**:
  - Nominal growth averaged 11% during reflation period
  - Sovereign long rates averaged 2%
  - Growth well above interest rates enabled debt reduction
- **Employment**: unemployment declined 2 percentage points
- **Debt reduction**: debt/GDP fell 22% (14% annualized)

**Deleveraging Attribution: Reflation Period (1950-1951)**:
- Real growth (blue): large positive contribution to deleveraging
- Inflation (red): large positive contribution (negative real rates)
- Change in domestic debt (green): positive contribution from nominal debt increase of ~10% GDP
- Net result: debt/GDP fell -14% over period

> "Throughout this 'beautiful' period, the reduction in debt-to-income ratios came mostly from income rising, driven primarily by higher real growth."

- **Recovery timeline**: took 6 years before real GDP reached prior peak

**Central bank strategy**: stayed easy even as activity picked up sharply - accommodative policy brought nominal growth well above nominal interest rates

---

## US Chart Deck Appendix (1938-1957)

### Indebtedness
- Total debt: ~140% GDP in 1938, rose to ~260% peak around 1948, declined to ~200% by 1953
- Debt service: ~17% GDP in 1938, rose to ~25% peak in 1948, declined to ~20% by 1953
- FX debt: no data available for period

### Debt Dynamics
- Debt growth: peaked ~32% annual in mid-1940s, declined to ~5% by 1953
- Change in debt-GDP ratio: peaked ~30% annual around 1948, turned negative by early 1950s

### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- Nominal short rate: volatile in early 1940s peaking ~3.5%, held low ~0.5-1% in mid-late 1940s, rose to ~2.5% by mid-1950s
- Money level (M0): rose from ~11% GDP in 1938 to peak ~17% around 1948, declined to ~12% by mid-1950s
- Fiscal balance: war deficits reached -30% GDP in 1943-1944, swung to surpluses +6-8% in 1948-1953 period

### Economic Conditions
- Real GDP: indexed from ~75 in 1938, wartime boom to ~100 by 1943, dip to ~87 in 1946, strong recovery past ~100 by 1953
- Real growth: reached +15% peak in early 1940s, volatile during war, negative in immediate postwar, stabilized +3-5% in 1950s
- GDP gap: peaked +10% in mid-1940s, fell to -3% in 1949, closed by early 1950s
- Core inflation: peaked ~17% in 1942, fell during war, second spike ~12% in 1951, moderated to ~1% by 1954
- Nominal long rate: stable ~2-4% range during war, rose to ~4% by early 1950s
- Nominal growth: peaked ~24% in early 1940s, remained elevated ~10-12% in late 1940s/early 1950s
- Real short rate: reached +3% in early 1940s, deeply negative to -15% in 1947, volatile through early 1950s returning to -9% by 1954

### Markets
- Equity prices: from indexed ~60 in 1938, war rally to ~120 by 1943, peaked ~145 in 1946, corrected to ~80 in 1949, recovered to ~85 by mid-1950s
- Nominal long rate vs short rate: long rates stable ~3-4%, short rates varied widely creating changing spreads
- Yield curve: inverted -3% in early 1940s, gradually less inverted to -1% by mid-1950s
- Real FX TWI: peaked ~18% in early 1940s, fell to negative territory reaching -20% by mid-1950s
- FX return for foreign investors: indexed from ~130 in 1938, relatively stable ~100-105 range through late 1940s, sharp decline to ~70 by mid-1950s
- Gold price (local FX): indexed steady rise from ~80 in 1938 to ~120 by 1948, sharp spike to ~175 peak around 1951, declined to ~160 by mid-1950s

### External Position
- Gold reserves: ~60 troy oz million in 1943, peaked ~85 million around 1950, declined to ~50 million by mid-1950s
- Capital inflows/outflows: no data available
- Imports: ~14% GDP in 1938, wartime spike to ~20% in 1943, fell to ~13% in 1948, recovered to ~23% by early 1950s
- Current account: from -5% GDP in 1938, wartime deficit -17% in 1943, improved to near -2% surplus in 1948, returned to -3% to -4% deficit by early 1950s
- Exports: ~9% GDP in 1938, fell to ~3% in 1943, strong recovery to ~18% peak by early 1950s, moderated to ~16% by mid-1950s

### Government and Military
- Military personnel: ramped from ~1% population in 1938 to ~11% peak in 1945, demobilized to ~2% by 1953
- Military spending: from ~7% GDP in 1938 to ~43% peak in 1944, fell to ~8% by 1948, rose again to ~12% during Korean War early 1950s
- Government debt: from ~140% GDP in 1938, peaked ~235% around 1948, declined to ~165% by mid-1950s

## Norway 1984–1996 Case Study

### Overview: Classic Deflationary Deleveraging Cycle
- Norway experienced a **deflationary deleveraging cycle** from 1984 to 1996
- Three distinct phases: bubble (1984-1987), depression (1987-1992), reflation (1992-1996)
- Full cycle demonstrates typical debt crisis dynamics in a developed economy with its own currency

### The Bubble Phase (1984-1987)

#### Characteristics of the Bubble
- **Self-reinforcing cycle** of strong growth, strong equity returns, and strong housing returns
- Debt reached pre-crisis peak of **211% of GDP**
- Debt denominated in **domestic currency**, majority owned domestically
- Investment inflows averaged **4% of GDP**, financing current account deficit of 2% of GDP

#### Economic Conditions During Bubble
- Strong growth at **3%** annually
- High levels of economic activity: **GDP gap peaked at 5%**
- Strong asset returns: **housing prices averaged 19% annualized returns** over bubble period
- Capital inflows enabled strong growth alongside current account deficit

#### Policy Response During Bubble
- Policymakers initiated **large tightening**: short rates rose **~700 bps**
- Bubble pressures + tightening money and credit = unsustainable situation
- Despite policy tightening, bubble dynamics continued to strengthen

### The Depression Phase (1987-1992)

#### The Turn and Triggering Shock
- Dynamic eventually turned, producing **"ugly deleveraging"** from 1987-1992
- Debt service reached **58% of GDP** at pre-crisis peak — extremely vulnerable position
- Shock came via **ripples from commodity price declines**
- Norway as commodity exporter particularly exposed to this external shock

#### Depression Dynamics
- **Self-reinforcing declines** in GDP (falling 4%) and home prices (falling 38%)
- Financial institutions came under **considerable pressure**
- Despite need for deleveraging, **debt as % GDP roughly flat** through this period
- Classic case where debt burden doesn't decline despite economic contraction

#### Attribution of Depression Period
- Chart shows deleveraging components: real growth (negative), inflation (negative), change in domestic debt (positive)
- **Debt %GDP stayed at 0%** — no net deleveraging occurred
- Negative real growth and deflation offset by continued debt accumulation

### Policy Response and Gauges

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge
- Composite measure showing bubble/depression conditions and tightness/easiness of money and credit
- Difference from zero shows extent of bubble; crossing zero represents shifting into/out of bubble
- Gauge peaked at **~2.5** during 1987 bubble peak
- Fell to **-1.5** trough during 1992 depression bottom
- Clear cyclical pattern from bubble to depression to reflation

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge
- Shows monetary policy stance throughout cycle
- **Peak tightening** occurred around 1987 (at bubble peak)
- **Peak easing** occurred around 1992 (at depression bottom)
- Second tightening peak around 1992 as recovery began
- Gauge range: approximately +1.0 (tightening) to -1.5 (easing)

#### Core Inflation Dynamics
- High inflation during bubble: peaked around **10-11%** in mid-1980s
- Declined through bubble period to **~6%** by 1987
- Continued falling through depression: reached **~4%** by 1992
- Further declined during reflation to near **0%** by 1997
- Total decline of ~10 percentage points over full cycle

### The Reflation Phase (1992-1996)

#### Turning the Depression into "Beautiful Deleveraging"
- After relatively long bust phase, policymakers provided **enough stimulation** to turn deleveraging into beautiful one
- Period of **reflation** began in 1992
- Required aggressive policy response across multiple levers

#### Monetary Policy Response
- **M0 increased by 2% of GDP**
- Interest rates ultimately **pushed down to 4%**
- **Real FX averaged 3% depreciation** during stimulative phase
- Brought nominal growth close to nominal interest rates

#### Financial Sector Management (4 out of 9 classic policy levers)
- **Nationalized banks** and provided liquidity
- Aggressive management of financial institutions and bad debts
- Structural approach rather than just monetary stimulus

#### Structural Reforms
- Enacted reforms designed to **increase labor market flexibility**
- Complemented monetary and financial policies
- Supported long-term recovery beyond immediate crisis response

#### Reflation Results
- Nominal growth averaged **6.1%** during this period
- Sovereign long rates fell to **5.5%**
- **Unemployment rates declined by 4%**
- **Debt as % of GDP fell by 35%** (7% annualized)
- Took **5 years** before real GDP reached its prior peak

#### Attribution of Reflation Period
- Chart shows deleveraging components: real growth (blue), inflation (pink), change in domestic debt (green)
- **Debt %GDP declined -7%** overall
- Reduction came **mostly from income rising** (combination of real growth and inflation)
- Real growth was primary driver of deleveraging
- Some contribution from inflation
- Change in domestic debt showed modest positive contribution (green bar)

### Data Appendix: Key Economic Indicators

#### Indebtedness Metrics (1939-1954 baseline charts shown)
- Total Debt (%GDP): rose from ~100% in 1939 to peak ~200% in mid-1940s, declined to ~150% by 1954
- Debt Service (%GDP): rose from ~15% to peak ~25% around 1945-1947, declined to ~20% by 1954
- FX Debt (%GDP): No data available for this period
- Change in Debt-GDP Ratio: peaked at ~30% annual growth around 1944-1945
- Debt Growth (%GDP): peaked at ~35% around 1944-1945

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- Nominal Short Rate: near 0% through 1944, rose to ~2.5% by early 1950s, declined to ~1% by 1954
- Money0 Level (%GDP): ~13% in 1939, peaked ~16% around 1944, declined to ~10% by 1954
- Fiscal Balance (%GDP): massive deficit ~-30% around 1943-1944, improved to near balance by 1949, slight surplus by 1954

#### Economic Conditions
- Real GDP (Indexed): sharp growth from ~60 (1939) to peak ~100 (1944), dip to ~90, recovered to ~120 by 1954
- Real Growth (Y/Y): volatile, peaked ~18% early 1940s, trough ~-10% around 1945, recovered to ~5% by early 1950s
- GDP Gap: negative initially, peaked ~20% around 1944, fell to negative ~-8% around 1947, recovered to ~8% by 1954
- Core Inflation (Y/Y): rose from ~0% to peak ~15% around 1947, declined to ~0% by 1954
- Nominal Long Rate vs Nominal Growth: long rate stable ~3%, nominal growth highly volatile (peaked ~35%, fell to ~-10%)
- Real Short Rate: highly volatile, ranged from ~+4% to ~-14%, ended around +2% by 1954

#### Markets
- Equity Price USD (Indexed): rose from ~80 to ~140 by 1944, relatively stable through 1949, surged to ~280 by 1954
- Nominal Long Rate vs Short Rate: both low and stable, long rate ~2-3%, short rate rose from 0% to ~2.5% then fell
- Yield Curve (SR-LR): started around -2.5%, rose to near 0% by 1952, fell back to ~-2% by 1954
- Real FXvsTWI: volatile, deep trough ~-30% around 1945, recovered to ~+15% peak around 1950, ~+5% by 1954
- FX Return for Foreign Investors (Indexed): stable ~100 through 1944, surged to ~450 by 1954
- Gold Price (Local FX, Indexed): stable ~95-100 through 1944, rose to peak ~125 around 1948-1951, fell to ~100 by 1954

#### External Position
- Gold Reserves (Troy Oz Bln): rose from ~0.6 to ~0.7 by 1944, relatively stable ~0.6-0.7 through 1954
- Capital Inflows (%GDP): No data available
- Imports (%GDP): ~2.5% in 1939, stable through war, rose to ~3.2% peak around 1952, ~2.8% by 1954
- Current Account (%GDP): rose from ~1% to peak ~6% around 1944, declined steadily to ~1% by 1954
- Capital Outflows (%GDP): No data available
- Exports (%GDP): rose from ~3% to peak ~8% around 1944, declined to ~3.5% by 1954

#### Government and Military
- Military Personnel (% Total Pop): rose from ~0.5% to peak ~9% around 1944-1945, fell sharply to ~1% by 1947, ~2.5% by 1954
- Military Spending (%GDP): rose from ~2% to peak ~42% around 1944, fell sharply to ~7% by 1947, ~12% by 1954
- Govt Debt (%GDP): rose from ~60% to peak ~175% around 1945, declined steadily to ~100% by 1954

### Key Lessons from Norway Case

#### Vulnerability Creation
- High debt service ratios (58% of GDP) create extreme vulnerability to shocks
- Commodity-dependent economies face additional risk from price volatility
- Domestic currency debt doesn't eliminate crisis risk if debt levels high enough

#### Depression Dynamics
- Deleveraging doesn't automatically occur during depression — debt %GDP can stay flat
- Self-reinforcing negative dynamics affect both real economy and asset prices
- Financial system stress compounds economic contraction

#### Successful Reflation Requirements
- Comprehensive policy response: monetary + financial sector + structural
- All 4 classic levers pulled: nationalization, liquidity provision, rate cuts, FX depreciation
- Labor market reforms complement demand-side policies
- Income growth (not debt reduction) was primary deleveraging mechanism
- Recovery takes time: 5 years to return to prior GDP peak
- Sustained commitment required: 4-year reflation phase (1992-1996) to achieve results

<!-- pp. 701-720 -->

## Finland 1987–2001 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase (1987-1989)

- **Finland** experienced a **classic deflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1987 and 2001
- Bubble driven by **self-reinforcing cycle** of strong growth (5%) and strong equity returns (18% annualized)
- Debts reached pre-crisis peak of **272% of GDP** in domestic currency
- High share of debt **owned by foreigners** created exposure to foreign capital pullback risk
- Investment inflows low but positive at ~3% of GDP
- Current account deficit of 3% of GDP maintained during bubble
- Strong asset returns encouraged more borrowing and stimulated growth
- Policymakers initiated **large monetary tightening** with short rates rising ~700 bps
- **Competitiveness became an issue** as real FX peaked at +24%
- Bubble pressures + foreign financing dependence + tightening money/credit = unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (1989-1993)

- Dynamic turned, producing **self-reinforcing bust** and "ugly deleveraging"
- High debt levels left Finland vulnerable to shock via **asset price declines hitting bank solvency**
- **Self-reinforcing declines** in GDP (-12%), stock prices (-36%), and home prices (-32%)
- Unemployment rates increased by **13%**
- Financial institutions came under considerable pressure
- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt as % GDP went up by 32%** (9% annualized) as incomes declined
- Throughout depression, debt-to-income ratios rose from **income falling**, not debt rising

### Gauges and Metrics

- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: Measures bubble/depression conditions from compendium of stats
  - Peaked at bubble phase ~1989
  - Dropped into deep depression territory through ~1994
  - Returned to reflation zone by 1999
- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: Measures money/credit tightness
  - Reached peak tightening around 1989
  - Shifted to peak easing around 1994
  - Returned to neutral/slight tightening by 1999
- **Core inflation** declined from ~8% (1989) to near 0% (mid-1990s), then recovered to ~2%

### Deleveraging Attribution: Depression Period (1989-1993)

Breakdown of 9% annualized debt/GDP increase:
- **Real growth**: Large negative contribution (dark blue bar)
- **Change in domestic debt**: Negative contribution (green bar = deleveraging)
- **FX debt change due to FX move**: Positive contribution (orange bar = currency depreciation increased FX debt burden)
- **FX debt change due to other**: Positive contribution (light blue bar)
- **Inflation**: Small positive contribution (pink bar)
- Net result: **Debt %GDP rose to 9%** despite domestic deleveraging

### The Reflation Phase (1993-2001)

- After **slightly longer than average bust phase**, policymakers provided enough stimulation for "beautiful" reflation
- **M0 increased by 7% of GDP**
- Interest rates **pushed down to 3%**
- Real FX averaged **-10%** during stimulative phase (currency depreciation supported competitiveness)
- Very aggressive in managing financial institutions and bad debts, pulling **7 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- Nationalized banks, provided liquidity, directly purchased troubled assets
- Stimulation brought **nominal growth well above nominal interest rates**
  - Growth averaged 6% during reflation
  - Sovereign long rates fell to 4%
- Unemployment rates declined by **6%**
- **Debt as % GDP fell by 72%** (8% annualized)
- Reduction in debt-to-income ratios came mostly from **income rising**, driven by higher real growth
- Took **7 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- Equity prices in USD terms **recovered within 5 years**

### Deleveraging Attribution: Reflation Period (1993-2001)

Breakdown of 8% annualized debt/GDP decrease:
- **Change in domestic debt**: Large negative contribution (green bar = significant deleveraging)
- **Inflation**: Moderate negative contribution (pink bar)
- **FX debt change due to FX move**: Moderate negative contribution (orange bar)
- **Real growth**: Small negative contribution (dark blue bar)
- **FX debt change due to other**: Small negative contribution (light blue bar)
- Net result: **Debt %GDP fell to -8%**

### Chart Deck Appendix: Indebtedness

#### Total Debt and Debt Service
- **Total debt** peaked at ~300% GDP around 1994-09, then declined to ~250% by 1999-09
- **Debt service** peaked at ~7% GDP, tracking total debt trajectory

#### FX Debt
- **FX debt** rose sharply from ~80% GDP (1989-09) to peak at ~110% GDP (1992-93)
- Declined to ~60% GDP by 1999-09

#### Debt Growth Dynamics
- **Debt growth** (annualized) peaked at ~30% around 1989-09
- Fell to ~5% by late 1990s
- **Change in debt-GDP ratio** (annualized) rose from negative territory to peak ~15% (1989-09)
- Declined sharply to -20% by late 1990s (deleveraging)

### Chart Deck Appendix: Monetary and Fiscal Policy

#### Nominal Short Rate
- Rose from ~10% (1987) to peak at ~17% (1992)
- Fell steadily to ~4% by 1999-09

#### Money Supply (M0)
- Rose from ~13% GDP (1989-09) to peak at ~22% GDP (1994-09)
- Stabilized around 15% GDP by 1999-09

#### Fiscal Balance
- Peaked at ~1.5% GDP surplus (1989-09)
- Fell to -1.5% GDP deficit (1994-09)
- Recovered to small surplus by 1999-09
