## Sweden 1987-2000 Case Study

### Overview: Classic Deflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- **Sweden** experienced a **classic deflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1987 and 2000
- Three distinct phases: bubble (1987-1990), depression (1990-1993), reflation (1993-2000)
- Comprehensive case study with economic conditions, markets, and external position data

### The Bubble Phase (1987-1990)

#### Debt Dynamics
- **Debts rose 15% of GDP** during bubble, reaching pre-crisis peak of **239% of GDP**
- Debt denominated in **domestic currency**, majority **owned domestically**
- **Debt service reached 65% of GDP** at pre-crisis peak, creating extreme vulnerability

#### Economic Activity
- **Self-reinforcing cycle**: rising debt → strong growth → strong housing returns
- Growth moderate at **2%**, but economic activity high (**GDP gap peaked at 4%**)
- Investment inflows low but positive, averaging **~2% of GDP**
- Current account deficit of **3% of GDP**

#### Monetary Policy and Competitiveness
- Policymakers initiated **large tightening** — short rates rose **~500 bps**
- **Competitiveness became an issue**: Sweden's real FX peaked at **+15%**
- Tightening money and credit combined with bubble pressures created **unsustainable situation**

### The Depression Phase (1990-1993)

#### The Trigger
- Dynamic turned, producing **self-reinforcing bust** and **"ugly deleveraging"**
- Shock came via **housing price declines hitting bank solvency**
- Sweden suffered from **self-reinforcing declines** across multiple dimensions

#### Economic Contraction
- **GDP fell 6%**
- **Stock prices fell 34%**
- **Home prices fell 7%**
- **Unemployment rates increased 9%**
- Financial institutions came under **considerable pressure**

#### Debt Paradox During Depression
- Even though deleveraging was needed, **debt as % GDP went up 40%** (12% annualized)
- Driven by: incomes declined + government borrowed more in response to crisis
- **Peak fiscal deficit: 10% of GDP** during the ugly period
- Attribution chart shows debt increase driven by falling incomes and fiscal response, not new borrowing

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge Methodology
- Gauges measure bubble/depression conditions and tightness/easiness of money and credit
- Difference from zero conveys extent of bubble; crossing above/below zero = shifting in/out of bubble
- Composite gauges compiled from comprehensive stats in chart deck

### The Reflation Phase (1993-2000)

#### Policy Response: Aggressive and Comprehensive
- After relatively short bust, **policymakers provided enough stimulation** to create "beautiful" deleveraging
- **Sweden pulled 7 out of 9 classic policy levers** in managing crisis
- Monetary policy: **M0 increased 5% of GDP**, interest rates pushed to **3%**, real FX averaged **-5%**
- **Nationalized banks**, **provided liquidity**, **directly purchased troubled assets**
- **Structural reforms** to increase **labor market flexibility**

#### Recovery Dynamics
- Stimulation brought **nominal growth above nominal interest rates**
- Growth averaged **5%** during reflation; sovereign long rates fell to **4%**
- **Unemployment declined 1%**
- **Debt as % of GDP fell 28%** (4% annualized) — shown in attribution chart

#### Deleveraging Attribution (Reflation Period)
- Debt-to-income ratio reduction came **mostly from income rising**
- Driven primarily by **higher real growth**
- Took **3 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **Equity prices in USD terms recovered within 4 years**

#### Gauge Interpretation
- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: bubble phase peaked ~3, depression bottomed ~-2, reflation returned to ~+1.5
- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: peak tightening ~+1.0 during bubble, peak easing ~-1.5 during depression
- **Core inflation**: declined from ~9% during bubble to ~1% by 2000

### Market Behavior

#### Equity Markets
- **Massive equity rally** during reflation: equity price USD (indexed) rose from ~100 (1990) to ~800 (2000)
- Equities remained relatively flat during 1987-1993 bubble/bust, then exploded higher

#### Interest Rates and Yield Curve
- **Nominal long rate** and **nominal short rate** both declined from ~15% (late 1980s) to ~5% (2000)
- **Yield curve (SR-LR)**: fluctuated from +4% to -4%, normalizing near -2% by 2000
- **Real short rate**: spiked to ~17% during crisis (1992), normalized to ~4% by 2000

#### Currency and External Flows
- **Real FX vs TWI**: peaked at +30% (1990), crashed to -20% (1993), recovered to -10% (2000)
- **FX return for foreign investors** (indexed): volatile, ranging 80-140
- **Gold price (local FX, indexed)**: declined from ~140 (1987) to ~80 (2000)

### External Position

#### Capital Flows
- **Reserves data**: not available for this case
- **Capital inflows (%GDP)**: highly volatile, ranging 0-18%, spiked to ~18% by 2000
- **Capital outflows (%GDP)**: turned sharply negative, reaching **-22% by late 1990s** (major outflows)

#### Current Account
- **Current account (%GDP)**: swung from **-5% deficit (early 1990s)** to **+6% surplus (2000)**
- Major reversal reflecting improved competitiveness post-devaluation

#### Trade
- **Imports (%GDP)**: relatively stable around **30-35%** throughout period
- **Exports (%GDP)**: ranged **25-40%**, strengthened to ~40% by late 1990s

### Key Lessons from Sweden Case

- **Domestic currency debt** can still create severe crisis if debt service ratios reach extreme levels (65% of GDP)
- **Competitiveness matters**: real FX appreciation to +15% during bubble created vulnerability
- **Aggressive, comprehensive policy response** (7/9 levers) can transform ugly into beautiful deleveraging
- **Structural reforms** (labor market flexibility) complemented monetary/fiscal stimulus
- **Bank nationalization** and direct asset purchases were critical tools
- **Fiscal cost**: deficit reached 10% of GDP during crisis, but successful reflation restored fiscal health
- **Timeline**: 3 years to return to prior GDP peak, 4 years for equity recovery in USD terms


<!-- pp. 741-760 -->

## Japan 1987–2017 Case Auto-Summary

### Overview: Classic Deflationary Deleveraging Cycle

Japan experienced a classic **deflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1987 and 2017, consisting of three distinct phases: bubble, depression, and reflation.

### The Bubble Phase (1987-1989)

**Debt-Fueled Expansion**
- Driven by self-reinforcing cycle: rising debt → strong growth → strong asset returns
- Debts rose **24% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **307% of GDP**
- Debt characteristics: domestic currency, majority owned domestically, Japan was net creditor
- Capital repatriations helped maintain strong exchange rate despite shocks

**Economic Conditions**
- Investment inflows low: averaging ~**1% of GDP**
- Strong growth: **5%** annually
- GDP gap peaked at **4%**
- Equities averaged **28% annualized returns** over bubble period
- High economic activity levels sustained borrowing

**Policy Tightening**
- Policymakers initiated large tightening
- Short rates rising around **450 bps**
- Combined bubble pressures + tightening money/credit = unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (1989-2013)

**The Shock and Bust**
- Dynamic reversed → self-reinforcing bust and "ugly deleveraging"
- Pre-crisis peak: debt service reached **78% of GDP** → high vulnerability
- Shock arrived via **real estate and stock market busts**
- Stock prices fell **67%**
- Home prices fell **43%**
- Unemployment increased **3%**
- Financial institutions under considerable pressure

**Debt Paradox**
- Despite needing deleveraging, debt as % GDP rose **59%** (3% annualized)
- Driven primarily by **interest payments financed with new debt**
- Attribution chart shows debt increase despite deleveraging conditions

**Measurement Gauges**
- Bubble & Depression Gauge: tracks bubble/depression conditions and tightness/easiness of money/credit
- Distance from zero measures extent of bubble; crossing zero = shifting into/out of bubble
- Tightening & Easing Gauge: shows policy stance shifts
- Core Inflation tracked throughout cycle

### The Reflation Phase (2013-2017)

**Slow Initial Resolution**
- Resolution very slow initially
- Monetary policy struggled to push nominal GDP growth above nominal interest rates
- Eventually policymakers provided sufficient stimulation

**Aggressive Policy Response**
- Created "beautiful" deleveraging and reflation period beginning 2013
- **M0 increased 58% of GDP**
- Interest rates pushed to **0%**
- Real FX averaged **-10%** during stimulative phase

**Financial Sector Management**
- Very aggressive managing financial institutions and bad debts
- Pulled **7 out of 9 classic policy levers**:
  - Nationalized banks
  - Provided liquidity
  - Directly purchased troubled assets

**Structural Reforms**
- Enacted reforms to increase **labor market flexibility**

**Economic Results**
- Nominal growth well above nominal interest rates
- Growth averaging **2%** during reflation period
- Sovereign long rates falling to **0%**
- Unemployment rates flat
- Debt as % GDP fell **43%** (9% annualized)

**Deleveraging Attribution: Reflation Period**
- Reduction in debt-to-income ratios driven primarily by **monetization**
- Lesser extent by rising real incomes
- Minimal contribution from defaults and net new debt

### Key Data Points from Chart Deck

**Indebtedness Metrics**
- Total debt peaked near **400% GDP**
- Debt service peaked at **~80% GDP**
- FX debt remained low throughout (mostly domestic debt)
- Debt growth ranged from **-5% to 30%** over full cycle

**Monetary and Fiscal Policy**
- Nominal short rate: peaked **~8%**, fell to **0%** by 2013
- Money0 level: rose from **~10% PGDP** to **~80% PGDP** by 2017
- Fiscal balance: deteriorated from **~-1%** to **~-9% PGDP**, improved to **~-6%** by 2017

**Economic Conditions**
- Real GDP: steady upward trend despite volatility
- Real growth: highly volatile, ranging **-8% to +8%**
- GDP gap: fluctuated between **-4% to +6%**
- Core inflation: declined from **~3%** to negative territory, recovered to **~1%** by 2017

**Interest Rate Environment**
- Nominal long vs. short rates converged over time
- Real short rate: fell from **~6%** to **0%** then slightly negative

**Markets**
- Equity prices (USD indexed): massive bubble to ~100, crashed to ~40, partial recovery to ~80
- Yield curve (SR-LR): volatile early, compressed toward zero
- Real FX vs TWI: highly volatile, **-20% to +30%** range
- FX returns for foreign investors: peaked near **180**, declined to **~80** by 2017
- Gold price (local FX): steady increase from **~100** to **~250-300** over full period

**External Position**
- Reserves (USD indexed): rose from **~50** to peak **~200**, fell back to **~100**
- Capital inflows: surged to **~25% GDP** in early 1990s, fell negative to **-7%**, recovered to **~18%** by 2017
- Imports: rose from **~25% GDP** to **~35% GDP**
- Current account: volatile, ranging **-5% to +5% GDP**, ended at **~4% GDP**
- Capital outflows: ranged from **~0%** to **-20% GDP**, highly negative by 2017
- Exports: rose from **~25%** to **~40% GDP** then stabilized

### Template Characteristics

Japan's crisis exemplifies the **deflationary deleveraging** template where:
- Domestic currency debt dominates
- Net creditor status provides exchange rate stability
- Monetary policy effectiveness limited initially at zero bound
- Massive QE and direct asset purchases eventually succeed
- Structural reforms complement monetary/fiscal stimulus
- Long, slow recovery but ultimately successful reflation

<!-- pp. 761-780 -->

## United States 2004–2014 Case Auto-Summary

### Overview: Classic Deflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- **US experienced classic deflationary deleveraging between 2004-2014**
- Three distinct phases: bubble, depression, reflation
- Domestic currency debt, majority domestically owned

### The Bubble Phase (2004-2007)

#### Debt Dynamics
- **Debt rose 38% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **349% of GDP**
- Self-reinforcing cycle: rising debt → strong growth → strong asset returns
- Debt was in domestic currency, majority domestically owned

#### Economic Activity
- **Investment inflows averaged ~8% of GDP**, financing **6% current account deficit**
- Strong growth at **3%**, GDP gap peaked at **3%**
- High economic activity levels sustained by rising debt and capital

#### Asset Performance
- **Equities averaged 14% annualized returns** over bubble period
- Strong asset returns encouraged more borrowing, stimulated growth
- Created self-reinforcing feedback loop

#### Policy Response
- **Large monetary tightening: short rates rose ~400 bps**
- Bubble pressures + tightening money/credit → unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (2007-2009)

#### The Trigger
- **Housing bust** triggered the crisis
- Pre-crisis **debt service reached 68% of GDP** → vulnerability to shock
- Self-reinforcing bust and "ugly deleveraging" 2007-2009

#### Economic Collapse
- **GDP fell 4%**
- **Stock prices fell 50%**
- **Home prices fell 28%**
- **Unemployment increased 5%**

#### Debt Paradox During Crisis
- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt as % GDP increased 23% (15% annualized)**
- Driven primarily by **interest payments financed with new debt**
- Also driven by **falling real incomes**

#### Financial System Stress
- US financial institutions under considerable pressure
- Banking system required aggressive intervention

### The Reflation Phase (2009-2014)

#### Policy Response: "Beautiful Deleveraging"
- Policymakers provided **enough stimulation** to turn deleveraging into beautiful one
- Slightly shorter than average bust phase
- **US pulled 6 out of 9 classic policy levers**

#### Monetary Policy Tools
- **M0 increased 16% of GDP**
- **Interest rates pushed down to 0%**
- **Real FX averaged -10%** during stimulative phase

#### Direct Interventions
- **Recapitalized banks**
- **Provided liquidity**
- **Directly purchased troubled assets**
- Aggressive management of financial institutions and bad debts

#### Economic Recovery
- **Nominal growth well above nominal interest rates**
- Growth averaging **3%** during reflation period
- **Sovereign long rates fell to 2%**
- **Unemployment declined 3%**

#### Deleveraging Mechanics
- **Debt as % GDP fell 59% (11% annualized)** during reflation
- Reduction driven by mix of:
  - **Rising real incomes**
  - **Inflation**
  - **Paying down existing debt**
- Partially offset by interest payments financed with new debt

#### Recovery Timeline
- **4 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **Equity prices in USD terms recovered within 5 years**

### Gauges and Indicators

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge
- Measures bubble/depression conditions and tightness/easiness of money/credit
- Distance from zero shows extent of bubble
- Crossing above/below zero represents shifting into/out of bubble
- Sharp transition from peak bubble (~1.0) to deep depression (~-2.5)
- Reflation phase shows gradual recovery back toward neutral

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge
- Shows peak tightening (~1.0) during late bubble phase
- Sharp reversal to peak easing (~-3.5) during crisis
- Gradual normalization during reflation, returning to tight territory by 2017

#### Core Inflation
- Relatively stable throughout cycle
- Peaked around **2.5-3.0% Y/Y** during bubble
- Declined to **~1.0%** during depression
- Recovered to **~2.0%** range during reflation

### Detailed Chart Data

#### Indebtedness Metrics
- **Total Debt peaked ~73% GDP** in 2007-2009
- **Debt Service peaked ~350% GDP** pre-crisis
- **FX Debt remained stable ~10% GDP** (minimal foreign currency exposure)
- **Debt-GDP ratio change** swung from +15% annually to -10% annually

#### Monetary & Fiscal Policy
- **Nominal short rate**: 1% → 5.5% → 0% → 1%
- **Money supply (M0)**: 7% GDP → 24% GDP during crisis response
- **Fiscal balance**: Near 0% → -10% GDP → -3% GDP

#### Economic Conditions
- **Real GDP** (indexed): brief dip from ~100 to ~97, then steady climb to ~115
- **Real growth Y/Y**: 3% → -4% → +3%
- **GDP gap**: +3% → -4% → +1.5%
- **Core inflation Y/Y**: relatively stable 1.5-2.5% range
- **Nominal vs real long rates**: convergence during crisis as real rates fell

#### Markets
- **Equity prices** (indexed): 100 → 50 → 180 (full recovery and beyond)
- **Yield curve (SR-LR)**: +1% → -3% (inversion) → -1% (normalization)
- **Real FX vs TWI**: significant volatility, -15% to +15% range
- **FX return for foreign investors** (indexed): 150 → 95 → 140
- **Gold price** (local FX, indexed): 50 → 225 → 150

#### External Position
- **Reserves %GDP**: 3% → 30% (massive accumulation)
- **Reserves USD** (indexed): steady climb from ~75 to ~225, then modest decline
- **Capital inflows %GDP**: volatile, ranging -5% to +20%
- **Imports %GDP**: 8% → 22% → 18% → 16%
- **Current account %GDP**: improved from -6% to -2%
- **Capital outflows %GDP**: highly volatile, -15% to +6%
- **Exports %GDP**: 10% → 14% → 12%

### Key Takeaways

- **Domestic currency advantage**: debt in own currency provided policy flexibility
- **Aggressive policy response**: using 6 of 9 levers was critical to success
- **Financial system intervention**: direct asset purchases and bank recapitalization essential
- **Time required**: 4-5 years for full recovery metrics
- **Debt dynamics**: paradoxical increase during crisis, successful reduction during reflation
- **External position**: substantial reserve accumulation, improved current account

<!-- pp. 781-800 -->

## Austria 2005–2017 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase (2005-2008)

- **Debt composition vulnerability**: Debt denominated in **Euros** (technically domestic currency but Austria lacked control); high foreign ownership created exposure to capital flight
- Debt rose **19% of GDP** during bubble, peaking at **279% of GDP** pre-crisis
- **Growth**: 3% annual during bubble period, aided by foreign capital inflows
- **Equity returns**: 20% annualized returns encouraged more borrowing and stimulated growth
- **Policy response**: Moderate tightening with short rates rising ~200 bps
- **Unsustainable dynamics**: Bubble pressures + foreign financing dependence + tightening policy + weakened conditions in related countries = crisis setup

### The Depression Phase (2008-2009)

- **"Ugly deleveraging"**: Self-reinforcing bust from 2008-2009
- **Pre-crisis vulnerability**: Debt service reached **69% of GDP** before shock
- **Trigger**: 2008 global financial crisis
- **Economic collapse**: GDP fell **4%**, stock prices fell **66%**
- Financial institutions under considerable pressure
- **Debt paradox**: Despite needing deleveraging, debt/GDP rose **39% (32% annualized)** as incomes declined
- **Deleveraging attribution (Depression Period)**:
  - Change in domestic debt drove the 32% annualized increase
  - Real growth: negative contribution
  - Inflation: small positive contribution
  - Overall debt %GDP increase reflects income collapse overwhelming any debt reduction

### The Reflation Phase (2009-2017)

- **ECB intervention**: Provided enough stimulus to create "beautiful deleveraging" and reflation period beginning 2009
- **Monetary policy measures**:
  - M0 increased **19% of GDP**
  - Interest rates pushed to **0%**
  - Real FX averaged **-2%** during stimulative phase
- **Financial sector restructuring**: Austria aggressive in managing institutions and bad debts
  - Pulled **5 out of 9 classic policy levers**
  - Bank recapitalization and liquidity provision
- **Growth recovery**: Nominal growth well above nominal interest rates (growth averaged **3%** during period, sovereign long rates fell to **0%**)
- **Employment**: Unemployment rates remained flat during reflation
- **Debt reduction**: Debt/GDP fell **74% (9% annualized)**
- **Deleveraging attribution (Reflation Period)**:
  - **Inflation** drove most of debt-to-income ratio reduction (largest component)
  - Real growth: positive contribution
  - Change in domestic debt: modest positive contribution
  - Overall: **-9%** annualized debt/GDP reduction
- **Beautiful deleveraging mechanism**: Reduction in debt-to-income ratios came mostly from income rising (driven primarily by higher inflation), not just debt reduction
- **Incomplete recovery**: Took **3 years** before real GDP reached prior peak; equity prices in USD terms haven't fully recovered by 2017

### Chart Data Summary

**Indebtedness trends**:
- Total debt peaked ~70% GDP around 2007-2008
- Debt service peaked ~75% GDP around same time
- FX debt peaked ~50% GDP in 2007-2008, declined steadily to ~10% by 2017
- Debt-GDP ratio and debt growth both declined substantially post-crisis

**Monetary/Fiscal Policy**:
- Nominal short rate: rose to ~5% pre-crisis, fell to near 0% by 2012-2017
- M0 level: rose from ~7% GDP (2002) to ~27% GDP (2017)
- Fiscal balance: varied between +2% and -1% GDP across period

**Economic Conditions**:
- Real GDP (indexed): recovered from 2009 trough, exceeded pre-crisis peak by 2017
- Real growth: volatile around crisis (peak 4%, trough -4%), stabilized 2-3% by 2017
- Core inflation: ranged 1.5-3% throughout period
- Nominal long rate vs nominal growth: convergence by 2017 (both ~1-2%)
- Real short rate: turned negative post-crisis, remained around -2% to -3% through 2017

**Markets**:
- Equity prices (USD, indexed): peaked ~130 in 2007, fell to ~40 in 2009, recovered to ~75 by 2017 (incomplete recovery)
- Nominal long/short rates: both declined from 4-5% range to 0-1% range
- Yield curve (SR-LR): inverted deeply during crisis (-3.5%), normalized by 2017 (~-1%)
- Real FX vs TWI: peaked ~15% in 2008, fell to -10% trough, recovered slightly
- Gold price (local FX): surged from index ~75 to ~225 peak (2012), remained elevated ~175-200 through 2017

**External Position**:
- Reserves: No data available
- Capital inflows: peaked ~50% GDP in bubble, reversed sharply to -10% outflows during crisis, normalized to slightly negative by 2017
- Imports: steady ~50% GDP throughout
- Current account: peaked ~5% GDP surplus in 2007-2008, varied 2-3% surplus post-crisis
- Capital outflows: highly negative during bubble (-40% to -50% GDP), normalized to modest outflows (~10% GDP) by 2017
- Exports: rose from ~45% to ~57% GDP during bubble, stabilized ~55% GDP post-crisis

## Germany 2006–2017 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase (pre-2008)

- **Atypical bubble**: Germany didn't experience broad-based domestic bubble, but was "tied to other countries, economies, and financial markets that were experiencing bubble-like conditions"
- **Debt accumulation**: Built substantial debt stock reaching **261% of GDP** prior to crisis
- **Currency constraint**: Debt in **Euros** (technically domestic but Germany lacked control)
- **Debt ownership**: Most debt owned **domestically** (unlike Austria's foreign-owned debt)

### The Depression Phase (2008-2009)

- Cycle turned to "self-reinforcing bust and ugly deleveraging" from 2008-2009
- **Pre-crisis vulnerability**: Debt service reached **51% of GDP**
- **Trigger**: 2008 global financial crisis
- **Economic impact**: GDP fell **7%**, stock prices fell **53%**
- Financial institutions under considerable pressure
- **Debt paradox**: Despite needing deleveraging, debt/GDP rose **26% (18% annualized)** as incomes declined
- **Deleveraging attribution (Depression Period)**:
  - Change in domestic debt: primary driver of 18% annualized increase
  - Real growth: negative contribution (income collapse)
  - Inflation: minor contribution

### The Reflation Phase (2009-2017)

- **ECB intervention**: "Slightly shorter than average bust phase" followed by sufficient stimulus to create "beautiful deleveraging" and reflation beginning 2009
- **Monetary policy**:
  - M0 increased **14% of GDP**
  - Interest rates

## Germany 2006-2017 Case Study (Continued)

### Reflation Phase Performance (2009-2017)

#### Deleveraging Mechanics
- **Debt reduction**: Total debt fell 56% (7% annualized) from peak
- **Short rates**: Pushed to -1%
- **Real FX depreciation**: -4% average during stimulative phase
- **Nominal growth vs. rates**: Growth averaged 3%, sovereign long rates fell to 0%
- **Unemployment**: Declined by 4 percentage points
- **"Beautiful" deleveraging**: Debt-to-income reduction came primarily from income rising, driven by real growth
- **Recovery timeline**: Took 3 years for real GDP to reach prior peak
- **Equity markets**: Haven't fully recovered in USD terms

#### Policy Response Effectiveness
- **Policy lever deployment**: 6 of 9 classic levers used
  - Nationalized banks
  - Provided liquidity support
  - Directly purchased troubled assets
- **Stimulation quality**: Successfully brought nominal growth well above nominal interest rates
- **Attribution**: Real growth contributed most to deleveraging, with inflation and debt change playing smaller roles

## Greece 2005-2018 Case Study

### Overview
- **Crisis type**: Classic deflationary deleveraging cycle starting 2005
- **Currency constraint**: Euro-denominated debt (technically domestic but without sovereign control)
- **Foreign ownership**: High share of debt owned by foreigners, creating pullback vulnerability

### The Bubble Phase (2005-2008)

#### Debt Dynamics
- **Debt accumulation**: Rose 40% of GDP during bubble
- **Pre-crisis peak**: 206% of GDP
- **Currency risk**: Debt in Euros, which Greece didn't control

#### Capital Flows and External Position
- **Investment inflows**: Strong, averaging 22% of GDP
- **Current account deficit**: 13% of GDP
- **Foreign financing dependence**: Critical vulnerability

#### Growth and Asset Returns
- **Real growth**: Strong at 3%
- **GDP gap**: Peaked at 10% (high economic activity levels)
- **Equity returns**: 19% annualized over bubble period
- **Asset-debt spiral**: Strong returns encouraged more borrowing, stimulating further growth

#### Policy Stance
- **Monetary tightening**: Moderate (short rates rose ~200 bps)
- **Unsustainability**: Bubble pressures + foreign financing dependence + tightening money/credit + weakened conditions in related countries = untenable situation

### The Depression Phase (2008-2017)

#### The Trigger
- **Shock event**: 2008 global financial crisis
- **Pre-crisis vulnerability**: Debt service at 42% of GDP
- **Dynamic reversal**: Self-reinforcing bust and "ugly deleveraging"

#### Capital and Credit Collapse
- **Foreign funding withdrawal**: Capital inflows fell 34% of GDP
- **Credit tightening**: Short rates increased 86%
- **Self-reinforcing decline**: Tightening → GDP collapse → asset price collapse → unemployment spike

#### Economic Contraction
- **GDP decline**: -27%
- **Stock market crash**: -91%
- **Home prices**: -42%
- **Unemployment**: +15 percentage points
- **Financial institutions**: Under considerable pressure

#### Debt Dynamics (Paradox of Deleveraging)
- **Debt-to-GDP rise**: +101% (11% annualized) despite needing deleveraging
- **Primary driver**: Falling real incomes
- **Secondary factor**: Interest payments financed with new debt
- **Attribution breakdown**:
  - Real growth (negative) pushed debt/GDP sharply higher
  - Interest payments added burden
  - Defaults, new debt, and monetization provided partial offsets

#### Gauge Analysis
- **Bubble/Depression gauge**: Peaked around +1.5 in bubble, plunged to -2.0 in depression
- **Tightening/Easing gauge**: Peaked at +2.0 (peak tightening ~2010), then fell to -3.0+ (peak easing)
- **Core inflation**: Fell from 3-4% to -3%, then recovered toward 0-1%

### The Reflation Phase (2017-)

#### Limited Success
- **Nominal growth**: Never pushed above nominal interest rates by adequate monetary easing
- **"Beautiful" phase absent**: Incomplete transition to healthy deleveraging

#### Political Consequences
- **Populist emergence**: Crisis set stage for **Alexis Tsipras** to take power
- **Political shift**: Many considered Tsipras a populist leader

#### Deleveraging Attribution (2017-)
- **Debt change**: -19% annualized
- **Components**:
  - Net new debt (gray): Largest negative contributor
  - Defaults (yellow): Significant negative contribution
  - Real growth (blue): Small positive contribution
  - Interest (red): Moderate positive contribution
  - Inflation: Small contribution
  - Monetization: Minimal

### Chart Deck Data Summary

#### Indebtedness Metrics
- **Total debt trajectory**: Rose from ~30% (2002) to peak ~60% (2012), declined to ~50% (2017)
- **Debt service**: Rose from ~30% to peak ~42% (pre-crisis), declined to ~30% (2017)
- **FX debt**: Rose from ~5% to ~13% peak, declined to ~12%
- **Debt growth**: Peaked at 35% annualized (2007-2008), turned negative after crisis
- **Debt-GDP ratio change**: Peaked at +30% annualized, turned negative post-2012

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- **Nominal short rate**: Rose to 5% pre-crisis, fell to -1% by 2017
- **Money supply (M0)**: Rose from 7% to 28% of GDP (2002-2017)
- **Fiscal balance**: Ranged from -3% to +1% of GDP, improving post-crisis

#### Economic Conditions
- **Real GDP (indexed)**: Grew to 100 (2007), collapsed to ~93, recovered to ~110 (2017)
- **Real growth**: Ranged from -8% (trough) to +5% (peak)
- **GDP gap**: Peaked at +3%, fell to -5%, recovered to +2%
- **Core inflation**: Peaked at 2.0%, fell to near 0%, stabilized around 1%

#### Interest Rates and Growth
- **Nominal long rate vs. growth**: Long rates peaked at 6%, fell to 0%; growth more volatile
- **Real short rate**: Peaked at +3%, fell to -2%

#### Markets
- **Equity prices (USD)**: Rose from 35 to 120 (peak 2007), crashed to 50, recovered to 120 (2017)
- **Nominal rates**: Both long and short fell to 0-1% range by 2017
- **Yield curve (SR-LR)**: Ranged from -2.5% to +0.5%
- **Real FX trade-weighted**: Volatile, ranging from -10% to +8%

#### External Position
- **Reserves (USD)**: No data available for Greece
- **Capital inflows**: Peaked at 28% of GDP, fell to 0-5%
- **Capital outflows**: Ranged from -30% to +5% of GDP
- **Imports**: Peaked at 55% of GDP, fell to 35%
- **Exports**: Peaked at 60% of GDP, stabilized at 45%
- **Current account**: Deficit peaked at 8-9% of GDP (bubble), improved to 6% surplus range

<!-- pp. 821-840 -->

## Hungary 2005–2017 Deflationary Deleveraging Case Study

### Overview: Classic Deflationary Cycle

- **Hungary** experienced a textbook deflationary deleveraging between 2005-2017
- Three distinct phases: bubble (2005-2008), depression/ugly deleveraging (2008-2013), reflation/beautiful deleveraging (2013-2017)
- Case demonstrates how **foreign currency debt** and **reliance on foreign capital** amplify crisis vulnerability

### The Bubble Phase (2005-2008)

#### Debt-Fueled Growth Dynamics

- **Total debt** rose 45% of GDP during bubble, peaking at **214% of GDP**
- Debt denominated in **domestic currency** (Hungarian forint), but high share owned by foreigners
- This foreign ownership created exposure to **capital flight risk**
- Real **growth** averaged **3%** during bubble; **GDP gap** peaked at 5%

#### Capital Flows and Current Account

- **Investment inflows** moderately strong, averaging **6% of GDP**
- Financed **current account deficit of 8% of GDP**
- Rising debt and capital enabled strong economic activity above potential

#### Asset Price Inflation

- **Equities** delivered **14% annualized returns** over bubble period
- Strong asset returns encouraged more borrowing in self-reinforcing loop
- Bubble pressures combined with foreign financing dependence created unsustainable situation
- Weakened conditions in related countries added systemic fragility

#### Debt Service Burden

- Pre-crisis **debt service** reached **31% of GDP**
- Made Hungary highly vulnerable to external shocks

### The Depression Phase (2008-2013): "Ugly Deleveraging"

#### Crisis Triggers and Capital Flight

- **2008 global financial crisis** triggered the reversal
- **Portfolio inflows** collapsed, falling **12% of GDP**
- Foreign funding withdrawal forced severe economic contraction

#### Policy Response: Tightening

- Policymakers **hiked short rates by 5%** in response to capital flight
- Tightening amplified the self-reinforcing downturn
- Classic mistake: prioritizing currency defense over growth support

#### Economic Collapse

- **Real GDP** fell **7%** from peak
- **Stock prices** crashed **73%**
- **Home prices** fell **16%**
- **Unemployment** rose **3 percentage points**
- **Core inflation** plunged from ~4% to below zero, reaching **-3%**

#### Debt Dynamics Worsen Despite Deleveraging Need

- Paradoxically, **debt-to-GDP rose 37%** (7% annualized) during depression
- Income collapsed faster than debt could be paid down
- Government forced to **borrow more** to respond to crisis
- **Peak fiscal deficit**: 5% of GDP during ugly period
- Attribution chart shows negative real growth and inflation both increased debt burden
- Demonstrates why "austerity during depression" fails: incomes fall faster than debt

#### Financial System Stress

- Hungarian financial institutions came under "considerable pressure"
- Required aggressive intervention and bad debt management

### The Reflation Phase (2013-2017): "Beautiful Deleveraging"

#### Policy Stimulus Succeeds

- Policymakers provided sufficient stimulus to reverse the dynamic
- **M0 money supply** increased **4% of GDP**
- **Interest rates** pushed down to **0%**
- **Real FX** averaged **-6%** depreciation during stimulative phase (competitive devaluation)
- **IMF assistance program** provided external support and credibility

#### Aggressive Financial Sector Restructuring

- Hungary pulled **4 out of 9 classic policy levers** for managing bad debts
- Systematic approach to cleaning up financial institutions
- Created foundation for sustainable recovery

#### Growth Recovery

- **Nominal growth** well above **nominal interest rates** — critical for debt sustainability
- Real growth averaged **5%** during reflation period
- **Sovereign long rates** fell to **2%** as confidence returned
- **Unemployment** declined **7 percentage points**

#### Debt Reduction Through Growth

- **Debt-to-GDP** fell **51%** (12% annualized) during beautiful deleveraging
- Attribution chart shows reduction came "mostly from income rising"
- Driven primarily by **higher real growth** — not austerity
- Took **6 years** for real GDP to reach prior peak
- **Equity prices in USD** still haven't fully recovered by 2017

#### Political Consequences

- Crisis created conditions for **Viktor Orban** to take power
- Many consider Orban a **populist leader**
- Demonstrates how debt crises reshape political landscape
- Economic pain creates appetite for political change and strong leadership

### Key Metrics and Gauges

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge

- Composite measure of bubble/depression conditions
- Peaked around 1.5-2.0 during bubble phase
- Plunged to -2.0 during depression
- Gradually recovered toward zero during reflation
- Zero crossing represents shift from depression back to normal

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge

- Composite measure of monetary conditions
- **Peak tightening** occurred ~2008 during crisis response
- **Peak easing** occurred ~2013-2015 during reflation
- Shows how policy stance shifted 180 degrees between phases

#### Deleveraging Attribution Analysis

**Depression Period (2008-2013):**
- Real growth: large negative contribution (pink bar)
- Inflation: negative contribution (deflation increased real debt burden)
- Change in domestic debt: positive (green — had to borrow more)
- FX debt changes: significant contribution from currency moves
- Net result: **+7% annualized increase** in debt-to-GDP despite depression

**Reflation Period (2013-2017):**
- Real growth: large negative contribution to debt ratio (blue — growth reduces debt/GDP)
- Inflation: moderate contribution
- Change in domestic debt: small positive
- FX debt changes: large contribution from depreciation strategy
- Net result: **-12% annualized decrease** in debt-to-GDP

### Supporting Chart Data

#### Monetary Policy Evolution

- **Nominal short rate**: ~8-14% during bubble, spiked to ~12% during crisis, fell to near-zero by 2017
- **Money0 level**: steady rise from ~8% of GDP (2002) to ~16% of GDP (2017), accelerating during reflation

#### Fiscal Balance

- Improved from -8% to -10% of GDP (2002-2008) to near-zero by 2017
- U-shaped recovery during reflation phase

#### Economic Conditions

- **Real GDP (indexed)**: climbed to ~102 (2008), crashed to ~75 (2013), recovered to ~77 (2017)
- **Real growth Y/Y**: volatile, ranging from +7% to -10%, stabilized around 0-2% by 2017
- **GDP gap**: peaked at ~10% (2008), fell to -10% (2013), recovered to near-zero by 2017
- **Nominal long rate** vs **nominal growth**: long rates peaked at ~35% during crisis, fell to ~5-10% during reflation

#### Market Performance

- **Equity prices (USD, indexed)**: climbed from ~30 to ~120 during bubble, crashed to ~15, recovered to ~20 by 2017
- **Yield curve (SR-LR)**: spiked to +60% during crisis peak, normalized to near-zero
- **Real FX vs TWI**: volatile, ranging from -4% to +8%

#### External Position

- **Reserves (USD, indexed)**: collapsed from ~1,400 to ~100 during crisis, gradually recovered to ~400-500
- **Capital inflows**: peaked at ~35% of GDP (2008), crashed to near-zero, recovered to moderate levels
- **Imports**: rose from ~25% to ~50% of GDP during bubble, fell back to ~30%
- **Current account**: improved from -25% deficit to near-balance by 2017
- **Capital outflows**: volatile, ranging from -20% to +15% of GDP
- **Exports**: rose from ~15% to ~35% of GDP over full period

#### Real Interest Rates

- **Real short rate**: spiked to nearly +100% during crisis peak (nominal rates high, deflation made real rates extreme)
- Normalized to near-zero during reflation

### Lessons from Hungary Case

- **Foreign currency debt** and **foreign ownership** create acute vulnerability to capital flight
- **Tightening during crisis** amplifies damage — rate hikes to defend currency backfire
- **Debt-to-GDP rises during depression** even when deleveraging needed — incomes fall faster than debt
- **"Beautiful deleveraging" requires growth** — can't cut way to prosperity
- **Nominal growth > nominal rates** is the key sustainability condition
- **Aggressive policy response works** — monetary easing, currency depreciation, bad debt cleanup
- **Political consequences are severe** — crises empower populist leaders
- **Recovery is slow** — 6 years to prior GDP peak, equity markets still impaired

<!-- pp. 841-860 -->

## Ireland 2005–2017: Deflationary Deleveraging Case Study

### Overview: Classic Deflationary Cycle

**Ireland** experienced a **classic deflationary deleveraging cycle** between 2005 and 2017, progressing through three distinct phases: bubble, depression, and reflation.

### The Bubble Phase (2005-2008)

#### Debt Dynamics
- Debts rose **94% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **271% of GDP**
- Debt denominated in **Euros** — technically Ireland's domestic currency but **not under Irish control**
- High share of debt **owned by foreigners** → exposure to capital flight risk

#### Growth and Capital Flows
- **Investment inflows** averaged ~**95% of GDP** → financed **5% of GDP current account deficit**
- Strong growth at **5%** annually
- **GDP gap peaked at 8%** (high economic activity levels)
- **Moderate asset returns**: equities averaged **5% annualized returns** during bubble period

#### Policy and Competitiveness Issues
- **Moderate tightening**: short rates rose ~**220 bps**
- **Competitiveness became an issue**: Ireland's **real FX peaked at +17%**
- Bubble pressures + foreign financing dependence + tightening money/credit + weakened conditions in related countries = **unsustainable situation**

### The Depression Phase (2008-2013)

#### The Deleveraging Mechanism
- Self-reinforcing bust and **"ugly deleveraging"** (2008-2013)
- Pre-crisis: **debt service reached 77% of GDP** → made Ireland vulnerable to shock
- Shock came via **European debt crisis**

#### Economic Collapse
- **GDP fell 9%**
- **Stock prices fell 73%**
- **Home prices fell 53%**
- **Unemployment rates increased 9%**
- **Financial institutions under considerable pressure**

#### Debt Paradox During Depression
- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt as % GDP went up by 35%** (7% annualized)
- Driven primarily by:
  - **Interest payments financed with new debt**
  - **Deflation** (to lesser extent)
- Partially offset by **defaults**

#### Gauges and Indicators
- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: moved from bubble peak (~1.0) to deep depression (below -1.5)
- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: shifted from peak tightening (~1.5) to peak easing (below -1.5)
- **Core inflation**: fell from ~3% to below -2% (deflationary period)

### The Reflation Phase (2013-2017)

#### ECB Policy Response
- After relatively long bust phase, **ECB policymakers provided enough stimulation** to turn deleveraging into **"beautiful one"**
- Period of **reflation began in 2013**

#### Monetary Policy Tools
- **M0 increased by 14% of GDP**
- **Interest rates pushed down to 0%**
- **Real FX averaged -19%** during stimulative phase (currency depreciation support)

#### Structural Reforms (7 out of 9 Classic Levers)
- **Nationalized banks**
- **Provided liquidity**
- **Directly purchased troubled assets**
- Benefited from **IMF assistance program**
- Enacted **structural reforms to increase labor market flexibility**

#### Growth Recovery
- **Nominal growth well above nominal interest rates**
- Growth averaged **6%** during reflation period
- Sovereign long rates fell to **0%**
- **Unemployment rates declined 8%**

#### Debt Reduction Success
- **Debt as % of GDP fell by 116%** (25% annualized) — shown in **Deleveraging Attribution: Reflation Period** chart
- Debt reduction driven by mix of:
  - **Rising real incomes**
  - **Paying down existing debt**
- Took **6 years before real GDP reached prior peak**
- **Equity prices in USD terms haven't yet fully recovered** (as of 2017)

#### Deleveraging Attribution Charts
- **Depression Period (2008-2013)**: Net new debt dominant factor, 7% annualized debt change
- **Reflation Period (2013-2017)**: Real growth (blue) largest contributor to -25% debt reduction, supported by monetization (green) and some defaults (yellow)

### Chart Deck Appendix Data

#### Indebtedness Trends
- **Total Debt (%GDP)**: rose from ~150% (2002) to ~300% peak (2010), fell to ~200% (2017)
- **Debt Service (%GDP)**: tracked total debt, peaking near crisis
- **FX Debt (%GDP)**: peaked ~80% (2008), fell to ~15% (2017)
- **Change in Debt-GDP Ratio (Ann)**: peaked ~30% (2007), fell to -20% (2017)
- **Debt Growth (%GDP, Ann)**: peaked ~40% (2007), normalized near 0% (2017)

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- **Nominal Short Rate**: rose from ~2% (2002) to ~5.5% (2008), fell to ~0% (2017)
- **Money0 Level (%GDP)**: rose from ~8% (2002) to ~28% (2017), with spike during crisis response
- **Fiscal Balance (%GDP)**: collapsed from ~0% (2007) to -12% (2010), recovered to ~0% (2017)

#### Economic Conditions
- **Real GDP (Indexed)**: slight dip during crisis, strong recovery post-2012 to index ~130 (2017)
- **Real Growth (Y/Y)**: extreme volatility from +10% to -8% during crisis, stabilized ~5-10% post-2012
- **GDP Gap**: fell from +8% (2007) to -8% (2012), recovered to ~6% (2017)
- **Core Inflation (Y/Y)**: fell from ~5% (2002) to below -3% (2010), recovered to ~1% (2017)
- **Nominal Long Rate** and **Nominal Growth**: converged near 0-5% range by 2017
- **Real Short Rate**: peaked ~5% (2007), fell to -2% (2017)

#### Markets
- **Equity Price USD (Indexed)**: rose from ~20 (2002) to ~130 (2007), crashed to ~30 (2012), recovered to ~80 (2017) — **never fully recovered to peak**
- **Nominal Long Rate & Short Rate**: both fell from ~10-14% peaks (2007) to near 0% (2017)
- **Yield Curve (SR-LR)**: volatile, ranged from +4% to -2%, ended near -1.5% (2017)
- **Real FXvsTWI**: peaked ~+20% (2008), fell to -10% (2017)
- **Gold Price (Local FX, Indexed)**: rose from ~50 (2002) to ~250 (2012), stabilized ~220 (2017)
- **FX Return for Foreign Investors (Indexed)**: rose from ~60 (2002) to ~120 (2008), stabilized ~90 (2017)

#### External Position
- **Reserves (USD, Indexed)**: rose from ~50 (2002) to ~230 (2010), fell to ~80 (2017)
- **Capital Inflows (%GDP)**: rose from ~10% (2002) to ~27% (2007), fell to ~0% (2017)
- **Capital Outflows (%GDP)**: volatile, peaked ~7% (2012)
- **Imports (%GDP)**: rose from ~60% (2002) to ~140% (2008), fell to ~70% (2017)
- **Exports (%GDP)**: rose from ~50% (2002) to ~130% (2008), fell to ~70% (2017)
- **Current Account (%GDP)**: improved from -12% deficit (2007) to ~5% surplus (2017)

### Key Lessons from Ireland Case

1. **Euro constraint**: Ireland lacked monetary sovereignty despite Euro being "domestic" currency
2. **Foreign debt vulnerability**: High foreign ownership of debt amplified capital flight risk
3. **Policy response matters**: ECB's eventual aggressive stimulus (7 of 9 levers) enabled "beautiful deleveraging"
4. **Structural reforms critical**: Labor market flexibility reforms supported recovery
5. **Long recovery timeline**: 6 years to restore real GDP to prior peak; equity markets still hadn't fully recovered after 9+ years
6. **Competitiveness trap**: Real FX appreciation during bubble (+17%) created unsustainable position
7. **Debt paradox**: Even during deleveraging phase, debt/GDP rose due to interest payments and deflation — required sustained reflation to reverse

<!-- pp. 861-880 -->

## Italy 2005–2017 Case Auto-Summary

### Overview: Classic Deflationary Deleveraging Cycle

Italy experienced a **classic deflationary deleveraging cycle** between 2005 and 2017, divided into three distinct phases: bubble, depression, and reflation.

### The Bubble Phase (2005-2008)

#### Debt Dynamics
- **Self-reinforcing cycle** of rising debt and strong growth
- Debts rose **29% of GDP** during bubble period to pre-crisis peak of **270% of GDP**
- Debt denominated in **Euros** (technically Italy's domestic currency but not under Italy's control)
- High share of debt **owned by foreigners** → exposure to foreign capital pullback risk

#### Growth and Financing
- Investment inflows moderately strong: averaging **~7% of GDP**
- Financed current account deficit of **1% of GDP**
- Moderate growth at **2%**
- High levels of economic activity (**GDP gap peaked at 4%**)

#### Market Performance
- Strong asset returns: equities averaged **7% annualized returns** over bubble period
- Encouraged more borrowing and stimulated growth

#### Policy Response
- Moderate tightening: short rates rising around **200 bps**

#### Unsustainability
> These bubble pressures and Italy's dependence on foreign financing, combined with tightening money and credit and the weakened conditions of related countries, created an unsustainable situation.

### The Depression Phase (2008-2015)

#### The Turn
- Dynamic produced **self-reinforcing bust** and "ugly deleveraging"
- At pre-crisis peak, debt service reached **58% of GDP** → made Italy vulnerable to shock
- Shock came via **European debt crisis**

#### Capital Flight and Decline
- Fall in foreign funding: portfolio inflows falling by **14% of GDP**
- Self-reinforcing declines in GDP (falling by **9%**)
- Stock prices falling by **67%**
- Home prices falling by **15%**
- Unemployment rates increased by **6%**

#### Debt Paradox
- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt as % GDP went up by 48%** (7% annualized)
- Driven primarily by **interest payments financed with new debt**
- Lesser extent by falling real incomes

#### Financial Sector Pressure
- Italy's financial institutions came under considerable pressure

### The Reflation Phase (2015-2017)

#### ECB Policy Intervention
- **M0 increased by 16% of GDP**
- Interest rates ultimately pushed down to **0%**
- Real FX averaged **-1%** during stimulative phase

#### Domestic Reforms
- Italy aggressive in managing financial institutions and bad debts
- Pulled **4 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- Enacted **structural reforms** to increase labor market flexibility

#### Results
- Stimulation brought **nominal growth above nominal interest rates**
- Growth averaging **1.8%** during this period
- Sovereign long rates falling to **1.2%**
- Unemployment rates declined by **1%**
- Debt as % of GDP fell by **36%** (14% annualized)

#### Deleveraging Attribution (Reflation Period)
- Reduction in debt-to-income ratios driven by:
  - Mix of **monetization**
  - Paying down existing debt
  - Lesser role of real growth and inflation

#### Incomplete Recovery
- Real GDP has **not yet reached** its prior peak
- Equity prices in USD terms **haven't yet fully recovered**

#### Political Impact
> The crisis had a notable impact on the politics of Italy, as it helped set the stage for Giuseppe Conte, whom many people consider a populist leader, to take power in 2018.

### Measurement Gauges

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge
- Composite measure from chart deck statistics
- Distance from zero = extent of bubble
- Crossing above/below zero = shifting into/out of bubble
- Clear peak around 2007-2008, trough around 2012-2015, reflation beginning 2015

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge
- Peak tightening around 2007-2008
- Periods of moderate tightening/easing 2010-2015
- **Peak easing** beginning 2015-2017

#### Core Inflation
- Declining trend throughout period
- From ~3% in early 2000s to below 1% by 2017

### Chart Deck Appendix Data

#### Indebtedness
- **Total Debt** and **Debt Service** tracked closely, both peaking 2012-2015
- Debt service reached 350% GDP equivalent at peak
- **FX Debt** remained relatively stable at 1.5-2.5% GDP
- **Change in Debt-GDP Ratio**: peaked at ~25% annual change around 2008-2010, then declined
- **Debt Growth**: peaked at ~20% GDP annual around 2008, declined to negative by 2015-2017

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- **Nominal Short Rate**: peaked near 6% in 2007-2008, fell to near 0% by 2017
- **Money0 Level**: rose from ~8% GDP in 2007 to nearly 30% GDP by 2017 (massive expansion)
- **Fiscal Balance**: worsened from ~-2% GDP to ~-5% GDP during crisis, improved to ~-2% GDP by 2017

#### Economic Conditions
- **Real GDP (Indexed)**: peaked around 100 in 2007-2008, fell to ~90, recovered partially to ~95 by 2017
- **Real Growth**: highly volatile, ranging from +3% to -7%, stabilizing around 1-2% by 2017
- **GDP Gap**: peaked at 4-5% in 2007, fell to -3% during depression, recovered to 2% by 2017
- **Core Inflation**: declined from 3% to below 1%
- **Nominal Long Rate vs Nominal Growth**: rates exceeded growth during crisis, converged by 2017
- **Real Short Rate**: peaked near 4% pre-crisis, fell to -1% during reflation

#### Markets
- **Equity Price USD (Indexed)**: peaked at 130 in 2007, crashed to 40, recovered to 60 by 2017
- **Nominal Long/Short Rates**: long rates peaked near 8% in 2011-2012, both converged near 0% by 2017
- **Yield Curve (SR-LR)**: steepened dramatically during crisis (to -10%), normalized to -2% by 2017
- **Real FX vs TWI**: appreciated ~25% during bubble, depreciated to -25% during crisis, recovered to -3% by 2017
- **FX Return for Foreign Investors (Indexed)**: peaked near 110 in 2007, declined to 75 by 2017
- **Gold Price (Local FX, Indexed)**: rose from 50 to 230+ during crisis period, stabilized around 200 by 2017

#### External Position
- **Reserves (USD, Indexed)**: extreme volatility, peaked at 800+ in early 2000s, fell to near 100 in 2012, recovered to 300+ by 2017
- **Capital Inflows**: peaked at 250% GDP in 2007, crashed to -50%, recovered to 100% by 2017
- **Imports**: rose from 50% to 100% GDP during bubble, stabilized around 70-80% GDP
- **Current Account**: improved from -8% deficit to 12% surplus by 2017
- **Capital Outflows**: volatile, ranging from -250% to 50% GDP
- **Exports**: rose from 80% to 130% GDP during bubble, stabilized around 100% GDP by 2017

<!-- pp. 881-900 -->

## Netherlands 2006-2017 Case Study

### Case Overview: Classic Deflationary Deleveraging

- **Netherlands experienced classic deflationary deleveraging cycle** between 2006-2017
- Three distinct phases: bubble (2006-2008), depression (2008-2014), reflation (2014-2017)
- Debt denominated in **euros** — technically domestic currency but not under Netherlands' control
- **High foreign ownership** of debt created exposure to capital flight risk
- Charts show external position dynamics: reserves indexed in USD, capital inflows, imports/exports, current account

### The Bubble Phase (2006-2008)

#### Debt Dynamics
- **Debts rose 10% of GDP** during bubble period
- Pre-crisis peak: **355% of GDP** total debt
- **68% debt service to GDP** at peak — extremely vulnerable to shocks
- Foreign ownership created dependency on external financing
- Strong capital inflows averaging **~14% of GDP**

#### Growth and Asset Returns
- **Growth strong at 3%** during bubble phase
- GDP gap peaked at **5%** (high economic activity levels)
- **Equities averaged 11% annualized returns** over bubble period
- Strong asset returns encouraged more borrowing, creating self-reinforcing cycle
- High debt + rising capital enabled strong growth momentum

#### Monetary Conditions
- Policymakers initiated **moderate tightening**
- Short rates rose around **200 bps**
- Despite tightening, bubble conditions persisted
- Investment inflows remained strong despite policy attempts to cool economy

#### Vulnerability Factors
- Bubble pressures combined with **dependence on foreign financing**
- **Tightening money and credit** alongside weakened conditions in related countries
- Created fundamentally **unsustainable situation**
- Euro denomination meant no independent monetary control

### The Depression Phase (2008-2014)

#### The Shock and Reversal
- **2008 global financial crisis** triggered self-reinforcing bust
- Term used: **"ugly deleveraging"** ran from 2008-2014
- Dynamic turned from virtuous to vicious cycle

#### Economic Collapse
- **GDP fell 4%** during depression
- **Stock prices fell 57%**
- **Home prices fell 20%**
- **Unemployment increased 4%**
- Financial institutions came under considerable pressure

#### Paradoxical Debt Dynamics
- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt as % GDP rose 74%** (12% annualized)
- **Debt-to-GDP ratio increased** even though deleveraging was necessary
- Income declines outpaced debt reduction
- Attribution chart shows real growth negative, inflation negative, change in domestic debt positive

#### Gauge Measurements
- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: peaked at ~1.0 during bubble, fell to -1.5+ during depression trough
- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: sharp drop from moderate tightening (~0.5) to deep easing (-2.0+), then peak tightening (~1.5), then peak easing (-2.5)
- **Core inflation**: declined from ~2% to under 1%, showing deflationary pressures
- Gauges measure bubble/depression conditions and tightness/easiness of money and credit

### The Reflation Phase (2014-2017)

#### ECB Policy Response
- After "relatively long bust phase," **ECB provided enough stimulation**
- Turned deleveraging into **"beautiful deleveraging"**
- Period of **reflation began in 2014**

#### Monetary Stimulus
- **M0 increased 16% of GDP**
- **Interest rates pushed down to -1%**
- **Real FX averaged -2%** during stimulative phase
- Sovereign long rates fell to **0%**

#### Policy Levers Deployed
- Netherlands aggressive in managing financial institutions and bad debts
- Pulled **5 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- Specifically: **nationalized banks and provided liquidity**
- This stimulation brought nominal growth well above nominal interest rates

#### Growth Recovery
- **Nominal growth averaging 3%** during reflation period
- Growth well above nominal interest rates (sovereign long rates at 0%)
- **Unemployment declined 3%**
- Real GDP indexed recovered from ~100 to ~110

#### Deleveraging Success
- **Debt as % GDP fell 46%** (12% annualized)
- Attribution chart shows: mostly real growth (blue), some inflation (pink), negative change in domestic debt (green)
- Debt reduction came **mostly from income rising**, driven primarily by higher real growth
- Throughout "beautiful" period, debt-to-income ratios declined significantly

#### Incomplete Recovery
- Took **7 years before real GDP reached prior peak**
- **Equity prices in USD terms haven't fully recovered** by end of study period (2017)
- Despite successful deleveraging, full asset price recovery remained elusive

### Chart Deck Data

#### Indebtedness Metrics
- **Total debt peaked ~450% GDP** around 2012-2013
- **Debt service peaked ~80% GDP** in 2012
- **FX debt rose to ~35% GDP** by 2017 (from ~20% in 2007)
- **Debt-GDP ratio change**: rose sharply (~25% annualized) 2007-2012, then fell (-10% annualized) post-2014
- **Debt growth**: peaked ~25% annually in bubble, turned negative post-2012

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- **Nominal short rate**: rose to ~5% in 2007, fell to -1% by 2017
- **Money0 level**: rose from ~10% GDP (2007) to ~30% GDP (2017)
- **Fiscal balance**: volatile, ranged from -4% to +3% of GDP, improving sharply post-2014

#### Economic Conditions
- **Real GDP indexed**: peaked ~100 in 2008, bottomed ~96 in 2013, recovered to ~108 by 2017
- **Real growth**: collapsed to -5% in 2009, recovered to +3-4% by 2017
- **GDP gap**: peaked +5% in 2008, bottomed -3% in 2013, recovered to +2% by 2017
- **Core inflation**: declined from 2% (2008) to under 1% (2014-2017)
- **Nominal growth vs nominal long rate**: growth spiked to ~7% by 2017 while long rates fell to 0%
- **Real short rate**: peaked ~4% in 2008, fell to -2% by 2017

#### Markets
- **Equity price USD indexed**: peaked ~120 in 2007, bottomed ~45 in 2009, recovered to ~100 by 2017 (still below peak)
- **Nominal long rate**: fell from ~5% (2008) to ~1% (2017)
- **Nominal short rate**: fell from ~5% (2008) to -1% (2017)
- **Yield curve (SR-LR)**: compressed from +0.5% to -1.5% range throughout period
- **Real FXvsTWI**: volatile, ranged +8% to -6%, trending negative during reflation
- **FX return for foreign investors indexed**: peaked ~110 in 2008, declined to ~80 by 2017
- **Gold price (local FX, indexed)**: rose from ~50 (2002) to ~220 (2012), settled ~180 (2017)

#### External Position
- **Reserves (USD, indexed)**: rose steadily from ~100 to ~140 over 2002-2017 period (first page shows this)
- **Capital inflows**: spiked to ~18% GDP in bubble (2006-2008), collapsed to near 0% post-crisis, recovered to ~5% by 2017 (first page)
- **Imports**: peaked ~40% GDP in bubble, declined to ~25% GDP post-crisis, recovered to ~30% (first page shows different scale: 25-45%)
- **Current account**: deficit -4% GDP pre-crisis, turned to surplus +3% GDP by 2017 (first page)
- **Capital outflows**: highly volatile, ranged from -15% to +5% GDP (first page)
- Second set shows different scale: **Imports 50-90% GDP, Current account 0-14% GDP, Capital outflows -140% to +40% GDP**
- **Exports**: rose from ~20% GDP to ~40% GDP (2002-2008), stable ~30-35% post-crisis (first page shows 20-45%)
- Second set shows **Exports 40-100% GDP** — much higher scale, suggesting different measurement or data source

### Key Takeaways

- **Euro constraint**: lack of monetary sovereignty amplified crisis and limited policy options
- **Foreign dependence**: reliance on external capital created vulnerability when flows reversed
- **Paradox of deleveraging**: debt ratios can worsen during depression even as absolute debt falls
- **Beautiful vs ugly**: adequate monetary stimulus (ECB) transformed ugly into beautiful deleveraging
- **Income matters most**: successful debt reduction came primarily through real growth, not nominal debt paydown
- **Slow recovery**: even successful deleveraging took 7 years to restore real GDP, asset prices lagged further
- **Policy coordination**: national policy levers (bank nationalization, liquidity) combined with supranational monetary policy (ECB) to achieve reflation

<!-- pp. 901-920 -->

## Portugal 2007–2017 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase (2007-2008)

**Classic deflationary deleveraging cycle** from 2007-2017

#### Bubble Dynamics
- **Debt rose 36% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **273% of GDP**
- Debt denominated in **Euros** (technically domestic currency, but Portugal had no control over it)
- **High foreign ownership** of debt → exposure to foreign capital pullback
- **Investment inflows averaged 10% of GDP**, financing current account deficit of **11% of GDP**
- **Moderate growth at 2%**, high economic activity (GDP gap peaked at 3%)
- **Strong asset returns**: equities averaged **16% annualized returns** during bubble
- **Short rates rose ~220 bps** as policymakers initiated moderate tightening

> Bubble pressures and Portugal's dependence on foreign financing, combined with tightening money and credit and weakened conditions of related countries, created an unsustainable situation.

### The Depression Phase (2008-2013)

#### The "Ugly Deleveraging"
- **Debt service reached 48% of GDP** at pre-crisis peak → made Portugal vulnerable to shock
- Shock came via **European debt crisis**
- **Foreign funding collapsed**: portfolio inflows fell **40% of GDP**
- **Self-reinforcing declines**:
  - GDP fell **10%**
  - Stock prices fell **65%**
  - Home prices fell **18%**
  - **Unemployment rose 9%**
- **Financial institutions under considerable pressure**

#### Debt Dynamics During Depression
- **Debt/GDP rose 105% (21% annualized)** even though deleveraging needed
- Driven by mix of:
  - Falling real incomes
  - Interest payments financed with new debt
  - Net new borrowing (government had to borrow more in response to crisis)
- **Peak fiscal deficit: 8% of GDP** during ugly period

### The Reflation Phase (2013-2017)

#### ECB Intervention Creates "Beautiful" Deleveraging
**ECB policy makers provided enough stimulation** to turn deleveraging into beautiful one and create reflation period beginning 2013

#### Monetary Policy Actions
- **M0 increased 15% of GDP**
- **Interest rates pushed down to 0%**
- **Real FX averaged -1%** during stimulative phase

#### Policy Response
- **Pulled 4 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- Benefited from **IMF assistance program**
- **Structural reforms** designed to increase labor market flexibility
- **Aggressive management** of financial institutions and bad debts

#### Recovery Outcomes
- **Nominal growth above nominal interest rates** (growth averaged 2.0%, sovereign long rates fell to 1.7%)
- **Unemployment declined 9%**
- **Debt/GDP fell 77% (16% annualized)**
- Debt reduction driven by **mix of defaults and paying down existing debt**

> Throughout this "beautiful" period, the reduction in debt-to-income ratios was driven by a mix of defaults and paying down existing debt.

#### Recovery Timeline
- **9 years before real GDP reached prior peak**
- **Equity prices in USD terms haven't yet fully recovered**

### Portugal 2007–2017 Chart Deck Appendix

#### Indebtedness Metrics

**Total Debt (%GDP)**
- Rose from ~250% (2002) to peak ~360% (2012)
- Declined to ~300% by 2017

**Debt Service (%GDP)**
- Tracked closely with total debt
- Peak correlation at crisis point

**FX Debt (%GDP)**
- Declined from ~8% (2002-2007) to ~3% (2017)
- Steady downward trend post-crisis

**Change in Debt-GDP Ratio (Annualized)**
- Peak ~30% annualized during crisis
- Declined to negative territory during reflation

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

**Nominal Short Rate**
- Rose to ~5.5% peak (2007-2008)
- Fell toward 0% by 2017
- Sharp decline during crisis intervention

**Money0 Level (%GDP)**
- Increased from ~10% (2007) to ~27% (2017)
- Steady upward trend during reflation phase

**Fiscal Balance (%GDP)**
- Deteriorated from ~-2% to ~-8% during crisis (2012)
- Improved back to ~-2% by 2017

#### Economic Conditions

**Real GDP (Indexed)**
- Peaked ~100 (2007-2008)
- Fell to ~90 during crisis
- Recovered to ~100 by 2017

**Real Growth (Y/Y)**
- Highly volatile: ranging from -5% to +3%
- Negative during crisis years (2008-2013)
- Positive 2-3% during recovery

**GDP Gap**
- Peaked at +3% during bubble
- Fell to -6% during depression
- Returned toward 0% by 2017

**Core Inflation (Y/Y)**
- Declined from ~5% (2002) to ~1% (2017)
- Deflationary pressures during crisis

**Nominal Long Rate vs. Nominal Growth**
- Long rates spiked to ~15% during crisis (2012)
- Nominal growth went negative
- Convergence by 2017 (both ~2-5%)

**Real Short Rate**
- Peaked at ~4% during bubble
- Fell to -2% during reflation
- Sharp tightening then easing cycle

#### Markets

**Equity Price USD (Indexed)**
- Peak ~120 (2007)
- Crashed to ~30 during crisis
- Partial recovery to ~45 by 2017

**Nominal Long Rate and Short Rate**
- Long rates: peaked ~17% (2012), fell to ~4% (2017)
- Short rates: peaked ~5.5% (2008), fell to ~0% (2017)

**Yield Curve (SR-LR)**
- Ranged from +2% to -14%
- Most inverted at crisis peak (2012)
- Normalized to slightly negative by 2017

**Real FX vs TWI**
- Peaked at ~6% (2007)
- Fell to -3% range during reflation
- Significant real depreciation

**FX Return for Foreign Investors (Indexed)**
- Relatively stable 80-110 range throughout period

**Gold Price (Local FX, Indexed)**
- Rose from ~50 (2002) to peak ~240 (2012)
- Stabilized ~180-200 (2013-2017)

#### External Position

**Reserves (USD, Indexed)**
- Highly volatile
- Collapsed during crisis to ~100
- Recovered sharply to ~1,000+ by 2017

**Capital Inflows (%GDP)**
- Averaged 20-30% during bubble
- Collapsed to negative during crisis
- Partial recovery to ~5% by 2017

**Imports (%GDP)**
- Rose from ~35% to peak ~60% during bubble
- Declined to ~40% by 2017

**Current Account (%GDP)**
- Deficit peaked at -15% during bubble
- Improved to surplus ~2-3% by 2017

**Capital Outflows (%GDP)**
- Increased from near 0% to ~15% during crisis
- Moderated to ~5% during recovery

**Exports (%GDP)**
- Rose from ~30% to ~45% over period
- Steady upward trend

## Spain 2005–2017 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase (2005-2008)

#### Bubble Dynamics
- **Debt rose 93% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **313% of GDP**
- Debt in **Euros** (technically domestic currency, but Spain had no control)
- **High foreign ownership** → exposure to foreign capital pullback
- **Investment inflows averaged 27% of GDP**, financing current account deficit of **10% of GDP**
- **Strong growth at 4%**, very high economic activity (GDP gap peaked at **6%**)
- **Strong asset returns**: equities averaged **17% annualized returns**
- **Short rates rose ~200 bps** as policymakers initiated moderate tightening

> Spain's dependence on foreign financing, combined with tightening money and credit and the weakened conditions of related countries, created an unsustainable situation.

### The Depression Phase (2008-2013)

#### The "Ugly Deleveraging"
- **Debt service reached 64% of GDP** at pre-crisis peak → made Spain vulnerable to shock
- Shock came via **European debt crisis**
- **Foreign funding collapsed**: capital inflows fell **16% of GDP**
- **Self-reinforcing declines**:
  - GDP fell **9%**
  - Stock prices fell **60%**
  - Home prices fell **31%**
  - **Unemployment rose 17%**
- **Financial institutions under considerable pressure**

#### Debt Dynamics During Depression
- **Debt/GDP rose 86% (17% annualized)**
- Driven primarily by:
  - Interest payments financed with new debt
  - To lesser extent: falling real incomes
- Government fiscal deterioration less prominent than Portugal

<!-- pp. 921-940 -->

## Spain 2005-2017 Case Study (continued)

### The Reflation Phase (2013-2017)

**Policy Response Mechanics**
- **ECB** provided sufficient stimulus to transform deleveraging into "beautiful" reflation beginning 2013
- M0 increased 15% of GDP during reflation
- Interest rates pushed to -1%
- Real FX averaged 0% during stimulative phase

**Spain's Aggressive Deleveraging Strategy**
- Pulled 5 of 9 classic policy levers
- Provided liquidity directly to financial system
- Directly purchased troubled assets
- Enacted structural reforms for labor market flexibility

**Economic Outcomes**
- Nominal growth averaged 2% during reflation period
- Sovereign long rates fell to 1%
- Unemployment declined by 10%
- Debt-to-GDP fell 112% (23% annualized) during 2013-2017

**Deleveraging Attribution (Reflation Period)**
- Primary driver: paying down existing debt (net new debt contribution)
- Secondary contribution from interest payments financed with new debt
- Minimal contribution from inflation and real growth (compared to depression phase)
- Total debt change: -23% of GDP

**Recovery Timeline**
- Real GDP took 9 years to reach prior peak
- Equity prices in USD terms had not fully recovered by 2017

### Spain 2005-2017 Chart Deck Appendix

#### Indebtedness Metrics

**Total Debt Trajectory**
- Peak debt-to-GDP: ~80% (2012)
- Debt service peak: ~400% of GDP (2007-2008)
- Declined to ~250% by 2017
- Both metrics tracked closely through cycle

**Foreign Exchange Debt**
- Rose from ~3% of GDP (2002) to peak ~6.5% (2007-2009)
- Declined to ~5% by 2017
- Relatively stable compared to domestic debt movements

**Debt Growth Dynamics**
- Annual debt growth peaked ~50% (2007)
- Change in debt-to-GDP ratio peaked ~35% (2007)
- Both turned sharply negative post-2008
- Debt growth bottomed near -10% annually
- Change in debt-to-GDP ratio reached -20% trough

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

**Nominal Short Rate**
- Ranged 2-5% during bubble (2002-2007)
- Spiked to ~5% during crisis (2008-2009)
- Gradually declined to -1% by 2017
- Sharp easing characterized reflation period

**Money Supply (M0)**
- Relatively stable at 5-10% of GDP (2002-2012)
- Dramatic expansion beginning 2012
- Reached nearly 30% of GDP by 2017
- Key driver of reflation phase stimulus

**Fiscal Balance**
- Near balance to slight surplus pre-crisis (0-2% of GDP)
- Collapsed to -10% deficit during depression (2009)
- Gradually improved to near 0% by 2017
- Typical countercyclical pattern

#### Economic Conditions

**Real GDP**
- Indexed at ~85 (2002), rose to ~100 (2007-2008)
- Fell to ~92 during depression
- Recovered past prior peak by 2017 (~105)
- 9-year recovery to previous peak

**Real Growth (Y/Y)**
- Steady 3-4% pre-crisis
- Collapsed to -4% at trough (2009)
- Volatile recovery: -3% to +1% (2009-2013)
- Stabilized at 3-4% during reflation (2013-2017)

**GDP Gap**
- Rose from ~1% to ~7% during bubble
- Turned negative: bottomed at -5% (2012-2013)
- Recovered to positive ~4% by 2017

**Core Inflation**
- Relatively stable 2.5-3.5% pre-crisis
- Sharp decline post-2008
- Bottomed near 0% (2013-2014)
- Recovered to ~1.5% by 2017

**Nominal Long Rate vs. Nominal Growth**
- Long rates: 4-5% pre-crisis, spiked to ~7% during crisis
- Nominal growth: peaked ~8% (2007), collapsed to -4% (2009)
- By 2017: long rates ~2%, nominal growth ~4-5%
- Nominal growth above long rates = favorable deleveraging dynamic

**Real Short Rate**
- Near 0% pre-crisis, rose to ~2% during early bubble
- Spiked to ~4% at crisis peak (2008)
- Turned sharply negative: reached -2% by 2017
- Negative real rates supported reflation

#### Markets

**Equity Prices (USD)**
- Indexed at ~30 (2002), rose to ~120 (2007)
- Collapsed to ~40 (2012)
- Partial recovery to ~60-70 by 2017
- **Had not fully recovered to pre-crisis peak in USD terms**

**Interest Rate Structure**
- Nominal long rate: 4-5% (2002-2007), peaked ~7% (2012), fell to ~2% (2017)
- Nominal short rate: 2-5% (2002-2007), peaked ~5% (2008), fell to -1% (2017)
- Yield curve compressed significantly during reflation
- Short rates fell below long rates throughout

**Yield Curve (Short - Long)**
- Ranged from -2% to 0% pre-crisis (normal upward slope)
- Steepened during crisis to -3% to -4% (2008-2012)
- Flattened to -1% to -2% during reflation

**Real FX (Trade-Weighted Index)**
- Rose from ~2% to ~11% during bubble (2002-2008)
- Declined sharply post-crisis
- Bottomed near -4% (2012-2014)
- Stabilized at -2% to 0% during reflation

**FX Return for Foreign Investors**
- Indexed at ~80 (2002)
- Rose to ~110 peak (2008)
- Volatile decline to ~80 range (2012-2017)
- Poor returns for foreign equity investors

**Gold Price (Local FX)**
- Indexed at ~50 (2002)
- Steady rise to ~100 (2007)
- Continued climbing to ~230 peak (2012)
- Declined to ~170-190 by 2017
- Safe haven bid during crisis persisted

#### External Position

**Reserves (USD)**
- Indexed at ~100 (2002-2007)
- Brief spike and decline during crisis
- Steady climb from ~100 (2012) to ~500 (2017)
- Massive reserve accumulation during reflation

**Capital Flows**
- Inflows: rose from ~15% to ~32% of GDP during bubble (2002-2007)
- Collapsed to negative territory during crisis (-5% to -10%)
- Recovered to ~10% of GDP by 2017

**Imports**
- Rose from ~25% to ~48% of GDP during bubble
- Declined to ~27% by 2017
- Substantial import compression post-crisis

**Current Account**
- Deep deficit during bubble: -14% to -16% of GDP (2007-2008)
- Improved dramatically during deleveraging
- Reached surplus of ~2-3% by 2013-2017
- Major external rebalancing

**Capital Outflows**
- Negative during bubble (-10% to -25% of GDP)
- Spiked positive during crisis (reaching 0-5%)
- Turned negative again (-10%) during reflation
- Reflects changing risk perception and capital flight patterns

**Exports**
- Rose from ~25% to ~38% of GDP during bubble
- Relatively stable during crisis
- Declined to ~30% by 2017
- Less volatile than imports
