## United Kingdom 2005-2015 Case Study

**Overview**: Classic deflationary deleveraging cycle between 2005 and 2015

### The Bubble Phase (2005-2008)

**Debt Dynamics**
- Debts rose 89% of GDP during bubble
- Pre-crisis peak: 437% of GDP
- **Domestic currency debt**, majority owned domestically
- Self-reinforcing cycle of rising debt and strong growth

**Capital Flows and External Position**
- Investment inflows averaged ~14% of GDP
- Financed current account deficit of 3% of GDP
- Strong capital inflows helped sustain bubble

**Economic Activity**
- Growth: 3% during bubble
- GDP gap peaked at 4% (above potential)
- High levels of economic activity

**Asset Returns**
- Equities averaged 8% annualized returns during bubble
- Strong asset returns encouraged more borrowing
- Created wealth effect stimulating further growth

**Credit Conditions**
- Tightening money and credit conditions emerged
- Combined with weakened conditions in related countries
- Created unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (2008-2009)

**The Shock**
- Pre-crisis debt service: 82% of GDP (made UK vulnerable)
- Trigger: 2008 global financial crisis
- "Ugly deleveraging" ran from 2008 to 2009
- Self-reinforcing bust dynamics

**Economic Contraction**
- GDP fell 6%
- Stock prices fell 52%
- Home prices fell 19%
- Unemployment increased 3%

**Financial System Stress**
- UK financial institutions under considerable pressure
- Required aggressive policy response

**Debt Paradox During Depression**
- Despite needing deleveraging, debt-to-GDP rose 34% (24% annualized)
- Driven primarily by interest payments financed with new debt
- Lesser contribution from falling real incomes
- Attribution chart shows interest component dominated debt increase

**Composite Gauges**
- First two charts measure bubble/depression conditions
- Measure tightness/easiness of money and credit
- Distance from zero indicates extent of bubble
- Crossing above/below zero represents shifting into/out of bubble

**Bubble & Depression Gauge**
- Rose to ~1.0-1.2 during bubble phase (2005-2007)
- Crossed zero at crisis onset (2007-2008)
- Plunged to -2.5 during depression (2008-2009)
- Gradual recovery toward zero during reflation

**Tightening & Easing Gauge**
- Near zero to slightly positive during early bubble
- Sharp plunge to -4.0 at "Peak Easing" (2008-2009)
- Gradual normalization toward zero (2009-2017)
- Some volatility during reflation period

**Core Inflation**
- Ranged 1.5-2.0% during early bubble
- Rose to 3.0-3.5% at bubble peak (2007-2008)
- Fell sharply to ~1.0% during depression (2009)
- Recovered to 2.5-3.0% during reflation
- Returned to ~2.5% by 2017

### The Reflation Phase (2009-2015)

**Policy Response**
- "Slightly shorter than average bust phase"
- Policy makers provided enough stimulus for beautiful deleveraging
- Reflation began 2009

**Monetary Policy Actions**
- M0 increased 10% of GDP
- Interest rates pushed to 0%
- Real FX averaged -5% during stimulative phase

**Financial System Management**
- Very aggressive in managing financial institutions and bad debts
- Pulled 6 of 9 classic policy levers
- Nationalized banks
- Provided liquidity
- Directly purchased troubled assets

**Economic Outcomes**
- Nominal growth averaged 4% during reflation
- Sovereign long rates fell to 1%
- Unemployment declined 2%
- Debt-to-GDP fell 73% (12% annualized) during reflation

**Deleveraging Attribution (Reflation Period)**
- Driven by mix of rising real incomes and inflation
- Partially offset by interest payments financed with new debt
- Net new debt contribution (largest component, negative)
- Real growth and inflation both contributed meaningfully

**Recovery Timeline**
- Real GDP took 5 years to reach prior peak
- Equity prices in USD terms had not yet fully recovered by end of study period

### United Kingdom 2005-2015 Chart Deck Appendix

#### Indebtedness Metrics

**Total Debt and Debt Service**
- Total debt peaked ~85% of GDP (2007-2012)
- Debt service peaked ~80% of GDP (2007-2008)
- Both declined post-crisis
- Total debt fell to ~70% by 2017
- Debt service fell to ~60% by 2017

**FX Debt**
- Rose from ~120% to ~240% of GDP during bubble (2002-2007)
- High volatility during crisis
- Declined to ~120% by 2017
- Significant FX exposure for UK

**Debt Growth Dynamics**
- Debt growth peaked ~50% annually (2007)
- Change in debt-to-GDP ratio peaked ~30% (2007)
- Both turned sharply negative post-2008
- Debt growth bottomed near 0-10%
- Change in debt-to-GDP reached -10% to -20% trough

<!-- pp. 941-960 -->

## Germany 1918–1925: Wartime Hyperinflationary Deleveraging

### Overview: A Classic War-Driven Crisis

- Germany experienced a **wartime hyperinflationary deleveraging cycle** 1918-1925
- Unlike typical bubble-driven crises, roots lay in **WWI financing** through massive borrowing
- War economy disruption: shifted production to military, workforce to armed services
- **Debt rose to 158% of GDP** during war; 64% was foreign currency debt
- Growth severely hampered during war (-6% average)
- Losers of major wars face **far worse postwar depressions** than winners

### The War Phase (1914-1918)

#### Financing and Economic Structure
- Germany **borrowed heavily** to finance large fiscal deficits
- Economic statistics distorted by war production shifts
- Normal economic linkages broke down during wartime mobilization
- **Foreign currency exposure** created severe vulnerability (64% of GDP in FX debt)

### The Postwar Depression Phase (1918-1923)

#### Balance of Payments Crisis
- Postwar crisis ran 1918-1923 as both **depression and BOP crisis**
- Heavy foreign currency debt burden amplified crisis severity
- Self-reinforcing GDP decline of **-5%**
- Stock prices collapsed **-97%**
- Unemployment jumped **+16 percentage points**
- Financial institutions under severe pressure

#### The Debt Trap
- Even as Germany needed deleveraging, **debt/GDP rose +165%** (31% annualized)
- Mechanism: incomes falling while government absorbed **war-related costs**
- Peak fiscal deficit: **11% of GDP** during worst period
- Attribution analysis shows inflation couldn't offset the debt accumulation from falling incomes and fiscal costs

### The Reflation Phase (1923-1925)

#### Policy Response Dynamics
- **Key policy choice**: Allow financial tightening (painful but necessary) vs. print money (inflationary)
- Germany's initial tightening **insufficient** to produce needed adjustments
- Result: spiral of **declining exchange rates and hyperinflation**
- Inflation peaked at **over 10,000%**

#### Risk Factors for Hyperinflation
- Germany had most classic **"risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals**
- Biggest risk: **fiscal deficit** (peak 11% of GDP)
- Germany aggressive on **5 of 9 typical policy levers**:
  - Managing financial institutions
  - Handling bad debts

#### Breaking the Spiral
- Stopping hyperinflation required **significant structural changes**:
  - Abandoned hyperinflated **papiermark**
  - Adopted new **reichsmark** currency in 1924
- **15 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- Deleveraging attribution (reflation period): **-98% debt reduction** primarily through inflation
- Virtually complete debt wipeout via currency collapse

### Gauges and Metrics

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge
- Post-war depression phase clearly visible in gauge (deep negative territory)
- Reflation phase shows dramatic spike as hyperinflation took hold

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge
- Shows pattern of initial tightening attempts
- **Peak tightening** around 1923 before shift
- **Peak easing** as hyperinflation reached climax
- Sharp transition between extremes

#### Core Inflation
- Multiple spikes to **250-300% year-over-year**
- Shows episodic nature of hyperinflation attempts and stabilizations
- Brief stability periods punctuated by acceleration

### Monetary and Fiscal Data

#### Nominal Short Rate
- Relatively stable pre-war (~3-6%)
- Spike to **~7%** around 1920
- Collapse toward zero during hyperinflation
- Monetary policy ineffective once hyperinflation dynamic established

#### Money Supply (% GDP)
- Pre-crisis: ~4-5% of GDP
- Post-crisis expansion to **25-27% of GDP**
- Massive monetary expansion drove hyperinflation
- Clear visual of money printing response

#### Fiscal Balance
- Wartime: deficit **-50% of GDP** at trough (wartime financing)
- Brief improvement post-war
- Deterioration to **-10% of GDP** during depression
- Gradual improvement through reflation, approaching balance by 1928

### Economic Conditions

#### Real GDP
- Indexed decline from ~1.0 to **~0.65** (trough)
- Very gradual recovery through 1920s
- Full recovery only by late 1920s (15-year timeline confirmed)

#### Real Growth Rate
- High volatility during crisis period
- Swings from **-10% to +15%** year-over-year
- Reflects stops and starts of stabilization attempts

#### GDP Gap
- Pre-war: positive (~3-4%)
- Collapse to **-4%** trough
- Gradual closing through 1920s, returning positive by late decade

#### Core Inflation vs. Rates
- **Nominal long rate** and **nominal growth** initially tracked (both 4-6%)
- Massive divergence during hyperinflation
- Nominal growth spiked far above long rates as inflation dominated
- Convergence only after reichsmark adoption

#### Real Short Rate
- Pre-crisis: **+2% to +5%** (positive real rates)
- Collapse to **-2% to -3%** during hyperinflation (deeply negative)
- Money had negative real return—incentive to spend immediately

### Market Conditions

#### Equity Prices (USD-Indexed)
- Pre-war buildup from ~50 to ~120 (index)
- Catastrophic collapse to near zero
- Partial recovery to ~80-100 range by late 1920s
- Never fully recovered pre-war peak in USD terms

#### Interest Rate Environment
- Nominal long rate: **~5%** pre-war, declining to **~1-2%** by late 1920s
- Nominal short rate: similar pattern, falling to near zero
- Yield curve mostly positive throughout

#### FX and Real Assets
- **Real FX vs. TWI** (trade-weighted index): collapsed from +10% to **-20%** (currency depreciation)
- Partial recovery to neutral by late 1920s
- **Gold price (local FX, indexed)**: explosion from ~50 to **~250** peak (5x increase)
- Gold as inflation hedge worked dramatically
- **FX return for foreign investors**: climbed pre-war to ~110, collapsed to ~60 (near-half loss)

### External Position

#### Reserves
- Relatively stable ~100-120 (indexed) pre-war
- Brief dip during crisis to ~90
- Dramatic recovery to **~350** (indexed) by late 1920s
- Tripling of reserves as economy stabilized and capital returned

#### Capital Flows
- **Capital inflows**: pre-war buildup to **80-90% of GDP**
- Crisis: massive outflow, briefly to **-40% of GDP**
- Recovery to **+20-30% of GDP** by late 1920s
- Clear flight-to-safety and return pattern

#### Trade Position
- **Imports**: stable ~30-35% of GDP pre-war
- Dip during crisis to ~25%
- Recovery to ~27-30% by late 1920s

- **Current account**: small deficit **-1% to -3%** of GDP pre-war
- Widened to **-6% to -7%** during crisis
- Improvement to **-3% to -4%** by late 1920s (still deficit)

- **Capital outflows**: highly volatile
- Pre-war: **-20% to -60%** of GDP (capital flight)
- Crisis spike: **+60%** of GDP (repatriation/forced sales)
- Return to modest outflows by late 1920s

- **Exports**: steady ~25-33% of GDP
- Brief wartime dip
- Recovery to ~27-30% of GDP by late 1920s

### Key Lessons

- **War debts + foreign currency exposure = severe vulnerability** to postwar crisis
- **Insufficient tightening** can lead to worse outcomes (hyperinflation) than bold tightening
- **Fiscal deficits** are primary risk factor for hyperinflation spirals
- **Currency reform** ultimately necessary when hyperinflation becomes entrenched
- **Recovery timelines are long**: 15 years to regain real GDP peak
- **Hard assets (gold) protect** against currency collapse
- **Foreign investors suffer** massive losses in hyperinflationary episodes
- Once hyperinflation dynamic starts, **real interest rates go deeply negative**, accelerating the spiral

<!-- pp. 961-980 -->

## Argentina 1977–1988 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase (1977-1980)

- **Classic hyperinflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1977-1988
- Bubble driven by self-reinforcing cycle: rising debt + strong equity returns + strong growth
- By bubble's end: debt reached **39% of GDP** (peak), with **15% of GDP in foreign currencies**
- Large FX exposure created vulnerability to capital pullback
- Capital inflows modest but positive: averaging **~2% of GDP**
- Growth moderate at **2%**, but high economic activity (GDP gap peaked at **8%**)
- Equities averaged **52% annualized returns** during bubble — encouraged more borrowing, stimulated growth
- Competitiveness problem: real FX peaked at **+70%**
- Bubble pressures + dependence on foreign financing + weakened conditions in related countries = unsustainable

### The Depression Phase (1980-1985)

- Self-reinforcing bust triggered by **1980s Latin American Debt Crisis**
- Capital inflows fell **9% of GDP** — major shock
- Policymakers hiked short rates **>250%** (tightening)
- Real FX fell **93%** (currency collapse)
- GDP fell **14%**, stock prices fell **91%**
- Unemployment rose **3%**
- Currency weakness drove very high and rising inflation
- Financial institutions under considerable pressure
- Central bank spent down reserves to defend currency (reserves fell **83%**), eventually abandoned defense
- **Debt paradox**: despite needing deleveraging, debt/GDP rose **33%** (7% annualized) because:
  - Currency collapse increased burden of foreign-denominated debts
  - Government had to borrow more in response to crisis (peak fiscal deficit **5% of GDP**)

### The Reflation Phase (1985-1988)

- Key policy choice: allow tightening (painful but necessary) vs. print money (inflationary)
- Argentina's tightening wasn't enough — fell into spiral of declining exchange rates and hyperinflation
- Real FX bottomed at **-138%**, inflation peaked **>1,000%**
- Argentina had ~half the classic **"risk factors"** for bigger inflation spirals (biggest: low real short rates)
- Somewhat aggressive financial management: **3 out of 9 typical policy levers**
  - Nationalized banks
  - Provided liquidity
  - Benefited from **IMF assistance program**
- Stopping inflation ultimately required **structural changes**:
  - Abandoned hyperinflated **peso ley**
  - Adopted **peso argentino** in 1983
- Took **7 years** before real GDP reached prior peak

### Deleveraging Dynamics

**Depression period attribution** (1980-1985):
- Debt/GDP rose **7%** annualized despite deleveraging need
- Real growth negative (pink bar shows major GDP contraction)
- FX debt burden increased from currency collapse (orange)
- Inflation contributed (beige)
- Domestic debt changes (green)

**Reflation period attribution** (1985-1988):
- Debt/GDP fell **-16%**
- Massive inflation contribution (large pink bar — inflating away debt)
- Domestic debt reduction (green)
- Some FX effects from currency moves (orange/blue)

### Key Data Points

- Military personnel peaked at **~12% of total population** during WWI era
- Military spending peaked at **~60% of GDP** during WWI
- Government debt peaked at **~350% of GDP** in early 1920s
- Gold price (local FX) went exponential in 1923-1925 hyperinflation
- Gold reserves fluctuated between **0.5-3 billion goldmarks** (1913-1928)
- Equity markets closed during WWI; reopened with sharp volatility
- Nominal short rates spiked to **~5000%** during hyperinflation peaks
- Yield curve inverted dramatically (short-long spread reached **-250%**)
- Real FX for foreign investors collapsed from **160 to near 0** (1913-1923)
- Money supply (M0) peaked at **~35% of GDP** then collapsed to near **0%**

### Charts Context

The document includes comprehensive chart deck showing:
- **Indebtedness**: total debt, FX debt, debt service (all as % GDP)
- **Monetary/Fiscal Policy**: nominal rates, money supply, fiscal balance
- **Economic Conditions**: real GDP, growth rates, output gap, inflation, nominal rates vs. growth
- **Markets**: equity prices, yield curves, FX returns
- **External Position**: gold reserves, trade flows, capital flows
- **Government/Military**: personnel, spending, debt levels

Data shows classic pattern across multiple historical crises with "No Data Available" for many Argentina-specific early metrics, but rich data for 1975-1990 period.


<!-- pp. 981-1000 -->

## Brazil 1977-1987 Inflationary Deleveraging Case Study

### Overview: Classic Inflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- Brazil experienced **inflationary deleveraging** between 1977-1987, distinct from typical broad-based bubble cases
- Linked to other countries experiencing bubble-like conditions, building substantial debt through capital inflows
- **Pre-crisis debt: 158% of GDP**, with **70% foreign-currency denominated**—creating massive FX exposure
- Current account deficit: **5% of GDP**; capital inflows: **6% of GDP** average pre-crisis
- Crisis triggered by weakness in related economies, creating unsustainable dependency on foreign financing

### The Bubble Phase: Foreign Capital Dependency

- No broad domestic bubble, but **unsustainably strong capital inflows** driving debt accumulation
- High foreign-currency debt share (70% of GDP) left Brazil vulnerable to capital reversal
- Became dependent on continuous foreign financing to sustain current account deficit
- Combination of high debts + foreign financing dependency + weakened conditions in related countries = unsustainable

### The Depression Phase (1980-1983)

#### Crisis Trigger and Initial Impact

- **1980s Latin American Debt Crisis** triggered self-reinforcing bust and balance of payments/currency crisis
- **Capital inflows fell 5% of GDP**, forcing severe adjustment
- Policymakers responded with massive tightening: **short rates hiked 234%**
- **Currency (real FX) fell 20%**, amplifying crisis dynamics
- **GDP declined 6%**; **stock prices fell 51%**

#### Extreme Inflation Dynamics

- **Inflation peaked at 124%** during depression—high compared to similar cases
- Brazil had classic "risk factors" for **inflation spirals**, especially **low real short rates**
- Currency weakness contributed to rising inflation momentum
- Financial institutions came under severe pressure

#### Central Bank Response and Debt Dynamics

- Central bank **spent down 70% of reserves** defending the currency
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense**, allowing further depreciation
- Despite deleveraging need, **debt-to-GDP rose 54% (18% annualized)** during depression
- Two drivers of debt increase:
  1. **Currency depreciation** increased burden of foreign-denominated debts
  2. **Government borrowing** to respond to crisis (peak fiscal deficit: **11% of GDP**)

### The Reflation Phase (1983-1987)

#### Policy Choice: Inflation Over Deflation

- Key policy decision: whether to defend currency (painful but typically necessary) or **print money** (inflationary)
- Brazil **abandoned currency peg** after longer-than-average "ugly" phase
- Allowed sufficient **tightening** to reduce import spending and improve current account (**+3% of GDP improvement**)
- Made currency more attractive to hold, eventually stabilizing flows

#### Aggressive Financial Management

- Pulled **4 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- **Nationalized banks** and provided liquidity support
- Managed financial institutions aggressively, removing bad debts
- Received **IMF assistance program**

#### Deleveraging Through Nominal Growth

- **Debt-to-GDP fell 121% (27% annualized)** over reflation period
- Reduction came primarily from **nominal income rising**, not real deleveraging
- **Real FX bottomed at -33%** during "beautiful period," setting up renewed competitiveness

#### Recovery Timeline and Outcomes

- **4 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **Equity prices in USD terms recovered within 1.9 years**
- Lower currency positioned Brazil for export competitiveness
- Sustained high inflation (reaching **350%+ by 1990**) as cost of avoiding deflationary collapse

### Gauges and Attribution Analysis

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge

- Composite measure showing Brazil's movement through cycle phases
- Clear transition: Bubble → Depression (bottoming ~1983) → Reflation → new bubble formation
- Depression gauge reached approximately -1.8 before turning

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge

- **Peak tightening** around 1982-1983 coinciding with depression bottom
- Massive swing from tightening to **peak easing** during reflation
- Easing remained elevated through late 1980s, supporting nominal growth strategy

#### Core Inflation Trajectory

- Steady acceleration from ~50% (1975-1980) to **100%+** by 1982
- Dramatic spike to **300%+** by late 1980s
- Brief dip during depression (1983-1984) before resuming explosive growth
- Hyperinflationary dynamics by 1990

#### Deleveraging Attribution

**Depression Period (1980-1983):**
- **Real Growth**: Large negative contribution (red)
- **Inflation**: Positive contribution to nominal debt reduction
- **Domestic Debt Change**: Positive (green)
- **FX Debt Change due to FX Move**: Large positive contribution (orange)—currency depreciation increased FX debt burden
- **Net: +18% annualized** debt-to-GDP increase despite depression

**Reflation Period (1983-1987):**
- **Real Growth**: Modest positive contribution (blue)
- **Inflation**: Largest positive contribution (pink)—dominant deleveraging mechanism
- **Domestic Debt Change**: Significant positive contribution (green)
- **FX Debt Change**: Continued positive contribution
- **Net: -27% annualized** debt-to-GDP reduction

### Market Dynamics

#### Equity Markets

- **Depression**: 51% decline in USD terms
- Extreme volatility during 1980s crisis period
- Strong recovery during reflation, peaking ~450 (indexed) by late 1980s
- Pattern: crash → bottom (~100) → rally → peak → second decline

#### Interest Rates

- **Nominal short rates**: From ~50% (early 1980s) to **4000%** peaks (mid-1980s)
- **Nominal long rates**: Similar explosive pattern, though less volatile
- Real short rates: Highly volatile, swinging from **-50% to +200%**
- Rate volatility reflected ongoing inflation spiral and policy uncertainty

### External Position

#### Reserves and Capital Flows

- **Reserves (indexed)**: Rose from ~20 to peak ~110 (early 1980s), then declined to ~40 by 1990
- Dramatic drawdown during currency defense, partial rebuild during reflation
- **Capital inflows**: Peaked ~6% GDP (early 1980s), fell to **-4% GDP** during crisis
- Reversal to modest inflows during reflation, though never matching bubble-era levels

#### Current Account and Trade

- **Current account**: Swing from **-8% GDP deficit** (1982) to near-balance by mid-1980s
- **Imports**: Peaked ~20% GDP, compressed to **5% GDP** through crisis adjustment
- **Exports**: Rose from ~5% to peak ~16% GDP, then declined to ~7% by 1990
- **Capital outflows**: Peaked ~1.2% GDP during crisis before normalizing

### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

#### Money Supply

- **M0 as % GDP**: Declined from ~4.5% to ~1% by 1990
- Steady erosion reflected **inflation tax** and currency substitution
- Lower real money balances as inflation expectations became embedded

#### Fiscal Position

- **Fiscal deficit**: Widened to **-18% GDP** trough during depression
- Improved during reflation but remained elevated
- Fiscal dominance contributed to ongoing inflation pressures

### Economic Outcomes

#### Real GDP

- Indexed trajectory: Steady rise (1975-1980), **flat/decline** (1980-1983), resumed growth (1983+)
- **Real growth**: Highly volatile, swinging from **+10% to -4%**
- Recovery took 4 years to regain prior peak

#### Output Gap and Inflation

- **GDP gap**: Ranged from **-8% to +7%**, reflecting extreme cyclicality
- Negative gap during depression failed to contain inflation due to FX depreciation pass-through
- Core inflation accelerated throughout, reaching **350%** by 1990

### FX and Gold Markets

- **Real FX vs. TWI**: Peaked ~+90% (early 1980s), crashed to **-120% by 1990**
- Sustained real depreciation through both depression and reflation phases
- **FX return for foreign investors**: Peaked ~110 (indexed), crashed to ~10, partial recovery
- **Gold price (local FX)**: Exponential rise on logarithmic scale—hyperinflationary asset dynamics

### Key Lessons from Brazil 1977-1987

1. **Foreign-currency debt creates amplification**: 70% FX-denominated debt meant currency depreciation worsened solvency
2. **Policy choice matters**: Abandoning currency peg was painful but allowed eventual adjustment; defending would have depleted reserves without resolution
3. **Inflation as deleveraging tool**: Nominal income growth via inflation drove debt-to-GDP reduction, but at cost of **350%+ sustained inflation**
4. **Current account adjustment is essential**: Import compression and export promotion restored external balance
5. **Financial system support critical**: Nationalization and liquidity provision prevented complete collapse
6. **Recovery takes time**: 4 years to regain real GDP peak; equity markets recovered faster (1.9 years)
7. **Risk of inflation spiral**: Brazil's low real rates and FX depreciation created textbook conditions for runaway inflation
8. **IMF programs can help**: External assistance provided credibility and resources during adjustment


<!-- pp. 1001-1020 -->

## Chile 1978–1995 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase (1978-1981)

**Classic inflationary deleveraging cycle** driven by self-reinforcing dynamics:

- **Debt surge**: Rose 86% of GDP to pre-crisis peak of **145% of GDP**
- **Foreign currency exposure**: 32% of GDP in foreign currencies—massive pullback vulnerability
- **Strong capital inflows**: Investment flows averaged ~14% of GDP, financing 11% current account deficit
- **Growth acceleration**: 7% growth with GDP gap peaking at 11%
- **Asset returns**: Equities averaged **36% annualized returns** during bubble, encouraging more borrowing
- **Competitiveness loss**: Real FX peaked at **+36%**, undermining exports
- **Unsustainable dynamic**: Rising debt + foreign financing dependence + weakened regional conditions = inevitable reversal

### The Depression Phase (1981-1985)

**Self-reinforcing bust triggered by 1980s Latin American Debt Crisis:**

- **Foreign funding collapse**: Capital inflows fell 40% of GDP
- **Monetary tightening**: Policymakers hiked short rates by **31%**
- **Currency crash**: Real FX fell **50%**
- **Economic contraction**: GDP fell 14%, stock prices fell **74%**
- **Inflation spike**: Peaked at **33%** during depression—normal for these cases despite Chile's history of poorly-controlled inflation
- **Financial system stress**: Institutions under considerable pressure
- **Reserve depletion**: Central bank drew down reserves 53% defending currency before abandoning peg
- **Debt paradox**: Debt as % GDP rose 73% (19% annualized) despite deleveraging need—currency fall increased FX debt burden + government borrowed more (peak fiscal deficit 4% of GDP)
- **Debt service crisis**: Reached **45% of GDP** pre-crisis, making Chile vulnerable to shock

**Attribution insight**: Even though Chile needed deleveraging, debt/GDP increased because (1) currency depreciation raised FX-denominated debt burden, (2) government crisis borrowing

### The Reflation Phase (1985-1995)

**Key policy choice**: Let currency go vs. print money to replace capital outflows

Chile's approach after "ugly" phase:
- **Abandoned currency peg**, allowed tightening to flow through
- **Current account improvement**: +11% of GDP from reduced import spending
- **Currency attractiveness restored** through disciplined policy
- **Aggressive financial institution management**: Pulled **5 out of 9 classic policy levers**
  - Provided liquidity
  - Directly purchased troubled assets
  - IMF assistance program
  - Structural reforms for labor market flexibility
- **Debt reduction**: Fell 129% (13% annualized) over adjustment period
- **Deleveraging drivers**: Mostly from nominal income rising, not spending cuts
- **Currency bottom**: Real FX bottomed at **-32%** during "beautiful period"—restored competitiveness
- **Recovery timeline**: 5 years for real GDP to reach prior peak; equity prices in USD terms recovered within 9 years

**Deleveraging attribution (reflation period)**:
- Real growth: negative contribution
- Inflation: large positive contribution
- Change in domestic debt: positive contribution to deleveraging
- FX debt change due to FX move: modest contribution
- Net result: **-13% change in debt/GDP** over 1985-1995

### Key Metrics Summary

**Bubble indicators:**
- Total debt: 145% of GDP (pre-crisis peak)
- FX debt: 32% of GDP
- Debt service: 45% of GDP
- Capital inflows: ~14% of GDP average
- Current account deficit: -11% of GDP
- Real FX: +36% (peak overvaluation)
- Equity returns: 36% annualized
- Growth: 7%, GDP gap 11%

**Depression metrics:**
- Capital inflow collapse: -40% of GDP
- Rate hikes: +31 percentage points
- Real FX decline: -50%
- GDP contraction: -14%
- Stock price crash: -74%
- Peak inflation: 33%
- Reserve drawdown: -53%
- Debt/GDP increase: +73% (19% annualized)
- Peak fiscal deficit: 4% of GDP

**Reflation outcomes:**
- Current account swing: +11% of GDP
- Debt/GDP reduction: -129% (13% annualized)
- Real FX trough: -32%
- Recovery time to prior GDP peak: 5 years
- Equity recovery time (USD terms): 9 years

### Charts Included

**External Position**: Reserves (USD, indexed), Capital Inflows (%GDP), Imports (%GDP), Current Account (%GDP), Capital Outflows (%GDP), Exports (%GDP)

**Indebtedness**: Total Debt (%GDP), Debt Service (%GDP), FX Debt (%GDP), Change in Debt-GDP Ratio (Ann), Debt Growth (%GDP, Ann)

**Monetary and Fiscal Policy**: Nominal Short Rate, Money0 Level (%GDP), Fiscal Balance (%GDP)

**Economic Conditions**: Real GDP (Indexed), Real Growth (Y/Y), GDP Gap, Core Inflation (Y/Y), Nominal Long Rate, Nominal Growth, Real Short Rate

**Markets**: Equity Price USD (Indexed), Nominal Long Rate, Nominal Short Rate, Real FXvsTWI, Gold Price (Local FX, Indexed), FX Return for Foreign Investors (Indexed)

**Gauges**: Bubble & Depression Gauge, Tightening & Easing Gauge, Core Inflation (Y/Y) showing transitions between bubble (1981-11), peak tightening, depression, peak easing, and reflation (1991-11)

**Note on gauges**: Measure bubble/depression conditions and monetary policy tightness/easiness; crossing above/below zero represents shifting into or out of bubble phase

## Mexico 1979-1991: Inflationary Deleveraging Case Study

### Overview

- **Classic inflationary deleveraging cycle** from 1979-1991
- Three phases: bubble (1979-1981), depression (1981-1987), reflation (1987-1991)
- Crisis driven by foreign currency debt exposure and capital flow reversals

### The Bubble Phase (1979-1981)

#### Key Vulnerabilities Built Up

- **Debt reached 65% of GDP** at bubble peak
- **26% of GDP was foreign currency debt** — massive FX exposure
- **Capital inflows averaged 8% of GDP** — financing 6% current account deficit
- Strong growth at **9%** with GDP gap peaking at **9%**
- **Real FX appreciated +30%** — competitiveness became critical issue
- Combination of bubble pressures + foreign financing dependence + weakened regional conditions = unsustainable

#### The Self-Reinforcing Cycle

- Unsustainably strong capital inflows drove rising debt and strong growth
- Investment inflows financed current account deficits
- High economic activity levels accompanied by deteriorating competitiveness

### The Depression Phase (1981-1987)

#### The Triggering Shock

- **1980s Latin American Debt Crisis** + falling oil prices
- Dynamic reversed into self-reinforcing bust and balance of payments/currency crisis
- Pre-crisis **debt service reached 31% of GDP** — extremely vulnerable

#### The Cascade of Collapses

- **Capital inflows fell 17% of GDP**
- Policymakers **hiked short rates 128%** (tightening)
- **Real FX fell 74%** — meaningful currency decline
- **GDP fell 7%**
- **Stock prices fell 86%**

#### Extreme Inflation Dynamics

- **Inflation peaked at 151%** during depression — very high compared to similar cases
- Mexico had classic "risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals
- **Biggest risk factor: fiscal deficit**
- Currency weakness drove inflation higher

#### Central Bank Reserve Depletion

- Central bank **spent down reserves 66%** defending the currency
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense**
- Even though deleveraging was needed, **debt-to-GDP rose 41%** (7% annualized)

#### Why Debt Rose Despite Needing Deleveraging

- Currency collapse increased burden of **foreign-denominated debts**
- Government borrowed more to respond to crisis
- **Peak fiscal deficit: 13% of GDP**

### The Reflation Phase (1987-1991)

#### Critical Policy Choice

- Key determinant: how policymakers respond to adverse capital flows
- Option 1: Let currency fall + allow financial tightening (painful but typically necessary)
- Option 2: Print money to offset money leaving (inflationary)

#### Mexico's Approach

- **Abandoned the currency peg**
- After "relatively long ugly phase," allowed enough tightening to flow through
- **Current account improved 10% of GDP** — reduced import spending
- Made currency more attractive to hold

#### What Mexico Did NOT Do

- **Not aggressive managing financial institutions and bad debts**
- Only pulled **2 out of 9 classic policy levers**

#### Supporting Factors

- **IMF assistance program**
- **Structural reforms for labor market flexibility**

#### Deleveraging Mechanics

- **Debt-to-GDP fell 31%** (8% annualized) over adjustment period
- Reduction came mostly from **nominal income rising** — not real deleveraging
- Much lower currency (**real FX bottomed at -44%** during "beautiful period") set up renewed competitiveness

#### Recovery Timeline

- **7 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **Equity prices in USD terms recovered within 6 years**

### Attribution Analysis: Depression Period (1981-1987)

#### How Debt Changed Components

- **Real growth**: negative contribution (falling GDP increased debt burden)
- **Inflation**: large negative contribution (inflated away debt in nominal terms)
- **Change in domestic debt**: modest positive contribution
- **FX debt change due to FX move**: large positive contribution (currency collapse increased FX debt burden)
- **Net result: Debt %GDP rose 7% annualized** despite needing to deleverage

### Attribution Analysis: Reflation Period (1987-1991)

#### How Deleveraging Occurred

- **Inflation**: largest contributor to reducing debt burden (inflated nominal income)
- **Real growth**: modest positive contribution
- **Change in domestic debt**: substantial contribution (rising nominal debt in local terms)
- **FX debt changes**: small contributions both from FX moves and other factors
- **Net result: Debt fell -8% annualized**

### Gauges and Metrics

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge

- Composite measure of bubble/depression conditions
- Peaked during bubble phase at ~2.5
- Crashed to depression trough at ~-2.5
- Gradual recovery through reflation phase to ~1.5

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge

- Measures money and credit conditions
- Two major tightening episodes: early period and peak around 1986-1987
- Peak tightening reached ~2.0
- Sharp easing following peak tightening to ~-2.0 (peak easing)
- Crossing zero represents shifting into/out of bubble

### Economic Data Patterns

#### Indebtedness Metrics

- **Total debt**: rose from ~40% GDP to peak of ~85% GDP, then declined to ~60% GDP
- **Debt service**: rose from ~20% GDP to peak of ~120% GDP, fell back to ~40% GDP
- **FX debt**: surged from ~25% GDP to peak of ~80% GDP, then declined to ~30% GDP
- **Debt-GDP ratio change**: highly volatile, ranging from -10% to +15% annually
- **Debt growth %GDP**: peaked above 40% annually during crisis

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

- **Nominal short rate**: surged from ~20% to peak near **300%**, then fell to ~50%
- **Money level %GDP**: declined from ~3.5% to trough of ~2.2%, recovered to ~3%
- **Fiscal balance**: deteriorated from -4% GDP to trough of **-13% GDP**, recovered to positive territory

#### Economic Conditions

- **Real GDP**: strong growth during bubble, stagnation during depression, resumed growth in reflation
- **Real growth Y/Y**: peaked at ~10%, fell to ~-5%, recovered to ~6%
- **GDP gap**: peaked at ~9%, fell to ~-4%, recovered to ~3%
- **Core inflation Y/Y**: rose from ~30% to peak near **190%**, fell back to ~30%

#### Interest Rates

- **Nominal long rate**: tracked inflation, rising to peak near **170%**
- **Nominal short rate**: even more extreme, peaking near **300%**
- **Real short rate**: highly volatile, ranging from -100% to +100%

#### Markets

- **Equity prices (USD)**: bubble peak, crash of 86%, strong recovery to 10x initial levels
- **Nominal long/short rates**: both tracked extreme inflation dynamics
- **Real FX vs TWI**: appreciated +30% during bubble, crashed -80%, recovered to +20%
- **FX return for foreign investors**: stable during bubble, crashed during depression, gradual recovery
- **Gold price (local FX)**: exploded during crisis from near-zero to 12,000+ indexed

#### External Position

- **Reserves (USD)**: volatile, ranging from index of 50 to peaks above 400
- **Capital inflows**: surged to peak of ~15% GDP during bubble, reversed to negative during crisis, recovered to ~8% GDP
- **Imports %GDP**: rose from ~18% to peak of ~40%, declined during crisis, recovered to ~22%
- **Current account %GDP**: deteriorated from 0% to -10%, improved during crisis back toward 0%, then declined again to -4%
- **Capital outflows %GDP**: volatile swings from +1% to -6% of GDP

### Key Lessons

- **Foreign currency debt creates extreme vulnerability** to capital flow reversals
- **Currency collapse has dual effect**: inflates away local debt but increases FX debt burden
- **Inflation can deleverage** but at severe economic cost
- **Fiscal deficits are primary inflation risk factor** in these crises
- **Eventually allowing tightening** is typically necessary even if painful
- **Nominal income growth** (via inflation) was the primary deleveraging mechanism
- **Competitiveness gains from currency depreciation** set foundation for recovery
- **Recovery is slow**: 7 years to regain prior GDP peak

<!-- pp. 1041-1060 -->

## Peru 1980–1986 Case Study

### The Bubble Phase

**Pre-crisis debt accumulation**
- Peru lacked a traditional broad-based domestic bubble but was exposed to bubble conditions in connected economies and markets
- Total debt reached **107% of GDP** prior to crisis
- Foreign currency debt: **106% of GDP** — creating massive FX exposure vulnerability
- Current account deficit: **4% of GDP** — required continuous foreign financing inflows
- High debt levels combined with dependence on foreign capital and contagion from weakened neighboring countries created unsustainable conditions

### The Depression Phase (1982-1985)

**Crisis trigger and impacts**
- Shock came via **1980s Latin American Debt Crisis**
- GDP declined **13%** during depression
- Stock prices fell **56%**
- Unemployment increased **5%**
- Inflation peaked at **190%** during depression — high compared to other cases

**Why inflation spiraled in Peru**
- Peru had most classic "risk factors" for major inflation spirals
- **Low real short rates** were the biggest inflation risk factor
- Financial institutions came under severe pressure

**Central bank and currency dynamics**
- Central bank spent down reserves **44%** attempting currency defense
- Eventually policymakers abandoned currency defense
- Currency depreciated **53%** by end of crisis
- Despite needing deleveraging, debt as % GDP rose **163%** (42% annualized) because:
  - Currency collapse increased foreign-denominated debt burden
  - Government borrowed more to respond to crisis (peak fiscal deficit: **5% of GDP**)

### The Reflation Phase

**Policy response: abandoning the peg**
- Key choice in balance of payments/currency crises: let currency fall and allow tightening (painful but necessary) vs. print money to compensate for outflows (inflationary)
- Peru abandoned currency peg after slightly longer than average "ugly" phase
- Allowed enough tightening to reduce import spending — current account balance improved **13% of GDP**
- Made currency more attractive to hold

**Aggressive institutional reforms**
- Peru pulled **3 out of 9 classic policy levers** for managing financial institutions and bad debts
- **Nationalized banks** and provided liquidity
- Received **IMF assistance program**
- Enacted **structural reforms** to increase labor market flexibility

**Deleveraging mechanics**
- Debt as % of GDP fell **88%** (88% annualized) during 1985-1986 reflation period
- Reduction came mostly from **nominal income rising** rather than debt reduction
- Real FX bottomed at **-46%** during the "beautiful period" — set up renewed competitiveness
- Recovery timeline:
  - **4 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
  - **6 years** before equity prices (USD terms) recovered

**Political consequences**
- Crisis helped set stage for **Alan Garcia Perez** to take power — considered a populist leader by many

### Key Metrics Summary

**Bubble Phase Indicators:**
- Exports as % GDP rose from ~8% (1976) to ~18% (1991)
- Bubble & Depression Gauge peaked around 1.5 in early 1980s
- Core inflation climbed from ~50% to peaks of 190% and then 250%

**Depression Attribution (1982-1985):**
- Real growth: negative contribution
- Inflation: large positive contribution (~40-50% of debt change)
- FX debt change due to FX move: major contributor
- Net debt change: **+42% annualized**

**Reflation Attribution (1985-1986):**
- Real growth: positive contribution
- Inflation: largest contributor to deleveraging
- FX effects reduced debt burden
- Net debt change: **-88% annualized**

**Debt dynamics:**
- Total debt peaked around **75% of GDP** (1986), debt service around **250% of GDP**
- FX debt tracked similar pattern, peaking around **260% of GDP**
- Both declined sharply post-1986

**Monetary policy extremes:**
- Nominal short rate reached **100%** at peak (late 1980s)
- Money supply (M0) spiked to **70% of GDP** during crisis period
- Real short rate swung from slightly positive to **-150%** (deeply negative during hyperinflation)

**Market dynamics:**
- Equity prices (USD indexed) collapsed from ~600 to ~50-100 range, then recovered to ~400-500 by early 1990s
- Real FX trade-weighted index fell from 0% to **-100%+** (complete collapse)
- FX return for foreign investors: declined from index ~200 to nearly 0 by 1986
- Gold price in local FX (indexed): exponential rise reflecting hyperinflation — from 10 to 1,000,000+ on log scale

**External position:**
- Reserves (USD indexed): volatile, ranging 40-160, with sharp drawdowns during crisis
- Imports as % GDP: peaked around **28%** (early 1980s), fell to ~12%, recovered to ~22%
- Current account swung from **+4%** surplus to **-12%** deficit and back

## Philippines 1979–1992 Case Study

### Overview

**Crisis type:** Classic **transitory inflationary deleveraging cycle**
- Ran from 1979 to 1992
- Example of country "letting currency go" under external pressure
- Produces temporary inflation but gives policymakers more flexibility to set interest rates

### The Bubble Phase (1979-1982)

**Capital flow dynamics**
- Bubble characterized by **unsustainably strong capital inflows** and strong currency returns
- Debts rose **16% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **77% of GDP**
- Foreign currency debt: **56% of GDP** — large FX exposure
- Investment inflows averaged **9% of GDP** — financed current account deficit of **7% of GDP**

**Growth and competitiveness issues**
- Strong growth: **4%** during bubble
- Economic activity levels high — GDP gap peaked at **9%**
- Real FX peaked at **+18%** — competitiveness became an issue
- Bubble pressures plus dependence on foreign financing = unsustainable

### The Depression Phase (1982-1984)

**Crisis trigger**
- High debt left Philippines vulnerable to shock
<!-- pp. 1061-1080 -->

## Philippines 1979–1992: Depression Phase (continued)

### Crisis Mechanics

- **Capital flight triggers cascade**: Foreign funding fell 9% of GDP, forcing policy makers to hike short rates 34%
- **Currency collapse**: Real FX declined 16%, reinforcing GDP contraction (-11%) and stock market crash (-71%)
- **Inflation spike**: Peaked at 58% during depression—normal for this type of crisis given **Philippines had ~half the classic "risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals**, with high foreign-denominated debts being the biggest risk
- **Financial system stress**: Institutions under considerable pressure; central bank drew down reserves 100% defending currency before eventually abandoning defense
- **Debt dynamics paradox**: Despite needing deleveraging, debt/GDP remained roughly flat through crisis period—neither rising nor falling significantly

### Policy Response Framework: The Currency Decision

> "A key determinant of how balance of payments/currency crises play out is how policy makers respond to adverse capital flows: whether they let their currency go and allow a tightening of financial conditions to flow through (painful but typically necessary to resolve the crisis), or print money to make up for money leaving (which can be inflationary)."

- **Philippines chose currency abandonment**: After shorter-than-average "ugly" phase, allowed enough tightening to reduce import spending
- **Current account improvement**: Balanced improved 7% of GDP, making currency more attractive to hold
- **Aggressive financial sector management**: Pulled **7 out of 9 classic policy levers**:
  - Nationalized banks
  - Provided liquidity
  - Directly purchased troubled assets
  - IMF assistance program
  - Structural reforms to increase labor market flexibility

### Reflation Phase (1984-1992)

- **Deleveraging through nominal growth**: Debt/GDP fell 9% (1% annualized) over adjustment period
- **Reduction mechanism**: Came mostly from nominal income rising, not real deleveraging
- **Currency competitiveness**: Lower currency (real FX bottoming at -19% during beautiful period) set up renewed competitiveness
- **Recovery timeline**:
  - 6 years before real GDP reached prior peak
  - 5 years for equity prices in USD terms to recover

### Attribution Charts: Depression vs. Reflation

**Depression period (1982-1984)**:
- Debt %GDP: 0% (flat)
- Dominated by: FX debt change due to FX move (large negative contribution from currency collapse increasing foreign debt burden)
- Real growth: negative contribution
- Inflation: large negative deleveraging effect

**Reflation period (1984-1992)**:
- Debt %GDP: -1% net change
- Change in domestic debt: positive (green, largest component)
- FX debt change due to FX move: moderate positive
- Inflation: large negative contribution (inflating away debt)
- Real growth: moderate positive contribution

## Philippines 1979–1992 Chart Deck Appendix

### Indebtedness

- **Total debt peaked near 100% of GDP** in mid-1980s, then gradually declined to ~70% by 1992
- **Debt service burden**: Peaked at ~25% of GDP during crisis, fell to ~12% by 1992
- **FX debt exposure**: Rose from ~35% of GDP (1977) to peak ~80% of GDP (1987), then declined to ~60% (1992)
- **Debt-GDP ratio change**: Peaked at ~8% annualized growth during bubble, turned negative during reflation
- **Debt growth**: Peaked at ~18% annualized during bubble, moderated to ~10% during reflation

### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

- **Nominal short rate**: Rose from ~12% to peak above 50% during crisis, settled around 20-30% during reflation
- **Money supply**: Relatively stable at 3-4.5% of GDP throughout period
- **Fiscal balance**: Shifted from +2% surplus (early 1980s) to -4% deficit during crisis, improved to -1% by early 1990s

### Economic Conditions

- **Real GDP (indexed)**: Rose from 80 to ~105 during bubble, crashed to ~90, recovered to ~110 by 1992
- **Real growth**: Fell from +5% to -10% during crisis, recovered to +5-8% during reflation, moderated to ~1% by 1992
- **GDP gap**: Peaked at +10% during bubble, fell to -12% during depression, closed to near 0% during reflation, turned slightly negative by 1992
- **Core inflation**: Rose from ~10% to 60% during crisis, fell back to ~10-15% during reflation
- **Nominal long rate & nominal growth**: Both spiked above 40% during crisis, settled around 15-20% during reflation
- **Real short rate**: Highly volatile, ranging from -20% to +25%, reflecting inflation volatility

### Markets

- **Equity prices (USD)**: Rose from index ~300 to ~420 during bubble, crashed to ~50, recovered to ~300 by 1992
- **Nominal rates**: Short rates spiked to 50%+, long rates data not fully available
- **Real FX vs TWI**: Rose +20% during bubble (overvaluation), crashed -15%, partially recovered
- **FX return for foreign investors**: Declined from index ~120 to ~50 during crisis, recovered to ~120 by 1992
- **Gold price (local FX)**: Rose from index ~50 to ~280 during reflation period

### External Position

- **Reserves (USD)**: Built from index ~300 to ~600 during bubble, crashed to near 0 during crisis defense, partially rebuilt to ~250 by 1992
- **Capital inflows**: Averaged ~10% of GDP during bubble, turned negative to -2% during crisis, recovered to ~6% during reflation
- **Imports**: Rose from ~25% to ~33% of GDP during bubble, fell to ~15% during crisis, recovered to ~32% by 1992
- **Current account**: Deteriorated from -2% to -9% during bubble, improved to +4% during crisis adjustment, settled around -1% during reflation
- **Capital outflows**: Minimal during bubble, spiked to ~3% during crisis, normalized to ~1% during reflation
- **Exports**: Relatively stable around 20-27% of GDP throughout period

---

## Malaysia 1981–1990 Case Auto-Summary

**Classification**: Transitory inflationary deleveraging cycle (1981-1990)

**Key characteristic**: Classic example of **"letting currency go"** in face of external pressures—produces temporary inflation but gives policy makers more flexibility to set interest rates

### The Bubble Phase (1981-1984)

- **Debt buildup**: Rose 40% of GDP to pre-crisis peak of **153% of GDP**
- **Foreign currency exposure**: **42% of GDP in foreign currencies**—large pullback risk
- **Capital inflows**: Strong at ~14% of GDP, financing 10% current account deficit
- **Growth**: Strong at 7% with high economic activity (GDP gap peaked at 3%)
- **Competitiveness erosion**: Real FX peaked at +20% (overvalued)
- **Unsustainable dynamics**: Bubble pressures + dependence on foreign financing + weakened conditions in related countries

### The Depression Phase (1984-1987)

- **Trigger**: Commodity price declines hitting exports
- **Capital flight**: Inflows fell 11% of GDP
- **Currency collapse**: Real FX fell 19%
- **Economic contraction**: Stock prices fell 56%, unemployment rose 2%
- **Financial stress**: Institutions under considerable pressure
- **Central bank response**: Drew down reserves 26% defending currency, eventually abandoned defense
- **Debt paradox**: Despite needing deleveraging, debt/GDP rose **43% (17% annualized)**
  - Currency fall increased foreign-denominated debt burden
  - Government borrowing increased (fiscal deficit peaked at 10% of GDP)
<!-- pp. 1081-1100 -->

## Malaysia 1981-1990: The Reflation Phase (continued)

### Policy Response to Capital Flight: Currency vs. Inflation Tradeoff

- **Critical policy choice**: how to respond to adverse capital flows
  - Option 1: Let currency depreciate, allow financial tightening (painful but typically necessary)
  - Option 2: Print money to offset capital flight (inflationary risk)
- **Malaysia's approach**: Abandoned currency peg after slightly shorter than average "ugly" phase
  - Allowed sufficient tightening to reduce import spending
  - Current account improved by **9% of GDP**
  - Currency became more attractive to hold

### Aggressive Deleveraging Execution

- **Policy levers deployed**: 5 out of 9 classic tools
  - Nationalized banks
  - Provided liquidity to financial system
  - Managed bad debts aggressively
- **Debt reduction**: Total debt fell **74% (21% annualized)** during adjustment period
- **Mechanism**: Reduction primarily from **nominal income rising** (real growth driver), not deflation

### Recovery Dynamics

- **Currency competitiveness**: Real FX bottomed at **-12%** during "beautiful period"
  - Set foundation for renewed export competitiveness
- **GDP recovery timeline**: **1.8 years** to reach prior peak
- **Equity market recovery**: USD-denominated stock prices recovered within **6 years**

### Deleveraging Attribution (Reflation Period 1987-1990)

- Total debt/GDP decline: **-21%** annualized
- **Primary driver**: Real growth (largest component in chart)
- Domestic debt reduction and FX effects also contributed
- Modest inflation contribution compared to other cases

---

## Peru 1986-1995: Hyperinflationary Deleveraging Case Study

### The Bubble Phase (1986-1987)

**Debt dynamics:**
- Debt rose **55% of GDP** during bubble
- Pre-crisis peak: **184% of GDP**
- **Critical vulnerability**: High foreign currency exposure (**182% of GDP**)
  - Left Peru extremely exposed to capital flight risk

**Growth and activity:**
- Strong growth: **5%** annually
- GDP gap peaked at **11%** (economy running very hot)
- Current account deficit: **3% of GDP**

**Asset returns feedback loop:**
- Equities averaged **124% annualized returns** during bubble
- Strong returns encouraged more borrowing
- Self-reinforcing cycle of debt, equity gains, and growth
- **Unsustainable situation**: Dependence on foreign financing created fragility

### The Depression Phase (1987-1990)

**Trigger event:**
- Peru's leadership **refused to cooperate with international creditors**
- High debt levels left country vulnerable to this shock

**Economic collapse:**
- GDP fell **30%**
- Stock prices fell **91%**
- Financial institutions under "considerable pressure"

**Deleveraging paradox:**
- Despite ugly deleveraging, debt/GDP **fell 106% (30% annualized)**
- **Mechanism**: Reduction came from **income rising primarily through higher inflation**
- Nominal income growth offset debt burden despite real economic contraction

### Measurement Framework: Bubble & Depression Gauges

**Purpose**: Rough measures of cycle position
- First two charts show gauges measuring bubble/depression conditions
- Tightness/easiness of money and credit
- **Interpretation**: Distance from zero = extent of bubble; crossing zero = shift in/out of bubble

**Peru's gauge readings:**
- Clear bubble peak in 1986
- Sharp depression trough 1987-1990
- Reflation phase begins ~1991

### The Reflation Phase (1990-1995)

**Initial policy choice:**
- Insufficient tightening before falling into **hyperinflation spiral**
- Real FX bottomed at **-115%** (massive currency collapse)
- Inflation peaked at **over 10,000%**

**Risk factors for hyperinflation:**
- Peru had most classic "risk factors" for severe inflation spirals
- **Biggest risk**: Low real short-term interest rates
- Declining exchange rates and hyperinflation reinforced each other

**Policy response (somewhat aggressive):**
- Deployed **3 out of 9** typical policy levers
- **Nationalized banks**
- **Provided liquidity**
- Enacted **structural reforms** for labor market flexibility

**Critical turning point:**
- Stopping hyperinflation required "significant structural changes"
- **Abandoned hyperinflated inti, adopted sol in 1991**
- Major monetary reform essential to break spiral

**Recovery timeline:**
- **9 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- Much longer than Malaysia's 1.8 years
- Reflects severity of hyperinflationary path

### Deleveraging Attribution (Reflation Period 1990-1995)

- Total debt/GDP decline: **-9%** over period
- **Massive inflation component** (pink bar dominates chart)
- FX debt changes due to currency movements
- Small positive contribution from real growth
- Inflation eroded debt burden but at enormous economic cost

---

## Comparative Lessons: Malaysia vs. Peru

**Malaysia 1981-1990:**
- Abandoned peg early, allowed tightening
- Deleveraged via real growth primarily
- Fast recovery (1.8 years to GDP peak)
- Avoided hyperinflation

**Peru 1986-1995:**
- Insufficient tightening initially
- Fell into hyperinflation (>10,000%)
- Deleveraged via inflation eroding debt
- Slow recovery (9 years to GDP peak)
- Required radical monetary reform (new currency)

**Key insight**: Policy response to capital flight determines path—controlled adjustment (painful but quick) vs. hyperinflationary spiral (devastating and prolonged)

<!-- pp. 1101-1120 -->

## Argentina 1987-1993: Hyperinflationary Deleveraging Case Study

### Overview: Classic Hyperinflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- **Argentina** experienced a hyperinflationary deleveraging cycle between 1987-1993
- Pattern followed: bubble phase → depression phase → reflation phase
- Distinguishing feature: unlike many cases, Argentina lacked a broad-based domestic bubble before crisis
- However, connected to other economies/markets experiencing bubble-like conditions
- Built substantial **debt stock**: rose 15% of GDP to peak of **70% of GDP** during leveraging-up period
- Critical vulnerability: **39% of GDP in foreign currency debt** — created massive exposure to foreign capital pullbacks

### The Bubble Phase: Debt Accumulation Without Domestic Bubble

- No broad-based domestic bubble in traditional sense
- Debt accumulation nonetheless: from baseline to 70% of GDP
- **Foreign currency exposure** (39% of GDP) created structural weakness
- High debt + structural economic weaknesses = unsustainable situation
- Tied to external economies experiencing bubble conditions

### The Depression Phase (1987-1990)

#### The Trigger: Commodity Shock and Capital Flight

- Crisis triggered by **commodity price declines** hitting exports
- **Capital inflows collapsed**: fell 4% of GDP
- Policy response: **short-rate hikes exceeding 250%**
- **Currency collapse**: real FX fell 60%

#### Economic Devastation

- **GDP contraction**: fell 16%
- **Stock prices crashed**: down 33%
- **Unemployment surged**: increased 3%
- Currency weakness drove **extreme inflation** (rising sharply)
- Financial institutions under severe pressure

#### Central Bank Defense and Deleveraging

- Central bank **spent down reserves 26%** attempting to defend currency
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense**
- Despite ugly deleveraging, Argentina **reduced debt-to-GDP by 30%** (11% annualized) during this period
- Deleveraging source: primarily **income rising** (driven by higher inflation), not growth
- Attribution chart shows inflation as dominant factor in debt ratio reduction

#### Gauges and Metrics

- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: showed shift from mild bubble territory into deep depression, then reflation
- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: peak tightening reached ~2.0, followed by peak easing around -1.5
- **Core inflation**: spiked to extraordinary levels (chart shows peaks over 2000% Y/Y)

### The Reflation Phase (1990-1993)

#### Policy Response Decision Point

- Key question in balance-of-payments crises: how do policymakers respond to capital outflows?
  - Option 1: Allow financial tightening to flow through (painful but typically necessary)
  - Option 2: Print money to compensate (inflationary)
- Argentina initially chose insufficient tightening → **spiral of declining exchange rates and hyperinflation**

#### Hyperinflation Dynamics

- **Real FX bottomed at -135%** (massive currency depreciation)
- **Inflation peaked over 10,000%**
- Makes sense given Argentina had ~half the classic **"risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals**
- **Biggest risk factor**: long history of poorly-controlled inflation

#### Policy Interventions: Mixed Approach

- **Financial institution management**: aggressive, pulling **3 out of 9 typical policy levers**
- **Bad debt handling**: actively managed
- Benefited from **IMF assistance program**
- Enacted **structural reforms** for labor market flexibility
- Classic challenge: **stopping the inflationary spiral** required more fundamental changes

#### Structural Transformation

- Ultimately required **significant structural changes**:
  - **Abandoned hyperinflated austral** currency
  - Adopted **current Argentine peso**
  - Initially **pegged to US dollar**
- Recovery timeline:
  - **4 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
  - **2 years** for equity prices in USD terms to recover

#### Deleveraging Attribution: Reflation Period (1990-1993)

- Debt increase of **7% of GDP** during reflation
- Attribution breakdown shows:
  - Inflation contribution (negative, reducing debt burden)
  - FX debt changes due to FX moves
  - Real growth contribution
  - Change in domestic debt

### Political Impact

- Crisis had **notable political impact**
- Helped set stage for **Carlos Menem** to take power
- Many consider Menem a **populist leader**
- Crisis created political opening for significant leadership change

### Visual Data Summary

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy Charts

- **Nominal short rate**: extreme spikes to 500%+ during crisis (1987-1993)
- **Money0 level** (% GDP): peaked around 70% during crisis, fell sharply
- **Fiscal balance** (% GDP): fluctuated -5% to +4%, generally deteriorated through mid-1990s

#### Economic Conditions Charts

- **Real GDP** (indexed): deep V-shaped recovery from ~70 to above 105
- **Real growth** (Y/Y): volatility from -25% to +15%, severe negative spike 1989-1990
- **GDP gap**: ranged -15% to +15%, showed extreme overheating then collapse
- **Core inflation**: extraordinary spikes over 400-500% during hyperinflationary period
- **Nominal long rate vs. nominal growth**: both tracked together at extreme levels (400-500%)
- **Real short rate**: plunged below -200% during hyperinflation

#### Markets Charts

- **Equity prices USD** (indexed): remained flat ~100 until 1991, then explosive rise to 1000+ by 1996
- **Nominal long/short rates**: synchronized spikes to 500%+ during crisis
- **Real FX vs TWI**: collapsed to -120% during crisis, gradual recovery
- **FX return for foreign investors**: index collapsed from 200 to near 0 by 1991
- **Gold price** (local FX, indexed): exponential rise on logarithmic scale reflecting currency collapse

#### External Position Charts

- **Reserves** (USD, indexed): fell from ~150 to ~50 during crisis, then surged to 400+ by 1996
- **Capital inflows** (% GDP): negligible pre-crisis, surged to 14-15% by mid-1990s
- **Imports** (% GDP): fluctuated 10-30%, rose significantly post-crisis
- **Current account** (% GDP): improved from -12% deficit to near 0%, then deteriorated again
- **Capital outflows** (% GDP): peaked around 6%, highly volatile
- **Exports** (% GDP): relatively stable 15-20% range

#### Indebtedness Charts

- **Total debt** (% GDP): peaked ~70%, debt service ~12% GDP
- **FX debt** (% GDP): peaked near 65%, declined significantly during deleveraging
- **Change in debt-GDP ratio** (ann): volatile swings from -10% to +10%
- **Debt growth** (% GDP, ann): peaked ~30%, declined to under 10%

### Key Lessons

- **Foreign currency debt** creates existential vulnerability in crisis
- **Insufficient tightening** + **money printing** = hyperinflationary spiral
- **Structural reforms** (currency change, IMF assistance, labor flexibility) necessary but took time
- **Historical inflation problems** compound crisis severity
- **Political consequences** can be profound — crises create openings for populist leaders
- **Recovery possible but slow**: 4 years to restore real GDP, significant structural transformation required


<!-- pp. 1121-1140 -->

## Brazil 1987–1995 Case Study: Hyperinflationary Deleveraging

### Overview

**Brazil** experienced a **classic hyperinflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1987 and 1995, demonstrating the self-reinforcing dynamics of bubble formation, depression, and ultimately hyperinflation-driven reflation.

### The Bubble Phase (1987-1990)

#### Debt Dynamics
- Debt rose **69% of GDP** during bubble, reaching pre-crisis peak of **177% of GDP**
- **26% of GDP was foreign currency debt** — creating massive FX exposure vulnerability
- Capital inflows averaged **-3% of GDP** (outflows) during bubble period despite rising debt

#### Growth and Economic Activity
- Growth strong at **3%** during bubble phase
- **GDP gap peaked at 7%** — economy running very hot
- High levels of economic activity fueled by debt-driven spending

#### Asset Returns and Currency
- Equities averaged **16% annualized returns** during bubble — encouraging more borrowing
- **Real FX peaked at +35%** appreciation
- Strong asset returns created self-reinforcing cycle: rising debt → growth → asset gains → more borrowing
- **Competitiveness became a major issue** due to currency strength

#### Sustainability Issues
> "Taken together, these bubble pressures, combined with the weakened conditions of related countries, created an unsustainable situation."

The bubble was fundamentally unstable: high foreign currency debt, capital outflows, currency overvaluation, and contagion risk from neighboring countries.

### The Depression Phase (1990-1991)

#### The Trigger
- **Collapse of price control regulations** and resulting **inflation shock**
- High debt levels left Brazil vulnerable to any negative shock
- Dynamic reversed into self-reinforcing bust and **balance of payments/currency crisis**

#### Economic Contraction
- GDP fell **7%** during depression
- Stock prices collapsed **70%**
- Unemployment rose **4%**
- Self-reinforcing declines across all metrics

#### Currency and Reserves
- Central bank **drew down reserves 28%** defending the currency
- By end, policymakers **abandoned currency defense**
- Currency ultimately fell **19%**
- Real short rates spiked to **1000%+** at peak

#### Debt Burden Increases
- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt/GDP rose 40% (37% annualized)**
- Increased because:
  1. **Currency depreciation** increased burden of FX-denominated debts
  2. **Government borrowing** to respond to crisis (peak fiscal deficit **19% of GDP**)

#### Financial System Stress
- **Financial institutions under considerable pressure**
- Banking system fragility added to economic weakness

### Attribution of Debt Changes

The **Deleveraging Attribution charts** reveal the composition of debt changes:

**Depression Period (1990-1991):**
- Total debt change: **+37% annualized** (debt rose despite contraction)
- Major components:
  - **FX Debt Change due to FX Move** (currency depreciation mechanically increased FX debt burden)
  - **Change in Domestic Debt** (government crisis borrowing)
  - **Real Growth** was negative but overwhelmed by other factors
  - **Inflation** contributed to nominal debt increase

### The Reflation Phase (1991-1995)

#### Policy Response Choice
> "A key determinant of how balance of payments/currency crises play out is how policy makers respond to adverse capital flows: whether they allow a tightening of financial conditions to flow through (painful but typically necessary to resolve the crisis), or print money to make up for money leaving (which can be inflationary)."

**Brazil chose insufficient tightening**, leading to hyperinflationary spiral.

#### Hyperinflation Dynamics
- **Real FX bottomed at -16%** (massive depreciation)
- **Inflation peaked over 5,000%** year-over-year
- **Core inflation** hit extreme levels shown in charts
- **Nominal growth** reached **4000%+** at peaks
- **Nominal short rates** exceeded **4000%**

#### Why Hyperinflation?
Brazil had **classic "risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals**:
1. **Long history of poorly-controlled inflation** — worst risk factor
2. **High foreign currency debt exposure**
3. **Weak financial institutions**

#### Policy Actions Taken
- **2 out of 9 typical policy levers** pulled — inadequate response
- Brazil was **not aggressive in managing financial institutions and bad debts**
- Received **IMF assistance program**
- Enacted **structural reforms for labor market flexibility**

#### Ultimate Resolution
> "Stopping the inflationary spiral ultimately required Brazil to make more significant structural changes, including abandoning the hyperinflated cruzado and adopting first the cruzeiro and then the modern real in 1994."

- Required **abandoning hyperinflated cruzado currency**
- Introduced **cruzeiro**, then **modern real (1994)**
- Took **1.4 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **Equity prices in USD terms recovered within 3 years**

### Reflation Attribution (1991-1995)

**Deleveraging Attribution: Reflation Period**
- Total debt change: **-27%** over period (successful deleveraging through inflation)
- Composition:
  - **Inflation** was dominant force reducing real debt burden (largest component)
  - **Change in Domestic Debt** (some real reduction)
  - **FX Debt Changes** (both from FX moves and other factors)
  - Real growth contribution visible but secondary

### Gauges and Summary Metrics

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge
- Shows clear bubble formation (1987-1989)
- Sharp transition to depression (1990-1991)
- Extended reflation period (1991-1995+)
- Gauge crosses from positive to deeply negative during crisis

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge
- **Peak Tightening** around 1989-1990 crisis
- **Peak Easing** in early 1990s
- Gradual normalization through mid-1990s

#### Core Inflation
- Spike to **~10,000% Y/Y** at 1989-1990 peak
- Second spike to **~5,000%** around 1993-1994
- Demonstrates multiple waves of hyperinflation before stabilization

### Market Behavior

#### Equity Prices
- Rose dramatically during bubble (indexed from ~100 to 1,100+)
- Collapsed **70%** during depression
- Volatile recovery through 1990s
- Reached ~1,000 index level by late 1990s

#### Interest Rates
- **Nominal long and short rates** hit **2000%** during crisis peaks
- Multiple spikes corresponding to inflation waves
- Real short rate showed extreme volatility (±1000% range)

#### FX and External Position
- **Real FX vs TWI** fell from +40% overvaluation to -120% undervaluation
- **FX Return for Foreign Investors** volatile: peaked ~130 index, crashed to ~10
- **Gold price in local FX** increased exponentially (from 0.1 to 1,000,000+ indexed) — classic hyperinflation hedge

### External Accounts

#### Reserves and Capital Flows
- **Reserves (USD)** rose from index ~100 to 600+ through 1990s (rebuilt after crisis)
- **Capital Inflows** shifted from slight outflows to **+18% of GDP** by late 1990s
- Foreign capital returned as reforms took hold

#### Trade Balance
- **Imports** fell to **~3% of GDP** during crisis (1990), then rose to **18% by 1999**
- **Current Account** swung from small surplus to **-6% of GDP** deficit by late 1990s
- **Capital Outflows** peaked around **-8% of GDP** during crisis, then reversed
- **Exports** bottomed around **5% of GDP**, recovered to **16% by 1999**

### Key Lessons

1. **Foreign currency debt creates amplification**: When currency falls, FX debt burden rises mechanically
2. **Insufficient tightening prolongs crisis**: Half-measures led to multi-year hyperinflation
3. **Institutional credibility matters**: History of inflation made spiral worse
4. **Structural reform eventually required**: Currency changes, IMF program, labor reforms all needed
5. **Recovery takes time**: 1.4 years to GDP recovery, 3 years for equity markets in USD terms
6. **Attribution matters**: Understanding components of debt changes reveals crisis mechanics

### Data Presentation

The case study includes comprehensive chart deck showing:
- **Economic Conditions**: Real GDP, growth, GDP gap, core inflation, nominal rates
- **Markets**: Equity prices, interest rates, yield curves, FX indicators, gold
- **External Position**: Reserves, capital flows, imports, current account, exports
- **Debt Metrics**: Total debt, FX debt, debt service, debt growth
- **Policy Variables**: Fiscal balance, money supply, short/long rates

**Gauges provide rough composite measures** of bubble/depression conditions and monetary tightness, showing phase transitions clearly.

<!-- pp. 1141-1160 -->

## Turkey 1990–1995 Case Auto-Summary

### The Bubble Phase

- **No broad-based bubble** in years before crisis, but substantial debt buildup to 41% of GDP
- **26% of GDP in foreign currency debt** — large exposure to foreign capital pullback risk
- **Foreign financing dependency**: investment inflows averaging 2% of GDP pre-crisis
- High debts + foreign financing dependence = unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (1993-1994)

#### Crisis Trigger
- Government moves to undermine **central bank independence** triggered the crisis
- **Capital inflows collapsed** by 8% of GDP
- Policy makers hiked **short rates by 203%** (tightening response)
- **Real FX fell 26%** — meaningful currency decline
- **GDP fell 12%**, **stock prices fell 70%**

#### Inflation Dynamics
- Currency weakness drove **inflation to 117%** peak during depression
- High compared to similar cases despite Turkey having only ~25% of classic "risk factors" for major inflation spirals
- Biggest inflation risk factor: long history of poorly-controlled inflation

#### Financial System Pressure
- **Central bank spent down reserves 99%** defending currency before abandoning defense
- Financial institutions under considerable pressure
- **Debt-to-GDP rose 9% (17% annualized)** during depression despite deleveraging need
- Currency depreciation increased burden of foreign-denominated debt

### The Reflation Phase (1994-1995)

#### Policy Response Choice
- Key choice: allow tightening to flow through (painful but necessary) vs. print money (inflationary)
- After short "ugly" phase, policy makers **allowed enough tightening** to resolve crisis
- **Current account improved 5% of GDP** via reduced import spending
- Currency became more attractive to hold

#### Debt Management
- Turkey **aggressive on bad debts**: pulled 3 of 9 classic policy levers
- **IMF assistance program** provided support
- **Debt-to-GDP fell 11% (7% annualized)** over adjustment period
- Reduction mostly from **nominal income rising**, not debt reduction

#### Recovery Metrics
- **Real FX bottomed at -21%** during beautiful period — set up renewed competitiveness
- **1.6 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **Equity prices in USD terms recovered within 4 years**

### Gauges and Metrics

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge
- Clear bubble peak ~1993
- Sharp depression trough 1993-1994
- Reflation phase follows through late 1990s

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge
- Peak tightening 1993-1994 aligned with crisis
- Gradual easing through late 1990s and early 2000s

#### Core Inflation
- Peaked ~120% Y/Y during 1993-1994 crisis
- Gradual decline through 1990s-2000s, reaching ~20% by 2003

### Chart Deck Appendix Data

#### Indebtedness
- **Total debt** rose from ~30% (1988) to peak ~55% (2003)
- **Debt service** climbed from ~15% to peak ~75% of GDP around 2001
- **FX debt** increased from ~25% to ~60% of GDP (1988-2003)
- Debt-GDP ratio change: mostly positive, peaking ~7% annualized
- Debt growth ranged 10-22% of GDP annually

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- **Nominal short rates**: extreme volatility, spiking to 300% during crisis, ranging 50-200% through late 1990s
- **Money supply**: relatively stable 4-7% of GDP throughout period
- **Fiscal balance**: deteriorated from balanced (1993) to -10% deficit (2003)

#### Economic Conditions
- **Real GDP**: growth interrupted by sharp 1994 contraction, resumed climb to index ~135 by 2003
- **Real growth**: volatile, ranging -10% to +15%, averaging 5-8% post-crisis
- **GDP gap**: swung from +10% (pre-crisis) to -10% (1994), recovering to near zero by 2003
- **Core inflation**: peaked 120% (1994), declined to ~20% by 2003
- **Nominal long rate vs growth**: both extremely elevated during crisis (100-140%), declining together
- **Real short rate**: extreme volatility, reaching 200% during crisis, fluctuating 0-150% afterward

#### Markets
- **Equity prices (USD)**: highly volatile — multiple boom-bust cycles, ranging index 0-250
- **Nominal long rate**: followed short rate patterns (data shows alignment with growth dynamics)
- **Nominal short rate**: 50-300% range, extreme volatility
- **Yield curve data**: not available for this period
- **Real FX trade-weighted**: peaked ~20% (1990), crashed to -40% (1994), partial recovery to -10% (late 1990s)
- **FX returns for foreign investors**: relatively flat until mid-1990s, then explosive growth to index ~550 by 2003
- **Gold price (local FX)**: exponential rise from near zero to 12,000+ index, reflecting currency collapse

#### External Position
- **Reserves (USD)**: grew from index ~100 (1988) to ~800 peak (2000), fell to ~400 (2001)
- **Capital inflows**: rose from near zero to peak ~9% of GDP (1999-2000), fell to ~3% (2001)
- **Imports**: increased from ~6% to peak ~14% of GDP (mid-1990s), declined to ~8% (2003)
- **Current account**: swung from +2% surplus to -5% deficit, highly volatile
- **Capital outflows**: ranged 0% to -3.5% of GDP, increased outflows late 1990s
- **Exports**: rose from ~8% to peak ~14% of GDP (1994-1996), declined to ~8% (2003)

### Key Deleveraging Dynamics

#### Depression Period Attribution (1993-1994)
- **Real growth**: negative contribution (bottom portion, red/pink)
- **Inflation**: massive positive contribution (orange, top portion)
- **FX debt change due to FX move**: significant contribution (light blue)
- **Change in domestic debt**: smaller contribution (green)
- Net result: **+17% debt-to-GDP** despite deleveraging pressures

#### Reflation Period Attribution (1994-1995)
- **Inflation**: largest contributor to deleveraging (orange, negative ~30% contribution)
- **Real growth**: negative but smaller (red/pink bottom)
- **Change in domestic debt**: positive contribution (green, ~7%)
- **FX debt changes**: relatively small
- Net result: **-7% debt-to-GDP** annualized decline

<!-- pp. 1161-1180 -->

## Mexico 1991-2005 Case Study

### Case Overview: Classic Inflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- Mexico experienced a complete debt cycle between 1991-2005: bubble, crisis, and reflation phases
- **Inflationary deleveraging** distinguished this case from deflationary cases
- Timeline: bubble (1991-1994) → depression/crisis (1994-1995) → reflation (1995-2005)

### The Bubble Phase (1991-1994)

#### Debt Accumulation Dynamics

- **Debt rose 10% of GDP** during bubble, reaching **85% of GDP** pre-crisis peak
- **25% of GDP was foreign currency debt** — creating major vulnerability to capital flight
- Self-reinforcing cycle: capital inflows → rising debt → strong equity returns → growth
- **Capital inflows averaged ~8% of GDP**, financing **7% of GDP current account deficit**

#### Economic Conditions During Bubble

- **Strong growth at 4%** with **GDP gap peaking at 3%** (economy running hot)
- **Equity returns averaged 25% annualized** over the bubble period
- Investment inflows around 8% of GDP sustained the expansion
- High levels of economic activity created unsustainable situation given foreign financing dependence

#### Key Vulnerability

> Mexico's dependence on foreign financing created an unsustainable situation

- Large share of foreign currency debt (25% of GDP) left Mexico exposed to capital pullback
- Current account deficit of 7% of GDP required continuous foreign capital inflows
- Strong asset returns encouraged more borrowing, feeding the bubble

### The Depression Phase (1994-1995)

#### Trigger: Political Violence Shock

- Crisis triggered by **outbreak of political violence** (not purely economic)
- High debt levels left Mexico vulnerable when shock arrived
- Self-reinforcing bust and balance of payments/currency crisis ensued
