#### Economic Collapse

- **GDP fell 10%** during depression phase
- **Stock prices crashed 66%**
- **Unemployment increased 3%**
- Financial institutions came under considerable pressure

#### Currency Crisis Dynamics

- **Currency fell 37%** despite intervention efforts
- Central bank **spent down reserves 100%** trying to defend the currency
- Eventually policymakers abandoned currency defense
- Currency weakness drove **inflation to 43% peak** during depression

#### Inflation Characteristics

- 43% peak inflation was **normal for this type of crisis**
- Mexico had **~half the classic "risk factors"** for bigger inflation spirals
- **Biggest risk factor: long history of poorly-controlled inflation**
- Makes sense given Mexico's inflationary history

#### Debt-to-GDP Ratio Remained Flat

- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt as % GDP roughly flat** through depression period
- Inflation offset nominal debt burden (inflationary deleveraging mechanism)
- Attribution chart shows minimal change in debt/GDP ratio during 1994-1995

### The Reflation Phase (1995-2005)

#### Policy Response: Currency Adjustment + Aggressive Bank Management

- Key choice: **let currency go vs. print money vs. tighten**
- Mexico chose to **abandon currency peg** after initial defense
- After "ugly phase," allowed **enough tightening to reduce import spending**
- **Current account improved 7% of GDP** through reduced imports

#### Aggressive Financial Institution Management

- Pulled **6 out of 9 classic policy levers** for bank crisis management:
  - **Nationalized banks**
  - **Provided liquidity**
  - **Directly purchased troubled assets**
- Also benefited from **IMF assistance program**
- Enacted **structural reforms for labor market flexibility**

#### Deleveraging Through Nominal Growth

- **Debt/GDP fell 41%** over reflation period (**4% annualized reduction**)
- Reduction came **mostly from nominal income rising** (inflation + real growth)
- Not from actual debt paydown — inflationary mechanism at work

#### Currency Competitiveness Reset

- **Real FX bottomed at -26%** during the "beautiful period"
- Much lower currency set up Mexico for **renewed competitiveness**
- Exports as % GDP rose from ~12% to ~22% by 2005

#### Recovery Timeline

- **Took 2 years** for real GDP to reach prior peak
- **Equity prices in USD terms recovered within 10 years**
- FX returns for foreign investors eventually exceeded pre-crisis levels

#### Deleveraging Attribution (1995-2005)

- Real growth (negative impact on debt ratio shown as blue)
- Inflation (substantial pink/orange section) — major deleveraging force
- Change in domestic debt (green) — modest contribution
- FX debt changes from currency moves and other factors
- Net result: **-4% annual debt/GDP reduction**

### Chart Deck Data: Key Metrics

#### Indebtedness

- Total debt peaked ~45% GDP (1994-1995), fell to ~35% by 2005
- Debt service peaked over 100% GDP equivalent measure during crisis
- FX debt reached ~40% GDP peak, declined to ~20% by 2005
- Debt growth spiked ~20% annual during bubble, normalized to ~5% by 2005

#### Monetary Policy

- **Nominal short rates spiked to ~110%** during crisis (1995)
- Rates gradually normalized from ~40% to under 10% by 2005
- Money supply (M0) as % GDP dropped during crisis, recovered steadily to ~3% by 2005

#### Fiscal Position

- Fiscal balance improved from -4% GDP (early 1990s) to slight surplus (~5% GDP peak 1992)
- Returned to near-balanced position (~0 to -1% GDP) through reflation period

#### Economic Indicators

- Real GDP: sharp V-shaped recovery after 10% drop
- GDP gap plunged to -10%, recovered to positive territory by late 1990s
- Core inflation: 43% peak (1995), declined steadily to ~5% by 2005
- Real short rate spiked to ~90% during crisis peak

#### Markets

- Equity prices (USD): 66% crash, full recovery by mid-2000s
- Nominal rates (short/long): extreme spike to 110% short rate during crisis
- Yield curve showed severe inversion during crisis period
- Real FX vs TWI: -40% trough during crisis, partial recovery
- Gold price in local FX terms: massive spike during crisis (indexed to ~250), stabilized

#### External Position

- Reserves: 100% drawdown during crisis, rebuilt 7-8x by 2005
- Capital inflows: -2% GDP during crisis, recovered to ~3% GDP
- Imports: rose from ~15% GDP to ~25-30% GDP by 2005
- Current account: improved from -7% deficit to near-balance
- Capital outflows: volatile, ranging -3% to +3% GDP

### Gauges: Measuring Bubble/Depression Dynamics

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge

- Composite measure of money/credit conditions and tightness/easiness
- Clear bubble peak (1.5+) before 1994
- Sharp plunge to depression (-2.0 trough) in 1994-1995
- Gradual reflation back toward neutral through 2005

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge

- Peak tightening (~1.2) during 1994-1995 crisis
- Gradual shift to peak easing (~-0.5) by mid-2000s
- Measures policy stance shifts relative to neutral

#### Core Inflation

- Visual confirmation of 43% peak (1995)
- Steady disinflation from 30%+ (early 1990s) to ~5% by 2005

### Key Lessons from Mexico Case

#### Critical Policy Choice in Currency Crisis

- **Whether to let currency go vs. print money vs. tighten** determines crisis path
- Mexico initially defended (spending reserves), then abandoned peg
- Allowing tightening was "painful but typically necessary to resolve the crisis"
- Alternative (printing money) "can be inflationary"

#### Inflationary Deleveraging Mechanism

- Debt/GDP can fall even without nominal debt reduction
- **Inflation + nominal growth reduces debt burden** as % of rising GDP
- Mexico's debt/GDP fell 4% annually through this mechanism during reflation
- Contrasts with deflationary deleveraging where debt/GDP rises despite austerity

#### Vulnerability Factors

- **Foreign currency debt exposure** (25% of GDP) amplified crisis
- **Current account deficit** (7% of GDP) required ongoing capital inflows
- **History of inflation** made inflation spiral more likely
- Political shock can trigger crisis when underlying vulnerabilities exist

#### Effective Crisis Management

- **Aggressive bank intervention** (6 of 9 policy levers deployed)
- **IMF assistance** supplemented domestic response
- **Structural reforms** (labor market flexibility) supported recovery
- **Currency depreciation** (-26% real FX) restored competitiveness
- Combined approach enabled relatively quick recovery (2 years to GDP peak)

<!-- pp. 1181-1200 -->

## Bulgaria 1995-2003: Hyperinflationary Deleveraging

### The Bubble Phase

- **Bulgaria** did not experience a broad-based bubble before the crisis, but built up substantial debt stock
- **82% of GDP in foreign currency debt** created large exposure to foreign capital pullback
- Current account deficit of **4%** reflected dependence on continuous foreign financing
- High debts plus foreign financing dependency created unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (1995-1997)

#### Initial Shock and Currency Crisis
- Wave of losses from over-indebted companies/banks triggered the downturn
- Foreign funding collapsed: **capital inflows fell by 6% of GDP**
- Policy tightening: **short rates hiked by 228%**
- **Real FX fell by 96%** — massive currency decline
- **GDP fell by 13%** in self-reinforcing spiral
- Currency weakness drove very high and rising inflation
- Central bank spent down **75% of reserves** defending currency before abandoning defense

#### Deleveraging Attribution During Depression
- Debt-to-GDP rose **13%** despite deleveraging need
- Breakdown: large inflation component, FX debt increases from currency collapse
- Real growth negative, domestic debt changes modest
- **FX debt burden increased** as currency fell, offsetting other deleveraging efforts

### The Reflation Phase (1997-2003)

#### Policy Response — The Tightening Trap
- Key decision point: allow tightening (painful but necessary) vs. print money (inflationary)
- Bulgaria's initial tightening insufficient → fell into **hyperinflation spiral**
- **Real FX bottomed at -63%**, **inflation peaked over 500%**

> "Bulgaria had most of the classic 'risk factors' for bigger inflation spirals (with the biggest risk factor being a long history of poorly-controlled inflation)."

#### Aggressive Policy Response
- **Pulled 5 out of 9 typical policy levers**:
  - Nationalized banks
  - Provided liquidity
  - Directly purchased troubled assets
  - Received **IMF assistance program**
  - Managed financial institutions and bad debts aggressively

#### Structural Reform Required
- Stopping inflation spiral required **significant structural changes beyond initial measures**
- **Redenominated the lev**, pegging it to the **Deutsche Mark**
- Backed new currency by **foreign exchange reserves**
- **8 years** before real GDP reached prior peak — extended recovery period

#### Risk Factors for Hyperinflation
- Bulgaria possessed classic risk factors for inflation spirals
- **History of poorly-controlled inflation** = biggest risk factor
- Made hyperinflation outcome more likely when crisis hit

---

## Thailand 1993-2004: Transitory Inflationary Deleveraging

### Overview
- Classic example of **"letting currency go"** in face of external pressures
- Produces temporary inflation but gives policymakers **more flexibility to set interest rates**
- Transitory inflationary deleveraging cycle (1993-2004)

### The Bubble Phase (1993-1996)

#### Bubble Drivers
- Self-reinforcing cycle: **unsustainably strong capital inflows** + rising debt + strong equity returns + strong growth
- **Debts rose by 44% of GDP** during bubble, reaching pre-crisis peak of **183% of GDP**
- **51% of GDP in foreign currency debt** — large FX exposure to capital pullback
- Investment inflows strong: **averaged 15% of GDP**
- Financed **current account deficit of 9% of GDP**

#### Economic Strength During Bubble
- **Growth at 8%** — strong real economic performance
- **GDP gap peaked at 8%** — high levels of economic activity
- **Equities averaged 12% annualized returns** over bubble period
- Strong asset returns encouraged more borrowing and stimulated growth
- Bubble pressures plus foreign financing dependency = unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (1996-1998)

#### Crisis Trigger and Initial Impact
- Debt service reached **49% of GDP** at pre-crisis peak — extreme vulnerability
- Shock came as **wave of losses from over-indebted companies/banks**
- Foreign funding collapsed: **capital inflows fell by 34% of GDP**
- Policy tightening: **short rates hiked by 11%**
- **Real FX fell by 19%** — meaningful currency decline
- **GDP fell by 14%**, stock prices **fell by 87%** — self-reinforcing declines

#### Inflation Response — Moderate Outcome
- Currency weakness contributed to moderate and rising inflation
- Inflation **peaked at 8%** during depression phase
- **Low compared to other similar cases** — Thailand avoided hyperinflation

> "That makes sense given that Thailand had only about a quarter of the classic 'risk factors' for bigger inflation spirals (with the biggest risk factor being their current account deficit)."

#### Central Bank Defense and Abandonment
- Central bank spent down **100% of reserves** defending currency
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense**
- Financial institutions came under considerable pressure

#### Deleveraging Paradox
- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt-to-GDP rose by 36%** (18% annualized)
- Driven partly by **currency collapse increasing foreign-denominated debt burden**
- Attribution chart shows FX component significantly increased debt burden

### Recovery Dynamics
- Thailand's limited inflation risk factors (only ~25% of classic risks)
- **Current account deficit** = biggest risk factor present
- Moderate inflation gave more policy flexibility than hyperinflationary cases like Bulgaria


<!-- pp. 1201-1220 -->

## Thailand 1993-2004 Case (continued)

### The Reflation Phase

#### Policy Response to Capital Outflows

- **Critical policy choice**: Let currency depreciate and allow financial tightening (painful but necessary) vs. print money to replace outflows (inflationary)
- **Thailand's approach**: Abandoned currency peg after shorter-than-average "ugly" phase
- Allowed sufficient tightening to reduce import spending — **current account improved by 21% of GDP**
- Made currency more attractive to hold through adjustment process

#### Aggressive Financial Sector Management

Thailand deployed **8 out of 9 classic policy levers**:
- **Nationalized banks** to stabilize system
- **Provided liquidity** to prevent collapse
- **Directly purchased troubled assets** to clean balance sheets
- Received **IMF assistance program** for external support

#### Deleveraging Dynamics (1996-1998)

**Debt reduction**: Debt/GDP fell **88%** (15% annualized) during depression period

**Attribution breakdown**:
- Primarily driven by **nominal income rising** (real growth component)
- Real GDP recovery was the main deleveraging mechanism
- Currency depreciation initially increased foreign debt burden, then stabilized

#### Deleveraging Dynamics (1998-2004)

**Debt reduction**: Debt/GDP fell **-15%** during reflation period

**Debt-to-income reduction sources**:
- Mostly from **nominal income rising**
- Driven primarily by **higher real growth**
- Lower real FX (bottomed at **-16% during beautiful period**) set up renewed competitiveness

#### Recovery Timeline

- **5 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **23 years** before equity prices (USD terms) recovered to pre-crisis levels
- Depreciated currency created foundation for export competitiveness

#### Political Impact

Crisis helped create conditions for **Thaksin Shinawatra** (populist leader) to take power in 2001

---

## Indonesia 1994-2012 Case Auto-Summary

**Crisis type**: Transitory **inflationary deleveraging cycle** (1994-2012)
**Key characteristic**: Country "letting its currency go" in face of external pressures — produces temporary inflation but gives policymakers flexibility on interest rates

### The Bubble Phase (1994-1997)

#### Debt Buildup

- Debts reached **104% of GDP** by bubble's end
- **51% of GDP in foreign currencies** — massive FX exposure vulnerability
- Large exposure to foreign capital pullback

#### Capital Flows and Growth

- Investment inflows: Low but positive, averaging **~5% of GDP**
- Current account deficit: **-3% of GDP**
- **Strong growth at 7%** fueled by rising debt and capital
- GDP gap peaked at **13%** (economy running very hot)

#### Asset Returns and Competitiveness Issues

- Equities averaged **12% annualized returns** during bubble
- Strong returns encouraged more borrowing, stimulating growth
- Real FX peaked at **+19%** — competitiveness became major issue
- Unsustainable combination: bubble pressures + foreign financing dependence + weakened regional conditions

### The Depression Phase (1997-1998)

#### The Shock: Asian Financial Crisis

High debt levels left Indonesia vulnerable when 1997 crisis hit

#### Capital Flight and Policy Response

- **Capital inflows fell 13% of GDP** — sudden stop in foreign funding
- Policymakers **hiked short rates by 43%** (peak tightening)
- Real FX fell **-110%** — massive currency collapse
- Central bank **spent down reserves by 23%** defending currency before abandoning defense

#### Economic Collapse

- **GDP fell 14%** (self-reinforcing decline)
- **Stock prices fell 89%**
- Currency weakness drove **inflation to 59%** peak during depression
- Normal pattern vs. other cases despite having only **~1/4 of classic "risk factors"** for bigger inflation spirals
  - Biggest risk factor: **high foreign-denominated debts**

#### Financial System Stress

Financial institutions under considerable pressure during crisis

#### Deleveraging Paradox (1997-1998)

Despite needing deleveraging, **debt/GDP rose 132%** (132% annualized)

**Why debt burden increased**:
- Primarily due to **currency collapse** increasing foreign-denominated debt burden
- FX debt became much heavier in local currency terms
- Real growth eventually resumed but couldn't offset initial FX shock

#### Measurement Framework

**Bubble & Depression Gauge**: Compendium of stats measuring bubble/depression conditions
**Tightening & Easing Gauge**: Measures money and credit tightness/easiness
- Difference from zero shows extent of bubble
- Crossing above/below zero = shifting into/out of bubble

### The Reflation Phase (post-1998)

#### Policy Choice and Execution

- Abandoned currency peg after relatively short "ugly" phase
- Allowed tightening to flow through, reducing import spending
- **Current account improved by 4% of GDP**
- Made currency more attractive to hold

#### Aggressive Financial Management

Pulled **7 out of 9 classic policy levers**:
- **Nationalized banks**
- **Provided liquidity**
- **Directly purchased troubled assets**
- Received **IMF assistance program**

#### Deleveraging Success (reflation period)

**Debt/GDP fell 178%** (13% annualized)

**Debt-to-income reduction mechanism**:
- Primarily through **nominal income rising** (real growth dominant)
- Real growth was the main engine of deleveraging
- Currency stabilization helped after initial depreciation shock

<!-- pp. 1221-1240 -->

## Indonesia 1994–2012 Case Study: Recovery and Deleveraging

### Deleveraging Attribution: Reflation Period (1998-2012)

- **Debt reduction of 13% of GDP** during 1998-2012 reflation period driven by multiple factors:
  - Real growth: primary driver of income rising faster than debt
  - Inflation contribution: nominal income increases
  - Domestic debt changes: modest reduction
  - FX debt changes due to currency movement: significant contributor
  - Other FX debt changes

### Recovery Timeline and Outcomes

- **5 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **13 years** before equity prices in USD terms recovered
- Real FX bottomed at **-90% during beautiful period** (massive currency devaluation)
- Devalued currency set Indonesia up for **renewed competitiveness** post-crisis

---

## Korea 1994–2001 Case Study

### Overview: Transitory Inflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- Classic example of **"letting the currency go"** in face of external pressures
- Produces temporary inflation but gives policy makers **more flexibility to set interest rates**
- Crisis period: 1994-2001

### The Bubble Phase (1994-1997)

**Debt Dynamics:**
- Debts rose **23% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **163% of GDP**
- High foreign currency exposure: **27% of GDP** in FX debt — creating vulnerability to capital pullback
- Total debt service reached **42% of GDP** at pre-crisis peak

**Capital Flows and Growth:**
- Capital inflows moderately strong: averaging **~8% of GDP**
- Financed current account deficit of **3% of GDP**
- Strong growth: **8%** real GDP growth
- GDP gap peaked at **5%** (economy running hot)

**Unsustainable Situation:**
- Self-reinforcing cycle: rising debt and capital → strong growth
- Heavy dependence on foreign financing
- Weakened conditions in related Asian countries created vulnerability

### The Depression Phase (1997-1998)

**The 1997 Asian Financial Crisis Shock:**

**Capital and Currency Collapse:**
- Foreign funding dried up: capital inflows fell **9% of GDP**
- Real FX fell to **-50%** (currency halved)
- Central bank spent down reserves **24%** defending currency, then abandoned defense

**Policy Response:**
- Policy makers hiked short rates by **14%** (tightening)
- Allowed meaningful currency decline

**Economic Damage:**
- GDP fell **8%**
- Stock prices fell **75%**
- Home prices fell **13%**
- Unemployment rates increased **6%**
- Currency weakness contributed to moderate inflation: peaked at **7%** (low compared to similar cases)

**Why Low Inflation Despite Currency Collapse:**
- Korea had only ~1/4 of classic "risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals
- Biggest missing risk factor: **low central bank reserves** (already spent down defending currency)

**Financial Sector Pressure:**
- Financial institutions came under considerable pressure
- Debt as % GDP rose **19%** (13% annualized) despite deleveraging need
- Increase due to currency fall raising burden of foreign-denominated debt

**Attribution During Depression (1997-1998):**
- Debt rose 13% annualized driven by:
  - Real growth: negative (contraction)
  - Inflation: moderate contribution
  - Domestic debt changes: contributed to increase
  - **FX debt changes due to currency move: major driver** of debt burden increase

### The Reflation Phase (1998-2001)

**Policy Choice: Currency Devaluation Path:**
- Abandoned currency peg
- After "slightly shorter than average ugly phase," allowed enough tightening to:
  - Reduce spending on imports: current account improved **7% of GDP**
  - Make currency more attractive to hold

**Aggressive Policy Response (7 of 9 classic levers):**
1. **Nationalized banks**
2. **Provided liquidity**
3. **Directly purchased troubled assets**
4. IMF assistance program
5. Structural reforms for labor market flexibility

**Deleveraging Results:**
- Debt as % of GDP fell **25%** (10% annualized) over adjustment period
- Reduction came mostly from **nominal income rising** (higher real growth primarily)
- Real FX bottomed at **-17% during beautiful period** — set up renewed competitiveness

**Recovery Timeline:**
- **1.7 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- **9 years** before equity prices in USD terms recovered

### Korea Case Gauges and Metrics

**Bubble & Depression Gauge:**
- Bubble peaked ~1997
- Sharp drop into depression phase
- Gradual recovery into reflation phase through 2007

**Tightening & Easing Gauge:**
- Peak tightening occurred during 1997-1998 crisis
- Followed by sustained easing period post-1998

**Core Inflation:**
- Spike to ~8% during crisis (1997-1998)
- Gradual decline to 2-3% range during reflation

**Key Lesson:**
> "A key determinant of how balance of payments/currency crises play out is how policy makers respond to adverse capital flows: whether they let their currency go and allow a tightening of financial conditions to flow through (painful but typically necessary to resolve the crisis), or print money to make up for money leaving (which can be inflationary)."

### Chart Deck Appendix Data

**Indebtedness:**
- Total debt peaked ~163% of GDP at crisis (1997)
- Debt service ratio peaked ~42% of GDP
- FX debt peaked at 50%+ of GDP (1997-1998)
- Post-crisis deleveraging visible through 2007

**Monetary and Fiscal Policy:**
- Nominal short rate spiked to ~70% during crisis
- Money supply (M0) as % GDP rose steadily from 4% to 8% (1992-2007)
- Fiscal balance moved from small surplus to -1.5% deficit during crisis, gradual recovery

**Economic Conditions:**
- Real GDP indexed: sharp drop during crisis, strong recovery above prior peak
- Real growth Y/Y: plunged to -15%, recovered to sustained 5-7% range
- GDP gap: compressed from +15% to -7%, gradual normalization
- Core inflation: spiked to 80%+, normalized to 10% range
- Real short rate: extreme volatility during crisis (peak 50%, trough -10%)

**Markets:**
- Equity prices (USD): collapsed during crisis, recovered to new highs by 2007
- Nominal rates: short rate spiked to 70%, long rate to 60%+ during crisis
- Yield curve (SR-LR): inverted during crisis recovery
- Real FX vs TWI: crashed -90%, gradual recovery
- FX returns for foreign investors: collapsed, gradual recovery
- Gold price (local FX): spiked 5x during crisis due to currency collapse

**External Position:**
- Reserves (USD): built from index 60 to 180 (1992-2007)
- Capital inflows: positive 6-8% pre-crisis, plunged to -8% during crisis
- Current account: deficit -4% pre-crisis, swung to surplus +4% post-crisis
- Imports: declined from 33% to 20% of GDP during crisis
- Exports: sustained ~30% of GDP throughout
- Capital outflows: volatile, peaked ~2% of GDP post-crisis

<!-- pp. 1241-1260 -->

## Malaysia 1994–2001 Case Auto-Summary

### Overview: Inflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- **Malaysia 1994-2001**: Classic example of **transitory inflationary deleveraging cycle**
- Pattern: "letting currency go" in face of external pressures
- Produced temporary inflation but gave policymakers **more flexibility to set interest rates**
- Three distinct phases: Bubble → Depression → Reflation

### The Bubble Phase (1994-1997)

#### Debt Accumulation Dynamics

- **Debt rose 53% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **212% of GDP**
- Major vulnerability: **39% of GDP was foreign currency debt** — large exposure to capital flight
- Self-reinforcing cycle: unsustainably strong capital inflows + rising debt + strong growth
- **Capital inflows averaged ~6% of GDP**, financing **8% of GDP current account deficit**
- Growth strong at **10%** while economic activity levels high (**GDP gap peaked at 9%**)

#### The Unsustainable Situation

- Bubble pressures + dependence on foreign financing + weakening in related countries
- Created fundamentally **unsustainable situation**

### The Depression Phase (1997-1998)

#### The Trigger: 1997 Asian Financial Crisis

- **Capital inflows fell 5% of GDP** — sudden stop in foreign funding
- Policymakers responded with **4% short rate hike**, causing tightening
- **Currency (real FX) fell 24%**
- **GDP fell 9%**, **stock prices fell 83%**

#### Inflation and Risk Factors

- Currency weakness → inflation peaked at **5% during depression**
- Relatively **low inflation** compared to similar cases
- Why? Malaysia had only ~**1/4 of classic "risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals**
  - Biggest risk factor: **current account deficit**

#### Financial System Stress

- Financial institutions under **considerable pressure**
- Central bank **spent down reserves 27%** to defend currency
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense**

#### Debt Service Crisis

- Pre-crisis debt service reached **45% of GDP** — made Malaysia vulnerable to shock
- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt/GDP rose 10% (7% annualized)** during depression
- Increase partly due to **currency fall** (increased burden of foreign-denominated debt)

### The Reflation Phase (1998-2001)

#### Policy Response: Currency Abandonment

- Key decision: How to respond to adverse capital flows?
  - Option 1: Let currency go + allow tightening (painful but typically necessary)
  - Option 2: Print money to make up for money leaving (inflationary)
- Malaysia chose: **Abandoned currency peg**
- After slightly shorter than average "ugly" phase, allowed enough tightening to flow through

#### Current Account Adjustment

- Tightening reduced spending on imports
- **Current account balance improved 19% of GDP**
- Made currency **more attractive to hold**

#### Aggressive Financial System Management

- Malaysia pulled **7 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- **Nationalized banks**
- **Provided liquidity**
- **Directly purchased troubled assets**

#### Deleveraging Results

- **Debt/GDP fell 41% (18% annualized)** over adjustment period
- Reduction in debt-to-income ratios came mostly from **nominal income rising**, driven primarily by **higher real growth**
- **Real FX bottomed at -9%** during beautiful period — set up country for renewed competitiveness

#### Recovery Timeline

- Took **2 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- Equity prices in USD terms recovered within **14 years**

### Gauges and Metrics

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge

- Measures bubble/depression conditions and tightness/easiness of money and credit
- Shows clear transition: strong bubble → sharp depression → gradual reflation

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge

- Peak tightening coincided with crisis
- Gradual shift to easing during reflation phase

#### Core Inflation Trajectory

- Peaked during crisis around **5%**
- Gradually declined to **~1-2%** range during reflation

### Chart Data Highlights

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

- **Nominal short rate**: Peaked ~27% during crisis (1997-05), fell to ~5% by 2007
- **Money0 level**: Rose from ~6% to peak ~7% of GDP, then fell to ~5% post-crisis
- **Fiscal balance**: Deteriorated from surplus to deficit during crisis, gradual improvement

#### Economic Conditions

- **Real GDP**: Indexed growth from ~70 (1992) to ~160 (2007), with sharp dip 1997-98
- **Real growth Y/Y**: Crashed from ~14% peak to **-7%** trough, recovered to 5-7% range
- **GDP gap**: Peaked ~6%, crashed to **-9%**, recovered to 2-3%
- **Core inflation**: Volatile 4-7% range pre-crisis, spike to ~8% at crisis, settled to 2-3%
- **Real short rate**: Peaked ~20% during crisis, normalized to 2-5%

#### Markets

- **Equity prices (USD indexed)**: Rose from ~75 to ~175 (1997), crashed to ~30, recovered to ~200 by 2007
- **Yield curve (SR-LR)**: Highly volatile, peaked ~9%, eventually flattened to 0-1%
- **Real FX vs TWI**: Crashed ~50% during crisis, gradual recovery to +20% by 2007

#### External Position

- **Reserves (USD indexed)**: Rose from ~50 to ~600 over entire period (massive accumulation)
- **Capital inflows**: Peaked ~10% of GDP, crashed to negative during crisis, recovered to 6-8%
- **Imports**: Rose from ~25% to ~50% of GDP over period
- **Current account**: Swing from **-8% deficit to +10% surplus** during crisis, settled to 0-2%
- **Capital outflows**: Increased volatility, fell to **-6% of GDP** post-crisis
- **Exports**: Rose from ~30% to ~50% of GDP — export-led recovery

### Deleveraging Attribution Charts

#### Depression Period (1997-1998)

- **Total debt change: +7% of GDP (annualized)**
- Components:
  - Real growth: Negative (contraction)
  - Change in domestic debt: Negative (deleveraging)
  - Inflation: Small positive
  - **FX debt change due to FX move**: Large positive (currency depreciation increased FX debt burden)
  - FX debt change due to other: Positive

#### Reflation Period (1998-2001)

- **Total debt change: -18% of GDP (annualized)**
- Components:
  - **Real growth**: Largest contributor (negative debt/GDP via income growth)
  - Change in domestic debt: Moderate positive
  - **Inflation**: Significant negative contribution (reduced real debt burden)
  - FX debt change due to FX move: Small
  - FX debt change due to other: Small

### Key Indebtedness Metrics

- **Total debt**: Peaked ~212% of GDP (1997), fell to ~200% range by 2007
- **Debt service**: Peaked ~250% of revenues (1997), fell to ~130% by 2007
- **FX debt**: Peaked ~70% of GDP (1997), fell to ~40% by 2007
- **Debt growth**: Peaked ~38% annual (1997), normalized to ~20% range
- **Change in debt-GDP ratio**: Peaked ~20% annual increase (1997), became negative during reflation

<!-- pp. 1261-1280 -->

## Philippines 1994–2008 Case Auto-Summary

### Overview: Transitory Inflationary Deleveraging

- Philippines experienced a **transitory inflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1994 and 2008
- Classic example of **"letting its currency go"** in the face of external pressures
- Strategy produced temporary inflation but gave policymakers more flexibility to set interest rates

### The Bubble Phase (1994-1997)

#### Capital Flows and Debt Accumulation

- **Unsustainably strong capital inflows** and strong currency returns characterized the bubble
- Debts rose **12% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **95% of GDP**
- **51% of GDP** was foreign currency debt—creating large exposure to capital pullback
- Investment inflows averaged **12% of GDP**, financing a current account deficit of **5% of GDP**

#### Economic Performance

- Growth strong at **5%** with moderate economic activity (GDP gap peaked at **2%**)
- Equity returns averaged **8% annualized** over bubble period, encouraging more borrowing
- Competitiveness became an issue: real FX peaked at **+23%**
- Combined bubble pressures and foreign financing dependence created unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (1997-1998)

#### The Crisis Trigger

- **1997 Asian financial crisis** was the shock that exposed vulnerability
- Pre-crisis debt service reached **25% of GDP**, making Philippines vulnerable
- Fall in foreign funding: capital inflows fell by **19% of GDP**

#### Policy Response and Currency Collapse

- **Tightening**: policymakers hiked short rates by **9%**
- **Currency collapse**: real FX fell by **29%**
- Central bank spent down reserves to defend currency (drawing down reserves by **60%**)
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense**

#### Economic and Market Impact

- GDP fell by **3%**
- Stock prices fell by **79%**
- Currency weakness contributed to moderate and rising inflation, peaking at **10%** during depression
- Low inflation compared to similar cases—Philippines had only about **a quarter of the classic "risk factors"** for bigger inflation spirals
- Biggest risk factor for inflation spirals: **low central bank reserves** (which Philippines lacked)
- Financial institutions came under considerable pressure

#### Debt Dynamics

- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt as % GDP went up by 24%** (15% annualized)
- Increase partly due to currency fall, which increased burden of foreign-denominated debt
- Attribution chart shows FX movement was major driver of debt increase

### The Reflation Phase (1998-2008)

#### Policy Response

- Abandoned currency peg after "slightly shorter than average 'ugly' phase"
- Allowed enough tightening to flow through to reduce spending on imports
- **Current account balance improved by 11% of GDP**
- Made currency more attractive to hold

#### Financial Sector Management

- **Somewhat aggressive** in managing financial institutions and bad debts
- Pulled **3 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- Benefited from **IMF assistance program**
- Enacted **structural reforms** to increase labor market flexibility

#### Recovery Outcomes

- **Debt as % of GDP fell by 51%** (5% annualized) over adjustment period
- Reduction in debt-to-income ratios came mostly from **nominal income rising**
- Real FX bottomed at **-24%** during "beautiful period," setting up renewed competitiveness
- **GDP contraction was brief**
- **Equity prices in USD terms recovered within 16 years**

#### Political Impact

- Crisis had notable impact on Philippine politics
- Helped set stage for **Joseph Estrada** (considered a populist leader) to take power

### Gauges and Measurement Framework

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge

- Composite measure from chart deck statistics
- Measures bubble/depression conditions and tightness/easiness of money and credit
- Difference from zero shows extent of bubble; crossing above/below zero represents shifting into/out of bubble

#### Key Phases Shown in Gauges

- **Bubble phase** (1994-1997): gauge peaks around 1.5-2.0
- **Depression phase** (1997-1998): sharp drop to around -1.0
- **Reflation phase** (1998-2008): gradual recovery back toward zero

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge

- Shows **Peak Tightening** during 1997-1998 crisis (around +0.5 to +1.0)
- **Peak Easing** occurred during early 2000s (around -1.5)

#### Core Inflation

- Started high (around 16% in early 1990s)
- Moderated before crisis (6-8% range)
- Spiked to **10%** during crisis
- Declined during reflation phase to around 5-6%

### Attribution Analysis: Depression Period (1997-1998)

- **Real Growth**: negative contribution (around -5% to -10%)
- **FX Debt Change due to FX Move**: largest component (around 15-20% of change)
- **FX Debt Change due to Other**: smaller contribution
- **Change in Domestic Debt**: minimal
- **Inflation**: positive contribution (around 5%)
- **Net Debt %GDP**: increased by 15% annualized

### Attribution Analysis: Reflation Period (1998-2008)

- **Real Growth**: negative contribution (reduced debt burden)
- **Inflation**: largest contributor to debt reduction
- **Change in Domestic Debt**: positive (green, around 4-5%)
- **FX Debt Change due to FX Move**: minimal
- **FX Debt Change due to Other**: small contribution
- **Net Debt %GDP**: fell by -5% annualized

### Economic Conditions Charts

#### Real GDP and Growth

- Real GDP (indexed): sharp dip in 1997-1998, then steady recovery to index 150 by 2007-2008
- Real Growth (Y/Y): dropped from 10%+ to around -10% at crisis trough, recovered to 5-7% range

#### GDP Gap and Inflation

- GDP gap peaked at around 10% pre-crisis (1997), fell to -5%, recovered to around 2%
- Core inflation: peaked at 5% during crisis (1997-1998), declined to 1-2% during reflation

#### Interest Rates and Growth

- Nominal long rate: declined from around 8% pre-crisis to 5-6% post-crisis
- Nominal growth (red): highly volatile, spiking to 20%+ during crisis, moderating to 10-12% range
- Real short rate: spiked from around 3% to 6% during crisis, then declined to 0-2% range

### Market Conditions

#### Equity Prices

- Equity Price USD (indexed): peaked around 120-130 pre-crisis, crashed to around 40-50, recovered to around 100 by 2007-2008
- 79% decline during crisis; 16-year recovery period

#### Interest Rate Structure

- Nominal long rate and short rate: both spiked during crisis (short rate to 12%, long rate to 8%)
- Both declined during reflation, converging around 3-5% by late 2000s

#### Yield Curve and FX

- Yield Curve (SR-LR): spiked to +3% during crisis, normalized to -1% to 0% during reflation
- Real FX vs TWI: peaked at +18% pre-crisis, crashed to -20%, recovered to around 0%
- FX Return for Foreign Investors (indexed): steady rise to 100+ pre-crisis, crashed to 60, recovered to 80

#### Gold Prices

- Gold Price (Local FX, indexed): flat around 100-120 pre-crisis, spiked dramatically during crisis to 150, then surged to 280 by 2007-2008

### External Position

#### Reserves and Capital Flows

- Reserves (USD, indexed): gradual rise from 100 to 150 pre-crisis, flat during crisis, explosive growth to 450+ by 2007-2008
- Capital Inflows (%GDP): volatile, peaked around 18% in early 1990s, dropped negative during crisis, recovered to 10-12%

#### Trade and Current Account

- Imports (%GDP): rose from 80% to 130% during bubble, crashed during crisis, stabilized around 80-90%
- Current Account (%GDP): improved from -8% deficit to +18% surplus, reflecting adjustment
- Capital Outflows (%GDP): shifted from +4% to -15% during crisis period
- Exports (%GDP): rose from 80% to 130% during bubble, fell during crisis, recovered to 100-110%

### Chart Deck Appendix Data

#### Indebtedness Metrics

- Total Debt (%GDP): rose from around 15% (1991) to peak of 30%+ (1997), declined to 20% by 2006
- Debt Service (%GDP): tracked total debt, peaking around 120% during crisis period
- FX Debt (%GDP): rose from 60% to peak of 75% during bubble, declined to 45% post-crisis

#### Debt Growth Dynamics

- Debt Growth (%GDP, Ann): peaked at 20%+ during crisis (1997), declined to near 0% by 2006
- Change in Debt-GDP Ratio (Ann): spiked to +12% during crisis, fell to -10% during reflation

### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

#### Monetary Policy

- Nominal Short Rate: extremely volatile, ranging from 10-25% in early 1990s, spiking to 20%+ during crisis, declining to 5-8% by late 2000s
- Money0 Level (%GDP): relatively stable around 4-5% throughout period, brief spike to 5.5% during crisis

#### Fiscal Policy

- Fiscal Balance (%GDP): improved from -4% deficit (1991) to +1% surplus (1996), deteriorated to -5% deficit post-crisis, recovered to -1% by 2007-2008

<!-- pp. 1281-1300 -->

## Russia 1996–2006 Case Auto-Summary

### Overview: Transitory Inflationary Deleveraging

- Russia experienced a **transitory inflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1996-2006
- Classic example of "letting its currency go" in the face of external pressures
- Produced temporary inflation but gave policymakers more flexibility to set interest rates

### The Bubble Phase

#### Debt Accumulation Without Broad-Based Bubble

- Unlike many cases, Russia didn't experience broad-based domestic bubble pre-crisis
- **Tied to other countries** experiencing bubble-like conditions in their economies and financial markets
- Built substantial debt stock driven by **unsustainably strong capital inflows**
- Total debt reached **112% of GDP** prior to crisis
- **38% of GDP in foreign currencies**—creating large exposure to foreign capital pullback
- Became dependent on continuous foreign financing: investment inflows averaged **5% of GDP** in pre-crisis years
- High debts + dependence on foreign financing + weakened conditions in related countries = unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (1997-1998)

#### Triggers and Contagion

- **Balance of payments/currency crisis** ran from 1997-1998
- Pre-crisis peak: debt service reached **90% of GDP**—making Russia vulnerable to shock
- Shock came as ripples from **1997 Asian financial crisis** and **falling oil prices**

#### The Cascade

- Foreign funding fell: capital inflows dropped by **5% of GDP**
- Monetary tightening: policymakers hiked short rates by **more than 250%**
- Currency collapse: real FX fell by **72%**
- GDP decline: falling by **10%**
- Stock market crash: falling by **85%**
- Inflation spike: peaking at **91%** during depression phase—high compared to other similar cases

#### Risk Factors for Inflation

- Russia had ~half the classic "risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals
- **Biggest risk factor: long history of poorly-controlled inflation**

#### Central Bank Reserve Depletion

- Central bank spent down reserves to defend currency: drawing down reserves by **55%**
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense**

#### Debt Dynamics Paradox

- Despite needing deleveraging, debt as % GDP **increased by 63%** (58% annualized)
- Increase driven by:
  - Currency fall (increased burden of foreign-denominated debts)
  - Government had to borrow more in crisis response
- Peak fiscal deficit: **5% of GDP**

### The Reflation Phase (1998-2006)

#### Policy Response: Currency Devaluation Strategy

- Key decision: whether to let currency go (allowing tightening to flow through, painful but necessary) or print money to compensate for capital leaving (inflationary)
- **Abandoned the currency peg**
- After relatively short "ugly" phase, allowed enough tightening to flow through
- Reduced spending on imports: current account balance improved by **8% of GDP**
- Made currency more attractive to hold

#### Aggressive Financial Institution Management

- Pulled **6 out of 9 classic policy levers**
- **Nationalized banks**
- **Provided liquidity**
- **Directly purchased troubled assets**
- Received **IMF assistance program**
- Enacted **structural reforms** to increase labor market flexibility

#### Deleveraging Results

- Debt as % GDP fell by **119%** (15% annualized) during adjustment period 1998-2006
- Reduction in debt-to-income ratios came **mostly from nominal income rising**

#### Currency and Competitiveness

- Real FX bottomed at **-43%** during "beautiful period"
- Set up country for renewed competitiveness
- Took **1.8 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- Equity prices in USD terms recovered within **6 years**

### Key Charts and Metrics

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge
- Shows shift from mild bubble conditions through sharp depression into reflation
- Depression gauge bottomed near -2.0 in 1997-98

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge
- Peak tightening in 1997-98
- Peak easing in later recovery period

#### Core Inflation
- Dropped from 300% in early 1990s to ~100% spike during crisis, then stabilized around 10-20%

#### Markets
- Real short rates: peaked at 16% during crisis, turned negative by 2006-12
- Equity prices (USD): bubble peak ~100 in 1997, crashed to ~20, recovered to ~120 by 2006
- Nominal rates: both short and long rates dropped from 20-30% to 5-10% range
- Yield curve (SR-LR): volatile, ranging from 0% to -10%
- Real FX vs TWI: peaked near 30% in 1997, bottomed at -20%, recovered to near 0%
- FX returns for foreign investors: steady appreciation from index 80 to 120 over 1991-2006
- Gold price (local FX): massive appreciation from index 100 to 350+ over period

#### External Position
- Reserves (USD): grew from index 50 to 350 over 1991-2006
- Capital inflows: peaked at 20% GDP in 1997, collapsed, recovered to 5% by 2006
- Imports: surged from 30% to 75% GDP peak in 1997, settled around 45% by 2006
- Current account: improved from -8% GDP deficit to +4% surplus by 2006
- Capital outflows: volatile, ranging from -12% to +4% GDP
- Exports: rose from 25% to 60% GDP peak in 1997, settled around 40%

#### Indebtedness
- Total debt: peaked at 170% GDP in 1998, fell to 60% by 2007
- Debt service: peaked at 90% GDP in 1997, fell to below 20% by 2007
- FX debt: peaked at 100% GDP in 1998, fell to 30% by 2007
- Debt growth: peaked at 50% annualized in 1998, stabilized around 5-10%

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- Nominal short rate: extreme spikes above 400% during crisis, normalized to 20-50% range
- Money0 level: grew from 4% to 11% GDP over 1992-2007
- Fiscal balance: improved from -8% deficit to +10% surplus by 2007

#### Economic Conditions
- Real GDP: U-shaped recovery from index 100 crisis level to 180 by 2007
- Real growth: recovered from -10% to sustained 5-10% range
- GDP gap: bottomed at -15% in 1998, closed to positive by 2007
- Core inflation: collapsed from 200% to 20% range
- Nominal long rate vs nominal growth: both declining from 100-140% to 20-40% range


<!-- pp. 1301-1320 -->

## Colombia 1995–2008 Case Study

### Overview: Inflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- Colombia experienced a **transitory inflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1995 and 2008
- Classic example of **"letting its currency go"** in face of external pressures
- Currency depreciation produced temporary inflation but gave policymakers more flexibility to set interest rates

### The Bubble Phase (1995-1998)

#### Debt Accumulation Dynamics

- Bubble driven by self-reinforcing cycle: unsustainably strong capital inflows + rising debt + strong growth + strong housing returns
- **Debt rose 11% of GDP** during bubble to pre-crisis peak of **58% of GDP**
- **30% of GDP was foreign-currency denominated** — creating major vulnerability to capital flight
- Investment inflows averaged **8% of GDP**, financing current account deficit of **5% of GDP**

#### Economic Indicators

- Growth strong at **3%** with GDP gap peaking at **5%**
- Competitiveness deteriorated: real FX peaked at **+16%**
- Bubble pressures + dependence on foreign financing + weakened conditions in related countries = unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (1998-2003)

#### The Trigger and Initial Collapse

- High debt left Colombia vulnerable to shock from **1997 Asian financial crisis**
- Capital inflows fell **8% of GDP**, triggering tightening and currency collapse
- Policymakers hiked short rates **20%**
- **Real FX fell 45%** in meaningful decline
- Self-reinforcing declines: GDP fell **7%**, stocks fell **66%**
- Unemployment rates increased **6%**

#### Inflation Dynamics — The Colombia Paradox

- Currency weakness contributed to moderate inflation, peaking at **20%** during depression
- **Low compared to other similar cases** despite Colombia having ~half the classic "risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals
- Biggest risk factor: **long history of poorly-controlled inflation**
- Colombia's financial institutions under considerable pressure

#### Policy Response During Depression

- Central bank spent down reserves to defend currency (reserves fell **37%**)
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense**
- Debt as % GDP rose **12%** (2% annualized) during depression due to:
  - Currency fall increased burden of foreign-denominated debts
  - Government had to borrow more in response (peak fiscal deficit **5% of GDP**)

### The Reflation Phase (2003-2008)

#### Policy Choice: Let the Currency Go

- **Key determinant**: how policymakers respond to adverse capital flows
- Two options: (1) let currency go and allow tightening to flow through (painful but necessary), or (2) print money to compensate (inflationary)
- Colombia **abandoned the currency peg**
- After slightly longer than average "ugly" phase, allowed enough tightening to flow through

#### Deleveraging Mechanics

- Current account balance improved **5% of GDP** via reduced import spending
- Made currency more attractive to hold
- Debt as % of GDP fell **16% (3% annualized)** over adjustment period
- Reduction in debt-to-income ratios came mostly from **nominal income rising**
- Real FX bottomed at **-29%** during "beautiful period" — set up renewed competitiveness

#### Aggressive Financial Sector Management

- Colombia pulled **6 out of 9 classic policy levers**:
  - Nationalized banks
  - Provided liquidity
  - Directly purchased troubled assets
- Benefited from **IMF assistance program**
- Enacted **structural reforms to increase labor market flexibility**

#### Recovery Timeline

- Took **4 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- Equity prices in USD terms recovered within **7 years**

### Attribution Analysis

#### Depression Period (1998-2003)

- Debt %GDP change: **+2% annualized**
- Components:
  - **Real growth**: negative (largest drag)
  - **Inflation**: moderate positive contribution
  - **Chg in Dom. Debt**: positive
  - **FX Debt Chg due to FX Move**: large positive (currency fall increased FX debt burden)
  - **FX Debt Chg due to Other**: small

#### Reflation Period (2003-2008)

- Debt %GDP change: **-3% annualized**
- Components:
  - **Chg in Dom. Debt**: largest contributor to deleveraging (green bar)
  - **Real Growth**: negative contribution (blue bar below zero)
  - **FX Debt Chg due to FX Move**: negative contribution
  - **Inflation**: largest negative bar (helped reduce debt burden)

### Data Appendix: Key Charts

#### Indebtedness

- Total Debt peaked ~**70% GDP** in 2003, declined to ~**55% GDP** by 2008
- Debt Service peaked ~**23% GDP** in early 2000s
- FX Debt peaked ~**43% GDP** in mid-2000s before declining to ~**20% GDP**

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy

- Nominal Short Rate: declined from ~**45%** in 1998 to ~**10%** by 2008
- Money0 Level fell from ~**6% GDP** to ~**4% GDP** during crisis, recovered to ~**6.5% GDP**
- Fiscal Balance: deteriorated from near 0% to **-5% GDP** trough, improved to **-2% GDP**

#### Economic Conditions

- Real GDP (indexed): dip during crisis, steady recovery to index ~**140** by 2008
- GDP Gap: swung from **+5%** to **-6%** and back to **+4%**
- Core Inflation: declined steadily from **~25%** to **~5%**
- Real Short Rate: extreme volatility (**30%** peak in 1998), normalized to **~5%**

#### Markets

- Equity Price USD (indexed): collapsed from ~**150** to ~**50**, recovered to **~550** by 2008
- Yield Curve (SR-LR): spiked to **>300%** during crisis, normalized near **0%**
- Real FXvsTWI: peaked **+25%**, bottomed **-30%**, recovered to **+15%**
- Gold Price (local FX, indexed): flat during bubble, surged from **~100** to **~450** during/after crisis

#### External Position

- Reserves (USD, indexed): grew from **~50** to **~400** during reflation
- Capital Inflows: peaked **~13% GDP**, fell to **-2% GDP**, recovered to **~19% GDP**
- Current Account: deteriorated from **+3% GDP** to **-2% GDP**, improved to **+12% GDP**, moderated to **~7% GDP**
- Imports: rose from **~10% GDP** to **~30% GDP**
- Exports: rose from **~10% GDP** to **~40% GDP**
- Capital Outflows: volatile between **0%** and **-12% GDP**

### Key Takeaways

- Letting currency depreciate provided necessary adjustment mechanism despite inflation risks
- Aggressive financial sector intervention helped stabilize system
- Nominal income growth (via inflation and real growth) was primary deleveraging mechanism
- Currency competitiveness restoration set foundation for sustainable recovery
- Recovery was prolonged (4-7 years) but ultimately successful

<!-- pp. 1321-1340 -->

## Ecuador 1995-2009 Case Study

### Overview: Classic Inflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- Ecuador experienced an **inflationary deleveraging cycle** between 1995-2009
- Unlike many debt crises, Ecuador had no domestic bubble before the crisis
- Instead, was tied to other bubble economies and financial markets experiencing bubble conditions
- Built substantial debt stock: **85% of GDP** pre-crisis
- **56% of GDP in foreign currency debt** — massive exposure to capital flight risk
- Running **4% of GDP current account deficit**, with investment inflows averaging 3% of GDP pre-crisis
- High debt + foreign financing dependence + contagion from other countries = unsustainable situation

### The Bubble Phase

#### Pre-Crisis Debt Accumulation
- No broad domestic bubble, but connected to bubble economies elsewhere
- Total debt reached 85% of GDP
- Foreign currency debt: 56% of GDP (extremely high)
- Current account deficit: 4% of GDP
- Capital inflows averaging 3% of GDP annually
- Heavy dependence on continuous foreign financing created vulnerability

### The Depression Phase (1998-2000)

#### Crisis Trigger: Asian Contagion
- Shock came from **1997 Asian financial crisis** contagion
- Capital inflows fell by **11% of GDP**
- Real FX collapsed by **60%**
- GDP fell **6%**
- Stock prices fell **62%**
- Unemployment rose **2%**

#### Inflation Spiral
- Currency weakness drove high and rising inflation
- Inflation peaked at **76%** during depression — high compared to similar cases
- Ecuador had classic **"risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals**, especially **low real short rates**

#### Financial System Stress
- Financial institutions under considerable pressure
- Central bank spent down reserves defending currency (**reserves fell 61%**)
- Eventually abandoned currency defense

#### Debt Dynamics During Depression
- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt-to-GDP rose 46%** (42% annualized)
- Currency collapse increased burden of foreign-denominated debt
- **Deleveraging Attribution (Depression Period)**:
  - Real growth: negative (blue, significant)
  - Inflation: large positive contribution (orange, ~150% of bar)
  - Change in domestic debt: small green contribution
  - **FX debt change due to FX move**: large contribution (light orange)
  - Net debt-to-GDP change: **+42%** annualized

### The Reflation Phase (2000-2009)

#### Policy Response: Aggressive Management
- Key decision: **abandoned currency peg** after short "ugly" phase
- Allowed enough tightening to flow through, reducing import spending
- Current account balance improved by **11% of GDP**
- Made currency more attractive to hold

#### Nine Classic Policy Levers
- Ecuador pulled **9 out of 9 classic policy levers**:
  1. **Nationalized banks**
  2. **Provided liquidity**
  3. **Directly purchased troubled assets**
  4. Benefited from **IMF assistance program**
  5. Enacted **structural reforms for labor market flexibility**

#### Deleveraging Success
- Debt-to-GDP fell **79%** (8% annualized) over adjustment period
- Reduction came mostly from **nominal income rising**, not default
- Real FX bottomed at **-55%** during "beautiful period"
- Lower currency set up renewed competitiveness
- **1.8 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- Equity prices in USD terms recovered within **5 years**

#### Deleveraging Attribution (Reflation Period, 2000-2009)
- Real growth: positive contribution (blue)
- Inflation: large negative contribution (pink/red, dominant factor)
- Change in domestic debt: positive green contribution
- FX debt change due to FX move: small contribution
- Net debt-to-GDP change: **-8%** annualized

### Gauges and Metrics

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge
- Pre-crisis: mild positive territory (0.5 to 1.0 range)
- Depression trough: approximately **-2.0** (1998-2000)
- Reflation period: sharp recovery back above zero by 2003
- Remained slightly positive through 2008

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge
- **Peak tightening** before crisis (~1.5)
- Rapid shift to **peak easing** (~-2.0) during crisis
- Gradual return to mild easing/neutral through 2000s

#### Core Inflation
- Pre-crisis: 30-40% range
- Crisis peak: approximately **110%** (1998-1999)
- Rapid decline post-crisis
- Stabilized below 10% by 2003, remained low thereafter

### Economic Indicators Detail

#### Indebtedness
- Total debt peaked at ~130% of GDP (1998)
- Debt service peaked at ~16% of GDP
- FX debt peaked near **100% of GDP** (1998)
- Both metrics fell steadily during reflation period
- By 2009: total debt ~70% of GDP, FX debt ~30% of GDP

#### Debt Growth Dynamics
- Debt growth spiked to **~42% annualized** during crisis (1998-2000)
- Change in debt-to-GDP ratio peaked at **~15% annualized**
- Both metrics turned negative during reflation
- Deleveraging continued steadily through 2009

#### Monetary Policy
- **Nominal short rates**: peaked near 60% before crisis
- Remained elevated (40-50%) through crisis
- Declined to single digits by 2003
- **Money supply** (%GDP): fell from ~5.5% to ~1.5% during crisis
- Gradually recovered to ~3% by 2009

#### Fiscal Position
- Fiscal balance fluctuated between +3% and -2% of GDP
- Generally improved during reflation period
- Moved toward surplus in mid-2000s

#### Growth and Output
- **Real GDP**: declined sharply 1998-2000
- Recovered steadily from 2000 onward
- **Real growth**: ranged from -8% to +10% (highly volatile)
- **GDP gap**: trough at approximately **-10%** (1998-1999)
- Closed by mid-2000s

#### Inflation Dynamics
- Core inflation (YoY): surged from 30% to **110%+**
- Nominal long rate tracked inflation, peaking near 80%
- **Nominal growth** similarly peaked near 80%
- All declined sharply post-crisis
- **Real short rate**: plunged below -100% during peak inflation
- Normalized as inflation fell

#### Market Performance
- **Equity prices (USD)**: collapsed from ~230 index to ~40 (1998)
- Recovered strongly, reaching ~350 by 2008-2009
- **Nominal rates**: short rates fell from 60% to near 5%
- Long rates tracked similar path
- **Real FX vs TWI**: fell from +20% to approximately **-50%**
- Partial recovery but remained depressed

#### External Position
- **Reserves**: relatively flat pre-crisis, plunged during crisis
- Recovered strongly from 2003, rising from index 100 to 350+
- **Capital inflows**: peaked near 10% of GDP pre-crisis
- Plunged to **-20% of GDP** during crisis (massive outflow)
- Stabilized around 3-5% of GDP post-crisis
- **Imports**: fell from 28% to ~15% of GDP during crisis
- Recovered to ~37% by 2009
- **Current account**: swung from -7% deficit (1998) to +8% surplus (1999)
- Moderated to balanced/surplus in later years
- **Capital outflows**: volatile, ranging from +1% to -5% of GDP

#### Market Indicators
- **FX return for foreign investors**: collapsed from index 130 to 30 (1998)
- Remained depressed throughout period
- **Gold price (local FX)**: exploded during currency collapse
- Rose from index ~100 to ~1,200+ by peak
- Reflected massive currency devaluation

### Political Impact

- Crisis helped set stage for **Lucio Gutierrez** to take power in 2003
- Many consider Gutierrez a **populist leader**
- Political shift was direct consequence of economic crisis pain

### Key Lessons

- **Foreign currency debt exposure** creates severe vulnerability in crisis
- Currency collapse increases debt burden even as economy needs deleveraging
- Abandoning unsustainable currency defense can be necessary despite short-term pain
- **Aggressive policy response** using all available levers can work
- Allowing currency to depreciate substantially can restore competitiveness
- Inflation can be tool for deleveraging, though painful
- Political consequences of crises can be profound and lasting

<!-- pp. 1341-1360 -->

## Turkey 1997–2003 Case Study

### Overview: Classic Inflationary Deleveraging Cycle

- **Turkey** experienced classic inflationary deleveraging between 1997-2003
- Driven by self-reinforcing cycles of debt, equity returns, and growth

### The Bubble Phase (1997-2000)

#### Debt Dynamics
- Debts rose **17% of GDP** during bubble, reaching pre-crisis peak of **60% of GDP**
- **46% of GDP** in foreign currency debt — massive FX exposure vulnerability
- High debt service burden: peaked at **30% of GDP** pre-crisis

#### Capital Flows and Growth
- Investment inflows low but positive: ~**3% of GDP**
- Growth moderate at **2%** annually
- GDP gap peaked at **9%** (high economic activity levels)
- Strong equity returns: **22% annualized** over bubble period encouraged borrowing

#### Currency and Competitiveness
- Foreign financing dependence created unsustainable situation
- Bubble dynamics combined with FX exposure set up crisis

### The Depression Phase (2000-2001)

#### The Trigger
- Political turmoil and violence served as shock
- High debt service (**30% of GDP**) left Turkey extremely vulnerable

#### Capital Flight and Currency Collapse
- Capital inflows fell **10% of GDP**
- Policymakers hiked short rates **157%** in response
- Real FX fell **12%** (currency devaluation)
- Central bank spent down reserves **100%** defending currency before abandoning peg

#### Economic Contraction
- GDP fell **10%**
- Stock prices crashed **78%**
- Unemployment rose **3%**

#### Inflation Spiral
- Inflation peaked at **62%** during depression — high vs other cases
- Currency weakness drove rising inflation
- Turkey had classic "risk factors" for inflation spirals, especially **long history of poorly-controlled inflation**

#### Debt Paradox During Deleveraging
- Despite needing to deleverage, debt/GDP **rose 9%** (6% annualized)
- Two key drivers:
  1. Currency fell, increasing burden of foreign-denominated debts
  2. Government borrowed more in crisis response (fiscal deficit peaked at **11% of GDP**)

#### Financial System Stress
- Financial institutions under considerable pressure
- Government response: nationalized banks, provided liquidity, directly purchased troubled assets

### The Reflation Phase (2001-2003)

#### Policy Response Choice
- Key determinant: how policymakers respond to adverse capital flows
- Options: (1) let currency fall and allow tightening (painful but necessary), or (2) print money to compensate (inflationary)
- Turkey **abandoned currency peg** after "ugly" phase
- Allowed enough tightening to reduce import spending

#### Policy Levers Deployed
- Turkey pulled **7 out of 9 classic policy levers** — very aggressive
- **Nationalized banks**
- **Provided liquidity**
- **Directly purchased troubled assets**
- **IMF assistance program**
- **Structural reforms** for labor market flexibility

#### Current Account Adjustment
- Current account improved **5% of GDP**
- Made currency more attractive to hold

#### Deleveraging Mechanics
- Debt/GDP fell **6%** (3% annualized) over adjustment period
- Reduction came mostly from **nominal income rising**, not actual debt paydown
- Real FX bottomed at **-18%** during "beautiful period"
- Set up renewed competitiveness

#### Recovery Timeline
- **2 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- Equity prices in USD terms recovered within **6 years**

### Key Charts and Gauges

#### Bubble & Depression Gauge
- Shows transition from bubble peak (~1.5) to depression trough (~-1.7)
- Reflation phase brings gauge back above zero by 2005

#### Tightening & Easing Gauge
- Peak tightening spike to ~1.0 in 2000-01
- Peak easing to ~-2.0 during reflation
- Gradual normalization post-2003

#### Core Inflation
- Started high (~80-90% in late 1990s)
- Spiked to ~60% during crisis (2000-01)
- Declined steadily through reflation to ~10% by 2010

### Chart Deck Data

#### Indebtedness
- Total debt climbed from ~30% (1995) to ~60% (2010)
- Debt service spiked dramatically during crisis period
- FX debt peaked near **60% of GDP** around 2000-03, then declined to ~40%

#### Debt Growth Patterns
- Debt growth peaked at ~22% annualized during bubble
- Change in debt-GDP ratio shows sharp positive spike during crisis despite deleveraging need

#### Monetary and Fiscal Policy
- Nominal short rates: extreme volatility in crisis (150-200%+ range)
- Gradually declined to 0-50% range by 2010
- Money supply (M0) grew from ~4% to ~10% of GDP
- Fiscal balance: deep deficit (~-10%) during crisis, improved to near balance by 2010

#### Economic Conditions
- Real GDP: sharp contraction 2000-01, then steady recovery
- GDP gap: from +9% to -10%, gradual recovery
- Core inflation: sustained decline from 80%+ to 10%
- Nominal rates and growth converged post-crisis

#### Markets
- Equity prices (USD): crashed during crisis, strong recovery through 2000s
- Yield curve (SR-LR): highly volatile during crisis, normalized to 0-2% range
- Real FX: appreciated sharply pre-crisis (+39%), crashed during crisis, depreciated through reflation
- FX returns for foreign investors: massive gains post-crisis (indexed to 500+)
- Gold prices (local FX): steady climb from ~100 to 1000+ (inflation hedge behavior)

#### External Position
- Reserves: crashed during crisis defense, rebuilt strongly post-2003
- Capital inflows: volatile, recovered to 15%+ of GDP by late 2000s
- Imports: climbed from ~20% to ~45% of GDP pre-crisis, pulled back, then resumed growth
- Current account: near balance pre-crisis, worsened to -8% by late 2000s
- Capital outflows: relatively stable 1-3% range with crisis spike
- Exports: steady climb from ~20% to ~35% of GDP through 2000s

## Argentina 1998–2012 Case Study

### Overview: Transitory Inflationary Deleveraging

- **Argentina** experienced transitory inflationary deleveraging 1998-2012
- Classic example of **"letting currency go"** in face of external pressures
- Produces temporary inflation but gives policymakers more flexibility on interest rates

### The Bubble Phase (1998-2001)

#### Debt and Capital Flows
- Debts reached pre-crisis peak of **78% of GDP**
- **47% of GDP** in foreign currencies — large FX exposure
- Bubble characterized by **unsustainably strong capital inflows** and **strong currency returns**
- Investment inflows strong: averaged **11% of GDP**

#### Growth and Activity
- Growth weak at **0%**
- Economic activity levels high (GDP gap peaked at **9%**)
- Current account deficit **5% of GDP** financed by capital inflows

#### Competitiveness Problem
- Real FX peaked at **+39%** (overvalued)
- Competitiveness became major issue
- Unsustainable situation: bubble pressures + foreign financing dependence + weakened conditions in related countries

### The Depression Phase (2001-2002)

#### High Debt Vulnerability
- High debt levels left Argentina vulnerable to shock
- Shock materialized (specifics not detailed in this section)


<!-- pp. 1361-1380 -->

## Argentina 1998-2012: Depression Phase

### Contagion from Late 1990s EM Crises

- **Argentina** experienced ripple effects from late 1990s crises in other emerging markets
- **Capital inflows fell by 10% of GDP**, triggering severe external pressure
- Policy makers responded with aggressive tightening: **short rates hiked 173%**
- **Currency collapsed**: real FX fell 77%
- **GDP contracted 15%**, stock prices fell 82%
- Unemployment increased 3%
- Currency weakness drove **inflation to 32%** during depression—normal for this type of crisis given Argentina's history of poorly-controlled inflation

### Risk Factors for Inflation

- Argentina had approximately **half of the classic "risk factors"** for bigger inflation spirals
- Biggest risk factor: **long history of poorly-controlled inflation**
- Financial institutions under considerable pressure during crisis
- Central bank **depleted reserves by 66%** defending the currency before abandoning the peg

### Debt Dynamics in Depression

- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt-to-GDP rose 118% (62% annualized)**
- Two drivers of debt increase:
  1. **Currency depreciation** increased burden of foreign-denominated debts
  2. Government borrowed more to respond to crisis (peak **fiscal deficit of 4% of GDP**)
- Deleveraging attribution chart shows these components visually

### The Reflation Phase

#### Policy Choices: Currency vs. Printing

- Key determinant of balance of payments/currency crises: how policy makers respond to adverse capital flows
- Two options:
  1. Let currency depreciate and allow financial tightening (painful but typically necessary)
  2. Print money to compensate for capital outflows (can be inflationary)
- **Argentina abandoned currency peg** after shorter-than-average "ugly" phase
- Allowed sufficient tightening to:
  - Reduce import spending
  - **Improve current account by 7% of GDP**
  - Make currency more attractive to hold

#### Aggressive Policy Response

- Argentina **very aggressive in managing financial institutions and bad debts**
- Used **6 out of 9 classic policy levers**:
  - **Nationalized banks**
  - Provided liquidity
  - IMF assistance program
  - Structural reforms for labor market flexibility

#### Deleveraging Success

- Over adjustment period, **debt-to-GDP fell 140% (15% annualized)**
- Reduction came mostly from **nominal income rising**
- **Real FX bottomed at -42%** during the "beautiful period" (post-depression recovery)
- Devalued currency set up Argentina for **renewed competitiveness**

#### Recovery Timeline

- **5 years** before real GDP reached prior peak
- Equity prices (in USD terms) recovered within **7 years**

#### Political Impact

- Crisis helped **Eduardo Duhalde** (populist leader) take power
- Crisis created political opportunity through economic devastation

### Chart Deck Appendix Summary

The extensive chart appendix (multiple pages of data visualizations) tracks:

**Indebtedness:**
- Total debt peaked ~180% of GDP around 2002
- Debt service peaked near 250% of GDP
- FX debt peaked ~140% of GDP around 2002-2003
- Debt growth spiked 60%+ annualized, then fell sharply

**Monetary and Fiscal Policy:**
- Nominal short rates spiked to nearly 200% at crisis peak
- Money supply (M0) rose from ~4% to ~11% of GDP post-crisis
- Fiscal balance swung from -4% deficit (2001-2002) to +3% surplus (mid-2000s)

**Economic Conditions:**
- Real GDP (indexed) fell to ~90, then recovered to ~145 by 2012
- Real growth bottomed near -15% Y/Y, recovered to +10-13% range
- GDP gap reached -18% at trough
- Core inflation spiked to 35% Y/Y, remained elevated (20-28%) through 2012
- Nominal long rates and growth diverged significantly post-crisis

**Markets:**
- Equity prices (USD, indexed) collapsed from ~160 to ~20, recovered to ~170 by 2012
- Nominal rates (short and long) both spiked above 150-200%
- Yield curve (SR-LR) showed extreme stress, reaching near 10% at peak
- Real FX vs TWI fell -60% to -80%, then partially recovered
- FX returns for foreign investors (indexed) collapsed to ~60, then surged to ~270
- Gold price (local FX) increased more than 20x from pre-crisis levels

**External Position:**
- Reserves (USD, indexed) fell from ~110 to ~40, then recovered to ~160-180
- Capital inflows fell from ~18% to -8% of GDP
- Imports fell from ~20% to ~5% of GDP, then recovered to ~20%
- Current account improved from -6% to +4% of GDP
- Capital outflows volatile, ranging -10% to +2% of GDP
- Exports rose from ~13% to ~25% of GDP post-crisis

---

## Iceland 2005-2016: Inflationary Deleveraging Case

### Overview

- **Transitory inflationary deleveraging cycle** between 2005 and 2016
- Classic example of a country **"letting its currency go"** in face of external pressures
- Produces temporary inflation but gives policy makers **more flexibility to set interest rates**

### The Bubble Phase (2005-2008)

#### Core Dynamics

- Self-reinforcing cycle of:
  - Unsustainably strong capital inflows
  - Rising debt
  - Strong equity returns
  - Strong housing returns

#### Debt Accumulation

- **Debts rose 565% of GDP** during bubble phase
- Pre-crisis peak: **1173% of GDP**
- High share in foreign currencies: **691% of GDP**
- Left Iceland with **large exposure to pullback in foreign capital**

#### Financing and Growth

- Investment inflows averaged **37% of GDP**
- Financed **current account deficit of 18% of GDP**
- Combined with rising debt and capital, growth was strong at **7%**
- **Equities averaged 12% annualized returns** over bubble period
- Strong asset returns encouraged more borrowing and stimulated growth

#### Unsustainable Situation

- Bubble pressures + dependence on foreign financing + weakened conditions in related countries = unsustainable
- Iceland particularly vulnerable due to foreign financing dependence

### The Depression Phase (2008-2010)

#### Crisis Trigger

- High debt levels left Iceland vulnerable to shock
- Shock came via **2008 global financial crisis**

#### Capital Flight and Policy Response

- **Capital inflows fell 49% of GDP**
- Policy makers **hiked short rates by 4%** (tightening)
- **Real FX fell 29%**—meaningful currency decline

#### Economic Collapse

- **GDP fell 11%**
- **Stock prices fell 96%**
- **Home prices fell 15%**
- **Unemployment increased 5%**

#### Moderate Inflation

- Currency weakness contributed to moderate inflation
- Peaked at **18% during depression phase**
- **Low compared to other similar cases**
- Makes sense: Iceland had only **~quarter of classic "risk factors"** for bigger inflation spirals
- Biggest risk factor: **high foreign-denominated debts**

#### Financial Sector Stress

- Financial institutions under considerable pressure
- Central bank **drew down reserves by 18%** defending currency
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense**

#### Debt Dynamics

- Despite needing deleveraging, **debt-to-GDP rose 122% (46% annualized)**
- Two causes:
  1. Currency depreciation increased burden of foreign-denominated debts
  2. Government borrowed more in response (peak **fiscal deficit of 5% of GDP**)

### Measurement Notes

- Gauges shown are composite measures from comprehensive chart deck
- First two charts: measure bubble/depression conditions and tightness/easiness of money/credit
- For each gauge, difference from zero = extent of bubble
- Crossing above/below zero = shifting into/out of bubble phase

<!-- pp. 1381-1400 -->

## Iceland 2005-2016: Reflation Phase (continued)

### Policy Response to Capital Flight

- **Critical policy choice**: Whether to allow financial tightening (painful but necessary) or print money to compensate for capital outflows (inflationary)
- **Iceland's approach**: After longer-than-average "ugly" phase, allowed sufficient tightening to:
  - Reduce import spending (current account improved 13% of GDP)
  - Make currency more attractive to hold
- **Aggressive financial management**: Pulled 6 of 9 classic policy levers
  - Nationalized banks
  - Provided liquidity
  - Received IMF assistance program

### Deleveraging Results (2010-2016)

- **Debt reduction**: Fell 1,037% (170% annualized) as % of GDP
- **Composition of deleveraging**:
  - Balanced mix of outright debt reduction
  - Rising income (primarily higher real growth)
- **Currency devaluation impact**: Real FX bottomed at -22% during beautiful period, creating renewed competitiveness
- **Recovery timeline**: 8 years before real GDP reached prior peak
- **Incomplete recovery**: Equity prices in USD terms haven't fully recovered

---

## Russia 2005-2011 Case Study

### The Bubble Phase (2005-2008)

**Classic inflationary deleveraging** — example of "letting its currency go" under external pressure, producing temporary inflation but giving policymakers flexibility to set interest rates

#### Bubble Dynamics

- **Self-reinforcing cycle**: Strong capital inflows → rising debt → strong growth → strong asset returns
- **Peak debt levels**: 66% of GDP, with high FX exposure (21% of GDP in foreign currencies)
- **Vulnerability**: Large exposure to pullback in foreign capital
- **Investment inflows**: Averaged ~10% of GDP
- **Growth**: Strong at 8% annually
- **GDP gap**: Peaked at 8% (high economic activity levels)
- **Asset returns**: Equities averaged 46% annualized returns
- **Unsustainable combination**: Bubble pressures + dependence on foreign financing + weakened conditions in related countries

### The Depression Phase (2008-2009)

**Self-reinforcing bust** triggered by 2008 global financial crisis and oil price collapse

#### Crisis Impact

- **Foreign funding collapse**: Capital inflows fell 21% of GDP
- **Policy tightening**: Short rates hiked 19%
- **Currency crash**: Real FX fell 21%
- **GDP contraction**: Fell 8%
- **Stock market collapse**: Down 71%
- **Unemployment**: Rose 3%
- **Inflation**: Peaked at 15% during depression (moderate vs. similar cases)
  - **Why relatively low**: Russia had only ~25% of classic "risk factors" for bigger inflation spirals
  - Biggest missing factor: No long history of poorly-controlled inflation
- **Financial institutions**: Came under considerable pressure
- **Reserve drawdown**: Central bank spent down reserves 44% to defend currency, then abandoned defense

#### Deleveraging Attribution (Depression Period)

- **Debt as % GDP rose 17%** (12% annualized) despite needing deleveraging
- **Reason**: Currency depreciation increased burden of foreign-denominated debt
- **Charts show**: Rough gauges composed from compendium of stats
  - Bubble/depression gauge: Distance from zero = extent of bubble; crossing zero = shift into/out of bubble
  - Tightening/easing gauge: Measures money/credit conditions

### The Reflation Phase (2009-2011)

**Key policy choice**: Allow tightening (painful but necessary) vs. print money to replace outflows (inflationary)

#### Russia's Response

- **Shorter "ugly" phase** than average
- **Allowed sufficient tightening** to make currency attractive to hold
- **Aggressive management**: Pulled 4 of 9 classic policy levers
- **Debt reduction**: Fell 14% (8% annualized) as % of GDP
- **Source of reduction**: Mostly from nominal income rising (not outright debt paydown)
- **Currency impact**: Real FX bottomed at 11% during beautiful period, setting up renewed competitiveness
- **Recovery timeline**: 3 years before real GDP reached prior peak
- **Incomplete recovery**: Equity prices in USD terms haven't fully recovered

- Debt service: Peaked at ~120% GDP
- FX debt: Fell from ~35% to ~20% GDP
- Debt growth: Peaked at ~17% annualized, turned negative post-crisis

- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: Peaked at bubble (~2.0), crashed to depression (~-0.5), reflated to (~0.5) by 2017
- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: Showed peak tightening during crisis, eased significantly during reflation to peak (~1.5) by 2017
- **Core Inflation**: Ranged from ~6% to peak ~18% Y/Y, settled to ~3% by 2017
