## Russia 2012–2016 Case Study: Inflationary Deleveraging

### Overview: A Textbook Inflationary Deleveraging

- **Russia 2012-2016** represents a **transitory inflationary deleveraging cycle**
- Classic example of "letting its currency go" in response to external pressures
- Currency depreciation produces temporary inflation but gives policymakers more flexibility on interest rates
- Unlike many cases, Russia lacked a broad-based bubble pre-crisis but built substantial debt

### The Bubble Phase (Pre-2014)

#### Debt Accumulation Without Typical Bubble

- No broad-based bubble, but **substantial debt buildup driven by unsustainably strong capital inflows**
- Debt rose **19% of GDP** to pre-crisis peak of **89% of GDP** during leveraging phase
- **15% of GDP in foreign currency debt** — created major vulnerability to capital flight
- Average capital inflows of **6% of GDP** in years before crisis
- Created dependence on continuous foreign financing — an unsustainable situation

### The Depression Phase (2014-2016)

#### Trigger and Mechanisms

- **Oil price collapse** was the external shock that exposed vulnerabilities
- Pre-crisis debt service burden: **32% of GDP** — made Russia highly vulnerable
- Capital inflows reversed dramatically: **fell by 8% of GDP**
- Policymakers responded by **hiking short rates by 7%**
- **Real FX fell 30%** — severe currency depreciation
- **GDP declined 4%**, stocks fell 46%

#### Inflation Dynamics

- Currency weakness contributed to **moderate inflation peaking at 18%** during depression
- Relatively low compared to similar cases because Russia had **only ~25% of classic "risk factors" for inflation spirals**
- Biggest missing risk factor: **no long history of poorly-controlled inflation**
- Contrast with countries prone to hyperinflation

#### Central Bank Response

- Initially **defended currency by drawing down reserves 26%**
- Eventually **abandoned currency defense** — let ruble fall
- This was the critical policy choice that defined the deleveraging path

#### Debt Dynamics During Depression

- **Debt/GDP rose 19% (9% annualized)** despite need for deleveraging
- Two drivers of increase:
  1. **Currency fall** increased burden of foreign-denominated debt
  2. **Government borrowing** to respond to crisis (peak fiscal deficit: **4% of GDP**)
- Financial institutions came under considerable pressure

#### Composite Gauges Summary

- **Bubble & Depression Gauge**: Shows clear bubble peak followed by sharp depression
- **Tightening & Easing Gauge**: Peak tightening during crisis before eventual easing
- **Core Inflation**: Spiked to 18% during depression phase

### The Reflation Phase (2016+)

#### Policy Response

- After "ugly phase," policymakers **allowed sufficient tightening to stabilize currency**
- Made ruble more attractive to hold
- **Aggressive management of financial institutions and bad debts**
- Pulled **4 out of 9 classic policy levers**

#### Deleveraging Mechanics

- **Debt/GDP fell 7% (14% annualized)** during reflation period
- Reduction came from **balanced mix**:
  - Outright debt reduction
  - Rising income (nominal GDP growth)
- **Real FX bottomed at -17%** during reflation — created **renewed competitiveness**

#### Recovery Status

- Real GDP **has not yet reached prior peak**
- Equity prices in USD terms **haven't fully recovered**
- Demonstrates painful but necessary adjustment process

### Key Policy Lessons

#### The Central Choice in Currency Crises

- Critical decision: How to respond to adverse capital flows
- Two options:
  1. **Allow tightening** to flow through (painful but resolves crisis)
  2. **Print money** to replace outflows (inflationary)
- Russia chose option 1 after initially trying to defend the currency

#### Why the Approach Worked

- Shorter-than-average "ugly phase" because policymakers allowed sufficient tightening
- Currency stabilization made assets more attractive
- Aggressive financial sector cleanup prevented zombie institutions
- Lower currency improved export competitiveness

### Data Visualizations Context

- **Nominal Short Rate**: Spiked to ~25% during 2007-12 crisis period
- **Money0 Level**: Rose from 8% to 11% GDP (2002-2012), then stabilized around 10%
- **Fiscal Balance**: Swung from +10% surplus to -6% deficit
- **Real GDP Growth**: Collapsed from +10% to -8% during crisis
- **GDP Gap**: Peaked at +9% bubble, fell to -4% depression
- **Core Inflation**: Volatile, spiking from 8% to 18% during crisis
- **Nominal Growth vs Nominal Long Rate**: Dramatic divergence during crisis (nominal growth fell to -10% while rates stayed elevated)
- **Equity Prices**: Rose from index 20 to 130, crashed to 30, recovered to 60
- **Yield Curve**: Steepened dramatically to 16% during crisis
- **Real FX**: Rose 30% during bubble, crashed 30% during crisis
- **Gold Price (Local FX)**: Surged from index 100 to 400+ during crisis period
- **Reserves**: Built from index 20 to 140, then drew down to 80
- **Capital Inflows**: Peaked at 20% GDP, reversed to -5% during crisis
- **Current Account**: Fell from 12% surplus to near 0%
- **Imports/Exports**: Both fell as share of GDP during crisis

### Deleveraging Attribution Analysis

#### Depression Period (2014-2016)

- **Total debt change: +9%** annualized
- **Inflation**: Largest positive component (orange)
- **FX Debt Change due to FX Move**: Major contributor (light orange)
- **Change in Domestic Debt**: Positive (green)
- **Real Growth**: Negative drag (red)
- Net effect: Debt increased despite depression

#### Reflation Period (2016-2016)

- **Total debt change: -14%** annualized
- **Inflation**: Largest reduction component (orange bottom)
- **Change in Domestic Debt**: Major reduction (green)
- **FX Debt Change**: Significant reduction (light orange/salmon)
- **Real Growth**: Positive contribution (blue top)
- Achieved substantial deleveraging through all mechanisms


<!-- pp. 1421-1440 -->

## Appendix: Macroprudential Policies

### Definition and Purpose

- **Macroprudential policies** = tools for directing credit through central bank's regulatory authorities
- Distinct from monetary policy: monetary policy makes credit broadly available without choosing winners; fiscal policy apportions it; macroprudential sits between
- Need arises from credit differentiation: bubbles in one area, starvation in another
- Classic countercyclical approach: easier credit standards during busts (lower down payments in housing bust), harder standards during bubbles

### Historical Context and Usage

- **Not a new tool** — used throughout modern monetary policy history
- Federal Reserve changed margin requirements for equity holdings **23 times** since formation
  - Pattern: tightened during rallies/excess stock market credit, eased during bear markets/depressed volumes
- **1990s-mid-2000s = historical exception** when macroprudential tools became rare
- Post-crisis: global return to macroprudential tools as key management instrument

### When Macroprudential Policies Are Deployed

**Used when interest rate policy loses effectiveness:**
- Economy needs stimulation but rates already at zero
- Tightening needed in specific areas but overall rate hikes would drag growth

**Used to redirect credit strategically:**
- From credit-starved sectors
- Away from frothy assets/lending

**Resemblance to fiscal policy:**
- Can benefit some at expense of others (generally avoided)
- Useful during deleveraging to manage differentiated conditions

### QE and Macroprudential Interaction

- **QE often fuels pockets of frothiness** in asset markets while broader economy still recovering
- Great Depression and WWII examples: central banks used macroprudential measures to reduce froth pockets while keeping overall monetary policy easy

### Policy Tools Taxonomy

**Demand-side measures (changing demand for credit):**
- Loan-to-value ratios
- Debt service-to-income ratios
- Loan maturity requirements
- Margin requirements for financial assets
- Cost of loans via interest rate subsidies/tax policy/regulations

**Supply-side measures (changing supply of credit):**
- Capital/reserve requirements for certain lending types
- Portfolio restrictions on assets financial institutions can hold
- Accounting rules on different assets
- Supervisor pressure for certain lending behaviors
- Interest/lending rate ceilings and limitations

### Coordination Requirements

- **Most successful cases** = Federal Reserve, Congress, executive branch, regulatory bodies coordinated
- Lawmakers and executive branch armed government bodies (including Fed) with tools and authority
- New institutions created to implement and monitor regulations
- **Example: WWII effort** — Congress revised laws to give Fed needed authority, Roosevelt used executive orders to supplement

### Implementation Success Factors

**Mixed track record — what worked:**
- Effective policies used for long periods or expanded; unsuccessful ones ended within months
- **Significant experimentation and flexibility** critical to success
- Financial system evolution → changing toolset as cost/benefit trade-offs shifted
- Financial innovations enabled circumvention → policies adapted or abandoned

**What didn't work:**
- Policies with bad second-order consequences continued too long
- **Regulation Q example**: caps on deposit interest rates helped smaller banks initially (1930s), but by 1950s created distortions driving deposits into shadow banking
- **S&L deregulation (early 1980s)**: meant to help institutions cope with tight monetary policy, contributed to S&L crisis beginning mid-1980s

### Key Questions for Policy Makers

**1. Where are bubbles emerging? How confident are you they are bubbles?**
- **1935-36 tightening example**: responded to rapid stock appreciation, feared bubble, but tightening was excessive for deleveraging economy → severe recession, stocks fell 60% (1937-1938)
- **"Mopping up" approach (recent decades)**: avoid pricking bubbles, deal with aftermath instead
  - Rationale: difficulty knowing when market is mispricing
  - Example: despite housing market concerns, regulators didn't aggressively target frothy mortgage lending in 2006

**2. Which sectors are credit-starved? Need for differentiated policy?**
- **1950s framework** — four tests developed:
  - How effective is general monetary policy in balancing credit provision?
  - How potentially destabilizing is credit growth in the specific sector?
  - How important is credit to the sector's growth?
  - How effective would selective credit controls be?

**3. Political costs of picking winners/losers?**

**4. Which policy tools — demand vs. supply, or both?**

**5. How to measure effectiveness?**

**6. Second- and third-order consequences? How to handle them?**
- Often emerge years later
- Flexibility crucial for adaptation

**7. Policy coordination — legislators, executive, central bank?**
- **Most successful US cases** = broad government coordination
- **WWII example**: Congress + Roosevelt executive orders gave Fed authority
- **Volcker era**: policy makers given flexibility to shift nimbly as circumstances changed
- Biggest failures from low flexibility (interest rate caps not flexibly adapted → negative consequences)
- **Current structural differences**: UK vested power in BoE and Financial Policy Committee (FPC); US uses multiple agencies coordinated through Financial Stability Oversight Council (FSOC) post-crisis

## Historical Cases: Margin Requirements

### Mechanism
- **Margin requirement** = collateral (often cash) investors must provide to buy investment using credit
- Increasing requirement → downward pressure on credit for financial asset purchases

### Post-1934 Usage Pattern
- Securities Exchange Act of 1934 gave Fed ability to set margin requirements
- **Used counter-cyclically**: increase when asset prices booming and credit for purchases rising; decrease in opposite situation
- **Regulation T** = margin requirements for brokers
- **Regulation U** = margin requirement for banks

### Decline After 1974
- Federal Reserve mostly stopped using this lever post-1974
- Derivatives and other credit-based asset purchase methods made it easy to sidestep

## Historical Cases: Underwriting Standards

### Great Depression Era

**Federal Home Loan Bank System (1932):**
- Quasi-central bank for S&Ls
- Provided liquidity via advances or collateralized lending
- Set underwriting standards and collateral restrictions

**Federal Housing Administration (1934):**
- Insured home loans
- **Easier standards**: 80% loan-to-value, 20-year maturity for eligible loans

**Electric Home and Farm Authority (1934):**
- Cheap loans for home electric appliances
- <10% interest rate, 5% down payment, up to 36 months
- Ended 1942

**1935 National Bank Changes:**
- Lowered loan-to-value from 50% to 60%
- Extended maturity restrictions from up to 5 years to 10 years

**1934-1937 FHA Property Improvement Insurance:**
- Insured up to 20% of loans (up to 5-year maturity) for improving residential properties

**1938 FHA Home Loan Expansion:**
- 90% loan-to-value, 25-year maturity now eligible

### World War II

**Roosevelt Executive Order (1941):**
- Limited installment credit for consumer durable goods purchases
- Goal: direct production toward national defense vs. domestic consumption
- Quote: "liberal terms for such credit tend to stimulate demand for consumers' durable goods, the production of which requires the materials, skills, and equipment needed for national defense"

**Regulation W:**
- Federal Reserve enforced tighter underwriting on all consumer installment loans

### Postwar Period

**Regulation X (October 1950):**
- Worried about rising mortgage debt, aimed to tighten housing credit
- Loan-to-value and maturity caps on residential real estate loans, increasingly restrictive as loan size grew
- **Target**: reduce 1951 housing production by one-third of 1950 levels

**1950 FHA/VA Tightening (Truman's request):**
- Raised down payment requirement 5%
- Decreased maximum FHA loan on single-family homes from $16,000 to $14,000
- Maximum maturity of 25 years
- **First restrictive actions** in FHA/VA's nearly 20-year history

**Reversal (mid-1953 recession + political pressure):**
- Measures removed
- August 1954: Congress reversed FHA/VA actions, reducing down payment requirements and raising maximum FHA loan

**Late-1954 Frothy Housing Market:**
- Fed didn't want to tighten (economy just recovering from recession)
- **Multi-pronged macroprudential approach instead**:
  1. FHA/VA raised down payments, reduced maximum maturities from 30 to 25 years (tweaked and eventually reversed in subsequent years)
  2. Field offices instructed to "intensify surveys of local housing markets, and take coordinated steps to restrain federal underwriting of mortgages in localities where housing surpluses were found"
  3. Federal Home Loan Bank Board (FHLBB) asked Federal Home Loan banks to curb loan commitments to thrifts; September 1954 implemented official restraints on S&L bank lending
  4. Federal Reserve Bank of New York restrained mortgage warehouse lending (commercial banks extending interim loans to finance mortgage origination by nonbank lenders) — **more than doubled** August 1954-August 1955

**Credit Control Act (1969):**
- Gave Federal Reserve ability to use credit controls targeting certain sectors
- Kept overall monetary policy easy while tightening inflationary sectors
- Levers: "maximum rate of interest, maximum maturity, minimum periodic payment, maximum period between payments, and other specification or limitation of the terms and conditions of any extension of credit"
- **Not used until Volcker era**

**1982 Deregulation:**
- Congress abolished loan-to-value ratio and maturity restrictions for national banks

## Historical Cases: Voluntary Guidelines

### Purpose
- Policy makers imposed voluntary restraints/guidelines on banks
- Goal: curb lending for speculative or non-productive ends

**1947 Congressional Encouragement:**
- Banks encouraged to "restrict voluntarily their lending and investing programs"
- Implemented after Fed's consumer installment loan limits expired

**Defense Production Act of 1950 (Korean War):**
- Gave Federal Reserve authority to establish "voluntary" credit restraints

<!-- pp. 1441-1452 -->

## Historical Macroprudential Measures (Continued)

### Voluntary Credit Restraint Programs

- **1965 Balance of Payments Committee**: Recommended "voluntary" lending restraints monitored by Federal Reserve
  - Goal: Reduce net capital outflows by over 15% from previous year
  - Set initial 5% ceiling on foreign loan growth—first quantitative credit target
  - **1968**: President Johnson passed Executive Order allowing Fed to make program mandatory, but Fed declined citing high voluntary participation
  - Programs continued until 1974

- **1966**: Fed prompted banks to slow business loans during high credit creation and rising inflation
  - Non-cooperating banks had their discount window access limited
  - Policy ended after economy slowed

- **Carter's Special Credit Restraint Program (March-July 1980)**
  - Banks, bank holding companies, finance companies, and foreign branches asked to limit loan growth to 6-9%
  - Targeted speculative/inflationary lending while protecting small businesses, farmers, homebuyers
  - Banks encouraged to treat credit for automobiles, home mortgages, and home improvements "normally"
  - Restraints on credit cards, unsecured consumer loans, and "speculative" uses: inventory buildup beyond needs, takeovers/mergers that could be postponed, commodity/precious metals speculation
  - Required periodic reports on lending activities

- **European parallels**: National credit councils in France and Italy announced which sectors needed more credit or were over-burdened, especially post-war

### Reserve Requirements as Macroprudential Tools

**Origins and Evolution**

- **1800s**: State governments instituted first reserve requirements to ensure state-chartered banks had enough reserves (gold/specie) for bank notes and deposit withdrawals
- **National Bank Act (1863)**: Introduced first nationwide reserve requirements
- **Federal Reserve Act of 1913**: Congress eased requirements, viewing them as less critical with Fed as lender of last resort

**Active Counter-Cyclical Use**

- **1930s onward**: Reserve requirements became useful counter-cyclical tool
- **1935**: Congress gave Fed authority to set reserve requirements
- **1938**: Fed lowered reserve requirements in conjunction with Roosevelt's recovery program
- **1940s adjustments**:
  - 1942: Requirements eased three times
  - During war: Fed held requirements steady
  - 1948: Requirements tightened three times back to statutory caps
  - 1949-1951: Adjusted nine times (eased 1949, tightened 1951)
- **Tightening periods**: Fed raised reserve requirements during 1966-69, 1973, and 1979-80

**Loopholes and Evasion**

- New funding instruments emerged to bypass requirements: commercial paper, eurodollars, repurchase agreements, large-denomination CDs
- **1969 Fed response**: Addressed loopholes
  - Capped bank borrowing from overseas branches at 10% (to stop Eurodollar borrowing)
  - Set 10% limit on asset sales to overseas branches
  - **1970**: Limits increased to 20%
  - **1973**: Both lowered to 8% (matching large-denomination CD requirements)

**Federal Home Loan Bank Board (FHLBB)**

- **Late 1960s**: Used reserve requirements to affect mortgage lending
  - Reduced requirements when savings declined (increasing liquidity)
  - Raised requirements when lending was high or liquidity abundant

**Volcker's Anti-Inflation Measures**

- **October 1979**: Fed increased rates and reserve requirements to combat inflation
  - Reserve requirements on wholesale liabilities rose to 8% (included time deposits, Eurodollar borrowings, repos with government/agency collateral, federal funds borrowings)
  - **March 1980**: Requirements increased to 10%
  - **April 1980**: Lowered to 5%, then eliminated entirely in July

**1980 Asset-Based Reserve Requirements**

- Fed imposed unprecedented asset-based reserve requirements under 1969 Credit Control Act
  - All lenders required to hold 15% special deposit on specific consumer credit types
  - Money market funds subject to same requirements
  - Both later lowered to 7.5%
  - Money market funds required to file monthly reports
- **August 1980**: Fed removed all restrictions
- **End of 1980**: Congress terminated Credit Control Act

**Later Adjustments**

- **1990 and 1992**: Fed lowered reserve requirements to promote credit availability
- **1968-69 and 1973-74**: FHLBB eased requirements when mortgage lending liquidity was squeezed, with "at most a limited, positive impact"

**European Approaches**

- France and Italy used differentiated reserve requirements to direct credit—e.g., giving public banks more lenient requirements or excluding long-term/export loans
- Regulators often capped loan amounts or central bank financing access for lending—similar downward pressure on credit supply
  - France had "rediscount ceiling" until 1972: cap on borrowing from central bank
  - Caps often differed by sector or loan type to encourage/discourage certain lending
  - Example: Banque de France set lending quotas in agricultural sectors facing overproduction
  - Sometimes caps targeted all lending to corporations or households

### Interest Rate Ceilings as Macroprudential Tools

**Early State Programs**

- **Early 1900s**: States limited deposit interest rates as part of deposit insurance programs

**Federal Reserve Interest Rate Controls**

- **1927 Federal Reserve Act Amendment**: Cap placed on interest paid by national banks to state banks
- **Banking Act of 1933**: Fed could regulate maximum interest rates on time and savings deposits
  - **November 1933**: Regulation Q imposed 3% ceiling
  - After market rates declined, Fed lowered to 2.5% to reduce bank costs
  - Regulation Q allowed Fed to set maximum FDIC rates to non-member insured banks
  - Fed later raised maximum back to 3% (for savings/time deposits with 6+ month maturities) to allow banks to attract depositors
  - **1960-1961**: Fed raised Regulation Q ceilings as market rates rose

**Federal Home Loan Bank Board (FHLBB) Dividend Ceilings**

- **1934-1989**: FHLBB supervised savings and loans
  - Set informal ceilings on dividends paid by S&Ls, generally 25-50 basis points above Fed funds rate

**Interest Rate Regulation Act of 1966**

- FDIC and FHLBB could set interest rate ceilings on deposits at mutual savings banks and S&Ls
- Fed could set rates for specific deposit classes
  - Set 5.5% limit on single-maturity CDs
  - Lowered maximum interest rates on time deposits
  - **1973**: CD ceiling removed

**Deregulation**

- **1978**: Fed allowed banks to issue money market certificates with floating rate ceilings pegged to six-month Treasury bill—banks could now compete with money market funds (relaxation of Regulation Q)
- **March 1980**: Depository Institutions Deregulation and Monetary Control Act eliminated Regulation Q (deemed unsuccessful due to workarounds)
- **1986**: Ceilings gradually phased out

### Supervisory Guidance and "Direct Pressure"

**Guidance to Curtail Lending in Booms**

- **Post-WWI**: Fed wanted to direct credit toward productive rather than speculative uses via supervisory "direct pressure"
- **Mid-1927**: Policymakers nervous about stock market speculation
  - Previous year: stocks rose almost 100%, credit creation booming
  - **February 1929**: Fed issued statement denouncing speculation, stating it wouldn't lend to banks extending credit for speculation
  - Statement was "largely ineffectual" at stemming speculation

- **November 24, 1947**: Supervisors urged bank caution due to excessive credit creation and falling standards
  - Banks should "curtail all loans either to individuals or business for speculation in real estate, commodities or securities"

- **1990s-2000s**: Supervisors released several statements on deteriorating lending standards
  - **1995**: Fed warned examiners to watch for excessive easing in underwriting standards and risks of cyclical downturn in regional real estate (due to S&L crisis in 1980s)
  - **1999**: Statement detailed subprime lending risks, suggested raising capital standards for institutions engaged in practice (after several banks failed in previous year)
  - **Two years later**: Agencies quantified new capital standards—banks should hold 1.5x-3x more capital against subprime loans vs. similar assets
  - **Early and mid-2000s**: Federal officials expressed concern over frothy credit growth
  - **2005**: Guidance issued on home equity, commercial real estate lending, and nontraditional mortgages
  - **2013**: Fed and OCC tightened regulation of leveraged loans with 6x leverage limit and threatened fines

**Credit Availability During Busts**

- **April 1938**: President Roosevelt asked regulators to "agree on a more liberal bank examination policy"
  - Treasury and three federal supervisors issued joint statement outlining unified treatment of loans and securities
  - Moved away from mark-to-market accounting for securities
  - Distinguished "investment" vs. "speculative" securities (investment securities assigned top four rating agency grades)

- **End of S&L Crisis**: Administration moved to ease credit crunch
  - **May 1990**: OCC, Fed, FDIC leaders urged senior bank officials to extend loans
  - **March 1991**: Supervisors published report to "clarify regulatory policies"—did not ease standards despite stating "some depository institutions may have become overly cautious in their lending practices"

- **President Clinton**: Pursued new credit availability initiative with concurrent supervisory statement encouraging banks to lend
  - Attempted to make small business lending easier by reducing "appraisal burden" and enhancing appeals of examiners' decisions

- **2007-2009**: Supervisors encouraged banks to lend and work with distressed borrowers without weakening examination standards
